401 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Courtesy Sandwich Shop, Inc. v. Port of New York Authority


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


12 N.Y.2d 379; 190 N.E.2d 402; 240 N.Y.S.2d 1; 1963 N.Y. LEXIS 1263


February 28, 1963, Argued  
April 4, 1963, Decided



DISPOSITION: In the declaratory judgment action: Order reversed, without costs, and matter remitted to Special Term for entry of a judgment that chapter 209 of the Laws of 1962 is constitutional. Question certified answered in the negative.

In the condemnation proceeding: Order of Appellate Division reversed and that of Special Term reinstated, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant port authority challenged the decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court (New York), which reversed the order of the lower court denying respondent landowners' motion for a temporary injunction in its declaratory judgment action. Appellant also challenged the appellate court's decision that reversed the grant of appellant's petition for condemnation and dismissed the condemnation proceeding.


OVERVIEW: Respondent landowners brought a declaratory judgment action seeking a declaration that the statute allowing condemnation for development of the World Trade Center was unconstitutional. Subsequently, appellant port authority brought a condemnation proceeding. The lower court denied respondents' motion for temporary injunction, but the appellate court reversed. The appellate court also reversed the lower court's decision granting the petition for condemnation and order of possession and dismissed the condemnation proceeding. The court reversed, finding that improvement of the port by facilitating the flow of commerce and centralizing all activity incident thereto was a public purpose supporting the condemnation of property for any activity functionally related to that purpose. Further, the public purpose of the development as a whole was not vitiated by allowing portions of structures otherwise devoted to project purposes to be used for the production of incidental revenue for the expenses of all or part of the port development project. Finally, the condemnation procedures prescribed by statute fully protected respondents against any taking for nonpublic purposes.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the orders appealed from, the orders of the lower court in the condemnation proceeding were reinstated, and a judgment was rendered that the statute in question was constitutional. The public purpose of the development of the World Trade Center was not vitiated by allowing portions of the structures to be used for the production of incidental revenue for expenses of the development.


HEADNOTES

Eminent domain -- public use -- "portions" of structures may be used for "incidental revenue" (L. 1962, ch. 209); statute so construed avoids any constitutional doubt -- Congress' consent in 1921 and 1922 to New York-New Jersey plan for Port of New York Authority includes 1962 plan (L. 1962, ch. 209) -- statute not unconstitutional for failure to require that business good will be evaluated -- New Jersey did not concur in New York statute providing for hearing by Authority (L. 1924, ch. 623), hence no such hearing is required -- although statute (L. 1962, ch. 209) prohibits Authority's subsidiary corporation from contracting indebtedness, it clearly authorizes it to condemn properties -- condemnation of railroad properties first does not violate Legislature's determination that railroad portion of plan is inseparable from World Trade Center portion.

1. The State of New York (L. 1921, ch. 154, as amd.) and the State of New Jersey by concurrent legislation created a Port of New York Authority and empowered the Authority to develop the port and to submit supplemental or amendatory plans for such development to the Legislatures of the two States; and Congress consented thereto (U. S. Const., art. I, § 10; 42 U.S. Stat. 174, 822). Such congressional consent is deemed to include a recent plan (in New York, L. 1962, ch. 209; in New Jersey, L. 1962, ch. 8) under which the Authority has organized a subsidiary corporation to condemn and acquire (a) the Hudson & Manhattan Railroad system and (b) an area of about 13 blocks in lower Manhattan to be entirely reconstructed as a World Trade Center except that "portions of such buildings, structures, improvements and areas may * * * be devoted to * * * the production of incidental revenue * * * for the expenses of all or part of the port development project" (L. 1962, ch. 209, § 2). The quoted words should be so interpreted as to avoid doubt under the United States Constitution and the New York Constitution (art. I, § 7) that this condemnation is "for public use". So construed, the quoted words allow only "portions" of structures or sites to be used for revenue purposes. Accordingly, the statute is constitutional, and a temporary injunction against condemnation proceeding is reversed, and an order vesting title in the Authority's subsidiary corporation is reinstated. The procedures of our condemnation statutes will afford a full hearing to any owner of property hereafter sought to be condemned who believes that his particular property is being unconstitutionally taken for revenue purposes primarily.

2. Business good will is ordinarily not compensable, and the statute is not unconstitutional for failing specifically to require such valuation.

3. The Port of New York Authority need not hold any hearing before determining to condemn any particular property in the statutorily designated area (L. 1962, ch. 209). If an earlier statute (L. 1924, ch. 623, § 1) required such a hearing, such statute was conditioned, as usual, on New Jersey's concurrence which was consistently denied by that State.

4. The Port of New York Authority's subsidiary corporation, Port Authority Trans-Hudson Corporation, has clearly been given power of condemnation, even though the statute also says that such subsidiary shall not contract indebtedness (L. 1962, ch. 209, §§ 12, 14, 16).

5. The Legislature's determination that the two parts of the projected development -- the Hudson & Manhattan Railroad system and the World Trade Center -- are inseparable and should "proceed as rapidly as may be practicable" (L. 1962, ch. 209) is not violated by the circumstance that the Port Authority Trans-Hudson Corporation has begun by condemning property required for the railroad project.

 

402 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


New York C. R. Co. v. Lefkowitz


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


12 N.Y.2d 305; 189 N.E.2d 695; 239 N.Y.S.2d 341; 1963 N.Y. LEXIS 1300


February 28, 1963, Argued  
March 28, 1963, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondents, 10 railroad companies brought an action against appellants, the Attorney General for the State of New York and the Public Service Commission, for a declaratory judgment and for a permanent injunction. Four unions intervened. Appellants challenged the judgment of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), affirming an order denying their motion for a change of venue.


OVERVIEW: The railroads sought a declaratory judgment that N.Y. R.R. Law §§ 54-a, 54-b, and 54-c were unconstitutional, and the railroads sought an injunction to restrain further enforcement of those statutes. Appellants sought a change of venue to Albany County. The appellate division certified the question whether its affirmance was appropriate. The State asserted that the railroads had no right to sue in any county except Albany and primarily relied upon N.Y. Civ. Prac. Act § 879. Appellants asserted that a trial where none of the railroads had a principal office was not proper and that N.Y. Civ. Prac. Act § 1287 provided for proceedings against the commission to be in Albany. The court affirmed the judgment. Because two of the railroads operated lines through Westchester County, they were considered as a residents in that county for venue purposes. Because N.Y. Civ. Prac. Act § 879 applied to the granting of temporary injunctions only and not to final injunctions incidental to other relief, it did not require the case to be tried in Albany. The main thrust of the action was to test the constitutionality of the statutes, and the temporary injunctive relief sought was only incidental.


OUTCOME: Noting that it answered the question certified to it in the affirmative, the court affirmed the judgment of the appellate division of the supreme court.


HEADNOTES

Trial -- venue -- injunction -- section 879 of Civil Practice Act, which provides that injunction restraining performance of statutory duty or preventing execution of statute shall not be granted except by Supreme Court in department in which officer or board restrained is located, or duty required to be performed, applies to temporary injunction only and is not applicable to the present action where final injunction is sought as incidental to other relief -- railroad companies operating lines in Westchester County are considered residents of that county for venue purposes (Civ. Prac. Act, § 182).

1. Section 182 of the Civil Practice Act requires that a Supreme Court action be tried "in the county in which one of the parties resided at the commencement thereof". Two of plaintiff railroad companies operated their lines through Westchester County and, for venue purposes, are considered residents in that county and, accordingly, under that statute, Westchester County was the proper venue.

2. Section 879 of the Civil Practice Act provides that: "Where a duty is imposed by statute upon a state officer or board of state officers, an injunction order to restrain him or them, or a person employed by him or them, from the performance of that duty, or to prevent the execution of the statute, shall not be granted except by the supreme court at a term thereof sitting in the department in which the officer or board is located, or the duty is required to be performed; and upon notice of the application therefor to the officer, board or other person to be restrained." The present action was brought by 10 railroads for judgment declaring that sections 54-a, 54-b and 54-c of the Railroad Law are unconstitutional and for a permanent injunction restraining further enforcement of any of the three statutes. Section 879 refers to temporary injunctions only and is not applicable to the present action. It does not apply to final injunctions incidental to other relief.

3. Section 1287 of the Civil Practice Act is a part of article 78 of the Civil Practice Act and does not refer to actions but to statutory proceedings in the nature of certiorari, mandamus and prohibition.

 

403 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Bradley v. Waterfront Com. of New York Harbor


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


12 N.Y.2d 276; 189 N.E.2d 601; 239 N.Y.S.2d 97; 1963 N.Y. LEXIS 1354; 52 L.R.R.M. 2571


January 22, 1963, Argued  
February 28, 1963, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff union appealed from an order of the trial court finding that amendments to the Waterfront Commission Act (WCA) §§ 8, 8-a, were constitutional. The union contended that the National Labor Relations Act preempted the WCA.


OVERVIEW: The union represented longshoremen registered and licensed by the commission. The Waterfront Commission Act (WCA) §§ 8, 8-a, was amended to expand a prohibition on the union from employing persons convicted of felonies or other specified offenses. The union contended that the National Labor Relations Act and the Labor-Management Reporting and Disclosure Act of 1959 preempted the WCA. The court affirmed the judgment, finding that the amendment only expanded the provision, previously found constitutional, to include employees and crimes of moral turpitude and that state laws regulating the responsibility of union officials were exempted from the Labor-Management Reporting and Disclosure Act of 1959. The court also concluded that barring convicted felons from the union was a reasonable means for eliminating corruption.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment finding that amendments to The Waterfront Commission Act were constitutional.


HEADNOTES

Labor unions -- Waterfront Commission Act -- section 8 of Waterfront Commission Act had previously been declared valid ( De Veau v. Braisted, 363 U.S. 144, affg. 5 N Y 2d 236) -- amendments necessary to achieve original objectives also valid -- subject matter not pre-empted by Federal legislation -- no violation of due process.

In a prior action it was determined that the subject matter of section 8 of the Waterfront Commission Act had not been pre-empted by Federal legislation and that it did not violate the due process clause of the Fourteenth Amendment. ( De Veau v. Braisted, 363 U.S. 144, affg. 5 N Y 2d 236.) Thereafter, the Waterfront Commission, having concluded that the provisions of the statute were being circumvented, said section 8 was amended. The amending legislation is not invalid. It did no more than effect such changes as were deemed necessary to achieve the original objectives of the statute. Congress did not intend to pre-empt the State regulation contained in section 8 either in its original or amended form and the statute in its amended form does not offend against due process.

Wholesale Laundry Bd. of Trade, Inc. v. New York


Court of Appeals of New York


12 N.Y.2d 998; 189 N.E.2d 623; 239 N.Y.S.2d 128; 1963 N.Y. LEXIS 1357; 47 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P50,789

February 28, 1963. - Decided

405 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Schwartz v. Heyden Newport Chem. Corp.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


12 N.Y.2d 212; 188 N.E.2d 142; 237 N.Y.S.2d 714; 1963 N.Y. LEXIS 1428; 4 A.L.R.3d 814


January 8, 1963, Argued  
January 23, 1963, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant individual sought to recover damages based on negligence and breach of warranty from defendant corporation for injuries allegedly caused by the corporation's product, to which the individual was exposed 15 years earlier. The trial court dismissed the complaint based upon limitations. The Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York) affirmed, and the individual appealed.


OVERVIEW: A product manufactured by the corporation was inserted into the individual's sinuses in 1944 to make them perceptible in X-rays. Some of the substance allegedly remained in the individual's head and produced a carcinoma that required the removal of an eye in 1957. The lawsuit was filed in 1959. The court held that the causes of action for breach of warranty ran from the date of sale. The court held that, where the nature and origin of the asserted liability was a negligent act, the statute of limitations could not be avoided through an allegation that the corporation had a continuing duty to warn of possible damaging consequences and that any duty to recall the product from the market had no bearing on the rights of those to whom it was administered years previously. The court refused to depart from the rule that the individual's injury occurred when the harmful substance was introduced into his body. The court held that the simple assertion that the accrual rule for negligence claims was unjust did not justify abandonment of prior holdings and that changes in limitations were the legislature's responsibility.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment granting the corporation's motion to dismiss the complaint on the ground that the individual's action for negligence and breach of warranty was barred by the statute of limitations.


HEADNOTES

Limitation of actions -- warranty -- negligence -- "Umbrathor", manufactured by defendant, was inserted in plaintiff's sinuses in 1944 and allegedly produced carcinoma, requiring removal of eye in 1957 -- causes of action based on warranty barred (Civ. Prac. Act, § 48, subd. 1) -- defendant's alleged continuing duty to warn of danger did not extend time -- Schmidt v. Merchants Desp. Transp. Co. (270 N. Y. 287) controlling as to time when negligence cause of action accrued.

1. In 1944, when plaintiff was in a Naval hospital, a product called "Umbrathor", manufactured by defendant, was inserted into his sinuses for the purpose of making them perceptible in X rays. Some of the substance allegedly remained in plaintiff's head and produced a carcinoma requiring the removal of an eye in 1957. The present action was commenced in 1959. The causes of action based on warranty were barred under section 48 (subd. 1) of the Civil Practice Act, having been brought more than six years after the warranty was allegedly breached.

2. If the nature and origin of the liability asserted is a negligent act, the bar of the Statute of Limitations cannot be avoided through an allegation that defendant had a continuing duty to warn plaintiff of the possible damaging consequences resulting from the act alleged to be negligent. Any duty that may arise to recall the product from the market has no bearing on the rights of those to whom it had been administered years previously.

3. Schmidt v. Merchants Desp. Transp. Co. (270 N. Y. 287) is controlling as to the time when the negligence cause of action accrued.

 

406 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Chiropractic Ass'n of N.Y., Inc. v. Hilleboe


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


12 N.Y.2d 109; 187 N.E.2d 756; 237 N.Y.S.2d 289; 1962 N.Y. LEXIS 800


October 1, 1962, Argued  
December 31, 1962, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant chiropractic association sought to overturn the judgment of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed a judgment of a supreme court and held that respondent commissioner of health had issued proper regulations governing the use of x-rays pursuant to the authority granted to the commissioner under N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 225(4)(a).


OVERVIEW: The commissioner of health promulgated N.Y. Comp. Codes R. & Regs. tit. 10, § 16.19, which prohibited the application of radiation to human beings unless the application was done under the supervision of one licensed to practice medicine, dentistry, podiatry, or osteopathy. The regulation had the effect of limiting the use of x-rays by chiropractors because they were not licensed to practice medicine. The lower court upheld the regulation, and the court affirmed. The court held that there was substantial evidence that x-rays were harmful. Once the record reflected at least a divergence of scientific opinion, the court was required to defer to the commissioner of health because the regulation was within the police power of the state. The court also held that, under N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 225(4)(a), the delegation of the authority to the commissioner to establish sanitary regulations was proper because flexibility was needed in regulating complex matters in the area of public health.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment that the regulation of the application of radiation to human beings by the commissioner of health was a valid exercise of the police power of the state.


HEADNOTES

Physicians and surgeons -- chiropractors -- X rays -- regulation 19 of chapter XVI of New York State Sanitary Code, which prohibits application of radiation to human being by anyone not licensed or otherwise authorized to practice medicine, dentistry, podiatry or osteopathy, but does not prohibit use of radiation by technician, nurse or other person if such use is directed or ordered by person so licensed or authorized to practice for use in his field, was adopted by Public Health Council under valid delegation of legislative power and is not unconstitutional.

1. Regulation 19 of chapter XVI of the New York State Sanitary Code prohibits the application of radiation to a human being by anyone not licensed or otherwise authorized to practice medicine, dentistry, podiatry or osteopathy but does not prohibit the use of radiation by a technician, nurse or other person if such use is directed or ordered by a person so licensed or authorized to practice for use in his field. This regulation was adopted by the Public Health Council under a valid delegation of legislative power and is not unconstitutional.

2. Section 225 of the Public Health Law (subds. 3, 4, par. [a]) authorizes the Public Health Council, with the approval of the Commissioner of Health, to establish the Sanitary Code which may "deal with any matters affecting the security of life or health or the preservation and improvement of public health in the state of New York". Although standards or guides must be prescribed where legislative power is delegated, it need be done in only so detailed a fashion as is reasonably practicable in the light of the complexities of the particular area to be regulated. It lay within the technical competence of the Public Health Council to determine the principal sources of ionizing radiation, which has been found to have a deleterious, additive and cumulative effect upon human beings, particularly with respect to their reproductive life, and to adopt measures to limit exposure thereto where, in its judgment, the extent of such exposure exceeds the benefits derived therefrom. In such situations the principle is well established that a general grant of power may be made to an expert board, leaving its implementation to the members thereof who are chosen from experts in the field.

3. It did not transcend the police power of the State for the Public Health Council to rule that the unfamiliarity of chiropractors with the effects of radiation and the danger of their ordering or applying too much of it through ignorance constituted a danger to the public health, especially in view of the wholesale use of X ray in their domain.

4. Regulation 19 does not contain an absolute prohibition that no chiropractor shall ever be permitted to take X rays. Any qualified person may do so provided that he takes them only by the direction, for use in his field, of one licensed or authorized by law to diagnose and treat human ailments.

 

407 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Five Boro Elec. Contractors Ass'n v. City of New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


12 N.Y.2d 146; 187 N.E.2d 774; 237 N.Y.S.2d 315; 1962 N.Y. LEXIS 803


November 1, 1962, Argued  
December 31, 1962, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of a judgment of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court (New York), which affirmed an order of the trial court, which granted in part and denied in part a motion by respondent electricians for an order striking the city's denials and defenses and granted judgment on pleadings of an action initiated by the electricians seeking to recover excess money paid to the city for excessive, unconstitutional license fees.


OVERVIEW: The electricians initiated an action against the city to recover excess money paid to the city for excessive, unconstitutional license fees. The trial court granted judgment on the pleadings in favor of the electricians and granted in part and denied in part a motion for an order striking the city's denials and defenses. The appellate court affirmed the order of the trial court, and the city sought review. The court held that payments of illegal taxes were allowed to be recovered when they had been made under protest. The court further held that protest was not required in order to recover money paid pursuant to an illegal statute or ordinance. The court held that, in view of the compulsory nature of the required payments, formal protest by the electricians was not required to recover such payments. The court held that the excessive license fees imposed on the electricians as a condition of their rights to pursue their occupation was an invasion of present liberty of person. The court affirmed judgments of the lower courts.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed judgments of the lower courts, which granted in part and denied in part the electricians' motion for an order striking the city's denials and defenses and granted judgment on the pleadings in favor of the electricians in an action to recover excess money paid to the city for excessive, unconstitutional license fees.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- recovery of fees -- coercion -- electricians paid license fees, subsequently found to be so excessive as to be unconstitutional -- electricians entitled to recover excess over proper fee although payments not made under protest since they were involuntary.

The license fees for electricians which ranged from $ 100 to $ 250 during the years 1954 through 1959 were held to be so excessive as to be unconstitutional ( Adlerstein v. City of New York, 6 N Y 2d 740). In the present action several hundred electricians were properly permitted to recover the excess over $ 25 apiece (the correct fee amount) paid to the City of New York during the years 1954 through 1959. These license payments were not made under protest, but it was properly determined that the payments were made under duress as matter of law and that consequently protest was not required. They were involuntary since failure to make them would have required plaintiffs to discontinue the conduct of their business.

 

408 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Lipsett v. Gillette


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


12 N.Y.2d 162; 187 N.E.2d 782; 237 N.Y.S.2d 326; 1962 N.Y. LEXIS 805; 52 L.R.R.M. 2184; 46 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P50,729


December 3, 1962, Argued  
December 31, 1962, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employees association appealed from an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Fourth Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed an order of the supreme court that dismissed the association's petition. The supreme court found that Rochester, N.Y., Ord. § 3-24 was entirely valid and that the association had shown no grounds for relief against respondents, mayor and others.


OVERVIEW: Section 3-24 authorized the city manager to recognize city employees' unions or employees associations, to negotiate with the recognized bargaining agents, and to execute collective bargaining agreements with the bargaining agents. The employes association argued that the ordinance was unconstitutional for a lack of standards and that the city manager's alleged favoritism to a particular union was discriminatory and against public policy. The supreme court, which the lower appellate court affirmed, dismissed the association's petition. On appeal, the court reversed the order. The court found that the ordinance was not invalid because a requirement of fairness and reasonableness was implied. However, the city manager failed to reveal by what process and steps he decided on the bargaining unit. Thus the court remanded the case to the supreme court to determine whether the city manager's procedures were fair and reasonable and whether they were taken with due regard to the claims of the association and the rights of the city employees.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order that dismissed the association's petition and remanded the case to determine whether city manager's procedure in recognizing a union was fair and reasonable.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- collective bargaining -- ordinance of City of Rochester authorizing City Manager to recognize union or employee organization as bargaining agent for appropriate unit of city employees not invalid for lack of prescribed standards -- proceeding asking for relief in nature of mandamus treated under circumstances as one for certiorari review -- matter remitted to Special Term.

1. The City of Rochester adopted an ordinance authorizing the City Manager to recognize a duly organized union or employee organization as the bargaining agent for an appropriate unit of city employees upon submission to him of satisfactory proof that said union or organization is representative of the unit. The ordinance is not invalid for lack of prescribed standards. Although it contains no instructions as to how to determine "an appropriate unit" or how to decide which organization is the appropriate "bargaining agent" for that unit, a requirement of fairness and reasonableness is to be implied. Such determinations by the City Manager are to be made after reasonable inquiry into the facts, including the right of interested organizations to be heard. The City Manager must have a measure of discretion in determining the procedures to be followed.

2. The petition asked for relief in the nature of mandamus. However, the City Manager, by signing a collective bargaining agreement with the AFL-CIO union, made a final determination of a kind reviewable by proceedings in the nature of certiorari. The court may now treat the proceeding as one for certiorari review. Accordingly, the matter is sent back to Special Term for a trial to determine by what process and steps the City Manager decided on the unit and recognized the bargaining agent, and whether those procedures were fair and reasonable and whether they were taken with due regard to the claims of petitioner and the rights of the city employees.

 

409 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Chodorov


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


12 N.Y.2d 176; 188 N.E.2d 124; 237 N.Y.S.2d 689; 1962 N.Y. LEXIS 807


October 25, 1962, Argued  
December 31, 1962, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Motion for Reargument, and to Amend Remittitur, See 12 N Y 2d 948, 949.



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The people appealed from an order of the Court of Special Sessions of the City of New York, Appellate Part, in the First Judicial Department (New York), which, upon reargument, reversed, on the law only, a judgment of a magistrates' court convicting defendant of a violation of New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § D26-3.1, in failing to file a registration statement with respect to "owning" a multiple dwelling, and dismissing the complaint.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's conviction was reversed and complaint dismissed because § D26-3.1 imposed a liability solely upon "the true owner." The court reversed the order and remitted the case to the appellate term. The court found that if the words in § D26-2.2 were to have their reasonable and natural sense, the term "owner," "whenever used" in the N.Y. Mult. Dwell. Code, included an "agent, or any other person in control of a multiple dwelling." It followed that the defendant, as the one indisputably in control of the premises, was properly convicted. Defendant contended that § D26-2.2(15) rendered only the "true owner" responsible for filing a registration statement. The court held that when § D26-3.1 was read in the light of § D26-2.2(15), it became manifest that all who were included within the term "owner" shared a responsibility for complying with the filing provision and that, if the owner failed to file the statement, the agent or other person in control of the premises was liable. Similarly, N.Y. Mult. Dwell. Law § 325 also required the owner "and" an agent or other person in control to file an equivalent of the registration statement.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the appellate term, which reversed the judgment of conviction for failing to file a multiple dwelling house registration statement, and dismissed of the complaint, for errors of law only, in favor of defendant. The case was remitted to the appellate term for determination of possible questions of fact.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- failure to file "registration statement" -- defendant, person in control of multiple dwelling in City of New York, properly convicted of failing to file "registration statement" -- defendant was "owner" under definition of that term (Administrative Code of City of New York, § D26-2.2, subd. 15).

1. Defendant, the person in control of a multiple dwelling in the City of New York, was properly convicted of failing to file a "registration statement" as required by New York City's Multiple Dwelling Code.

2. Section D26-3.1 of the Administrative Code of the City of New York directs that "Every owner of a multiple dwelling shall file * * * a written registration statement" describing the premises, giving the owner's name and address and the name and address of "a managing agent in control of and responsible for the [dwelling's] maintenance and operation". Defendant was an "owner" under section D26-2.2 (subd. 15) of the Administrative Code of the City of New York which defines such term to "mean or include" the "owner of the freehold of a multiple dwelling * * *, a mortgagee or vendee in possession, * * * trustee, lessee, agent, or any other person, firm or corporation, directly or indirectly in control of a multiple dwelling." Since the term "owner" includes an "agent, or any other person * * * in control of a multiple dwelling", it follows that defendant was properly convicted of the offense charged against him.

 

410 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Sierra Constr. Co. v. Board of Appeals


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


12 N.Y.2d 79; 187 N.E.2d 123; 236 N.Y.S.2d 53; 1962 N.Y. LEXIS 839


October 2, 1962, Argued  
December 6, 1962, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner construction company challenged an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Fourth Judicial Department (New York), which unanimously affirmed an order of trial court denying a petition for an order directing respondent town to issue a building permit to the construction company and confirming a determination of the zoning board of appeals.


OVERVIEW: The town had upheld a denial by the town's building inspector of the construction company's application for a building permit, and the denial had been on the ground that the dwelling, which the construction company proposed to build, violated a setback provision of the local zoning ordinance. The appellate court upheld the trial court's order denying a petition for an order directing the town to issue a building permit to the construction company. The court affirmed that judgment finding that the construction company's proposal to build not only violated the setback provision of the zoning ordinance, but also violated the ordinance in that the building was to be constructed too close to other buildings. The court also found that the construction company could not claim that if the ordinance was enforced, the consequent restrictions upon his property precluded its use for any purpose to which it was reasonably adapted.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court in favor of the town, which unanimously affirmed an order of trial court denying the construction company's petition for an order directing the town to issue a building permit to the construction company and confirming a determination of the board.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- zoning -- zoning ordinance provided that, when building was erected in residential district on street where other buildings were located within 300 feet, setback should be not less than average setback of such buildings for distance of 300 feet on each side of proposed building -- order of Appellate Division affirming order of Special Term, which confirmed determination of Zoning Board of Appeals upholding denial of building permit for violation of said provision, affirmed.

A building permit was denied on the ground that the dwelling which petitioner proposed to build violated a setback provision of the local zoning ordinance which, in addition to requiring a minimum 60-foot setback in the area in which petitioner's lot is located, contained a provision that: "When any building is hereafter erected, constructed or built within a residential district on a road or street on the same side of which buildings or structures are located within three hundred (300) feet on either side, the line or setback shall be not less than the average setback of all such buildings for a distance of three hundred (300) feet on each side of said proposed new building." At the time of the permit application there were, immediately south of the lot on which petitioner proposed to build and within 300 feet therefrom, four residences each of which was set back 82 feet from the easterly line of Mt. Read Boulevard. An order of the Appellate Division affirming a Special Term order confirming a determination of the Zoning Board of Appeals of the town which upheld a denial of the building permit is affirmed.

414 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Pennsylvania R. Co. v. State


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


11 N.Y.2d 504; 184 N.E.2d 588; 230 N.Y.S.2d 1004; 1962 N.Y. LEXIS 1034


June 11, 1962, Argued  
July 6, 1962, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York) affirmed a trial court ruling and granted appellee state declaratory judgment in an action brought by appellant railroad in which the railroad claimed it had contractual rights to certain benefits under state legislation passed to benefit railroads and later amended. The railroad appealed.


OVERVIEW: Out of great concern for the welfare and viability of railroads, the state legislature passed legislation allowing railroads to restructure as redevelopment corporations and, thereby, enjoy certain tax and other benefits. Later, that legislation was amended so that the benefits once conferred were not altogether present. The railroad challenged the amendment and claimed that when the initial legislation was passed it was granted a contractual right to certain benefits thereunder, benefits which were later wrongfully withheld through legislative amendment. The trial court and the appellate division found no contractual rights existed and dismissed the railroad's claim accordingly. On appeal, the court affirmed and found the legislation at issue clearly conferred no contractual rights on any party. The court held that it was never to be assumed that the state had fettered its power in the future, except upon clear and irresistible evidence that the engagement was in the nature of a private contract, as distinguished from a mere act of general legislation. This case was one of only general legislation under which the railroad obtained no contractual right.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the ruling of the appellate division and held that the legislation at issue was general legislation that conferred no contractual rights on the railroad.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- impairment of obligation of contract -- railroads -- original statutory grant of power to railroad which qualified as railroad redevelopment corporation to increase fares and charges without advance approval by Public Service Commission did not constitute contract between State and railroad -- subsequent statute requiring advance approval by Public Service Commission did not impair obligation of any contract.

1. Article 7 of the Railroad Law, enacted in 1951 (L. 1951, ch. 359), provided for the formation of railroad redevelopment corporations. Section 307 of a new article 7 adopted in 1954 provided that, should the revenues of a railroad which qualified as a railroad redevelopment corporation under the revised article be insufficient, the company would be entitled to raise its fares subject to subsequent review by the Public Service Commission to determine whether the new fares were needed to provide the required revenues. The Long Island Rail Road Company qualified as such a redevelopment corporation and became one in August, 1954. A 1958 amendment to section 307 of the Railroad Law (L. 1958, ch. 386) requires that prior application must be made to the Public Service Commission for approval of rates. The original grant of power to increase fares and charges without advance approval by the Public Service Commission did not constitute a contract between the State and the Long Island Rail Road and the Pennsylvania Railroad which was its sole stockholder and principal creditor. Consequently, the statute does not impair the obligation of any contract in violation of the Federal or State Constitutions (U. S. Const., art. I, § 10; N. Y. Const., art I, § 15).

2. Before a law may be deemed to amount to a contract between the State and a third party, the statutory language must be found to be susceptible of no other reasonable interpretation than that a contract was intended. There is nothing in the 1954 legislation to support the idea of a contract with the Long Island Rail Road or any other particular railroad.

 

415 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Bucho Holding Co. v. Temporary State Housing Rent Com.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


11 N.Y.2d 469; 184 N.E.2d 569; 230 N.Y.S.2d 977; 1962 N.Y. LEXIS 1031


June 12, 1962, Argued  
July 6, 1962, Decided



DISPOSITION: In each case: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, two landlords and a tenant, challenged orders of the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which dismissed their constitutional challenge to § 4 of the Emergency Housing Rent Control Law, as amended by 1962 N.Y. Laws ch. 21, § 4.


OVERVIEW: The constitutional challenge to the rent control statute was brought on grounds of due process and equal protection. The court affirmed the judgments that found the statute constitutional. The court found no violation of due process because landlords were allowed a reasonable rent and because rent control was a reasonable exercise of the police power. The court held that the use of prior year equalization rates was a classification having a reasonable basis because the use of the most recent equalization rates was likely to result in a wave of rent increases; hence, the statute did not offend against the equal protection clause, U.S. Const. amend. XIV.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgments upholding the constitutionality of the rent control statute.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and tenant -- rent regulation -- constitutional law -- Emergency Housing Rent Control Law, as amended in 1962 (§ 4, subd. [a], as amd. by L. 1962, ch. 21, § 4), revoked 1961 law which had employed equalization rates for "most recent year" and restored formula which had been in use from 1957 to 1961, providing that value of rent-controlled residential property, on which landlord is entitled to 6% return, should be fixed by applying 1954 equalization rates to current assessed valuation of such property -- no denial of due process or equal protection of laws -- Legislature adopted reasonable means of protecting public from abnormal rent increases in time of rental housing shortage -- no constitutional right to return of 6% even on market value -- statute not discriminatory.

1. Section 4 (subd. [a]) of the Emergency Housing Rent Control Law, as amended in 1962 (L. 1962, ch. 21, § 4), provides that the value of rent-controlled residential property, on which the landlord is entitled to a 6% return, is to be fixed by applying 1954 equalization rates to the current assessed valuation of such property. It revokes the 1961 amendment which had employed equalization rates for "the most recent year" (L. 1961, ch. 337) and restores the formula which had been in use from 1957 to June 30, 1961 (L. 1957, ch. 755; L. 1959, ch. 695). The 1961 rates reflected a sharp drop from those of 1954 and it became obvious that their impact would have a drastic or undesirable effect upon the maximum rents of a substantial number of controlled tenants. The Legislature was faced with the immediate danger of "serious threats to the public health, safety and general welfare" which would result from the "disruptive practices and abnormal conditions" produced by the mass rent increases which would follow if the 1961 equalization rates were applied to current assessments. The protection of the public against such increases in a time of a rental housing shortage being a valid legislative purpose, the Legislature adopted a reasonable means of effectuating that purpose by restoring the use of the 1954 rates. The legislation does not effect a denial of due process.

2. There is no constitutional right to a return to the landlords of 6% even on market value. Even assuming that the landlords are precluded from receiving a 6% return on market value by the use of 1954 equalization rates, the statute may not be invalidated on the ground that it is confiscatory, since there is no suggestion that the return which they are allowed is so low as to be unreasonable.

3. The 1962 amendment is not discriminatory on its face and did not deprive appellants of equal protection of the laws.

4. The fact that the statutory scheme results in inequality as between landlords and tenants in different assessing units, since the ratio between 1954 and 1961 equalization rates is not uniform throughout the State, does not result in any violation of the equal protection of the laws. The fact that, in carrying out the scheme devised by the Legislature, a particular landlord or tenant may suffer does not render the method selected discriminatory or render the statute unconstitutional.

 

416 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


I. L. F. Y. Co. v. City Rent & Rehabilitation Administration


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


11 N.Y.2d 480; 184 N.E.2d 575; 230 N.Y.S.2d 986; 1962 N.Y. LEXIS 1032


June 12, 1962, Argued  
July 6, 1962, Decided



DISPOSITION: In each case: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, owners of rent-controlled residential properties, challenged the judgment of the Supreme Court, New York County (New York), which dismissed the owners' complaints against respondent city rent and rehabilitation administration. The owners challenged the constitutionality of the provisions of the City Rent and Rehabilitation Law that related to where landlords were able to obtain maximum rent adjustments under the fair net provision.


OVERVIEW: In an action against the City Rent and Rehabilitation Administration, the owners of rent-controlled residential properties (owners) challenged the constitutionality of portions of the City Rent and Rehabilitation law, which related to the procedure for obtaining maximum rent adjustments under the fair net provision. The trial court dismissed the complaint. The owners appealed. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court found that the city council acted within its power by imposing more stringent police power restrictions. According to the court, there had been a gradual relaxation of the rent control measures, and it was not until the more favorable provisions were found to have been abused that new restrictions were adopted to combat that evil. The court rejected the owners' claim that the combination of the two-year hiatus between applications and the 15 percent maximum increase during any two-year period was unconstitutional because it left the landlord in a position where he was earning less than the statutory "6 and 2" return until his applications were favorably acted upon. According to the court, there was no constitutional right to a "6 and 2" return.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment that dismissed the complaint against the City Rent and Rehabilitation Administration.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and tenant -- rent regulation -- enactment in New York City Rent and Rehabilitation Law (Local Laws, 1962, No. 20 of City of New York) of two-year provisions relating to pre-filing, repeated applications and 15% maximum increase, in connection with "hardship" applications for nonservice connected rent increases, was not unjustified and provisions were not unconstitutional because allegedly harsher than needed, made applicable to pending proceedings, or allegedly operating to deprive landlords of reasonable, as opposed to statutory, return.

1. The New York City Rent and Rehabilitation Law (Local Laws, 1962, No. 20 of City of New York) provided that a "hardship" application for a nonservice connected rent increase (Administrative Code of City of New York, § Y41-5.0, subd. g, par. [1], subpar. [a]) may not be filed less than two years after the filing date of the last prior application which resulted in an increase (Administrative Code, § Y41-5.0, subd. g, par. [1], subpar. [a], cl. [2], subcl. [i]) or less than two years after the last sale of the property where the application was based on a sale price higher than the assessed valuation (§ Y41-5.0, subd. g, par. [1], subpar. [a], cl. [2], subcl. [ii]); that the total adjustments ordered may not exceed 15% in any two-year period (§ Y41-5.0, subd. g, par. [2]), and that the law should apply to pending administrative proceedings (§ Y41-14.0, subds. a, b, par. [1]). Enactment of such provisions, supported by the presumption of constitutionality, was not unjustified inasmuch as the reports of various administrative and legislative bodies, numerous documents and the testimony at a public hearing as to the inflationary effect of "hardship" sales and applications, if unabated, gave the City Council the right to a fear of future difficulties which was sufficient to justify the exercise of police power. Accordingly, the complaints of landlords, whose applications for rent increases were adversely affected by the provisions of the city law, were properly dismissed.

2. Allegations that the statutory provisions were harsher than needed were inappropriate on a direct appeal which brings up constitutional questions only (Civ. Prac. Act, § 588, subd. 4). In any event, where the challenged legislation is reasonably related to an actual, manifest evil, the courts may not dictate the choice of remedy, and questions of wisdom, need or appropriateness are for the Legislature.

3. The provisions of the City Rent and Rehabilitation Law were not invalid because they were inconsistent with the former State law. The enabling legislation expressly gave the city the right to adopt local laws, rules or regulations appropriate to meet the emergency housing situation despite such inconsistency (L. 1962, ch. 21, § 1, subd. 5).

4. Landlords, although mandated by the Constitution to be allowed to earn a reasonable return, do not have and never have had any constitutionally guaranteed right to the statutory 6% return on the value of the property and 2% depreciation. Accordingly, the combination of the two-year hiatus between applications and the 15% maximum increase in any two-year period was not unconstitutional because a landlord may be earning less than the return provided by statute until his applications are approved.

 

417 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Finkelstein


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


11 N.Y.2d 300; 183 N.E.2d 661; 229 N.Y.S.2d 367; 1962 N.Y. LEXIS 1130


March 1, 1962, Submitted  
May 17, 1962, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgments affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed the judgments of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court (New York), which affirmed their convictions for selling or possessing with the intent to sell any obscene book in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 1141.


OVERVIEW: A manager of a bookstore and the cashier at another bookstore were arrested after telling a detective the price of two books. The detective asked the owner of the second store if he knew the books were pornographic. The owner was arrested after responding that he had seen worse than those books. Defendants, the manager and owner, were convicted of selling or possessing with the intent to sell any obscene book. The appellate court affirmed. Defendants appealed. The court affirmed. The court held that defendants' voluntary admissions that they had seen worse books than the ones involved and the lurid statements and bold prices on the covers warranted the conclusion that scienter or defendants' knowledge of the contents was established beyond a reasonable doubt. The court charged defendants with knowledge of the scope of a subsequent interpretation of § 1141 as requiring scienter. The court held that the books containing descriptions of sexual experiences with emphasis on the abnormal met the test for obscenity. The court held that the trial court properly excluded as irrelevant the evidence that other publications were seen on magazine stands in the city.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendants' convictions for obscenity.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- obscenity -- defendants properly convicted of selling obscene books ( Penal Law, § 1141) -- necessary proof of scienter submitted -- no error in refusing to receive evidence allegedly bearing on current community standards regarding obscenity.

1. Defendants were properly convicted of the crime of violating section 1141 of the Penal Law, which provides that "A person who sells * * * or has in his possession with intent to sell * * * any obscene * * * book * * * [is] guilty of a misdemeanor".

2. The voluntary admission on the part of each defendant that he had seen worse books than those here involved, the lurid statements on the covers, the $ 5 selling price boldly stamped on the front cover of each paper-covered book, taken together, warranted the trial court's conclusion that the necessary proof of scienter (see People v. Finkelstein, 9 N Y 2d 342) had been submitted.

3. Defendants were not prosecuted under an ex post facto law adopted by the Court of Appeals on the previous appeal. The court did not legislate scienter into section 1141, but interpreted that section as requiring proof of scienter.

4. The books involved are obscene.

5. The trial court did not err in refusing to receive evidence allegedly bearing upon current community standards regarding obscenity. The fact that certain other and different publications were seen in bookstores and on magazine stands in New York City is no indication that they were sold or read or that, to the average person applying contemporary community standards, they were not obscene.

 

418 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Lanza v. Wagner


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


11 N.Y.2d 317; 183 N.E.2d 670; 229 N.Y.S.2d 380; 1962 N.Y. LEXIS 1132; 97 A.L.R.2d 344


January 24, 1962, Argued  
May 17, 1962, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment modified, without costs, in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant board members sought review of the judgment of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York) that dismissed their complaint against respondent mayor.


OVERVIEW: Board members sought review of the dismissal of the complaint. The board members were relieved of their duties prior to the expiration of their term. The board members contended the new law violated the home rule provisions of the state constitution. The court held that the legislature could have modified a term in the public interest even though the effect might have been to curtail an incumbent's unexpired term. The board members contended that the statute was a bill of attainder. The court held that the statute did not have any of the objectionable attributes of a bill of attainder. The court determined that the home rule provision did not apply to nonlocal or newly created offices. The court held that the board of education was not a department of the city government, but was an independent corporate body. The board members argued that the method of appointment was an unlawful delegation of legislative authority. The court did not find this argument persuasive in light of contrary authority. The court held that there was no constitutional bar to the legislative designation of a nominating panel made up of people representing a knowledgeable cross section of city interests.


OUTCOME: The court modified the judgment without costs. The court ordered the complaint reinstated, declared a portion of a statute governing a city's board of education unconstitutional, and declared the mayor's appointments valid.


HEADNOTES

Schools -- Board of Education -- statute (L. 1961, ch. 971) providing for appointment of members of Board of Education of City of New York from list of nominees submitted by heads of three universities and presidents of eight educational, civic, business, labor and professional organizations, not unconstitutional.

1. Pursuant to chapter 971 of the Laws of 1961, the terms of those then comprising the Board of Education of the City of New York were to come to an end on September 20, 1961, and appointments of new members of the board, as well as of their successors, were to be made by the Mayor of the City of New York from a list of nominees to be submitted to him by a board consisting of the heads of three universities located in New York City and the presidents of eight other organizations representing educational, civic, business, labor and professional groups interested in city affairs including education. In an action by former members of the board for judgment declaring the statute unconstitutional, the courts below correctly decided that it was valid.

2. The statute does not violate the home rule provisions of the State Constitution (art. IX, § 9) concerning the election and appointment of "city officers". Members of New York City's Board of Education are not "city officers" within the meaning of those provisions.

3. Since education is a State and not a local function, and members of the Board of Education are State and not local officers, such officers may be "elected by the people or appointed, as the legislature may direct." (N. Y. Const., art. IX, § 9.)

4. There is no constitutional inhibition against the shortening of the term of an existing statutory office by legislation aimed at the office rather than at its incumbent.

5. The statute is not a bill of attainder.

6. The method of appointment provided by the statute is not an unlawful delegation of legislative authority in violation of section 1 of article III of the State Constitution.

7. It was error to dismiss the complaint in an action for a declaratory judgment merely because plaintiffs were not entitled to a declaration of their rights as they claim to be.

 

419 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Dinan


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


11 N.Y.2d 350; 183 N.E.2d 689; 229 N.Y.S.2d 406; 1962 N.Y. LEXIS 1134


March 29, 1962, Argued  
May 17, 1962, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgments affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants challenged, by permission, judgments from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York) rendered upon verdicts convicting defendants of book-making and conspiracy, and convicting one defendant only of conspiracy. The trial court also denied defendants' motion for a new trial in the prosecution under § 605 of the Federal Communications Act (FCA).


OVERVIEW: The charges against defendants were based upon evidence of conversations recorded from telephone interceptions. Defendants argued that the trial court erred in admitting the wiretaps into evidence for lack of "foundation" testimony. They also argued that the admission of the evidence violated their Fourth Amendment rights. The state argued that the trial court properly admitted into evidence both voice identification and conversations recorded from telephone interceptions. The court affirmed the judgments and held that: (1) in many criminal prosecutions evidence obtained by wire tapping authorized by orders granted under N.Y. Const. art. I, § 12 and N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. § 813-a was introduced in criminal trials in New York State courts; (2) Congress had chosen to protect the secrecy of telephone messages by making them, when intercepted, inadmissible in evidence in federal criminal trials, by direct legislation, and thus departed from the common law of evidence; (3) the FCA itself contained no language forbidding the admission of such evidence in any courts; and (4) New York courts had adopted a contrary policy under its reserved powers.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgments denying defendants' motion for a new trial and convicting of book-making and conspiracy and convicting one defendant of conspiracy alone.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- intercepted telephone conversations -- intercepted telephone conversations properly admitted in evidence notwithstanding section 605 of Federal Communications Act -- Mapp v. Ohio (367 U.S. 643) not intended to change rule.

Intercepted telephone conversations on which defendants' convictions depend were admissible. State-obtained wire taps may be admitted into evidence in criminal trials in State courts notwithstanding section 605 of the Federal Communications Act. Mapp v. Ohio (367 U.S. 643) was not intended to change the rule.

421 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Fifth Ave. Coach Lines, Inc. v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


11 N.Y.2d 342; 183 N.E.2d 684; 229 N.Y.S.2d 400; 1962 N.Y. LEXIS 1133


April 24, 1962, Argued  
May 17, 1962, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff bus company sought to permanently restrain defendant city from acquiring the company's omnibus properties and franchises and to declare invalid the statute under which the city purported to act. The trial court denied the company's motion for temporary injunction. The Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York) reversed the order. The city sought review.


OVERVIEW: The issue was whether N.Y. Gen. City Law § 20 (1962), which authorized a municipality to condemn private property for a public use without providing for personal notice to the owner in advance of the condemnation, violated the Due Process Clause. The company urged that the property was taken to reconvey their property to another corporation to perform the same services and, therefore, was for a private use. On appeal, the court held that the exercise of eminent domain in a summary fashion was did not violate due process. The power of eminent domain could be exercised by simply entering into possession without either prior notice or court order. So long as a condemnee was given an adequate opportunity to be heard regarding compensation, there was no need for any other notice of an intended appropriation. Although § 20 did not require prior notice, the company knew of the city's intent to take the company's bus lines. The taking of the property of a public service corporation in order to provide the same service under governmental auspices was a public use. The company would have an opportunity to prove its allegations of private use, conspiracy, and bad faith at trial.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order from the appellate court, which reversed the trial court's order denying the company's motion for a temporary injunction to restrain the city from acquiring the company's properties. The court reinstated the trial court's order.


HEADNOTES

Eminent domain -- notice of condemnation -- City of New York not required to give notice of condemnation in acquiring plaintiffs' omnibus properties and franchises -- taking was for public use -- temporary injunction denied but, since complaint not dismissed, opportunity is provided to prove that purpose was to reconvey plaintiffs' property to another corporation.

1. In an action permanently to restrain the City of New York from acquiring plaintiffs' omnibus properties and franchises and for judgment declaring invalid and void on constitutional grounds the statute under which the city purported to act, a temporary injunction should not have been granted.

2. The statute under which the city has acted (General City Law, § 20, as amd. by L. 1962, ch. 161, eff. March 19, 1962) is not unconstitutional for failure to provide for notice to the owners of the property condemned prior to the actual taking of possession.

3. The Legislature has the power to enact a law authorizing a municipality to condemn private property for a public use without providing for personal notice to the owner or owners in advance of the condemnation.

4. So long as a condemnee is given an adequate opportunity to be heard on the subjects of compensation and public use, there need not be any other notice of an intended appropriation. The statute contains provisions completely adequate in this respect.

5. The taking over by government of the property of a public service corporation for the purpose of providing the same service under governmental, as opposed to private, auspices is a public use.

6. There is no proof in the record concerning plaintiffs' allegation that the true purpose of the condemnation is to reconvey their property to another corporation to perform the very services which they have heretofore been providing. Since the complaint is not being dismissed, plaintiffs are not being deprived of any opportunity to prove their allegations in this action if they so choose.

People v. Bellfield


Court of Appeals of New York


11 N.Y.2d 947; 183 N.E.2d 230; 228 N.Y.S.2d 830; 1962 N.Y. LEXIS 1180
