442 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


New York Mobile Homes Ass'n v. Steckel


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


9 N.Y.2d 533; 175 N.E.2d 151; 215 N.Y.S.2d 487; 1961 N.Y. LEXIS 1286; 86 A.L.R.2d 270


March 29, 1961, Argued  
April 27, 1961, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayers challenged the judgment of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Fourth Judicial Department (New York), which modified and, as modified, affirmed a judgment of granting a motion by defendants, state, town, and county, to dismiss the taxpayers' complaint and declared N.Y. Tax Law § 2(6-a) constitutional.


OVERVIEW: The litigation stemmed from a statute, underlying which was an attempt to secure reimbursement from the taxpayers, trailer dwellers, for some share of the expense of providing them with police and fire protection, educational facilities and the various other services rendered by a municipality to persons residing within its confines. By making his more or less permanent home in a trailer, an individual could derive the benefit of all the services provided by the local government, while leaving the burden of paying for them upon his neighbors who lived in more conventional type dwellings. Although the inequities of this situation were readily apparent, there was seemingly no solution of the problem since the New York State tax against personal property had been repealed and trailers were traditionally considered to be personalty. The court affirmed the appellate court judgment finding that it was not for the court to determine whether the tax was fair, only whether the state had the authority to pass it, which it did.


OUTCOME: The judgment finding the statute constitutional and affirming dismissal of the taxpayers' complaint was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- real property -- trailers -- Legislature had power to classify trailers as real property for purposes of taxation -- trailers may be assessed against real property on which they are located (Real Property Tax Law, § 102, subd. 12, par. [g]) -- statute not unconstitutional.

1. Subdivision 6-a of section 2 of the Tax Law (added by L. 1954, ch. 726) provided that the terms "land", "real estate", and "real property", as used in the Tax Law, shall "include all the forms of housing which are * * * commonly called * * * 'trailers'; except (1) transient trailers which have been located within the boundaries of a tax district for less than sixty days and (2) trailers which are for sale and which are not occupied" and that "Trailers shall be assessed to the owners of the real property on which they are located." The present statute (Real Property Tax Law, § 102, subd. 12, par. [g]) is in effect a re-enactment of former subdivision 6-a of section 2 of the Tax Law and provides that "The value of any trailer or mobile home shall be included in the assessment of the land on which it is located". While it now directs the assessment against the land, instead of against the owner of the land, section 304 of the Real Property Tax Law still provides as heretofore that "All assessments shall be against the real property itself". The Legislature intended the original statute to be read as it was re-enacted.

2. The Legislature has the power to classify trailers as real property for purposes of taxation.

3. There is a rational basis for the legislative classification.

4. The trailers may be assessed against the real property on which they are located. The situation is no different from that where a lessee erects a building or other improvement on the realty of his landlord. Where the fee is privately owned, the real property tax attaches to the combined interests of all the parties interested in the land and the improvements thereon. The trailer park owner has the means at his disposal, by way of rent, to allocate the increased tax upon the owner of the trailer.

5. The statute is not unconstitutional because the aggregate tax assessed against the land is computed by reference to the value of the property of more than one owner.

6. The law is not unconstitutional on its face because inequities may result from its application. If the statute is not properly administered, a proceeding may be brought to challenge the tax allegedly improperly assessed, and the action complained of may be reviewed.

 

443 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Benz v. New York State Thruway Authority


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


9 N.Y.2d 486; 174 N.E.2d 727; 215 N.Y.S.2d 47; 1961 N.Y. LEXIS 1281


March 1, 1961, Argued  
April 27, 1961, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff injured party sought review of an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Fourth Judicial Department (New York,) which granted a motion for the defendant transportation authority to dismiss the injured party's complaint.


OVERVIEW: The injured party filed an equity action against the transportation authority, and the transportation authority filed a motion to dismiss. The trial court granted the motion to dismiss, which was affirmed by the appellate court on the ground that the trial court had no jurisdiction in the equity suit. The injured party appealed that decision, and the court affirmed. The court held that the transportation authority was an arm or agency of the state and that the legislature could in creating the transportation authority have refused to waive immunity as to it and thus could have forbidden suits to be maintained against the transportation authority in any court or tribunal. While the legislature created jurisdiction in certain tribunals for actions against the transportation authority, under no circumstances did the legislature create jurisdiction for a tribunal to entertain an equity action. While this left the injured party without any remedy by suit, the court noted that the immunity of a state agency was in no way affected by the lack of any other remedy.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the court of appeals was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Courts -- jurisdiction -- Thruway Authority -- Supreme Court has no jurisdiction of equitable action against New York State Thruway Authority for rescission or reformation of contract.

The Supreme Court has no jurisdiction of an equitable action against the New York State Thruway Authority for rescission or reformation of a contract for the sale of land to the Thruway Authority ( Easley v. New York State Thruway Auth., 1 N Y 2d 374, controlling).

 

444 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Old Republic Life Ins. Co. v. Wikler


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


9 N.Y.2d 524; 175 N.E.2d 147; 215 N.Y.S.2d 481; 1961 N.Y. LEXIS 1285


March 30, 1961, Argued  
April 27, 1961, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant insurers sought review of an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York) that reversed a trial court's order granting the insurers' petition to annul a regulation promulgated by respondent New York State Superintendent of Insurance, which set premium rates for credit life insurance.


OVERVIEW: The Superintendent promulgated a regulation that set forth standards that insurers were required to meet for premium rates on credit life insurance. The insurers petitioned the trial court to have the regulation annulled on the grounds that it was discriminatory and eliminated competition. The Superintendent filed a motion to dismiss the petition, claiming that the insurers did not have standing because they had not been injured in any way by the regulation. The trial court denied the motion to dismiss and granted the insurers' petition. The appellate court reversed the trial court's order and dismissed the insurers' petition. The insurers' appealed, and the court affirmed the appellate court's order. The court held that the Superintendent, as an administrative official, was authorized by statute to disapprove premium rates that were unreasonable in relation to the benefits provided and to prescribe regulations for determining the procedures and terms applicable to credit insurance policies. The court found that the standards under the regulation were not vague or otherwise inadequate so as to amount to an unconstitutional delegation of legislative authority.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's order that reversed the trial court's order denying the Superintendent's motion to dismiss the insurers' petition to annul a regulation promulgated by the superintendent.


HEADNOTES

Insurance -- credit life insurance -- rates -- pursuant to statutory authority (Insurance Law, § 154, subd. 7; § 204, subd. 1, par. [c]), Superintendent of Insurance promulgated regulation setting forth premium rates for credit life insurance which would be considered adequate and not unreasonable in relation to benefits provided and permitted insurers to establish, independently, factors justifying higher rates than those suggested by Superintendent -- regulations were within Superintendent's statutory powers -- petitioners may maintain present proceeding before applying for approval of rates -- regulation does not shift burden of proof as to rates from Superintendent to insurer -- statutory standard that premium rates approved be not "unreasonable in relation to the benefits provided" valid and sufficiently specific.

1. Subdivision 1 of section 154 of the Insurance Law had required the filing of accident or health insurance policy forms and rates with the Superintendent of Insurance and their approval by him. By the addition of subdivision 7 to that section, the same requirements were prescribed for credit life insurance. That subdivision 7 provides that "the superintendent shall not approve any such forms or premium rates if such premium rates are unreasonable in relation to the benefits provided" and it authorizes the Superintendent "from time to time" to "prescribe, in writing, official regulations" dealing with credit insurance. Subdivision 1 (par. [c]) of section 204 of the Insurance Law, which deals with group life insurance premium rates, was similarly amended and contains almost identical provisions for the Superintendent's approval of rates and his right to make official regulations. Pursuant to the statutory amendments, the Superintendent promulgated Regulation 27A. Subdivision A of section 7 of the regulation set forth premium rates, later referred to as "standards" for premium rates, which "will be considered adequate and not unreasonable in relation to the benefits provided". The Superintendent later amended Regulation 27A by adding a new subdivision C to section 7 thereof to permit insurers to establish independently factors or conditions to justify higher rates than those suggested by the Superintendent in subdivision A of section 7. Subdivision 7 of section 154 and section 204 (subd. 1, par. [c]) of the Insurance Law authorize the Superintendent to disapprove premium rates which are "unreasonable in relation to the benefits provided" and direct him to prescribe regulations for determining procedures and terms applicable to credit insurance policies. Whether or not this permits him to make or fix rates, it is within his statutory powers to suggest reasonable ones while affording insurers freedom to show that higher rates would not be unreasonable.

2. Petitioners are entitled to maintain the present proceeding before they apply for approval of their rates. Section 34 of the Insurance Law provides that any regulation as well as order or decision of the Superintendent is subject to review under article 78 of the Civil Practice Act. The statute does not require that only action taken in pursuance of a regulation may be reviewed.

3. The regulation does not shift the burden of proof with respect to reasonableness of rates from the Superintendent to the insurer. Whether an insurer files its rates for approval or seeks to prove that the statutory conditions have been met, the requirement for obtaining such approval is the same. The premium rates must be not "unreasonable in relation to the benefits provided". The regulation does not alter the insurer's statutory responsibility.

4. The standards laid down in subdivision 7 of section 154 and subdivision 1 (par. [c]) of section 204, that the premium rates approved be not "unreasonable in relation to the benefits provided", are valid and sufficiently specific.

 

445 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Fruhling v. Amalgamated Housing Corp.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


9 N.Y.2d 541; 175 N.E.2d 156; 215 N.Y.S.2d 493; 1961 N.Y. LEXIS 1287


March 22, 1961, Argued  
April 27, 1961, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment modified in accordance with the opinion herein and, as so modified, affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff tenant, a stockholder of a private limited dividend co-operative corporation organized under the N.Y. State Housing Law, filed an against respondents, Commissioner of Housing of the State of New York and co-operative corporation. The Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York) held the tenant was subject to a 25 percent surcharge or eviction, N.Y. Pub. Hous. Law § 182(3)(b). The tenant appealed.


OVERVIEW: N.Y. Pub. Hous. Law § 182(3)(b) provided that the apartments were to be available for persons or families whose probable aggregate annual income did not exceed specified ratios. If tenants' income increased, they might have been permitted to remain, subject to the commissioner's approval. In 1960, the section was amended to specify that the tenant's rent should have been increased in proportion to his ability to pay more. Rejecting the tenant's argument that a contract was impermissibly impaired, the court noted that the specific object of the statute was to eradicate slum dwellings by providing the occupants of those premises safe and sanitary low-cost apartments to which they could remove, and the co-operative corporation's charter incorporated the statute and any amendments the legislature might have made. Affirming the decision in part, the court noted that the provisions imposing a surcharge in lieu of eviction upon attaining a certain income level also had a tendency to promote the statutory purpose as did the eviction provisions, but the commission did not have the authority to impose the surcharge before the legislative amendment.


OUTCOME: In the declaratory action the tenant brought against the commissioner and the co-operative corporation, the court affirmed the portion of the decision that held that the tenant was subject to eviction or a 25 percent surcharge, but modified the part of the decision that held that the tenant was subject to the surcharge before the 1960 legislative amendment became effective.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- public housing -- tenant stockholder of private limited dividend co-operative corporation and said corporation may constitutionally be subjected to provisions of section 182 (subd. 3, par. [b]) of Public Housing Law which contains income qualification, surcharge and eviction provisions as to tenants in such housing.

1. Plaintiff, a tenant stockholder of Amalgamated Housing Corporation, a private limited dividend co-operative corporation organized under the State Housing Law, and said corporation may constitutionally be subjected to the provisions of section 182 (subd. 3, par. [b]) of the Public Housing Law containing income qualification, surcharge and eviction provisions.

2. Subdivision 3 (par. [b]) of section 182 of the Public Housing Law, relating to co-operative housing companies and projects, provided at the time this action was instituted that apartments in such projects "heretofore or hereafter constructed" shall be available for persons or families whose probable aggregate annual income does not exceed a stated income to rental ratio, and that an occupant whose income increases beyond the prescribed ratios may be permitted to remain in occupancy for three years with possible extensions, subject to approval by the Housing Commissioner. While plaintiff's appeal in this action was pending in the Appellate Division, the Legislature added the following clause to paragraph (b) of subdivision 3, effective March 8, 1960: "However, the rent of such occupant [allowed to remain by permission of the commissioner though his income has increased above the statutory maximum] shall be increased in proportion to his ability to pay more, the amount of increase to be ruled upon and prescribed by the housing company with the approval of the commissioner." (L. 1960, ch. 160.) The court passes upon the applicability and validity of the statute as it existed on the date of the appeal, rather than the date of the commencement of the action.

3. The provisions of the Public Housing Law, which replaced the State Housing Law in 1939, are applicable to plaintiff and the housing corporation. The 1949 amendments (L. 1949, chs. 616, 761) of paragraphs (a) and (b) of subdivision 3 of section 182, introducing the word "heretofore" into the subdivision, which previously read "hereafter" only, made clear the intention to render section 182 applicable to all housing company projects.

4. Such application does not violate constitutional guarantees. There is no impairment of contractual obligations or taking of property without due process of law.

5. By the express terms of the State Housing Law, any contract or rights acquired thereunder were qualified ones. The housing corporation expressly agreed in its certificate of incorporation "that all real estate acquired by it and all structures erected by it, shall be deemed to be acquired for the purpose of promoting the public health and safety and subject to the provisions of the state housing law." (State Housing Law, § 30, subd. 13.) The State Housing Law, incorporated by reference into the corporation's charter, was always subject to change in the future.

6. The amendments to section 182 here in issue were reasonable statutory revisions designed to promote the objects of the statute and the public health and safety.

7. The provisions imposing a surcharge in lieu of eviction upon attaining a certain income level have a tendency to promote the statutory purpose, as does the eviction provision, since a tenant subjected to a surcharge may thereby be induced to remove to better accommodations. The Legislature, having the power to require removal upon the tenant's attaining a certain income level, could properly enact alternative provisions within its discretion.

8. Until March, 1960, paragraph (b) of subdivision 3 did not contain a surcharge provision. Prior to that time the surcharge was imposed solely by virtue of a regulation of the Housing Commissioner. The Commissioner's regulation was invalid. It contravened the legislative intent to accord co-operatives separate and special treatment as to surcharges until the time of the amendment.

9. The 1960 amendment is not intended to have retroactive effect to validate the Commissioner's imposition of surcharges nunc pro tunc.

10. Plaintiff was not subject to a surcharge prior to March 8, 1960.

 

446 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Lanvin Parfums, Inc. v. Le Dans, Ltd.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


9 N.Y.2d 516; 174 N.E.2d 920; 215 N.Y.S.2d 257; 1961 N.Y. LEXIS 1284; 129 U.S.P.Q. (BNA) 190


March 21, 1961, Argued  
April 27, 1961, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed, with costs in this court and in the Appellate Division, and matter remitted to Special Term for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff importer appealed from the judgment of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York) that reversed an order of the trial court denying defendant buyer's motion to dismiss the complaint and that dismissed the complaint.


OVERVIEW: The importer brought an action against the buyer, alleging that the buyer had violated N.Y. Penal Law § 2354(6) by rebottling or repackaging the importer's toilet water and selling them at prices less than those charged by the importer. The trial court denied the buyer's motion to dismiss. The appellate court reversed the denial and ordered the case dismissed. The court reversed the judgment and held that, under the statute's literal construction, the importer's complaint alleged a violation by the buyer. The court noted that the statute was designed to prevent adulteration and substitution of spurious goods for the original without discovery and to prevent fraud. The possibility of fraud, or at least of inadvertent adulteration of the original product, was not appreciably lessened by a label on the new container acknowledging that the product had been rebottled from the original. The buyer still traded upon the reputation of the importer's trademark. Accordingly, no exception was made in the statute for collateral use of the trademark. Because the alleged criminal acts threatened the importer's property rights, trademark, and good will, an injunction was warranted.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the appellate court was reversed and the motion to dismiss the importer's complaint against the buyer was denied, with costs.


HEADNOTES

Injunctions -- rebottling in violation of Penal Law -- complaint which alleges that corporate defendants purchase plaintiff's toilet waters and rebottle or repackage same and sell them at less than prices charged by plaintiff states cause of action under section 2354 (subd. 6) of Penal Law which renders person who offers goods for sale, which are represented to be the product of another person, firm or corporation, guilty of misdemeanor unless goods are contained in original package.

1. The complaint alleges that plaintiff corporation imports various perfumes and perfume extracts from France and sells the same either in original packages, as imported, or as toilet water which plaintiff packages in this State under labels, marks and names placed thereon for and by plaintiff, and under brand names, and that the corporate defendants are purchasers of plaintiff's toilet waters who systematically rebottle or repackage the same in containers of one dram each and sell them at prices far less than those charged by plaintiff. The complaint states a cause of action under subdivision 6 of section 2354 of the Penal Law which provides that "A person who: * * * 6. Knowingly sells, offers or exposes for sale, any goods which are represented in any manner, by word or deed, to be the manufacture, packing, bottling, boxing or product of any person, firm or corporation, other than himself, unless such goods are contained in the original package, box or bottle and under the labels, marks or names placed thereon by the manufacturer who is entitled to use such marks, names, brands or trade-marks; or, * * * Is guilty of a misdemeanor". If the statute is construed literally, the complaint alleges a violation by the defendants. The evident purpose of the statute is such that it may and should be read and construed literally.

2. In enacting the statute to prohibit transactions and business practices where fraud and deception are possible and to protect the trade-mark owner, the Legislature acted within its police power.

3. The alleged criminal acts also threaten plaintiff's property rights, trade-mark, and good will, and an injunction, therefore, is available.

4. The complaint sufficiently set forth causes of action against the individual defendants by alleging that they are officers, "and, directly or indirectly" are owners of the corporate stock and in control of corporate activities.

 

447 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Finkelstein


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


9 N.Y.2d 342; 174 N.E.2d 470; 214 N.Y.S.2d 363; 1961 N.Y. LEXIS 1359


January 12, 1961, Argued  
March 30, 1961, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgments reversed and new trials ordered.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants challenged, by permission, judgments from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York) affirming a trial court's judgments convicting defendants of violations of N.Y. Penal Law § 1141 making it a misdemeanor offense to possess with intent to sell any obscene book.


OVERVIEW: Defendants contended that § 1141 was on its face unconstitutional in that it was vague and the imputation of knowledge to defendants amounted to a denial of their inalienable "liberty" interest protected by due process. Defendants also argued that the trial court erroneously construed § 1141 to supply what the legislature deliberately omitted. The people countered that § 1141 was constitutional and defendants' guilt was established beyond a reasonable doubt. The appeal was the court's first opportunity to construe § 1141. The court reversed the judgments, ordered new trials, and held that: (1) the § 1141 definition of the crime was instinct with the idea of scienter; (2) New York jurisprudence neither expressly, nor by the court's construction, dispensed with the vital element of scienter and did not impinge upon the traditional freedom guarantees of speech and the press; (3) although § 1141 might possibly have been interpreted to exclude scienter it was not intended to unreasonably restrict or inhibit the inalienable "liberty" protected by due process; and (4) because § 1141 was at least susceptible of the differing interpretations the court was obliged to preserve its validity.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgments rendered by the intermediate court of appeals affirming a trial court's judgments convicting defendants of violations of a state statute making it a misdemeanor offense to possess with intent to sell any obscene book. The court ordered new trials.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- obscenity -- section 1141 of Penal Law which provides that person who sells, or has in his possession with intent to sell, any obscene book is guilty of misdemeanor was intended to apply only to those who were aware of character of such books -- scienter is intended element of crime.

1. Section 1141 of the Penal Law which provides that "A person who sells * * * or has in his possession with intent to sell * * * any obscene * * * book [is] guilty of a misdemeanor" was intended to punish only those who are in some manner aware of the character of such books. Scienter is an intended element of the crime.

2. In any event, where the statute is susceptible of more than one interpretation, the court is required to adopt the one which will preserve its validity. The requirement of scienter renders the statute constitutional (cf. Smith v. California, 361 U.S. 147).

3. Although defendants did not take the stand or offer proof as to scienter, a new trial is required in the interests of justice. The prevailing precedent at the time of the prosecution declared that scienter was not an element of the crime.

4. The informations, phrased in the statutory language are sufficient.

 

448 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Association for Preservation of Freedom of Choice, Inc. v. Shapiro


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


9 N.Y.2d 376; 174 N.E.2d 487; 214 N.Y.S.2d 388; 1961 N.Y. LEXIS 1364


February 20, 1961, Argued  
March 30, 1961, Decided



DISPOSITION: In proceeding No. 1: Order reversed, without costs, and the matter remitted to the Appellate Division for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein. In proceeding No. 2: Order affirmed, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review after the Appellate Division, Second Department (New York) denied appellant's petition for an order directing respondent justice to annul and revoke both of his opinions, and approve the certificate. Appellant also sought review of a ruling from the Appellate Division, First Department, which denied relief by way of mandamus against the Secretary of State in an action involving appellant's petition for incorporation.


OVERVIEW: Appellant and its subscribers filed an application to incorporate as a domestic membership corporation under § 10 of the Membership Corporation Law. In addition, appellant filed an application with the Secretary of State to accept and file a certificate of incorporation, which did not have the approval of respondent justice of the supreme court. The justice denied the approval. Subsequently, appellant filed a petition with appellate division for an order directing the justice to annul and revoke both of his opinions, and to approve the certificate. The justice filed a cross-motion to dismiss said petition. The appellant division denied appellant's petition, but granted the justice's motion. Further, the appellate division denied relief by way of mandamus against the Secretary of State. Appellant sought review of both orders on constitutional grounds. The appeals were consolidated. The court affirmed the order denying relief by way of mandamus but reversed the order granting the justice's motion to dismiss. The court found that the purposes expressed in the proffered certificate of incorporation were not unlawful and thus, could not find any legal basis for its rejection.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the appellate division, which denied the application for an order directing the justice to annul and revoke his opinions, without costs and remitted the matter. The court affirmed the order of the appellate division, denying relief by way of mandamus, without costs, in a matter involving appellant's incorporation as a domestic corporation.


HEADNOTES

Corporations -- approval by Justice of Supreme Court -- Justice of Supreme Court may not deny approval of certificate of incorporation (Membership Corporations Law, § 10) on ground of public policy or that purposes are injurious to community -- approval improperly denied where purposes in certificate of incorporation were not unlawful.

A proposed certificate of incorporation, whose charter purpose was, in part, to promote the right to individual freedom of choice and association, constituting the right of the individual to associate with only those persons with whom he desires to associate, was presented for the approval of a Justice of the Supreme Court. Denial of approval was based upon public policy, or injury to the community, or both. Section 10 of the Membership Corporations Law provides that: "Five or more persons may become a membership corporation for any lawful purpose * * *. Every certificate of incorporation filed under this chapter shall have endorsed thereon or annexed thereto the approval of a justice of the supreme court of the judicial district in which the office of the corporation is to be located." The function of the Justice before whom the application is made is to ascertain whether the proposed incorporation is for a lawful purpose. A Justice is not at liberty to grant or deny applications based on his personal notion of what is contrary to public policy or injurious to the community. The public policy of the State is not violated by purposes which are not unlawful. The test as to what may be injurious to the community is too vague, indefinite and elusive to serve as an objective judicial standard. The purposes expressed in the proffered certificate of incorporation are not unlawful, and hence there is no legal basis for its rejection.

 

449 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Roosevelt Raceway, Inc. v. Monaghan


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


9 N.Y.2d 293; 174 N.E.2d 71; 213 N.Y.S.2d 729; 1961 N.Y. LEXIS 1407


November 30, 1960, Argued  
March 23, 1961, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed and matter remitted to Special Term for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein, without costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant New York State Commissioner of Harness Racing (commissioner) challenged an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court, First Judicial Department, (New York), which affirmed a trial court order granting respondent racetrack's application for an order directing the commissioner to credit the racetrack's construction accounts with federal income taxes paid under 1956 N.Y. Laws ch. 837 of the New York Pari-Mutuel Revenue Law.


OVERVIEW: A racetrack filed a petition for an order directing the commissioner to credit its construction account funds with federal income taxes paid pursuant to 1956 N.Y. Laws ch. 837, § 45. The trial court granted the order and the appellate division affirmed. The commissioner sought review. The court reversed the appellate division's order and dismissed the petition. The lower courts properly construed § 45 in concluding that it provided for credits for federal income taxes, and an amendment of the statute, 1959 N.Y. Laws ch. 881, §§ 7, 10, did not retroactively alter the statute's plain meaning. However, the amendment specifically provided that no harness track should be reimbursed for federal income taxes paid before or after the amendment. The legislature was free to make a prospective tax change under N.Y. Const. art. XVI, § 1, which barred any contract for a pre-established limit on tax liability. The amendments did not impose an invalid retroactive change in the racetrack's tax liability because it did not alter any taxes already paid, but affected only the racetrack's future tax liability. The commissioner properly refused to credit the racetrack's account.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate division order affirming a supreme court order and dismissed a racetrack's petition for an order directing the commissioner to credit the racetrack's construction accounts with a sum representing federal income taxes paid by the racetrack.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- harness racing -- under 1959 amendment of Pari-Mutuel Revenue Law (§ 45-a), harness race track not entitled to reimbursement for Federal taxes on amount transferred to its general account from construction account to which had been transferred part of State's share of amount wagered at track -- amendment intended to apply to improvements already constructed -- no unconstitutional impairment of contract.

1. Under section 45-a, added to the Pari-Mutuel Revenue Law in 1956, any tax above an amount equal to that paid by a harness racing track in the year 1955 was to be divided, half to be paid to the State and the other half to be deposited by the track in its own name in a so-called "construction account". Subdivisions 6 (par. [b]) and 10 provided that, at the end of each year, a harness racing track which had completed a capital improvement with the approval of the Harness Racing Commission might transfer to its own general account the money in its construction account, "until such date as the amounts paid to such harness race track from the construction account, less income taxes paid thereon to the United States by such harness race track, equals the cost of the capital improvement". In 1959 the Legislature amended section 45-a to prohibit the approval of the Harness Racing Commission of any new capital expenditures thereunder (L. 1959, ch. 881, § 7) and provided that no harness racing track "shall be reimbursed" from the construction account "for any income taxes paid to the United States", whether paid before or after the effective date of the amendment (L. 1959, ch. 881, § 7, amdg. § 45-a, subd. 10; see, also, L. 1959, ch. 881, § 10). Roosevelt Raceway, Inc., completed capital improvements in 1957 at a cost of $ 19,605,281. The amount in its construction account for that year was $ 2,858,495, and that sum was transferred to its general account. Receipt of such sum resulted in a Federal income tax of $ 1,404,665. In view of the explicit declaration of the 1959 statute that no harness racing track may be reimbursed from the construction account for any income taxes paid to the United States, "whether paid before or after the effective date of this section", Roosevelt Raceway is not entitled to reimbursement for these Federal taxes and its petition to compel the Commission to record a credit for such taxes must be dismissed.

2. The 1959 amendment was intended to apply to improvements previously authorized and already constructed, and not, as claimed, to projects to be completed after the amendment became effective.

3. Resort may not be had to legislative history to construe the unambiguous language of the 1959 enactment; in any event, the legislative history is not in conflict with the plain meaning of this statute.

4. The 1959 amendment, insofar as it put an end to the State's obligation to reimburse for Federal taxes, does not represent an unconstitutional impairment of a contractual obligation (U. S. Const., art. I, § 10).

5. The 1959 amendment is entirely prospective in its operation.

6. The courts below rightly rejected the contention of the Attorney-General that the 1956 statute, properly construed, did not provide for inclusion of the Federal income tax in ascertaining and determining the amount of reimbursement.

7. The Legislature in 1959 "clarified" the 1956 statute by declaring that section 45-a, as written and enacted in 1956, was not intended to provide for the reimbursement of Federal income taxes (L. 1959, ch. 881, § 10). The Legislature has no power to declare, retroactively, that an existing statute shall receive a given construction when such a construction is contrary to that which the statute would ordinarily have received.

8. Legislation will be held unconstitutional only as a last resort. Arguments that section 45-a as originally enacted in 1956 is totally unconstitutional are not considered since the petition must be dismissed even if the constitutional questions raised were decided in petitioner's favor.

American Jewish Congress v. Carter


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


9 N.Y.2d 223; 173 N.E.2d 788; 213 N.Y.S.2d 60; 1961 N.Y. LEXIS 1446; 47 L.R.R.M. 2949; 1 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 17; 42 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P50,174; 1 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P9671

451 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Schillawski v. State


Claim Nos. 32769, 32760


Court of Appeals of New York


9 N.Y.2d 235; 173 N.E.2d 793; 213 N.Y.S.2d 68; 1961 N.Y. LEXIS 1448


November 16, 1960, Argued  
March 2, 1961, Decided



DISPOSITION: In each action: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In consolidated appeals, appellant property owners challenged the judgments of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Fourth Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed judgments dismissing the claims of the property owners for compensation by respondent state for encroaching on lands owned by the property owners.


OVERVIEW: Property owners filed claims for damages arising out of an alleged de facto appropriation of land for use in the reconstruction of a state highway, which was widened. The property owners claimed that in performing that work, the state encroached on lands owned by them. The trial court dismissed the property owners' claims, and the appellate court affirmed. The property owners appealed, but the court affirmed the dismissal. It was established as a fact that the highway at issue was the old Seneca Turnpike, which was laid out under color of statute. Thus, the state acquired a right of way by prescription to a width as provided for in the statute. The state had a prescriptive right to the width prescribed by the statute, even though it was greater than the extent of actual use. Compensation was properly disallowed for the alleged encroachment on land within the right of way, and because the property owners had not established any measure of damages relating to the encroachment on land that was not in the right of way, no award was made.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgments that affirmed the dismissal of the property owners' claims for compensation by the state for an alleged encroachment upon land owned by the property owners.


HEADNOTES

Highways -- encroachment on right of way -- claimants' property located within 99-foot-wide highway which was established by statute (L. 1800, ch. 78) -- highway improved for 20 feet of width and, in course of reconstruction, widened to 48 feet -- compensation properly disallowed -- statute, not user, established width of road -- State acquired 99-foot right of way by prescription as provided by statute.

1. In the course of the reconstruction of a State highway, the two-lane concrete sections of the road were widened from 20 feet to 48 feet, with the result that the State allegedly encroached on claimants' property on the north of the highway. The highway originally came into existence pursuant to a statute (L. 1800, ch. 78), which provided for a right of way 99 feet wide, but the highway was not improved to its full width. Where a road has been laid out under a statute, it is the statute and not user that determines the width. The failure of the State to occupy the full width, or to improve the road in the manner provided, does not constitute an abandonment of the easement of the unused portion. The alleged encroachment on the north of the highway being within the right of way, compensation was properly disallowed.

2. The parcels on the south side of the highway were outside of the right of way. In any event, claimants had not established any measure of damages relating to that encroachment.

3. Since the road was laid out under a statute, the State acquired a right of way by prescription to a width of 99 feet as provided for in the statute.

 

452 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re Coates


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


9 N.Y.2d 242; 173 N.E.2d 797; 213 N.Y.S.2d 74; 1961 N.Y. LEXIS 1449


January 11, 1961, Argued  
March 2, 1961, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, an alleged mentally ill person, sought review of an order entered in favor of respondent medical officer by the Supreme Court in the Fourth Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed in part the trial court's order that ratified and confirmed a county judge's order directing the alleged mentally ill person's admission to a hospital and denying her motion to declare invalid and unconstitutional N.Y. Mental Hyg. Law §§ 74, 76.


OVERVIEW: The alleged mentally ill person, who had not received personal service of notice or a hearing, was certified to the state hospital for observation and treatment for a period not exceeding 60 days by order of the county judge. The alleged mentally ill person challenged the constitutionality of §§ 74, 76 insofar as they permitted continued confinement without the prior notice or hearing, which she maintained was required by the due process clauses of the state and federal Constitutions. The court affirmed the order and held that: 1) the statute was not unconstitutional as denying due process because the finality of the order was subject to the right of review; 2) the continued confinement could be challenged immediately after the filing of a § 74 certificate; and 3) the 30 day period of limitation withdrawing the right to challenge the confinement did not begin to run until notice of the filing had been given.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the certification order.


HEADNOTES

Insane and other incompetent persons -- incompetency hearing -- notice -- appellant's temporary commitment to institution for mentally ill without personal notice of application valid -- commitment order achieves tentative finality upon filing of certificate of necessity of continued treatment -- continuance of confinement beyond temporary period not unconstitutional where patient has right to complete rehearing of determination -- certification order provides for disclosure of papers to parties and interested persons -- patient must receive actual personal service of notice before 30-day period during which demand may be made for jury trial challenging commitment commences to run -- court not compelled to imply provision that notice shall not be given -- due process satisfied where commitment order and finding as to need for continued treatment are subject to review before becoming conclusive.

I. Upon the petition of appellant's husband and the medical statements of two examining physicians, appellant was certified to an institution for the mentally ill by order of a County Judge for observation and treatment for a period not exceeding 60 days. The order further provided that, if within the 60-day period a person in charge of the institution filed a certificate that continued care and treatment were required, the temporary commitment order would become final. Appellant was not personally served with notice of the original application for the commitment order since, under section 74 of the Mental Hygiene Law, notice may be dispensed with, nor did she receive notice of the filing of the certificate. Appellant petitioned for a jury trial pursuant to section 76 of the Mental Hygiene Law, which provides that a committed person may obtain a review of the final order within 30 days after the making of such order. The petition was denied as being untimely, since it was not made within 30 days after the filing of the certificate. Appellant's temporary confinement without actual personal notice to her was valid.

2. The continuance of confinement beyond the temporary period is not violative of due process or otherwise unconstitutional, since the patient has the right to a prompt and complete rehearing of the determination ab initio under section 76, and thus the finality of the court order is subject to the right of review.

3. The fact that a hearing is not provided for until after the certification order has become final does not invalidate the statute.

4. Appellant's contention that at the hearing she will have no means of determining what proof will be offered against her is without merit, since the order of certification provides for disclosure of the papers to parties and properly interested persons upon court order.

5. The arguments in favor of dispensing with notice of the original application for the commitment order are not applicable once the patient has been received in custody. Nothing short of actual personal service of notice upon the patient that the 30-day period in which she must make her demand for a jury trial has commenced to run will suffice to commence the running of this period of limitation.

6. No express provision is contained in section 76 that notice shall not be given to the patient, and the court is not compelled to imply such a provision, especially where such a construction might render the statute violative of due process.

7. Inasmuch as the initial temporary order of commitment is subject to the review provisions of section 76, it cannot be final if review is invoked until after a determination by the court in the section 76 proceeding. Thus appellant is in fact offered the opportunity to challenge before the court the findings as to the need for continued treatment contained in the filed certificate before that finding becomes conclusive upon her and due process is thus satisfied.

 

453 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Hempstead v. Goldblatt


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


9 N.Y.2d 101; 172 N.E.2d 562; 211 N.Y.S.2d 185; 1961 N.Y. LEXIS 1531


October 18, 1960, Argued  
January 19, 1961, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, landowner and operator of a sand and gravel pit, appealed from a judgment of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which upheld the constitutionality of Ordinance No. 16 of respondent town, and enjoining them from operating a sand and gravel pit until a permit has been issued by the town, and certain violations of the said ordinance have been corrected.


OVERVIEW: The sand and gravel pit at issue was located in the midst of a densely populated residential area. In order to provide protection against the danger of cave-ins, falls, drowning and water pollution, the town passed Hempstead, NY, Ordinance No. 16 which made provision for setbacks, degrees of slope, barricades, fences, lights, retaining walls and maximum ground water level. The town sought an injunction against the landowner and the operator of the sand and gravel pit which conditioned future operation of the pit upon the issuance of a permit from the town and the correction of certain violations of the ordinance. The trial court upheld the constitutionality of the ordinance and issued the requested injunction. On appeal, the Court of Appeals of New York rejected the argument that ordinance was confiscatory and unconstitutionally violated the right to due process, noting that neither the injunction granted nor the ordinance proscribed any future excavation. Because there was a rational basis for the legislative choice and no factual support for declaring it arbitrary, the court affirmed the ordinance.


OUTCOME: The judgment upholding the conditional use ordinance was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- regulation of sand and gravel business -- ordinance of Town of Hempstead regulating defendants' sand and gravel business constitutional -- defendants properly enjoined from conducting business until permit issued and violations of ordinance corrected -- safety standards required not unreasonable.

Special Term properly upheld the constitutionality of an ordinance of the Town of Hempstead regulating defendants' sand and gravel business and enjoined defendants from conducting sand mining and excavation on certain property in the town until a permit had been issued and certain violations of the ordinance had been corrected. Defendants' proof failed to overcome the presumption of constitutionality and to establish the unreasonableness of the regulations. The sand and gravel pit lies in the midst of a densely populated residential area. In order to provide protection against the danger of cave-ins, falls, drowning and water pollution, the ordinance makes provision for setbacks, degrees of slopes, barricades, fences, lights, retaining walls and maximum ground water level. Future excavation is not enjoined. It has not been demonstrated that the minimal safety standards required by the town are an unreasonable means to protect against the hazards to both life and property accompanying the uncontrolled operation of these pits. There was a rational basis for the legislative action and no factual support for declaring it arbitrary.

People v. Munoz


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


9 N.Y.2d 51; 172 N.E.2d 535; 211 N.Y.S.2d 146; 1961 N.Y. LEXIS 1568


October 13, 1960, Argued  
January 12, 1961, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed and complaint dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed, by permission, the order of the Appellate Part of the Court of Special Sessions of the City of New York (New York), which affirmed the trial court's conviction of defendant for a violation of New York City Administrative Code § 436-5.2.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted for having had in his possession, in his right pocket, an ordinary two-bladed small pocket penknife in violation of § 436-5.2. The appellate court affirmed the conviction. Defendant appealed, arguing that carrying a penknife could not have been made a crime. On appeal, by permission, the court reversed defendant's conviction and dismissed the complaint. The court held that § 436-5.2 was too vague and too general to indicate what persons were included or what acts were prohibited. Carrying a pocket penknife or many of the other articles included in §436-5.2 was not evil in itself. Guilt, if any, consisted entirely in violation of a legislative fiat. Therefore, it was necessary that the elements of the offense were spelled out in words even more explicitly, so that the citizen could receive unequivocal warning before conduct otherwise innocent be made the cause of fine or imprisonment. While juvenile delinquents could have been highly dangerous, especially in large cities, they cannot be legislated out of existence, however, nor held guilty of offenses on the sole basis of status.


OUTCOME: The court reversed judgment of the appellate court, which affirmed defendant's conviction in the trial court for carrying a penknife. The court dismissed the complaint against defendant.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- carrying knife -- constitutional law -- section 436-5.2 of Administrative Code of City of New York which declares it unlawful to carry knife in public place is unconstitutional -- conviction for carrying two-bladed small pocket penknife reversed and charge dismissed.

1. Section 436-5.2 of the Administrative Code of the City of New York, which declares that it shall be unlawful for any person under 21 years of age to carry a knife or sharp pointed or edged instrument which may be used for cutting or puncturing, in any public place, is unconstitutional and, accordingly, judgment convicting defendant of a violation of that section upon evidence that he was carrying in his right pocket a two-bladed small pocket penknife is reversed and the charge dismissed.

2. The section is too vague and too general to indicate what persons are included or what acts are prohibited. The statute is not a proper exercise of police power.

People v. Merolla


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


9 N.Y.2d 62; 172 N.E.2d 541; 211 N.Y.S.2d 155; 1961 N.Y. LEXIS 1569; 47 L.R.R.M. 2802


November 30, 1960, Argued  
January 12, 1961, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court (New York), which affirmed defendant's conviction for loitering, in violation of § 7 of the Waterfront Commission Act (Act), 1953 N.Y. Laws ch. 882.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested for loitering after police officers observed defendant standing on a pier for an extended period of time. On appeal, defendant contended that the Act was unconstitutional and the evidence presented at trial was insufficient to support a conviction. Upon review, the court affirmed the judgment of conviction. The court held that the Act did not offend constitutional guarantees. The Act was directed at preventing the necessary evils that came with criminal activity that was known to occur at the waterfront, such as loansharking and book-making. The Act was not vague and indefinite and specifically prohibited loitering at the waterfront facility.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's judgment of conviction for loitering, in violation of the Waterfront Commission Act.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- loitering at waterfront -- section 7 of Waterfront Commission Act (L. 1953, ch. 882, as amd. by L. 1957, ch. 188), which prohibits loitering upon waterfront facility or within 500 feet thereof at Port of New York, not unconstitutional -- meaning of term "loiter" sufficiently clear and definite.

1. Section 7 of the Waterfront Commission Act (L. 1953, ch. 882, as amd. by L. 1957, ch. 188) which provides that "No person shall, without a satisfactory explanation, loiter upon any vessel, dock, wharf, pier, bulkhead, terminal, warehouse, or other waterfront facility or within five hundred feet thereof in that portion of the Port of New York district within the state of New York" is not unconstitutional. The meaning of the term "loiter", in context, is sufficiently clear and definite.

2. The use of the words "without a satisfactory explanation" serves to restrict the scope of the statute to the advantage of the accused.

3. Since defendant was within 100 feet of the pier structure itself and in the Port of New York district and not on a public street, defendant has no cause for complaint that the provision "within five hundred feet thereof" might unconstitutionally cover public streets and thoroughfares.

People v. Bunis


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


9 N.Y.2d 1; 172 N.E.2d 273; 210 N.Y.S.2d 505; 1961 N.Y. LEXIS 1596


December 1, 1960, Argued  
January 5, 1961, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged an order of the Supreme Court, Erie County (New York), which reversed the order of the city court that dismissed the information charging appellant with a violation of N.Y. Penal Code § 436-d, knowingly selling magazines from which the cover or title page had been removed. Appellant challenged the statute's constitutionality.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was the owner of a bookshop and was charged with violating N.Y. Penal Code § 436-d by knowingly selling coverless magazines. The city court dismissed the information, but the supreme court reversed and reinstated the information. On appeal, the court reversed the order of the supreme court and dismissed the information. The court held that § 436-d was unconstitutional. The statute actually enacted, went far beyond any purpose to avert criminal activity by booksellers or others to cheat magazine publishers out of their fees. By absolutely forbidding any person from selling any coverless magazine, regardless of the circumstances under which, or the reasons for which, the cover may have been removed, the legislature prohibited unquestionably legitimate sales and rendered criminal conduct that carried not the slightest taint of corruption or impropriety. The actual sale of the coverless magazines was not wrong, but their sale by a vendor who took part in a scheme to defraud a magazine publisher.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the supreme court and dismissed the information.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- sale of publication with cover removed -- section 436-d of Penal Law, which provides that person who sells or offers for sale any publication from which cover or title page has been removed is guilty of misdemeanor, is unconstitutional.

Section 436-d of the Penal Law, which provides that "Any person who knowingly sells, offers or exposes for sale, (except in bulk as waste paper) any newspaper, magazine, periodical or other publication * * * from which the cover or title page has been removed, or from which the title, trade name, trade mark or other identification mark has been removed or obliterated, is guilty of a misdemeanor", is unconstitutional. The purpose of the statute was to prevent the sale of magazines or other periodicals recalled by the publisher or returned for credit. The statute goes beyond its purpose. It prohibits legitimate sales and renders criminal conduct which is not corrupt or improper. Even if it be assumed that the Legislature had the power to prohibit the sales which it intended to prohibit, it is beyond the police power to prohibit all sales in order to prevent those which are improper.

458 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Wilson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


8 N.Y.2d 391; 171 N.E.2d 310; 208 N.Y.S.2d 963; 1960 N.Y. LEXIS 856


October 17, 1960, Argued  
November 30, 1960, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Supreme Court, Appellate Division, in Queens County (New York), that affirmed a judgment by the county court convicting defendant of the crime of attempted robbery in the third degree upon a plea of guilty.


OVERVIEW: Defendant claimed that he was denied due process when, after an indictment against him had been dismissed for want of a speedy trial, he was re-indicted for the same crime. Specifically, defendant claimed that his rights were infringed when, in reliance on N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 673, he was re-indicted on the same charge which was the basis of an indictment against him and dismissed nearly three years later on his motion made under N.Y. Crim. Proc. Law § 668, for failure of the people to bring the cause to trial. The court held that the appellate division erred when it affirmed the conviction because to allow such re-indictment, even though within the statute of limitations, would have denied defendant of his right to a speedy trial.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment that convicted defendant of attempted robbery in the third degree and dismissed the indictment against him.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- right to speedy trial -- where defendant was indicted in January, 1955 and arraigned in October, 1956 and Appellate Division later determined that he had been denied speedy trial, reindictment in November, 1958 for same crime denied him right to speedy and public trial (Code Crim. Pro., § 8; Civil Rights Law, § 12) -- delay extreme and reindictment not permissible under section 673 of Code of Criminal Procedure.

1. Defendant was indicted in January, 1955 and was arraigned in October, 1956. In December, 1957 the Appellate Division determined that he had been denied a speedy trial. In November, 1958 defendant was reindicted for the same crime. His motion to dismiss this indictment because of delay should have been granted. Defendant is entitled to a speedy and public trial (Code Crim. Pro., § 8; Civil Rights Law, § 12) and is entitled to this statutory guarantee even though it is not a constitutional guarantee. The right to a prompt trial is fundamental.

2. Section 673 of the Code of Criminal Procedure provides that an order for a dismissal of an indictment is not a bar to another prosecution "if the offense charged be a felony". Section 142 of the Code of Criminal Procedure provides that felonies must be prosecuted within five years. These statutes cannot be reconciled, or at least they cannot be literally applied in cases such as the present one.

3. There may be occasions when there may be a reindictment under section 673 of the Code of Criminal Procedure, but the delay in the present case was extreme and the prosecution failed to show good cause for the delay.

In re Turton


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


8 N.Y.2d 311; 170 N.E.2d 190; 206 N.Y.S.2d 761; 1960 N.Y. LEXIS 931


October 3, 1960, Argued  
October 21, 1960, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, with costs in all courts to appellant payable out of the estate, and the application of the Government of British Honduras to intervene in the proceedings now pending in the Surrogates' Court of New York County granted. Question certified answered in the negative.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant government of a foreign nation (government) challenged an order of the New York County Surrogate's Court (New York) insofar as it denied the government's application to intervene in a proceeding to probate the will of a testator who was domiciled in the foreign nation. The order granted its application to appear specially. A question was certified as to whether the order of the trial court was properly made.


OVERVIEW: The state's interest in the estate stemmed from stock certificates and bank or brokerage accounts located in the state. Both the state and the foreign government sought to recover on the basis that there were no known heirs entitled to inherit in intestacy. The testator left a will executed in 1918 and a version of that will was admitted to probate without erasures and interlineations in 1955. A probate proceeding concerning other wills was pending in the foreign nation when the state trial court assumed jurisdiction to probate the 1955 will. The court reversed the order insofar as appealed from. The court held that under N.Y. Surr. Ct. Proc. Act § 147, any person interested in a will as devisee, legatee, or otherwise could file objections to the probate of the will. The foreign government's claim on the assets of the estate in the event of intestacy qualified it to file objections under the statute because its rights could be affected if the will was probated in the state. There was no reason why the state's attorney general should be allowed to intervene while the government of the nation where the testator and all of his descendants were domiciled was excluded.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order insofar as it was appealed from, answered the certified question in the negative, and directed the foreign government's application to intervene in the proceedings to probate the testator's 1955 will to be granted.


HEADNOTES

Wills -- probate -- right to intervene -- alleged will of domiciliary of British Honduras, executed in 1918, admitted to probate there "until a later will be found" -- while action pending in British Honduras court to have 1918 will declared revoked and to probate alleged will of 1955, New York County Surrogate's Court assumed jurisdiction to probate 1955 will -- Government of British Honduras, claiming assets as in intestacy, entitled to intervene.

Testator was domiciled in British Honduras. Stock certificates in 50 corporations were discovered in New York County, as well as several bank or brokerage accounts, also belonging to decedent at the time of his death. A court of British Honduras ordered that a will purportedly executed in 1918 be admitted to probate "until a later will be found". Thereafter an action was brought in a British Honduras court to have the 1918 will declared revoked and to probate an alleged will of 1955 as a lost or destroyed testament and while that action remained undetermined the Surrogate in New York County assumed jurisdiction to probate the 1955 will. Section 147 of the Surrogate's Court Act permits any person to file objections to the probate of a will who is "interested in the event as devisee, legatee or otherwise". The Government of British Honduras, which claims the assets in this estate wherever situated under its statute entitling it to recover in the event of intestacy in default of lawful distributees, and enabling it to use the assets to "provide, in accordance with the existing practice, for dependents, whether kindred or not, of the intestate, and other persons for whom the intestate might reasonably have been expected to make provision", should be permitted to intervene in the New York proceeding.

Scarsdale Supply Co. v. Scarsdale


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


8 N.Y.2d 325; 170 N.E.2d 198; 206 N.Y.S.2d 773; 1960 N.Y. LEXIS 933


October 5, 1960, Argued  
October 21, 1960, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owner challenged a decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed the trial court's dismissal of the property owner's action against defendant village for a declaratory judgment that the village's rezoning of the property owner's property was unconstitutional because it was overburdensome, unreasonable, and confiscatory.


OVERVIEW: The property owner operated a building supply yard on its property. The village amended the building zone ordinance and placed the property in a new residential district. The property owner filed an action against the village for a declaration that the rezoning of its property was unconstitutional. Although the trial court found that the property owner produced evidence to prove that the property was not practical or profitable for residential purposes, it dismissed the property owner's complaint because there was no deprivation of property where a nonconforming use existed. The appellate court affirmed, and the court reversed and remitted. The court held that the property owner was entitled to equitable relief if the deliberate confiscation and depreciation of its property was a present invasion of its property rights. The court rejected the village's argument that the property owner failed to exhaust its administrative remedy. The property owner was not required to apply for a variance as a condition precedent to a judicial remedy because it alleged that the ordinance destroyed the marketability and value of the property for commercial and residential uses.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's decision that affirmed the dismissal of the property owner's action against a village to have the rezoning of its property declared unconstitutional. The court remitted the action to the trial court for a determination of the material issues presented by the record.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- zoning -- in action for judgment declaring that rezoning of plaintiff's property was unreasonable and confiscatory, trial court erred in failing to decide whether alleged confiscation and resulting depreciation in value of property, regardless of existence of nonconforming use, constitutes present invasion of property rights -- application for variance not condition precedent.

1. In an action for judgment declaring that the rezoning of plaintiff's property was unreasonable and confiscatory, the trial court, although finding that plaintiff produced evidence which might be sufficient to prove that the property could not be practically or profitably used for residential purposes, dismissed the complaint, stating that, because of the existence of a nonconforming use, there was no justiciable controversy or deprivation of property by the zoning restrictions placed upon plaintiff's property. The trial court erred in failing to decide whether the alleged deliberate confiscation and alleged resulting depreciation in value of plaintiff's property, regardless of the existence of a nonconforming use, constitutes a present invasion on plaintiff's property rights. If it does so, the existence and maintenance of the ordinance and the threat to continue it under these circumstances would entitle plaintiff to equitable relief. Plaintiff is entitled to try to prove that the property is so situated that it has no possibility for residential and zoned uses and is most readily adaptable to use now precluded under the zoning ordinance.

2. Where the pleadings allege that the ordinance in and of itself destroys the marketability and value of the property for commercial and residential uses, an application for a variance is not a condition precedent to a judicial remedy.

July 8, 1960, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner landlord appealed an order of the Municipal Court of the City of New York, Borough of Manhattan, Ninth District (New York), which dismissed the landlord's petition in summary proceedings to recover the possession of real property from respondent tenant.


OVERVIEW: The landlord brought the instant action to recover the possession of real property, and challenged the constitutionality of the 1959 re-enactment and extension, 1959 N.Y. Laws 809, of the Business Rent Law, 1945 N.Y. Laws 314, as amended, insofar as it was applicable to office space. The municipal court dismissed the landlord's petition, and the landlord appealed. On review, the court affirmed, finding that the landlord's evidence, as developed at the trial, served only to demonstrate that the intensity of the emergency which prevailed in 1955 had moderated to some extent, but not in substantial degree. However, the Legislature had implemented a program of gradual relaxation of controls. The court concluded that such a process of gradual, rather than abrupt, cessation of controls, commensurate with the moderation in market conditions, effectuated a transition from controls to normal landlord-tenant relations in a rational and orderly manner, without economic disruption and dislocation. Such a program was designed to protect and promote the public health, safety, and general welfare, and was hardly capricious and arbitrary or otherwise violative of constitutional guarantees.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the municipal court's order.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and tenant -- rent regulation -- constitutional law -- re-enactment and extension of Business Rent Law (L. 1945, ch. 314, as amd. by L. 1959, ch. 809), insofar as applicable to office space, not unconstitutional.

The re-enactment and extension of the Business Rent Law (L. 1945, ch. 314, as amd. by L. 1959, ch. 809), insofar as it is applicable to office space, is not unconstitutional. A rational basis for the re-enacted statute yet remains. The Legislature has adopted a process of gradual, rather than abrupt, cessation of controls commensurate with the moderation in market conditions. That process effectuates a transition from controls to normal landlord-tenant-relations in a rational and orderly manner, without economic disruption and dislocation. Such a program is not capricious or arbitrary or otherwise violative of constitutional guarantees.

Nat'l Psychological Ass'n for Psychoanalysis, Inc. v. Univ. of N.Y.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


8 N.Y.2d 197; 168 N.E.2d 649; 203 N.Y.S.2d 821; 1960 N.Y. LEXIS 1066


June 6, 1960, Argued  
July 8, 1960, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff psychologist association sought review of a judgment by the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed a declaration that N.Y. Educ. Law art. 153 was constitutional, in the association's action against defendant state university.


OVERVIEW: The association challenged the constitutionality of N.Y. Educ. Law §§ 7601-7614, which dealt with the certification of psychologists. The appellate division affirmed the constitutionality of the New York Education Law. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court found that the New York Education Law helped the public identify those who had appropriate training and background in the profession of psychology through the medium of official certification of qualifications. The court also found that the New York Education Law afforded protection against the incompetent practitioner by providing a yardstick by which the public could judge the competence of persons offering psychological services. The court further determined that the lack of a definition for psychologist did not render the New York Education Law unconstitutional because the law was easily complied with by the non-use of particular words.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the constitutionality of the New York Education Law in the association's action challenging the provisions that dealt with the certification of psychologists.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- certification of psychologists -- article 153 of Education Law, which proscribes professional use of title "psychologist" and its derivatives for remuneration by those not certified in accord with statute, not unconstitutional.

1. Article 153 of the Education Law, which proscribes the professional use of the title "psychologist" and its derivatives for remuneration by those who are not certified in accord with the statute, is not unconstitutional. The statute is reasonably related to the public health and welfare and constitutes a proper exercise of the State's police power.

2. The failure of the statute to include a formal definition of "psychologist" or its derivatives does not render the statute unconstitutional. There was no invalid delegation of legislative power. The statute prescribes discernible standards to govern the exercise of the power of certification. In practical effect a psychologist is defined as an individual who meets prescribed standards of fitness (Education Law § 7605). Since the statute merely proscribes the use of specific words to describe the rendition of services, without making it criminal for those not certified to render such services, no further definition of that term is constitutionally required or necessary.
