Church v. Islip


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


8 N.Y.2d 254; 168 N.E.2d 680; 203 N.Y.S.2d 866; 1960 N.Y. LEXIS 1074


May 16, 1960, Argued  
July 8, 1960, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants property owners challenged a judgment from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which reversed a previous judgment that declared an amendment to the Zoning Ordinance of the respondent town unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Property owners brought a declaratory judgment suit against the town to have declared unconstitutional and void a 1954 zoning change of defendant's corner lot in town. The referee found for the property owners holding that the change was illegal "spot zoning" and that the imposition by the town board of conditions was illegal "contract zoning." Town appealed to the appellate division which reversed and declared the amendment to be valid. Property owners appealed the reversal arguing that the rezoning was illegal as "contract zoning" because the town board, as a condition for rezoning, required the owners to execute and record restrictive covenants. The court of appeals affirmed. It held that the restrictive covenants were intended for the benefit of the neighbors. Because the owners accepted them, there was no one in a position to contest them. To meet increasing needs of the county's own population explosion, and at the same time to make as gradual and as little of an annoyance as possible the change from residence to business on the main highways, the town board imposed conditions. There was nothing unconstitutional about it.


OUTCOME: The appellate division's judgment which reversed the referee's decision that the zoning change of the property in question was unconstitutional was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- zoning -- change of zoning of corner lot from Residence A to Business upon stated conditions not unconstitutional.

In 1954 defendant Town Board changed the zoning of a corner lot in the Town of Islip from Residence A to Business and required the owners of the lot to agree to stated conditions. The Appellate Division properly determined that the change in zoning was not unconstitutional. This zoning is a legislative act and is presumably valid. There being a factual basis therefor, it may not be invalidated. The reasonable conditions imposed by the Town Board do not render such legislation invalid.

 

465 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Claim of Albertson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


8 N.Y.2d 77; 168 N.E.2d 242; 202 N.Y.S.2d 5; 1960 N.Y. LEXIS 1207


March 28, 1960, Argued  
May 26, 1960, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order of the Appellate Division modified in accordance with the opinion herein and, as so modified, affirmed, with costs to claimant-respondent against the Industrial Commissioner.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Industrial Commissioner challenged the judgment of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court, Third Judicial Department, (New York), which reversed the denial of unemployment insurance benefits to respondent former employee. The commissioner also sought review of the appellate division's reversal of the suspension of respondents, state and national communist parties, under the state unemployment insurance system.


OVERVIEW: The commissioner sought review of two appellate division judgments permitting the state and national communist parties and their former employee to participate in the state unemployment insurance program. The court first affirmed the appellate division judgment reversing the denial of unemployment insurance benefits to the former employee. It would have been unreasonably punitive to withhold benefits to the former employee because the employers, which paid unemployment taxes for the former employee, were communist. The court disagreed with the appellate division with regard to the participation of the state and national communist parties in the state unemployment insurance program. Under the Federal Communist Control Act (Act), 50 U.S.C.S. § 842, the Communist Party was not entitled to the rights, privileges, and immunities on the same basis as other entities. The commissioner was authorized under the Act to deprive the state and national communist parties of their status as employers in the program. The court reversed the decision to set aside the commissioner's suspension of the state and national communist parties' registration in the state unemployment insurance program.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment reversing the denial of unemployment insurance benefits to the former employee. The court also reversed the judgment that set aside the commissioner's suspension of the state and national communist parties' registration under the state unemployment insurance system.


HEADNOTES

Unemployment insurance -- Communist Party as employer -- Industrial Commissioner justified in suspending registration of Communist Parties as "employers" within meaning of Unemployment Insurance Law -- claimant, however, may not be denied unemployment insurance award solely because part of base period of employment was with Communist organizations -- Federal Communist Control Act (U. S. Code, tit. 50, § 842) not unconstitutional.

1. Claimant may not be denied an unemployment insurance award solely because part of his base period of employment was with Communist organizations. (Chief Judge Desmond and Judges Dye, Fuld and Froessel.)

2. The Industrial Commissioner was justified in suspending the registration of the Communist Parties as "employers" within the meaning of the Unemployment Insurance Law. The Federal Communist Control Act (U. S. Code, tit. 50, § 842) declared that the Communist Party is "not entitled to any of the rights, privileges, and immunities attendant upon legal bodies created under the jurisdiction of the laws of the United States or any political subdivision thereof; and whatever rights, privileges and immunities which have heretofore been granted to said party or any subsidiary organization by reason of the laws of the United States or any political subdivision thereof are terminated". Although paying the unemployment insurance tax may not be claiming an "immunity" or "right", whatever value the status may have which goes with payment of the tax -- enrollment as an employer -- is being sought and claimed by the Communist Parties. (Chief Judge Desmond and Judges Dye, Van Voorhis and Burke.)

3. The Federal Communist Control Act is not unconstitutional. It is not a bill of attainder or ex post facto legislation. There is no denial of due process in the deprivation of these organizations of their status without a hearing. (Chief Judge Desmond and Judges Dye, Van Voorhis and Burke.)

 

466 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Riela


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


7 N.Y.2d 571; 166 N.E.2d 840; 200 N.Y.S.2d 43; 1960 N.Y. LEXIS 1319


March 2, 1960, Argued  
April 21, 1960, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A grand jury charged defendant with 17 separate crimes of contempt in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 600. The County Court of Tioga County (New York) entered judgment, finding defendant guilty on all 17 counts, sentenced him to 60 days in jail upon each count concurrently, and imposed a fine of $ 250 on each count. On review, the appellate division affirmed. Defendant appealed again.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was called as a witness before the grand jury. He subsequently refused to answer 17 questions concerning a well-attended meeting at a country estate, arguing to do so might tend to incriminate him. Thereupon, at the request of the district attorney, the grand jury conferred immunity upon defendant and ordered him to answer the questions previously put to him. The same 17 questions were again asked and defendant, reiterating his reliance upon the privilege, again refused to answer. As a consequence of his refusal, the grand jury indicted him. After a bench trial, he was convicted on all counts. On appeal, the court reversed and remitted for resentencing because defendant's conviction of 17 separate crimes of contempt, each susceptible to punishment of a year in prison, was totally unjustified.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment that convicted defendant of 17 separate counts of contempt and remitted to the county court for resentencing.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- contempt -- privilege against self incrimination -- defendant, who refused to answer 17 questions regarding "Apalachin meeting", guilty of one crime of contempt, not 17.

1. Defendant, called as a witness before a Grand Jury which was investigating the so-called "Apalachin meeting", refused to answer 17 questions concerning that meeting on the ground that to do so might tend to incriminate him. He again refused to answer when he was granted immunity pursuant to section 2447 of the Penal Law. His conviction thereafter of 17 separate crimes of criminal contempt, each susceptible to a punishment of a year in prison, was not justified. It was apparent from the beginning of his interrogation that, relying upon his privilege, he would decline to answer any questions bearing on the "Apalachin meeting". The questions, although different from one another, all related to the one subject. He is, therefore, guilty of a single contempt.

2. The Grand Jury had power to confer immunity upon defendant and direct him to answer under pain of prosecution for contempt.

3. A witness may be compelled to answer in a state proceeding, as long as the immunity granted by the state protects against prosecution under its laws, even though it may not protect against prosecution by the federal government or by other states.

 

467 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Meola


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


7 N.Y.2d 391; 165 N.E.2d 851; 198 N.Y.S.2d 276; 1960 N.Y. LEXIS 1431


February 25, 1960, Argued  
March 3, 1960, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed and case remitted to the County Court for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state challenged a decision from the County Court of Orange County (New York), which reversed defendant's conviction for failing to dim her headlights in violation of N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 15(3).


OVERVIEW: Defendant had her highbeam headlights on when a state trooper passed her. The trooper signaled for her to turn them off, but she failed to do so. The officer issued her a summons. At her trial, defendant filed a motion to dismiss the information upon the ground that § 15(3) was invalid and unconstitutional. The lower court denied her motion. Defendant was convicted of violating § 15(3) and was fined. The county court reversed because the language of § 15(3) was vague. The court reversed. The court held that § 15(3) was sufficiently definite, clear, and positive to give "unequivocal warning" to citizens of the rule to be obeyed. The phrase "dazzling light" in § 15(3) referred to defendant's conduct in her use of high, as opposed to low, headlight beams. The word "interfere" in § 15(3) referred to hampering or hindering the vision of the approaching trooper. Defendant's conviction was proper because she used her highbeam headlights and hindered the trooper's vision as he approached her vehicle on the road.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the county court's order that reversed the lower court and entered judgment in favor of defendant. The court remitted the case to the county court for further proceedings.


HEADNOTES

Motor vehicles -- failure to dim headlights -- provision of Vehicle and Traffic Law ( § 15, subd. 3) that motorist proceeding toward approaching vehicle must operate multiple beam headlamps in such manner "that dazzling light does not interfere with the driver of the approaching vehicle" is not unconstitutional -- it is sufficiently definite, clear and positive to give warning of rule to be obeyed -- "interference" contemplated by statute is susceptible of factual proof -- matter remitted to County Court for determination upon questions of fact raised in that court.

1. Defendant was convicted of failing to dim her automobile's headlights in violation of that part of subdivision 3 of section 15 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law which provides that, when an approaching vehicle is 500 or less feet away, a motorist proceeding toward it must operate multiple beam headlamps in such a manner "that dazzling light does not interfere with the driver of the approaching vehicle". That portion of the statute is sufficiently definite, clear and positive to give unequivocal warning of the rule to be obeyed and is constitutional. The phrase "dazzling light" manifestly refers to the use of high, as opposed to low, headlight beams. The word "interfere" plainly means to hinder or hamper the vision of an approaching motorist.

2. The interference contemplated by the statute was hampering or hindering the vision of an approaching motorist, and this element of interference is susceptible of factual proof.

3. If the evidence shows that, under the conditions prevailing, the use of high beams could not reasonably have interfered with the driver of an approaching vehicle, then no infraction has been committed. The matter is remitted to the County Court for determination upon the questions of fact raised in that court.

 

468 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Grove Hill Realty Co. v. Ferncliff Cemetery Asso.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


7 N.Y.2d 403; 165 N.E.2d 858; 198 N.Y.S.2d 287; 1960 N.Y. LEXIS 1433


January 5, 1960, Argued  
March 3, 1960, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff realty company appealed the judgment of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed the trial court's declaratory judgment for defendants, State Cemetery Board (board) and cemetery association, that N.Y. Membership Corp. Law §§ 86-a, 87(2) were valid and constitutional insofar as they affected an agreement between the realty company and the cemetery association.


OVERVIEW: In 1910, the realty company and the cemetery association entered into an agreement to sell land for cemetery purposes. In consideration for the conveyance the cemetery association was to pay the realty company half the proceeds from the sales of burial lots from the tract, in conformance with N.Y. Membership Corp. Law § 70 (1909). The board then informed the cemetery association that 1949 amendments to N.Y. Membership Corp. Law §§ 86-a, 87(2) required it to deduct maintenance funds from the sales before remitting half of the purchase price to the realty company. The realty company brought an action to declare that §§ 86-a, 87(2) unconstitutionally impaired their contractual rights and deprived them of property without due process of law, but the trial court entered a declaratory judgment affirming the validity of the sections. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment that §§ 86-a, 87(2) were valid. The court held that the legislature intended for the new rule to apply to existing contracts, and that the laws were not invalid as to the realty company because disposal of funds from the sale of cemetery lots was subject to statutory regulation under the state's police power.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that the sections of the Membership Corporation Law were valid and constitutional to the extent they affected an agreement between the realty company and the cemetery association.


HEADNOTES

Cemeteries -- maintenance funds -- under 1949 amendments to Membership Corporations Law (§ 86-a; § 87, subd. 2), cemetery association which had contracted to pay its vendor one half of proceeds of sales of burial lots must, when it sells burial lot and establishes net sales price after selling expenses, deduct 10% for permanent maintenance fund and 15% for current maintenance fund and after these subtractions pay vendor 50% of remaining balance -- Legislature intended law to apply to existing contracts -- amendments are constitutional as to vendor -- funds derived from sale of cemetery lots are dedicated to public use and disposal is subject to State's police power.

1. By an agreement executed in 1910, plaintiff conveyed certain lands for cemetery purposes to defendant cemetery association and the cemetery association agreed that it would pay plaintiff "one-half of the proceeds" of sales of burial lots out of the tract. The 1949 amendments to section 86-a and section 87 (subd. 2) of the Membership Corporations Law ordered every cemetery corporation to set up a permanent maintenance fund of not less than 10% of the gross proceeds of cemetery lot sales and a current maintenance fund of an additional 15% of such proceeds and provided that where a contract for sales of lands for cemetery purposes called for payment to the contract vendor of part of the proceeds of sales of burial lots, the amounts to go into the current and permanent maintenance funds must first be deducted from such sales proceeds before determining the contract vendor's share. The statutes have been properly construed as meaning that the cemetery association, when it sells a burial lot and establishes a net sales price after selling expenses, must first deduct 10% and 15% for the maintenance funds, and only then, after these subtractions, may it pay plaintiff 50% of the remaining balance. The result is that plaintiff collects not half of the whole net price of the burial lot, but half of 75% thereof.

2. The Legislature intended the new rule to be applied to existing contracts.

3. The 1949 amendments are constitutional as to plaintiff. Funds derived from the sale of cemetery lots are dedicated to a public use and held in trust therefor and their disposal is subject to statutory regulation under the State's police power.

4. Plaintiff was on notice when it made its contract under the 1909 statute then in effect that the actual size of its 50% share of the proceeds of lot sales would depend on what amounts were first deducted before the "proceeds" became available, and that the Legislature which had asserted its right to regulate such agreements might reduce the "proceeds" by giving priority to additional expenditures required in the public interest.

People v. Peck


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York

Y. Const., art. VI, § 18) -- defendants, compelled to take shelter during air raid drill, not coerced into war preparation against religious scruples.

1. It was not unconstitutional for the Legislature in section 102 of the State Defense Emergency Act (L. 1951, ch. 784, as amd.) to give jurisdiction over violations of the act constituting misdemeanors to Magistrates' Courts in New York City (N. Y. Const., art. VI, § 18).

2. Defendants may constitutionally be compelled to take shelter during an air raid drill. They were not coerced into war preparation against their religious scruples.

 

470 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Barton Trucking Corp. v. O'Connell


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


7 N.Y.2d 299; 165 N.E.2d 163; 197 N.Y.S.2d 138; 1959 N.Y. LEXIS 904


November 18, 1959, Argued  
December 30, 1959, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent commissioner sought review of the judgment of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court (New York) that annulled his decision to deny an application by petitioner trucking corporation for public cart licenses and remanded the proceeding to respondent to reconsider the application. Petitioner cross-appealed from the order insofar as it remanded the proceeding to respondent and failed to direct that the license be issued.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner trucking corporation filed an application for public cart licenses for its five trucks, pursuant to the Administrative Code of the City of New York, § B32-93.0 art. 15, which made it unlawful for any person to operate or cause the operation of any public cart without a license therefor. The application for the licenses was denied by respondent commissioner on the ground that petitioner was not a fit and proper party to be licensed to operate public carts. The trial court sustained respondent's order. The appeals court reversed finding that respondent had no power to withhold public cart licenses on the basis of general character and fitness of the applicant or for other arrogated qualifications in the absence of express statutory authority. The court reversed the judgment of the appeals court. The license was denied because a principal stockholder was found to be personally unfit having been convicted of extortion. As a result, the court found that there was substantial evidence in the record to support respondent's decision and his actions were not tantamount to an arbitrary or capricious abuse of discretion.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the lower court was reversed because respondent commissioner's determination to deny petitioner trucking corporation's application for public cart licenses was supported by substantial evidence in the record and was not an arbitrary or capricious abuse of discretion.


HEADNOTES

Licenses -- public cart licenses -- Commissioner of Licenses of City of New York properly denied application by petitioner corporation, whose trucking services are carried on in garment industry, for public cart licenses (Administrative Code of City of New York, § B32-93.0) upon finding that petitioner was not fit and proper party to be so licensed -- discretionary power to consider character of applicant implicitly delegated to Commissioner -- determination supported by evidence -- petitioner's treasurer had been convicted of extortion in connection with garment trucking racketeering.

1. Plaintiff, a domestic trucking corporation whose trucking services are carried on in New York City's garment industry, filed an application for public cart licenses for its five trucks pursuant to section B32-93.0 of the Administrative Code of the City of New York. The application was denied by the Commissioner of Licenses of the City of New York upon a finding that petitioner was not a fit and proper party to be licensed to operate public carts, which finding was based on the fact that petitioner's treasurer had been convicted of extortion in 1937 in connection with garment trucking racketeering. An order of the court at Special Term which dismissed the petition for an order annulling the determination of the Commissioner and was reversed by the Appellate Division is reinstated.

2. Any discretionary powers exercised by an administrative officer must be delegated to him by statute and such delegation must be accompanied by standards to guide the exercise of administrative discretion. With respect to licensing officials, the power to withhold a license for good cause, as well as the standards defining good cause, need not be expressly delegated where, by fair implication, in the light of the statutory purpose, such power has been implicitly delegated.

3. The discretionary power of the Commissioner to consider the character of an applicant for a cartman's license has been implicitly delegated to him. All the relevant case law in this State expressly or impliedly holds that the licensing official has implicit discretion to pass upon the fitness of the applicant.

4. The determination is supported by the evidence. The conviction was for the crime of extortion, a felony. The licensing ordinance regulates the rates chargeable by public cartmen for the apparent purpose of preventing price gouging and related abuses, and hence is specifically directed against a class of offenses comprehending extortion. Moreover, the conviction was not for criminal activities remote from the licensed business but was linked to the very industry in which petitioner seeks licenses to pursue its calling.

5. The ordinance in question was designed to license the individual cartmen and is not merely concerned with the registration of property.

Pub. Serv. Coordinated Transp. v. State Tax Comm'n


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


6 N.Y.2d 178; 160 N.E.2d 448; 189 N.Y.S.2d 137; 1959 N.Y. LEXIS 1129


May 22, 1959, Argued  
July 8, 1959, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner taxpayer, an interstate transportation compnay, sought review of a decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York), which confirmed a determination of respondent State Tax Commission that denied its application for a refund of taxes and penalties imposed under N.Y. Tax Law § 186-a.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer was a New Jersey Corporation engaged in the transportation of passengers by omnibus and had no routes in the state except as part of an interstate carriage. The taxpayer paid taxes and penalties imposed by the commissioner under N.Y. Tax Laws § 186-a and then sought a refund of the amount paid. The taxpayer argued that the tax violated the commerce clause of the United States Constitution because it was imposed for the privilege of doing business within the state. The trial court agreed with the commissioner that the statute did not violate the commerce clause and denied taxpayer's application. The court affirmed the denial of taxpayer's application for a refund. The court concluded that although the statute was incorporated for convenience into N.Y. Tax Law § 186-a, the purpose of the omnibus tax was to require interstate carriers to make some contribution to the maintenance of the highways that they are using. The court found that the imposition of the tax in the same section under which domestic carriers were taxed demonstrated the absence of any intention to discriminate against interstate commerce.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of the taxpayer's application for a refund of taxes and penalties imposed by the commissioner.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- interstate commerce -- tax properly imposed on interstate omnibus carrier for period from July 1 through November 30, 1951 under section 186-a of Tax Law -- tax imposed to compensate State for use of highways, not for privilege of engaging in business -- tax not repugnant to commerce clause of Federal Constitution.

An interstate omnibus carrier, to whose rights and obligations petitioner has succeeded through a merger, was a New Jersey corporation engaged in the transportation of passengers by omnibus and had no routes in this State except as part of an interstate carriage. A tax was imposed on the company for the period from July 1 through November 30, 1951 under section 186-a of the Tax Law. That statute imposes an appropriate tax on interstate omnibus carriers to compensate New York for the use of its highways. It does not levy a tax upon them for the privilege of continuing to engage in business. No basis exists for the claim that the tax is repugnant to the commerce clause of the Federal Constitution.

 

473 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Steuding


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


6 N.Y.2d 214; 160 N.E.2d 468; 189 N.Y.S.2d 166; 1959 N.Y. LEXIS 1135


May 28, 1959, Argued  
July 8, 1959, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed the order of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York), which granted a motion by defendant, pursuant to N.Y. Jud. Law § 149, which dismissed the indictment filed against defendant.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was the target of investigation when he was subpoenaed by the grand jury, sworn as a witness, and compelled to give self-incriminating testimony. As the result of that testimony, an indictment was filed against defendant. Defendant then filed a motion to dismiss the indictment, asserting that his constitutional rights were violated. The trial court granted defendant's motion, and the state appealed. The court held that by virtue of the N.Y. Const. art, I, § 6, a prospective defendant or one who was the target of an investigation could not be called and examined before a grand jury, and if he or she was, his or her constitutionally-conferred privileged against self incrimination was deemed violated even though he or she did not claim or assert the privilege. Further, an automatic result of the violation of that privilege was that any defendant was protected not only from indictment based on any incriminating testimony, which he or she may have given, but also from the use of the evidence. Accordingly, the order appealed from was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The dismissal of the indictment filed against defendant was affirmed where the indictment was based upon defendant's forced testimony before a grand jury.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- privilege against self incrimination -- prospective defendant or one who is target of investigation may not be called and examined before Grand Jury (N. Y. Const., art. I, § 6) -- if he is, privilege against self incrimination deemed violated even though privilege not asserted -- protection from indictment and use of testimony results automatically from violation of privilege -- section 2447 of Penal Law prescribing method by which immunity may be conferred applies to witnesses but not to one in position of defendant -- violation of constitutional privilege carries with it dismissal of indictment.

1. A prospective defendant or one who is a target of an investigation may not be called and examined before a Grand Jury (N. Y. Const., art. I, § 6).

2. If he is, his privilege against self incrimination is deemed violated even though he does not claim or assert the privilege.

3. The protection from indictment and from use of any incriminating testimony by the defendant results automatically from the violation of the constitutional privilege.

4. Section 2447 of the Penal Law, which prescribes the method by which an immunity may be conferred upon witnesses, is constitutional and valid insofar as it is applied to witnesses who are, in truth and in fact, witnesses. The statute does not apply to a defendant insofar as it provides that the burden is cast upon him of claiming the privilege.

5. Since the right granted by the Constitution is automatically conferred, section 2447 is ineffectual to destroy or curtail it and any attempt to do so is unconstitutional.

6. A violation of the constitutional privilege carries with it a dismissal of the indictment returned by the Grand Jury before which the defendant testified.

Ahern v. Board of Supervisors


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


6 N.Y.2d 376; 160 N.E.2d 640; 189 N.Y.S.2d 888; 1959 N.Y. LEXIS 1160


May 26, 1959, Argued  
July 8, 1959, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed, without costs, upon the ground that the Appellate Division acted within its discretion when it dismissed this petition (see Matter of Andresen v. Rice, 277 N. Y. 271, 282). We pass upon no other question.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, a taxpayer, resident, and voter in the county, challenged the decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which reversed the supreme court's order to declare a resolution of respondent, the county board of elections, null and void. Although the chairman of the Democratic Committee had not recommended a commissioner of elections, the board had appointed one.


OVERVIEW: Four times, the county chairman of the Democratic Committee recommended himself as a commissioner of elections. Four times, the county's board of supervisors refused to appoint him. State law required the chairman to submit names of potential candidates. Although the chairman had not submitted the name of anyone other than himself, the board appointed an enrolled Democrat as the new commissioner. A third Democrat in the county filed for an injunction against the Republican Commissioner of Elections, argued that the board had no legal existence, and filed a petition for mandamus to declare the board's action null and void. The supreme court granted the petition; but the appellate court reversed. The court cited the appellate court's decision, which addressed the merits and stated that the appellate court had exercised its discretion to strike the mandamus order. The court agreed that the consequences of holding the appointment invalid would undermine the board's service to the public and disenfranchise county voters. Because the court affirmed on the appellate court's exercise of discretion, it did not decide whether the appellate court's holdings on the law were correct.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's exercise of discretion when it held that the public interest would be damaged by allowing the supreme court to declare the board's action null and void. The court declined to consider whether the appellate court had ruled correctly on whether the board was required to appoint only candidates certified by the Democratic party.


HEADNOTES

Proceeding against body or officer -- mandamus -- Appellate Division dismissed, in exercise of discretion, proceeding for order directing Board of Supervisors to revoke appointment of Commissioner of Elections and to desist from appointing person to Board of Elections as representing Democratic party unless certified by County Democratic Committee -- order of Appellate Division did not constitute abuse of discretion -- question whether dismissal upon law was correct not reached.

1. The Appellate Division dismissed a proceeding brought by petitioner against the Board of Supervisors of Suffolk County for an order directing the board to revoke an appointment of a Commissioner of Elections for the county, and directing the board to desist from appointing any person to the Board of Elections as representing the Democratic party of that county unless such person had been duly certified and recommended by the Chairman of the Suffolk County Democratic Committee. In dismissing the proceeding, the Appellate Division indicated that it was exercising the court's discretion because of the damage to the public interest which might result from a mandamus order, and also because the record does not fully set forth all the facts that might be pertinent to a determination of the question whether it would be possible for the Supervisors to appoint an Election Commissioner not recommended by the County Chairman. The Appellate Division's order did not constitute an abuse of discretion.

2. Since the reversal of the order at Special Term and dismissal in the exercise of discretion were proper, the question whether the determination insofar as it was on the law is correct is not reached.

 

475 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Merriweather v. Boland & Cornelius


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


6 N.Y.2d 417; 160 N.E.2d 717; 190 N.Y.S.2d 65; 1959 N.Y. LEXIS 1165


April 15, 1959, Argued  
July 8, 1959, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed, with costs in all courts, and matter remitted to Special Term for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Fourth Judicial Department (New York) affirmed the dismissal of third-party plaintiff ship owner's third-party complaint against third-party defendant employer in plaintiff stevedore's negligence action against the ship owner. The ship owner appealed.


OVERVIEW: The stevedore filed a negligence action against the ship owner and the ship owner filed a third-party complaint against the stevedore's employer for indemnity. The trial court dismissed the third-party complaint and the appellate court affirmed. On appeal, the court reversed, finding that § 5 of the Longshoreman's and Harbor Workers' Compensation Act, codified at 33 U.S.C.S. § 905, did not preclude the indemnity suit. The court found that an indemnity action based on a passive-active negligence theory would have been foreclosed. However, the court found that the ship owner had pleaded an actual contract between itself and the employer, wherein the employer warranted that it would perform its engagement to load the ship in a safe and seamanlike manner. Because the indemnity suit was based on a contract theory, it was not precluded by § 905.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order dismissing the ship owner's third-party complaint and remanded the case for further proceedings.


HEADNOTES

Indemnity -- negligence -- in action in negligence by stevedore against operator of ship upon which he was working when injured, third-party complaint of ship operator against stevedore's employer should not have been dismissed -- Federal law controlling -- under Federal law, contractual assumption of indemnity enforced -- third-party complaint pleads contract with and warranty by third-party defendant to load ship in safe and workmanlike manner.

1. In an action in negligence by a stevedore against the operator of the ship upon which he was working when injured, the third-party complaint of the ship operator against the stevedore's employer should not have been dismissed.

2. Section 5 of the Longshoremen's and Harbor Workers' Compensation Act (U. S. Code, tit. 33, § 905) provides that the liability of an employer "shall be exclusive and in place of all other liability of such employer to the employee * * * and anyone otherwise entitled to recover damages from such employer at law or in admiralty on account of such injury". Maritime law applies and Federal law is controlling. Under the Federal law, an independent contractual assumption of indemnity by the employer is regarded as a voluntary waiver of the limited liability afforded employers under the statute, whereas recovery over on the quasi-contractual theory of indemnity involving the distinction between active and passive negligence is considered to be precluded by the statute as a recovery "on account of" the injury. Third-party plaintiff pleads the existence of a contract with and a warranty by third-party defendant to perform its engagement to load the ship in a safe and workmanlike manner. Accordingly, the third-party complaint is sufficient in law and third-party plaintiff is entitled to a trial of its claim of indemnity against third-party defendant.

 

476 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Department of Welfare v. Siebel


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


6 N.Y.2d 536; 161 N.E.2d 1; 190 N.Y.S.2d 683; 1959 N.Y. LEXIS 1178


April 14, 1959, Argued  
July 8, 1959, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Department of Welfare (department) sought review of a judgment from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which reversed the domestic relations court's judgment that directed respondent stepmother, pursuant to the Domestic Relations Court Act (Act) of the city of New York, to pay for expenses incurred by the city because of the maintenance of a stepson, who was a public charge.


OVERVIEW: The stepson of the stepmother was adjudicated delinquent, but the stepson's father was unable to furnish all the support payments. Consequently, the department initiated an action in the domestic relations court, claiming the stepmother was obligated under the Act to help pay for expenses incurred by the city because of the stepson's maintenance. After the domestic relations court ordered the stepmother to make payments, the court of appeals reversed, and the department sought review. In reversing, the court held that under § 101 of the Act, the stepmother had become potentially liable for the payments because the stepson had been likely to become a public charge and the stepmother had known of the stepson's existence at the time of the stepmother's marriage. The court also held that because the father had been incapable of making the payments, the stepmother's liability had come into existence. Finally, the court held that § 101 had not enumerated the order in which other persons, such as the mother and the grandparents, had become liable and instead, found that a fair reading of § 101 had made everyone secondarily liable when the father failed to make the payments.


OUTCOME: In reversing a judgment from the court of appeals, the court upheld a judgment from the domestic relations court that directed the stepmother, pursuant to the Act, to pay for expenses incurred by the city because of the maintenance of a stepson, who was a public charge.


HEADNOTES

Parent and child -- support -- stepmother may be compelled to contribute to expense incurred by City of New York because of maintenance of her stepson, who is public charge, where stepmother had knowledge of child's existence at time of her marriage to child's father and father had been ordered to contribute to child's support, but amount so ordered was insufficient to reimburse city.

1. A stepmother may be compelled to contribute to the expense incurred by the City of New York because of the maintenance of her stepson, who is a public charge, where the stepmother had knowledge of the child's existence at the time of her marriage to the child's father and the father had been ordered to contribute to the child's support but the amount so ordered to be paid was insufficient to reimburse the city in full. Section 101 of the Domestic Relations Court Act of the City of New York provides who shall be legally liable for support and includes a provision that "The step parent of a child is hereby declared legally chargeable with the support of a step child likely to become a public charge provided it is shown to the satisfaction of the court that such step parent had knowledge of the child's existence at the time of said step parent's marriage." The two conditions for liability of a stepparent being present here, the order directing the stepmother to contribute to the support of the child was properly made.

2. The word "step parent" includes the stepmother.

3. Contribution may be compelled simultaneously from the stepmother and from the father where, as here, the father is unable to pay the full amount of the charges for support.

4. Article 4 of title III of the Domestic Relations Court Act of the City of New York, pursuant to which the proceeding was instituted, is not unconstitutional and the proceeding does not deprive the stepmother of her property without due process.

5. No answer or formal pleading by the stepmother was necessary. She was represented by counsel and had the right to cross-examine and to present evidence.

 

477 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Johnson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


6 N.Y.2d 549; 161 N.E.2d 9; 190 N.Y.S.2d 694; 1959 N.Y. LEXIS 1179


May 27, 1959, Argued  
July 8, 1959, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the County Court of Nassau County (New York), which affirmed a judgment of a trial court convicting defendant of a violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 722-b.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted, following a trial without a jury, of disorderly conduct in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 722-b for loitering in a high school. Defendant challenged the validity of the statute as a constitutional enactment for lack of standards and appropriate definition. The court affirmed, holding that: (1) § 722-b was sufficiently clear and unambiguous to inform the public of the proscribed act in the light of the restricted public nature of a school building; (2) the statute made definite and certain that if one did not qualify as an implied invitee or licensee who had written permission to frequent a school, he was a trespasser and could be punished for loitering; (3) the statute did not make criminal an otherwise innocent activity because loitering in school buildings or on school grounds was not an innocent activity since the nature of the activity carried on in schools compelled restriction against persons loitering in and about the building and grounds; and (4) defendant conceded that he did not come within the statutory exceptions, he did not have permission to enter the school premises, and he received a prior warning to keep out.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment convicting defendant of disorderly conduct.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- disorderly conduct -- loitering on school grounds -- defendant properly convicted of disorderly conduct on evidence that he loitered on school grounds in violation of section 722-b of Penal Law -- statute clear and unambiguous and not unconstitutional.

1. Section 722-b of the Penal Law provides that: "Any person not the parent or legal guardian of a pupil in regular attendance at said school who loiters in or about any school building or grounds without written permission from the principal, custodian or other person in charge thereof, or in violation of posted rules or regulations governing the use thereof, shall be guilty of disorderly conduct." Defendant and a male companion entered a high school to meet two friends who were in school. They stayed about the main lobby for about 10 minutes, and then went to the second floor and waited in the corridor. After several minutes and after class was dismissed, defendant and his companion and two student friends walked down the stairs and out onto the school grounds. There defendant put an unlighted cigarette in his mouth. A teacher asked him three times to remove it and, when he did not, the teacher reached out to take it and defendant knocked off the teacher's hat and punched him in the stomach. Defendant was properly convicted under the statute.

2. The statute is not unconstitutional. It is sufficiently clear and unambiguous to inform the public of the proscribed acts.

3. The statute does not make criminal an otherwise innocent activity. Loitering in school buildings, or on school grounds, is not an innocent activity.

Board of Education v. Allen


Court of Appeals of New York


6 N.Y.2d 871; 160 N.E.2d 119; 188 N.Y.S.2d 988; 1959 N.Y. LEXIS 1296
