479 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


La Porte v. State


Claim No. 33115


Court of Appeals of New York


6 N.Y.2d 1; 159 N.E.2d 540; 187 N.Y.S.2d 737; 1959 N.Y. LEXIS 1357


January 16, 1959, Argued  
May 14, 1959, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owners challenged a judgment of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York), which reversed the trial court's ruling in their favor. The property owners sought interest from respondent state for land appropriated for the construction of a thruway.


OVERVIEW: The property owners owned a house and lot. The house was not taken, but 30 feet were appropriated by the state for the New York State Thruway along one side of the lot. After appropriation, the house was 5 feet instead of 35 feet from the sideline. There was no dispute as to the value of the property appropriated, but the property owners sought interest from the time of the appropriation rather than the date of the filing of their claim or from the taking of possession for Thruway purposes. The trial court found in favor of the property owners. The appellate court reversed and dismissed their claim to interest. On review, the court affirmed. The court agreed with the appellate court's view of the case, which stated that if nothing occurred to bring home knowledge of the taking to the property owners, they had the full use and benefit of the land during the interval and reasonably could not expect both interest and possession. The court held that a claimant could not attack the constitutionality of N.Y. Ct. Cl. Act § 19(1) by contending that he should have been awarded interest to compensate him for a use of which he did not know that he had been deprived.


OUTCOME: The decision reversing the trial court's award of interest to the property owners for land appropriated by the state was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Eminent domain -- suspension of interest -- Court of Claims Act ( § 19, subd. 1) provides that no interest shall be allowed between six months from time of accrual of claim and time of filing of claim -- claimants allegedly not aware that appropriation map and description by which property had been appropriated had been filed -- statute suspending payment of interest during period when owner enjoyed full beneficial use of premises not unconstitutional.

The Court of Claims Act ( § 19, subd. 1) provides that "If a claim which bears interest, is not filed until more than six months after the accrual of said claim, no interest shall be allowed between the expiration of six months from the time of such accrual and the time of the filing of such claim." Claimants herein were allegedly not aware that the appropriation map and description by which their property had been appropriated had been filed (Highway Law, § 347). If this was so, they were not disturbed in the use of this property. No constitutional mandate is violated by denial of interest during periods when the owner has enjoyed the full beneficial use of his premises.

 

480 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Lalli


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


5 N.Y.2d 536; 158 N.E.2d 839; 186 N.Y.S.2d 262; 1959 N.Y. LEXIS 1421


January 21, 1959, Argued  
April 16, 1959, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgments reversed, the information dismissed and the fines remitted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed by permission from a judgment of the County Court of Westchester County (New York), which affirmed a judgment of a trial court, which convicted defendants of violating N.Y. Penal Law § 974.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were magazine distributors who sold so-called "dream books" and "tip-sheets" to which devotees of the game known as policy used in their search for the elusive winning number. Following a police raid and the seizure of the publications, an information containing six counts was filed against defendants. Defendants were found guilty upon two counts of violating N.Y. Penal Law § 974 in that they possessed articles commonly used in "promoting" policy. The convictions were affirmed by the intermediate appellate court. The court reversed, holding that defendants' activities were not within the compass of § 974 because the only activities forbidden by § 974 were those of the operators of games of policy, the entrepreneurs and their henchmen, and the players; nothing in § 974 rendered criminal the possession or sale of "dream books" or "tip-sheets;" the articles whose possession was forbidden were the essential "tools" of the game of policy, the items actually required to carry it on; and the possession of articles, having no integral connection with the playing of policy and not used in the actual policy operation, did not fall within the prohibition of § 974.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgments of conviction and dismissed the information.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- policy -- defendants, magazine distributors, improperly found guilty of violating section 974 of Penal Law upon evidence that they sold so-called "dream books" and "tip-sheets", to which those who play game known as policy referred in searching for winning number -- defendants did not "carry on" or "play" policy, nor were books and pamphlets used in "carrying on" or "playing" or "promoting" policy.

Defendants, magazine distributors, have been selling so-called "dream books" and "tip-sheets", to which those who play the game known as policy referred in searching for a winning number. Defendants were improperly found guilty of violating section 974 of the Penal Law, in that they possessed articles commonly used in "promoting" policy. The statute provides that "A person * * * who shall have in his possession, knowingly * * * any paper, print, writing, number, device, policy slip, or article of any kind such as is commonly used in carrying on, promoting or playing the game commonly called 'policy' * * * is a common gambler, and guilty of a misdemeanor." Defendants did not "carry on" or "play" policy, nor were the books and pamphlets which they sold used in "carrying on" or "playing" that game. Accordingly, since the term "promote" necessarily takes its sense and meaning from the terms "carry on" and "play", those articles were not used to "promote" policy. The possession of articles, having no integral connection with the playing of policy and not used in the actual policy operation, do not fall within the prohibition of section 974.

 

481 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Austin v. Board of Higher Education


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


5 N.Y.2d 430; 158 N.E.2d 681; 186 N.Y.S.2d 1; 1959 N.Y. LEXIS 1441


January 12, 1959, Argued  
April 9, 1959, Decided



DISPOSITION:  Judges DYE, FROESSEL, VAN VOORHIS and BURKE concur with Chief Judge CONWAY; Judge DESMOND dissents in an opinion in which Judge FULD concurs.

Ordered accordingly.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Appellate Division of the Supreme Court (New York) reversed, on the law, a judgment entered by the trial court granting a motion by defendant public employer to dismiss the complaint by plaintiff employees. The appellate division denied the motion. The question was certified: Was the order proper in denying the motion to dismiss the complaint for failure to state facts sufficiently to constitute a cause of action?


OVERVIEW: The employer, as a board of higher education of a city, discharged the employees during an investigation of Communist membership. The employees, during the investigation, claimed their privilege against self incrimination when asked questions about Communist party membership. The employees contended that their refusal to answer questions was the only reason for their dismissal. The employees contended that they were denied due process and that they were dismissed without a hearing, in violation of the statutory rights attendant upon their tenure of service. The court found that the appellate division should not have denied the employees' motion to dismiss the amended complaint for failure to state facts sufficient to constitute a cause of action. It did not matter whether the employer had exercised discretion in the dismissal. What mattered was that the employees' right to salary from the date of dismissal could more properly be determined in an article 78 proceeding. The court noted that the legislative intent was to relegate discharged employees to article 78 proceedings, without regard to whether the removal involved an exercise of discretion.


OUTCOME: The order of the appellate division was reversed and the judgment of the trial court was reinstated. The certified question was answered in the negative.


HEADNOTES

Schools -- salaries -- reinstatement -- plaintiffs, members of instructional staff of Board of Higher Education of City of New York, who had been discharged from their positions, seek in action at law to recover damages allegedly resulting from dismissal -- complaint properly dismissed -- discharged public employee must prove right to position in proceeding under article 78 of Civil Practice Act -- action to recover salary may not be brought until after reinstatement -- plaintiffs never sought reinstatement through proceeding under article 78.

1. Certain teachers employed by the Board of Education and various members of the teaching staff of the Board of Higher Education, including plaintiffs herein, were dismissed from their positions after they had claimed their Fifth Amendment privilege against self incrimination in their appearances before the Internal Security Subcommittee of the Committee on the Judiciary of the United States Senate. Eight of those persons removed by the Board of Education commenced a proceeding under article 78 of the Civil Practice Act seeking their reinstatement. Six former members of the instructional staff of the Board of Higher Education instituted a similar proceeding. Plaintiffs herein were not among the petitioners in either of those proceedings. Special Term dismissed the petitions in both of those proceedings and, during the pendency of appeals by the petitioners therein, plaintiffs herein entered into a stipulation with defendant Board of Higher Education. The stipulation bore the title of the Shlakman proceeding and provided that plaintiffs herein "shall for all purposes be considered as parties to this [Shlakman] proceeding to the end that the final order or judgment made herein shall apply to and benefit or bind them, as the case may be, with the same force and effect as though they were formal parties petitioner herein." The attorney representing plaintiffs at the time they signed the stipulation originally represented all of the petitioners in the Shlakman proceeding. After Special Term had dismissed the petition, Harry Slochower, one of the petitioners in the Shlakman proceeding, retained other counsel to represent him upon appeal. The Appellate Division affirmed Special Term, as did also the Court of Appeals. The Supreme Court dismissed the appeals of all of the petitioners, except Slochower, for want of a properly presented Federal question and thereafter held that Slochower's removal had been in violation of his constitutional rights. Slochower was eventually reinstated with back salary. After Special Term had entered the order directing Slochower's reinstatement, plaintiffs moved for an order to include within the terms of the order reinstating Slochower a provision "to the end that the determination of the Board of Higher Education terminating the employment of each of them be annulled" or a determination that the stipulation constituted a waiver of the four-month limitation provided for by section 1286 of the Civil Practice Act for the commencement of an article 78 proceeding. Special Term determined that an identical, uniform result affecting all of the Shlakman petitioners, rather than one result affecting one petitioner (Slochower) and another result affecting the other petitioners, was made a condition precedent to the entry of a final order to benefit or bind the stipulants and that, the condition having failed, no rights under the stipulation accrued in favor of either of the parties thereto. The court also stated that there was nothing to justify a finding that the limitation provided by section 1286 of the Civil Practice Act was waived. No appeal was taken from that order of Special Term. In the present action at law in which plaintiffs, six discharged members of the instructional staff of defendant, the Board of Higher Education of the City of New York, who have not been reinstated to their positions, seek to recover damages allegedly resulting from their dismissal, the complaint was properly dismissed.

2. The right to a position determines the right to the salary incident thereto. A discharged public employee must prove his right to the position in a proceeding under article 78 of the Civil Practice Act. An action to recover the salary may not be brought until plaintiffs have been reinstated in such a proceeding. The article 78 proceeding must be undertaken in those cases where the discharging body or officer has exercised no discretion as well as in those cases where the discharging body or officer has exercised discretion.

3. In the present case plaintiffs never sought reinstatement through an article 78 proceeding.

 

482 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Broady


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


5 N.Y.2d 500; 158 N.E.2d 817; 186 N.Y.S.2d 230; 1959 N.Y. LEXIS 1447; 74 A.L.R.2d 841


November 18, 1958, Argued  
April 9, 1959, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the decision from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed a trial court verdict that found him guilty of conspiracy, illegal possession of wiretap equipment, and aiding and employing others to wire tap illegally and using rooms and apparatus for illegal wire tapping in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 1423.


OVERVIEW: Defendant maintained that the offenses were not "willful" as used in § 1423 and that the state statute was preempted by § 605 of the Federal Communications Act (Act), thus barring his state prosecution. The court affirmed his conviction and held that the Act was not intended to displace the state's ability to make wire tapping illegal. The Act made illegal the interception of communication and the divulging of that communication. The mere act of wire tapping was not illegal under the Act. The state statute specifically made the act of wire tapping illegal and therefore was not in conflict with the federal statute. Reviewing the facts under the three criteria of Nelson, the court found that the Act was not intended to occupy the field of wire tapping, that the field of wire tapping was not so affected with a federal interest that state regulation was assumed to be precluded, and that enforcement of the state statute did not present a serious conflict the administration of the federal statute. Defendant's argument that "willful" meant "wantonly and maliciously" was without merit because the construction of the statute evidenced that it did not contain the element of malice.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- wire tapping -- defendant convicted of violation of section 1423 (subd. 6) of Penal Law which forbids unlawful and willful wire tapping -- charge that willful does not mean that person's act was motivated by spite, malice or hate, but means "intentionally" and "knowing that the act is being done" was correct -- "willfully" does not mean wantonly and maliciously as differentiated from intentionally or knowingly -- prohibition against wire tapping contained in statute (Penal Law, § 1423, subd. 6) not pre-empted by Federal Communications Act ( § 605) -- State statute does not impinge on Federal constitutional jurisdiction under commerce clause.

1. Subdivision 6 of section 1423 of the Penal Law provided that it should be a felony to "unlawfully and willfully cut, break, tap or make connection with any telegraph or telephone line, wire, cable or instrument, or read or copy in any unauthorized manner any message, communication or report passing over it, in this state". The trial court charged the jury that willful does not mean "that a person does an act motivated by feelings of spite, malice or hate. It merely means intentionally doing an act and knowing that the act is being done". The charge was correct. The word "willfully" does not mean wantonly and maliciously as differentiated from intentionally or knowingly. The prohibition against wire tapping was primarily designed to protect the privacy of telephone conversations and, accordingly, the word "wilfully" would refer to an act consciously and deliberately done and would not include the element of malice.

2. The prohibition against wire tapping contained in section 1423 (subd. 6) of the Penal Law was not pre-empted by section 605 of the Federal Communications Act which is entitled "Unauthorized publication or use of communications" and provides in part that "no person not being authorized by the sender shall intercept any communication and divulge or publish the existence, contents, substance, purport, effect, or meaning of such intercepted communication to any person".

3. The State's penal provision against wire tapping does not impinge on Federal constitutional jurisdiction under the commerce clause. It is not an attempted regulation of telephonic communications, but a local police measure designed to protect the people of this State against unwarranted intrusions into their privacy.

4. The Federal act seems to require both an interception and a divulgence or publication of "the existence, contents, substance, purport, effect, or meaning of such intercepted communication", and does not appear on its face to prohibit wire tapping as such. If the Federal statute does not make criminal the act of wire tapping, then there would appear to be no impingement on Federal criminal jurisdiction.

5. Assuming, however, that section 1423 (subd. 6) of the Penal Law does impinge on Federal jurisdiction, the State statute is not invalid since the Federal statutory provision was not intended to supersede the State statute. The States have not been pre-empted from punishing wire tapping. Congress intended to leave room for a State to punish the tapping of telephones within its borders. The State statute does not offend the supremacy clause of the Federal Constitution.

6. As to certain of the alleged errors in the court's charge, and allegedly prejudicial remarks made by the prosecutor in his summation, no ruling was preserved to review.

7. The charge as a whole was clear and thorough and provided the jury with a comprehensive summary of the evidence, the issues involved and the applicable legal principles. The trial court did not abuse its discretion in denying a motion for mistrial because of a remark by the prosecutor: "How does he [defense attorney] know what I know, or I don't know?". The prosecutor's remark was uncalled for but the jury was told to disregard it, and it lacks the prejudicial meaning ascribed to it by defendant and the trial court also cautioned the jury that "any suggestions * * * conveyed to your mind of things not in evidence * * * should be resolutely removed from any consideration by you".

8. The prosecutor also stated that "I am the fellow you should convict if I deliberately permitted that man [Ruh] to commit perjury, knowing it in my heart and soul?". The prompt and complete instructions of the trial court prevented the improper remark from having any serious consequence, and the court did not abuse its discretion in denying a motion made for a mistrial because of that remark.

 

483 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Engelsher v. Jacobs


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


5 N.Y.2d 370; 157 N.E.2d 626; 184 N.Y.S.2d 640; 1959 N.Y. LEXIS 1485


January 7, 1959, Argued  
March 13, 1959, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner hospital owner sought review of an order from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York) affirming an order that denied an application by the hospital owner to review and annul a determination of respondent commissioner of city hospitals that denied a previous application by the hospital owner for variances from the provisions of the city hospital code.


OVERVIEW: The hospital owner brought an action to compel the board of hospitals (board) to issue to him a license permitting him to operate a private proprietary hospital. The application was denied because the hospital owner had not complied with the city hospital regulations. The appellate court affirmed the trial court's denial of the hospital owner's application to review and annul the determination of the board. On further review, the court affirmed the order. The hospital owner argued that as those regulations did not equally apply to municipal hospitals and voluntary nonprofit hospitals, as well as private proprietary hospitals, there was unreasonable discrimination. Private proprietary hospitals were in a separate class because they were not subjected to close scrutiny by either the city or state. Under the regulations, all private proprietary hospitals were treated alike, therefore, the equal protection argument failed. The hospital owner contended that the new code constituted an unlawful taking. The hospital owner did not acquire immunity against the exercise of police power because he constructed it in full compliance with existing laws. The regulations were not unreasonable.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order from the appellate court affirming the trial court's denial of the hospital owner's application to review and annul the determination of the board that had denied the application to issue him a license permitting him to operate a private proprietary hospital.


HEADNOTES

Hospitals -- private proprietary hospital -- regulation -- Board of Hospitals of City of New York properly refused to issue petitioner license permitting operation of private proprietary hospital on ground that he had not complied with New York City Hospital Code effective October 1, 1956 -- new regulations, changing required minimum floor area per bed and providing for increase in nursing staff, do not constitute unreasonable discrimination and do not violate due process provisions of State and Federal Constitutions (U. S. Const., 14th Amdt.; N. Y. Const., art. I, § 6) -- hospital's compliance with superseded regulations does not prevent exercise of police power -- minimum space requirements not unreasonable as matter of law.

1. The Board of Hospitals of the City of New York properly refused to issue petitioner a license permitting him to operate a private proprietary hospital on the ground that he had not complied with new regulations of the New York City Hospital Code, which became effective on October 1, 1956.

2. The regulations changed the required minimum floor area per bed and also required an increase in the nursing staff for such hospitals. These new regulations do not constitute an unreasonable discrimination and do not violate the due process provisions of the State and Federal Constitutions (U. S. Const., 14th Amdt.; N. Y. Const., art. I, § 6). The fact that the regulations do not apply equally to municipal hospitals and voluntary nonprofit hospitals is not an unreasonable discrimination. The Legislature itself has established private proprietary hospitals in New York City in a separate class and has subjected them to special rules and regulations by the Board of Hospitals. The other institutions come under the close scrutiny of either the city or the State. Private hospitals, without regulation, do not.

3. Under the regulations, all private proprietary hospitals are treated alike. Equal protection is accomplished when all of the same class are treated in a like manner.

4. Moreover, it is not shown whether there are any similar regulations in other statutes or rules applicable to city hospitals or voluntary hospitals.

5. The new hospital code, which reduces the bed capacity of the hospital, does not constitute an unlawful taking in violation of due process of law because the hospital had been conducted in compliance with the regulations in the superseded hospital code. Compliance with existing laws does not prevent the exercise of the police power.

6. The minimum space requirements are not unreasonable as a matter of law and the Board of Hospitals did not act in an arbitrary manner in refusing a license to petitioner under the regulations.

 

484 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Grottano v. Kennedy


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


5 N.Y.2d 381; 157 N.E.2d 632; 184 N.Y.S.2d 648; 1959 N.Y. LEXIS 1486


November 12, 1958, Argued  
March 13, 1959, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order modified and, as modified, affirmed, with costs to appellant, and the matter remitted to the Police Commissioner for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant patrolman sought review of the decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which confirmed a determination of respondent, the Commissioner of Police of the City of New York, dismissing the patrolman from his position and dismissing the patrolman's petition.


OVERVIEW: The patrolman was suspended after being named as the finger-man in a series of payroll holdups by two members of an alleged holdup gang. Two sets of charges were filed against the patrolman. The first set accused him of guarding various payrolls and alleged four violations of the police department's rules and regulations. The second set of charges accused him of supplying information to the holdup gang and receiving a share of the proceeds of the robberies. The patrolman pleaded not guilty to both sets of charges. The patrolman refused to participate in his trial. The Commissioner determined that the patrolman knowingly and wilfully disobeyed an order by the trial Commissioner in failing to attend the trial and found him guilty of insubordination. The court affirmed the decision finding guilty of the escort and holdup charges. The court annulled the Commissioner's determination that the patrolman was guilty of insubordination. When the Commissioner ordered the patrolman to proceed to trial, he was not acting in the capacity of the patrolman's superior officer within the meaning and intent of the department rules.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed, as modified, the appellate court's decision. The court annulled the Commissioner's determination finding the patrolman guilty of insubordination and remitted the case to the Commissioner for a redetermination of the measure of punishment with respect to the charges that were not annulled.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- police -- suspension -- petitioner, patrolman in New York City Police Department, found guilty on charges with respect to escort of named persons and on insubordination charge resulting from refusal to participate in trial -- since insubordination charge may not be sustained, matter remitted for redetermination of punishment -- refusal to go to trial could not be basis of insubordination charge, since petitioner was entitled to adjournment and, moreover, Trial Commissioner was not acting as petitioner's superior officer -- refusal to grant adjournment beyond subsequent adjourned date not abuse of discretion -- Commissioner had power to try petitioner in his absence and could take into account attempt to render trial futile insofar as petitioner's pension was concerned.

1. Petitioner, a patrolman in the New York City Police Department, was suspended on April 28, 1956. On May 1 and 3, 1956 two sets of charges were filed against him. The first set contained 13 specifications. Violations of Department Rules and Regulations were alleged with respect to the escort of each of three named persons and the second set of charges contained specifications alleging various acts of supplying information to a holdup gang and receipt of a share of the proceeds of certain of the robberies. A hearing was scheduled for June 4. On that date the Corporation Counsel requested an adjournment so that he might serve a requested bill of particulars. The Corporation Counsel did not serve the bill of particulars on the escort charges until the adjourned date of June 27. The Commissioner denied a further adjournment and petitioner refused to participate in the trial on June 27. His refusal was made the subject of a third set of charges -- knowingly and willfully disobeying an order by the Trial Commissioner. On July 6 petitioner pleaded not guilty to the new charge and it and the escort charges were set down for trial for July 11. On the adjourned date, petitioner refused to participate in the hearing and the Commissioner proceeded to take testimony on the insubordination and escort charges. Petitioner was thereafter found guilty and dismissed. No hearing was ever had on the criminal charges nor were the charges dismissed and no prosecution was ever instituted against petitioner. Since the insubordination charge may not be sustained, the matter is remitted to respondent Commissioner of Police for a redetermination of the measure of punishment with respect to the charges which have not been annulled.

2. Petitioner's refusal to obey the order to proceed to trial on June 27 could not be made the basis of an insubordination charge. The order to proceed to trial under the circumstances constituted an abuse of discretion. He was entitled to an adjournment since the bill of particulars was not served until the morning of June 27.

3. Moreover, when the Trial Commissioner ordered petitioner to proceed to trial, he was not acting in the capacity of petitioner's superior officer within the meaning and intent of the department rules.

4. There is substantial evidence to support the finding of guilt on the various specifications in the escort charges.

5. The refusal to grant an adjournment beyond July 11 was not an abuse of discretion. Petitioner's attorney offered no valid reason why he had been unable to conduct the necessary investigation preparatory to trial nor why such investigation could not have been completed prior to the trial date.

6. The Commissioner had power to try petitioner in his absence after his refusal to participate in the trial under the circumstances here presented.

7. Since petitioner had applied for retirement to take effect on August 2, the Trial Commissioner could take into account petitioner's apparent attempt to render the trial futile insofar as his pension was concerned.

 

485 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Berkshire Fine Spinning Associates, Inc. v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


5 N.Y.2d 347; 157 N.E.2d 614; 184 N.Y.S.2d 623; 1959 N.Y. LEXIS 1500


January 20, 1959, Argued  
March 12, 1959, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff corporation sought review of the judgment from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court, First Judicial Department (New York) that reversed the trial court's judgment and dismissed its complaint against defendant city seeking a declaratory judgment that the city's tax laws were unconstitutional because the corporation's activities constituted interstate commerce exclusively.


OVERVIEW: The corporation sought a declaratory judgment that the city's tax laws were unconstitutional as applied to the corporation because the corporation's activities constituted interstate commerce exclusively. On appeal, the issue was whether the corporation's activities were so substantial and separable from its interstate commerce as to be subject to the local city tax. In affirming, the court held that the imposition on the corporation of the local tax did not violate the Interstate Commerce Clause of the Federal Constitution because the corporation's city activities were so substantial and separable from its interstate commerce as to be subject to the local tax. The court further held that because this general business tax applied on its face and not unconstitutionally to the corporation's city activities, no reason appeared why the corporation that contested the amount of the tax or the formula for determining the amount should have any relief except that provided for within the statute itself.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment from the appellate court that reversed the trial court's judgment and dismissed the corporation's complaint against the city that sought a declaratory judgment that the city's taxes were unconstitutional against the corporation.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- New York City General Business and Financial Tax -- New York City General Business and Financial Tax (Administrative Code of City of New York, § B46-2.0, subd. a) properly imposed on plaintiff -- plaintiff's activities in New York do not consist of negotiations of interstate sales only -- statute not unconstitutional for vagueness or improper delegation of power -- correctness of allocation formula cannot be litigated in plenary suit -- sole remedy is proceeding under article 78 of Civil Practice Act.

1. Plaintiff is a Massachusetts corporation with its principal business and executive office in Providence, R. I., authorized to do business in New York State and engaged in manufacturing textile fabrics and curtains in factories outside this State. One of plaintiff's out-of-State offices arranges for the purchase of cotton in the South, then has the cotton shipped to plaintiff's factories in Rhode Island, Massachusetts and Vermont from which plaintiff ships out to its customers in various States greige goods (unbleached fabrics) and curtains and curtain materials. Some of the unbleached fabrics are sent for refinishing by plaintiff to dyeing plants owned by others, where the greige goods are dyed or printed as required by accepted orders from plaintiff's customers. In New York City plaintiff has two separate offices. The New York offices employ altogether about 90 people (out of its total force of 8,500 people) of whom 20 are salesmen. In addition to its 20 salesmen working in New York City, plaintiff has other salesmen in other parts of the United States. Plaintiff has no factory, warehouse or store in New York State, keeps no stock of goods in this State and owns no property here except the furnishings of its New York City offices. The functions of plaintiff's New York City offices and staff are to maintain contact with the trade by soliciting established and prospective accounts; to receive and to reject, modify or accept offers to buy; to forward accepted offers to one of plaintiff's plants or warehouses outside the State of New York to be filled; to perform credit investigations and functions; to make up and dispatch invoices of sales of finished goods, but not of greige goods or curtains; and to receive and deposit payments from customers. Plaintiff's activities in the City or State of New York do not consist of the negotiation of interstate sales only. The record shows activities going much beyond the negotiating of sales. The Appellate Division was correct in holding that the imposition on plaintiff of the New York City General Business and Financial Tax (Administrative Code of City of New York, § B46-2.0, subd. a; see enabling act, General City Law, § 24-a) did not violate the Interstate Commerce Clause of the Federal Constitution.

2. A local privilege tax like that of New York City may not be imposed upon interstate sales or solicitations by an interstate business. However, the local authorities may validly tax the privilege of doing business locally if the local business operations, though related to interstate movements of goods, extend substantially beyond the sale and promotion of the products and include a local incident which is sufficient to bring the transaction within its taxing power. The local New York City activities of the present plaintiff include a substantial measure of control by plaintiff's officials in New York City of production, contracting, shipment and credit.

3. Section 22 of article III of the New York State Constitution, cited by plaintiff in support of an argument that the statute imposing this tax is unconstitutionally vague and represents an unconstitutional delegation of legislative power, does not apply to local taxes.

4. Other charges of unconstitutional vagueness and delegation of power have been rejected by this court as to this same tax and these questions are no longer open in this court.

5. Plaintiff's attack on the amount of the tax and the correctness of the formula for arriving at it cannot be litigated in a plenary suit like the present one. The sole remedy for relief from incorrectness of the allocation formula or the application thereof is a proceeding under article 78 of the Civil Practice Act.

 

486 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


De Veau v. Braisted


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


5 N.Y.2d 236; 157 N.E.2d 165; 183 N.Y.S.2d 793; 1959 N.Y. LEXIS 1545; 43 L.R.R.M. 2715; 36 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P65,239


January 15, 1959, Argued  
February 26, 1959, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a union officer and the union members, challenged the decision from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed the trial court's dismissal of plaintiffs' complaint against defendant district attorney in plaintiffs suit for declaratory judgment and for an injunction regarding the attorney's application of the Waterfront Commission Act, 1953 N.Y. Laws 882.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs' complaint alleged that the attorney threatened to prosecute the officer, under the Waterfront Commission Act, 1953 N.Y. Laws 882. Plaintiffs contended that the Act interfered with their right to self-organize and collectively bargain, was unconstitutional, and should not have applied to the officer whose sentence had been suspended for a conviction of grand larceny in the first degree. The trial court dismissed the complaint for insufficiency, and entered judgment in favor of the attorney. The appellate court affirmed. Plaintiffs appealed. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that the Act was not unconstitutional, under U.S. Const. art. VI, § 2. The court found that the Act was a proper exercise of the state's police power to deal with existing crime and disorder connected with unsavory waterfront conditions and waterfront crimes, and did not conflict with the Taft-Hartley Act, 29 U.S.C.S. § 157. The court further held that the Act prevented the officer from holding a waterfront union position even though he was not subjected to actual imprisonment for his felony conviction. The officer should have applied to the parole board for a certificate of good conduct.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's decision, which affirmed the trial court's dismissal of plaintiffs' complaint and judgment in favor of the attorney.


HEADNOTES

Labor unions -- Waterfront Commission Act -- convicted felon as representative -- section 8 of Waterfront Commission Act (L. 1953, ch. 882, as amd.), providing that no collections from employees may be made on behalf of labor organization if official or agent thereof has been convicted of felony unless he was subsequently pardoned or received certificate of good conduct from Board of Parole, does not conflict with section 7 of Taft-Hartley Act (U. S. Code, tit. 29, § 157), which provides that employees shall have right to self-organization -- Congress did not intend to prohibit States' exercise of police power -- statute not unconstitutional -- plaintiff "convicted" of felony within meaning of statute although he received suspended sentence -- not necessary to apply for "certificate of good conduct" before testing constitutionality.

1. Section 8 of the Waterfront Commission Act (L. 1953, ch. 882, as amd.) provides that no person shall make any collections from employees registered or licensed pursuant to the provisions of the act on behalf of any labor organization representing such employees, if any officer or agent of such organization has been convicted of a felony unless he was subsequently pardoned or received a certificate of good conduct from the the Board of Parole. This statute does not conflict with section 7 of the Taft-Hartley Act (U. S. Code, tit. 29, § 157), which provides that employees shall have the right to self-organization, to join labor organizations, to bargain collectively through representatives of their own choosing, and to engage in other concerted activities for the purpose of collective bargaining or other mutual aid or protection. That law did not intend to pre-empt the field and leave no room for State action. Congress has not manifested an intention to exclude States from exercising their police power.

2. The statute is not unconstitutional either as in conflict with the Federal statute or as interfering with the right of employees to self-organization and collective bargaining.

3. When plaintiff received a suspended sentence on a felony charge, he was "convicted" of the felony within the meaning of section 8 of the Waterfront Commission Act.

4. Plaintiff could properly test the constitutionality of the statute in this action without first applying for a "certificate of good conduct" ( Executive Law, § 242).

Utica v. Water Pollution Control Board


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


5 N.Y.2d 164; 156 N.E.2d 301; 182 N.Y.S.2d 584; 1959 N.Y. LEXIS 1586


November 5, 1958, Argued  
January 22, 1959, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of the order of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed an order by the trial court that dismissed the city's petition, in the nature of a writ of prohibition, to restrain respondent Water Pollution Control Board (Board) from continuing with a hearing of charges against it, pursuant to the Water Pollution Control Law (WPCL), N.Y. Pub. Health Law art. 12, § 1220.


OVERVIEW: The legislature enacted WPCL, N.Y. Pub. Health Law art. 12, §§ 1200 - 1263, which created the Board and directed it to develop a program to abate water pollution. Following extensive studies and surveys, the Board assigned classifications and standards of quality and purity to the waters within the city. It was noted in that plan that sewers were discharging untreated sewage into the city's waters. When two years went by and the city did nothing to abate the pollution, the Board brought a proceeding against it, and charged it with violations of WPCL, N.Y. Pub. Health Law art. 12, § 1220. The city then petitioned the trial court, in the nature of a writ of prohibition, to restrain the Board form continuing with a hearing of charges against it. The petition was dismissed, and the order was affirmed by the lower court. The court affirmed the order, by holding that the legislature constitutionally conferred discretion upon the Board because WPCL contained sufficiently definite standards to guide and limit the Board's exercise of power, as the legislature provided the manner in which it had to act and indicated the matters it had to consider.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order, which affirmed an order by the trial court that dismissed the city's petition, in the nature of a writ of prohibition, to restrain the Board from continuing with a hearing of charges against it.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- Water Pollution Control Law -- Water Pollution Control Law (Public Health Law, art. 12, §§ 1200-1263) creating Water Pollution Control Board and designed to prevent pollution of waters of State, not unconstitutional -- no invalid delegation of legislative authority -- standards for Board's guidance adequate under circumstances.

1. The Water Pollution Control Law (L. 1949, ch. 666; Public Health Law, art. 12, §§ 1200-1263), designed to prevent pollution of the waters of the State, created the Water Pollution Control Board. Acting under the authority of the statute, the Board held public hearings and adopted classifications and standards of quality and purity, and later, following extensive studies and surveys and after further public hearings, it assigned classifications and standards of quality and purity to the waters within the area of the City of Utica. When the City failed to abate the pollution caused by its discharging of untreated sewage into the Mohawk River and the Barge Canal, the Board brought a proceeding against the City charging it with violation of section 1220 of the Public Health Law. In the present proceeding by the City to restrain the Board from continuing with the hearing of charges against it, the petition was properly dismissed.

2. The Water Pollution Control Law does not constitute an invalid delegation of legislative authority and is not unconstitutional. It is not a grant of power without adequate standards for the Board's guidance in violation of the State Constitution (art. III, § 1). The problems involved in water pollution control varied considerably throughout the State, and the solution had to be approached generally. In view of the breadth of the problem of water pollution control, and its technical and intricate character, the Legislature could not have been more specific in prescribing essential guides. To require more precise standards than furnished by this statute would mean that the Legislature's power to control pollution could not be effectively exercised.

 

488 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Waterford v. Water Pollution Control Board


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


5 N.Y.2d 171; 156 N.E.2d 427; 182 N.Y.S.2d 785; 1959 N.Y. LEXIS 1587


October 17, 1958, Argued  
January 22, 1959, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, town and village, challenged a determination by respondent water control board regarding the classification of certain waters in a river. The Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York) affirmed the board's decision. The town and village appealed.


OVERVIEW: The water control board (board) classified certain waters within the town and village into a classification that might require appellants to cease their longstanding practice of discharging sewage into the river without any kind of treatment, and require construction of sewage treatment facilities. Appellants claimed that the board had failed to follow its statutory duty in deciding whether a particular classification was in the public interest by failing to consider the economic and fiscal impact of the classification upon the municipalities affected, and failed to accord proper notice and hearing to allow them to cross-examine the evidence or reports considered by the board in making its determination. The lower court's confirmation of the board's decision was affirmed because the court found that problems of municipal financing were not among the considerations contemplated in N.Y. Pub. Health Law. § 1209 subd. 3. The court further determined that the hearing held by the board comported with the requirements of the law because it was conducted upon due notice as required by statute, and no specific format for the hearing was required.


OUTCOME: The lower court's decision confirming the water classification made by the board was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- water pollution control -- in applying classification to waters of section of Mohawk River which may require town and village to construct sewage treatment facilities at great expense, Water Pollution Control Board was not required to take into account economic and fiscal impact of classification upon municipalities affected -- when Board evolves program to abate pollution in classified waters, municipality may apply for extension of time if financially unable to comply -- informal hearing conducted by Board before classification was proper -- statute providing for hearing (Public Health Law, § 1209, subd. 6) did not require that witnesses be sworn, testimony be taken, opportunity for cross-examination be given and findings of fact made.

1. The Water Pollution Control Board classified the waters of that section of the Mohawk River Drainage Basin extending from its mouth to the first dam above Cohoes Falls as "Class C" waters. Class C waters require sewage treatment and, as a result of such classification, the Town of Waterford and Village of Waterford, who for a long period had been discharging sewage into the river without any kind of treatment, may be required to cease such practice and eventually to construct sewage treatment facilities at great expense to the town and village. In applying its classification system to particular waters the Board was not required to take into account the economic and fiscal impact of the classification upon the municipalities affected. Problems of municipal financing need not be considered by the Board in classifying particular waters.

2. The Act does recognize (Public Health Law, § 1224) that financial disability might impede the construction of the facilities needed to comply with the adopted classifications. The relief provided, however, is, not the annulment of the classification, but an extension of time in which to effect the needed improvements. The time to raise the question of the fiscal and economic aspects of water purification is not at the time of classification. It is when the Board, in accordance with its previously assigned classifications, evolves a comprehensive program to abate pollution in the classified waters. If, at that time, a municipality demonstrates it is financially unable to provide the treatment works necessary to cure the pollution, the Board is empowered to grant extensions of time.

3. Subdivision 6 of section 1209 of the Public Health Law provides that "The adoption, alteration or modification of a classification of the waters of the state or the standards of quality and purity, above prescribed, shall be made by the board only after public hearing on due notice." The statute requiring a public hearing (§ 1209, subd. 6) does not prescribe the form or manner in which it is to be conducted, and it did not require that witnesses be sworn, testimony be taken, opportunity for cross-examination be given and findings of fact made. The hearing as conducted by the Board accorded with the type of hearing contemplated by the Legislature. In making specific provision for a quasi-judicial type of hearing with respect to violations of the statute or orders of the Board (§ 1208, subd. 3, par. [b]), the Legislature impliedly sanctioned the informal type of hearing conducted here.

Society of New York Hospital v. Johnson


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


5 N.Y.2d 102; 154 N.E.2d 550; 180 N.Y.S.2d 287; 1958 N.Y. LEXIS 705


October 16, 1958, Argued  
November 13, 1958, Decided



DISPOSITION:  Order affirmed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant public works superintendent challenged an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which modified the trial court's order denying respondent charity's motion to enjoin the public works' taking of land, declaring 1927 N.Y. Laws ch. 659 partially invalid, and dismissing the action. The appellate court granted the injunction and denied the public works' motion to dismiss.


OVERVIEW: The public works sought to appropriate part of the charity's hospital to build a highway. The charity brought an action to restrain the taking, and filed a motion for an injunction pendente lite, arguing that 1927 N.Y. Laws chs. 653, 659, specifically prohibit the public works from building a highway on the land or taking the land for any use. The trial court denied the motion, found ch. 659 invalid, and granted the public works' motion to dismiss the charity's action. The appellate court modified the trial court's order so that the injunction pendente lite was granted and the motion to dismiss was denied. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that the public works was bound by ch. 659, which specifically forbade the condemnation of the charity's land. The court further held that ch. 659 was not invalidated by the subsequently enacted N.Y. High. Law § 340(a), because § 340(a) did not express a clear legislative intent to repeal 1927 N.Y. Laws ch. 659.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's order, which modified the trial court's order and granted the charity's motion for an injunction pendente lite against the public works' condemnation of the charity's land and denied the public works' motion to dismiss.


HEADNOTES

Eminent domain -- exemption from appropriation -- property of Society of the New York Hospital exempted from appropriation by Laws of 1927 (chs. 653, 659) -- under statutes authorizing acquisition of lands for interstate connection between New England Thruway and New York State Thruway (Highway Law, §§ 30, 340-a, 340-b), Superintendent of Public Works was without power to appropriate strip of hospital's land -- State has right to terminate exemption from appropriation but it has not done so -- no clear legislative language in any statute repealing or abrogating exemption from appropriation.

Section 1 of chapter 659 of the Laws of 1927 provides that no street or avenue or road shall be laid out or opened through or upon any of certain described lands and premises in the City of White Plains and none of said land shall be taken for any use whenever and so long as it shall be owned or occupied for hospital purposes by the Society of the New York Hospital, provided, however, that the Society of the New York Hospital shall dedicate for street purposes certain other described parcels of land, and shall, in addition thereto, provide $ 150,000 for the paving and regulating of the street described in those parcels. Chapter 653 of the Laws of 1927, passed and signed by the Governor at the same time, amended the Charter of the City of White Plains to provide that "It shall be unlawful to open any streets through the grounds belonging to the society of the New York hospital * * * as long as the same is owned or occupied for hospital purposes." Plaintiff conveyed the real property to the city as required by chapter 659 and paid over the $ 150,000. The Superintendent of Public Works is authorized by sections 30 and 340-b of the Highway Law to appropriate lands for arterial highways by eminent domain, including those which will make an interstate connection between the New England Thruway and the New York State Thruway. Section 340-a of the Highway Law authorizes the location of one of those interstate routes as "passing through or northerly of White Plains". To carry out these purposes the Superintendent may not appropriate a strip of plaintiff's land presently vacant but used for hospital purposes. The State has the right to terminate the exemption from appropriation but it has not done so. The statutes passed after 1927 authorizing the Superintendent to acquire lands anywhere in the State for highway purposes and especially to make such acquisitions for a route "passing through or northerly of White Plains" did not discontinue plaintiff's exemption. The Legislature's positive direction not to take described lands should be cancelled only by a plain command or permission to appropriate. Their repeal or abrogation should be evidenced by clear legislative language, and such language is not found in any statute.

 

491 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Davis v. Board of Elections


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


5 N.Y.2d 66; 153 N.E.2d 879; 179 N.Y.S.2d 513; 1958 N.Y. LEXIS 758


October 16, 1958, Argued  
October 17, 1958, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state senatorial candidate challenged an order from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which unanimously affirmed a trial court's order denying his petition for an order directing respondent city board of elections to place his name on the ballot as a candidate for state senator at a general election.


OVERVIEW: The candidate challenged the constitutionality of N.Y. Elec. Law § 138 after the board declared his nominating petition to be invalid. On appeal, the court held that § 138 was not unconstitutional where the legislature conditioned the exercise of the right to sign an independent nominating petition upon registration at the time of the last preceding general election. The primary purpose for such a condition was to remedy practical difficulties in the administration of elections insofar as independent nominating petitions were concerned. The franchise conferred by N.Y. Const. art. 1, § 1 gave rise not only to a right, but also a duty, and § 138 merely attached reasonable consequences to the nonperformance of that duty in the interest of administrative necessity. The court declined to consider whether § 183 violated N.Y. Const. art. 1, § 1 because it affected persons who became eligible to vote only since the last general election where the candidate failed to prove that he was aggrieved by § 138.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.


HEADNOTES

Elections -- independent nominating petition -- Election Law ( § 138, subd. 6) provides that name of person signing independent nominating petition for election for which voters are required to be registered shall not be counted if such person was not registered at time of last preceding general election as qualified voter -- to extent that statute affects those who did not avail themselves of opportunity to register at last preceding general election, it is not unconstitutional (N. Y. Const., art. I, § 1) -- question whether statute violates Constitution insofar as it affects those who have become eligible to vote only since last general election not reached.

1. Subdivision 6 of section 138 of the Election Law provides that the name of a person signing an independent nominating petition for an election for which voters are required to be registered shall not be counted if such person was not registered at the time of the last preceding general election as a qualified voter. An independent nominating petition with 5,988 signatures was declared invalid upon a finding that 3,580 of the signatories had not registered at the time of the last preceding general election (Election Law, § 138, subd. 5, par. [c], cl. [4]). To the extent that the statute affects those citizens who did not avail themselves of the opportunity to register at the time of the last preceding general election, it is not unconstitutional (N. Y. Const., art I, § 1).

2. The question whether the statute violates the Constitution insofar as it affects those who have become eligible to vote only since the last general election is not reached. The record does not disclose that any one of the 3,580 signatories was not qualified to register for the last preceding general election. Petitioner has made no showing that he is aggrieved by the statute.

Grimm v. County of Rensselaer


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


4 N.Y.2d 416; 151 N.E.2d 841; 176 N.Y.S.2d 271; 1958 N.Y. LEXIS 852


May 16, 1958, Argued  
June 25, 1958, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a city treasurer and a county taxpayers' association, appealed a judgment entered on an order of the Court at Special Term (New York) that granted motions by defendants and intervenors-defendants, Rensselaer County and the Attorney General, for judgment on the pleadings. Plaintiffs sought a declaration that N.Y. Educ. Law §§ 6301-6307, which provided for financing of community colleges, was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs sought to enjoin the Board of Supervisors of Rensselaer County (Board) from issuing notes and bonds to finance the County's share of capital costs of a community college. Essentially, they claimed that the system of financing community colleges was unconstitutional because it did not apportion capital costs on the ratio of nonresident to resident students, and the college may be required to admit a disproportionate number of nonresidents. On appeal, the court affirmed because the statutory provisions were constitutional and the Board's resolution was final. The only question for decision was whether a county-established college could be regarded as an institution operated for a proper "county purpose" within the sense of N.Y. Const. art. VIII, § 2. Because the college was owned and operated as a county institution for a proper county purpose, it did not matter that it may have to admit nonresident students that may not be charged an allocable portion of the capital costs.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the court at special term's judgment for the County and Attorney General.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- community colleges -- article 126 of Education Law providing for establishment and financing of two-year community colleges and resolution of Board of Supervisors of County of Rensselaer authorizing acquisition of property and construction and equipment of buildings for Hudson Valley Technical Institute as community college, not unconstitutional -- establishment and maintenance of community college within county territory is county purpose (N. Y. Const., art. VIII, § 2).

Article 126 of the Education Law provides for the establishment and financing of two-year community colleges (Education Law, §§ 6301-6307). Section 6305 provides that "In addition to admitting residents of its own local sponsor * * *, each community college shall, within the quota and under the conditions prescribed by the state university trustees, admit non-resident students and out-of-state students." Section 2 of article VIII of the State Constitution provides that "No county * * * shall contract any indebtedness except for county * * * purposes". The County of Rensselaer established the Hudson Valley Technical Institute as a community college and the Board of Supervisors adopted a resolution authorizing the acquisition of real property and the construction and equipment of buildings thereon. The complaint states that the ratio of nonresident and out-of-state residents to residents in attendance at said Hudson Valley Technical Institute was about 2 to 1, and alleges that the attendance of nonresident students at the college has "no reasonable relation" to the public welfare or benefit of Rensselaer County. The statute and the resolution are not unconstitutional. The establishment and the maintenance of a community college within county territory is a county purpose.

 

493 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


F. & M. Schaefer Brewing Co. v. Gerosa


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


4 N.Y.2d 423; 151 N.E.2d 845; 176 N.Y.S.2d 276; 1958 N.Y. LEXIS 853


April 30, 1958, Argued  
June 25, 1958, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer sought relief from an order by the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York) that confirmed a determination made by respondent city tax comptroller assessing the taxpayer for certain business tax deficiencies under New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § B46-2.0(a). The taxpayer asserted that the tax violated his rights as an unconstitutional burden on interstate commerce.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the court affirmed. It found that the taxpayer was a domestic corporation that sold beer to distributors and others. Some of its distributors sold its beer to interstate clients. But, the taxpayer delivered all of the beer intrastate. The taxpayer reported its sales to out-of-state distributors as receipts from interstate commerce and based the required city business tax solely on revenue derived from sales to those of its customers doing business in the city. But, the transactions between the taxpayer and its distributors, which constituted the taxable event, were local and distinct from the transportation by the distributors that constituted interstate commerce. These transactions conducted within the taxpayer's city were local transactions that were taxable by the city, and neither the Commerce Clause nor the Fourteenth Amendment prevented the imposition of the city's tax on such receipts even though the total activities from which the local transaction derived had incidental interstate attributes. The validity of a tax, or of the statute imposing one, depended on substance, not labels. The city comptroller accurately assessed the taxpayer for deficiencies.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order confirming the determination against the taxpayer.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- intrastate commerce -- tax imposed on gross receipts for privilege of carrying on business within city of New York (Administrative Code of City of New York, § B46-2.0, subd. a) properly imposed on sales made by petitioner corporation to out-of-state distributors -- deliveries made to said distributors in New York City -- petitioner assumed no responsibility for beer from time it was loaded on distributors' trucks -- transaction is local and taxable.

The City of New York taxed receipts from sales by petitioner, a domestic corporation, made to out-of-state distributors. The customer's truck would be loaded at petitioner's brewery in Brooklyn and, after the load had been checked by the driver and one of petitioner's employees, the truck would be "sealed" and driven to the distributor's place of business. Some distributors own their own trucks or lease them while others use contract motor carriers or motor vehicle common carriers. The trucker taking delivery of the beer was selected by the distributor and had no connection with the brewery. No employee or representative of petitioner handled the beer or accompanied it once it left the brewery and it assumed no responsibility for the beer from that time on. On its New York City business tax return -- a tax imposed on gross receipts for "the privilege of carrying on * * * business" within the city (Administrative Code of City of New York, § B46-2.0, subd. a) -- petitioner reported its sales to out-of-state distributors as receipts from interstate commerce. Respondent comptroller ruled that, since the customer took delivery in the city, the transaction was local and intrastate and the moneys received were taxable and, following hearings, he decided that the tax should be imposed upon all receipts, including those from sales to distributors doing business in other states except where delivery for the out-of-state customer was made to a common carrier. The Appellate Division properly confirmed this determination. A sale of goods in which delivery is taken by the purchaser within the seller's state is a local transaction which is taxable. It is immaterial that the goods are to be transported out of the taxing state immediately on delivery.

 

494 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Diaz


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


4 N.Y.2d 469; 151 N.E.2d 871; 176 N.Y.S.2d 313; 1958 N.Y. LEXIS 860


April 29, 1958, Argued  
June 25, 1958, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgments reversed, the information dismissed and the fine remitted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order from the County Court of Chautauqua County (New York), which affirmed a judgment that convicted defendant of loitering in violation of Dunkirk, N.Y. Ordinance ch. 18, § 10.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with willfully, wrongfully, unlawfully and knowingly loitering on the sidewalk in front of a hotel with about 12 other persons. The officers claimed that defendant was ordered to move three times and he refused each time. Defendant claimed that the ordinance in question was too vague, indefinite, and uncertain to define a crime. On review the court reversed. The court found that defendant neither spoke nor understood English and the fact that the overall encounter lasted two or three minutes was corroborated by a witness. The court noted that the ordinance made no distinction between conduct that was calculated to harm and that which was essentially innocent. The court ruled that the State could not have supplied such a deficiency by reference to other unrelated enactments and the favorable rulings in other cases that arose thereunder.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction for loitering in violation of the local ordinance and dismissed the information.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- loitering -- ordinance of City of Dunkirk which provided that "No person shall lounge or loiter about any street or street corner in the City of Dunkirk" is too indefinite and uncertain to define crime and fails to provide standards or criteria by which prohibited conduct may be tested -- judgment of conviction reversed and information dismissed.

Defendant was convicted upon an information charging a violation of the Dunkirk City Ordinance (ch. XVIII, § 10) in that he "did * * * loiter on Third St. on the sidewalk in front of Park Ave. Hotel with about twelve (12) other persons. Defendant was ordered to move on three (3) times and he did refuse each time." His conviction was based on that part of the ordinance which provided that "No person shall lounge or loiter about any street or street corner in the City of Dunkirk." The ordinance is too indefinite and uncertain to define a crime and fails to provide any standards or criteria by which the prohibited conduct may be tested. The judgment of conviction is reversed and the information dismissed.

 

495 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Loeb v. Loeb


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


4 N.Y.2d 542; 152 N.E.2d 36; 176 N.Y.S.2d 590; 1958 N.Y. LEXIS 873


May 16, 1958, Argued  
June 25, 1958, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff wife sought review of a decision from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which dismissed the wife's action against defendant husband for a divorce, custody of their child, and spousal support.


OVERVIEW: The parties lived together during their marriage in Vermont. The husband obtained an ex parte divorce in Nevada. The wife filed an action for divorce, for custody and support of their child, and for spousal support in Vermont. The Vermont court awarded custody to the wife, awarded child support, and denied her spousal support. The wife filed the same action in New York. The trial court dismissed her action. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court stated that the trial court had correctly ruled that the issues that related to the validity of the Nevada divorce had been resolved against her in the Vermont proceeding. The court stated that N.Y. Civ. Prac. Act § 1170-b, did not apply. The court stated that § 1170-b was to protect the property of New York wives and that the wife was not a New York wife because at no time during the course of the marriage did the parties live in New York.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of the wife's action for a divorce, child support, and spousal support.


HEADNOTES

Husband and wife -- support -- divorce -- New York court properly refused to order support of plaintiff wife under section 1170-b of Civil Practice Act where husband had procured ex parte divorce in Nevada -- statute intended to apply only to "New York wives" -- plaintiff was at no time during course of marriage "New York wife".

Plaintiff, then a resident of New York, and defendant, a resident of Vermont, were married in Connecticut on September 26, 1942. In 1944 they established their matrimonial domicile in Vermont. In June, 1951 defendant left plaintiff and took up residence in New Hampshire. In 1952 he moved to Nevada where he obtained an ex parte divorce in July, 1952, which made no provision for the support of plaintiff or for the custody and support of their child. On November 15, 1952 plaintiff commenced an action in Vermont seeking custody of the child. While the action was pending, she moved to New York. She stayed at the home of defendant's mother in New York from October, 1953 through July, 1955 when she purchased a home in New York. She testified that in October, 1953 she formed the intention to become a resident of the State of New York. A Vermont County Court, on March 20, 1954, awarded plaintiff custody of the daughter, ordered defendant to pay a stated sum monthly for the support of the child and for plaintiff's support, and to pay a stated sum for attorneys' fees and disbursements. The court also ruled that defendant's Nevada divorce decree was entitled to full faith and credit. Upon appeal, the Vermont Supreme Court reversed so much of that determination as related to plaintiff's support, stating that the court did not have power to entertain a petition for support by a person who had been validly divorced in another jurisdiction. Prior to the decision of the Vermont Supreme Court plaintiff instituted an action in the New York court against defendant husband seeking, in part, such relief as she might be entitled to under sections 1170-a and 1170-b of the Civil Practice Act. She later filed a supplemental complaint setting forth the decision of the Vermont Supreme Court and amended her complaint to allege her residence in this State for more than one year to comply with section 1170-b of the Civil Practice Act. The New York court properly refused to accord plaintiff the requested relief under section 1170-b of the Civil Practice Act. The Legislature, in enacting that statute, intended it to apply only to "New York wives" and not to a spouse who at no time during the course of her marriage was a "New York wife". The purpose was to afford a remedy to wives here whose property rights might be jeopardized by an ex parte divorce by their spouses.

 

496 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Whalen v. Wagner


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


4 N.Y.2d 575; 152 N.E.2d 54; 176 N.Y.S.2d 616; 1958 N.Y. LEXIS 875


May 7, 1958, Argued  
June 25, 1958, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant residents sought review on constitutional grounds of a judgment of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed a trial court's judgment. The trial court granted respondent city board of estimate and intervenor-respondent Port Authority's motion to dismiss the residents' complaint and declared that 1955 N.Y. Laws 806-809 were constitutional and valid.


OVERVIEW: The residents brought a taxpayer's action to declare 1955 N.Y. Laws 806-809 unconstitutional and to restrain the Mayor and Board of Estimate of the City of New York from acting thereunder in the condemnation of real property necessitated by the construction of a bridge by the Port Authority. The court did not address the constitutional issues. Rather, the court affirmed the judgments of the lower courts and held that the home rule amendment did not apply to the challenged laws. The court reasoned that the Port Authority was a state agency and the bridge, which was to tie into the interstate highway system, did not involve an issue of solely local concern. Accordingly, the enabling legislation for the bridge construction and related condemnation did not require a city message to be valid.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgments of the lower court, which granted the motion filed by the city board of estimate and the Port Authority to dismiss the residents' complaint and declared that the statutes at issue were constitutional.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- Home Rule -- chapters 806-809 of Laws of 1955 providing for Throgs Neck bridge connecting Bronx and Queens and bridge over Narrows connecting Brooklyn and Staten Island and second deck to George Washington bridge between New York and New Jersey not invalid under Home Rule provisions of State Constitution (art. IX, §§ 11, 12) -- Home Rule provisions not applicable -- Port Authority engaged in matters of State concern and matters over which Authority has jurisdiction are not within Home Rule provisions.

1. Chapters 806-809 of the Laws of 1955 provide for the construction and operation of bridges over Throgs Neck connecting Bronx and Queens and over the Narrows connecting Brooklyn and Staten Island and for the construction of a second deck to the George Washington bridge between New York and New Jersey. The statutes are not invalid under the Home Rule provisions of the State Constitution (art. IX, §§ 11, 12). The Home Rule amendment does not apply to any of chapters 806-809 of the Laws of 1955. The Port Authority is engaged in matters of State concern and therefore the matters over which the Authority has jurisdiction are not within the Home Rule amendment.

2. The Narrows bridge terminates on Staten Island within the State of New York, but both chapters 807 and 808 require concurrent legislation by the State of New Jersey, and the plan calls for highway connections with bridges from Staten Island to New Jersey. The Narrows bridge, like the second deck on the George Washington bridge, is of an interstate character and therefore properly within the jurisdiction of the Port Authority and it is a matter of State concern.

3. The Throgs Neck bridge is authorized to be constructed by the Triborough Bridge & Tunnel Authority and will be under the sole jurisdiction of that Authority. Chapter 809 designates as among the existing or proposed main routes or thoroughfares the Cross-Bronx expressway from the George Washington bridge approach to Throgs Neck bridge, which indicates that the State of New York has adopted that portion of the national system of interstate highways which designates the Throgs Neck bridge as a continuation of the main route across the Hudson River over the George Washington bridge. This national system of interstate highways also contemplates traffic to and from New Jersey over the Throgs Neck bridge by way of the proposed Narrows bridge or one of the tunnels under the Hudson River. The Throgs Neck bridge is thus linked with the George Washington bridge, and with other existing or contemplated routes to New Jersey. All of these projects are joined together in chapters 806-809 of the Laws of 1955. They are all part of an interstate project or projects and consequently are matters of State concern.
