500 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Harbison v. Buffalo


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


4 N.Y.2d 553; 152 N.E.2d 42; 176 N.Y.S.2d 598; 1958 N.Y. LEXIS 874


February 17, 1958, Argued  
June 25, 1958, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city sought review of the order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court (New York), which directed city to issue a wholesale junk license to respondent cooperage owners.


OVERVIEW: Cooperage owners began their business in 1924. The surrounding area was zoned for residential use in 1926. However, cooperage owners applied for and received yearly business licenses for a preexisting non-conforming use. City adopted a new zoning ordinance in 1953 which required non-conforming uses to cease after three years. In 1956, city sent cooperage owners a notice to discontinue their business. Cooperage owners brought an article 78 proceeding for an order directing city to issue a wholesale junk license to them. The lower courts sustained cooperage owners. City appealed and argued that the ordinance was a valid exercise of police power. The court reversed the order of the appellate division and remanded the case for a trial on whether the resultant injury to cooperage owners was so substantial that the ordinance was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the appellate division and remanded the case for a trial on whether the resultant injury to cooperage owners was so substantial that the ordinance was unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- zoning -- elimination of nonconforming use -- under ordinance of City of Buffalo, petitioners were required to cease nonconforming use or change it to conforming use within three years from effective date of ordinance -- order directing that license to continue such nonconforming use be granted reversed and matter remanded for trial of material issues.

One of the petitioners purchased certain real property in the city of Buffalo in 1924. He erected a 30-foot by 40-foot-frame building thereon, and from that time has operated a cooperage business there. In 1924 the land was unzoned. Since 1926, except for the period between 1949 and 1953 when it was zoned for business, the land had been zoned for residential use, and it is presently in an "R3" dwelling district. In 1936, under an ordinance which included the operations of petitioners in a definition of "junk dealers", they applied for and received a license to carry on their business, and they have obtained licenses from then through the fiscal year 1956. The effect of an amendment of the ordinances of the City of Buffalo, effective as of July 30, 1953, was that petitioners were required to cease their nonconforming use or change it to a conforming use within three years from the effective date of the amendment. On November 27, 1956, the director of licenses of the City of Buffalo notified petitioners to discontinue the operation of their "junk yard" at once. The Appellate Division modified an order of the court at Special Term which had ordered that petitioners' application for a wholesale junk license for the premises be accepted and that the license be issued, and which had enjoined the City of Buffalo from prohibiting their nonconforming use of the premises by striking out the provision for an injunction. The order is reversed and the matter remanded to the court at Special Term for a trial of the material issues. The value of the property together with the improvements is said to be $ 20,000, but there is no indication of the relative value of the land and improvements separately. Petitioners claim that, at the insistence of the City of Buffalo, a few years before the ordinance went into effect, they were obliged to install a special sewage system at a cost of $ 2,000 and a boiler at a cost of $ 700. Evidence should be adduced relating to the nature of the surrounding neighborhood, the value and condition of the improvements on the premises, the nearest area to which petitioners might relocate, and the cost of such relocation, as well as any other reasonable costs which bear upon the kind and amount of damages which petitioners might sustain, and to whether petitioners might be able to continue operation of their business if not allowed to continue storage of barrels or steel drums outside their frame building.

 

501 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Kingsley International Pictures Corp. v. Regents of University


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


4 N.Y.2d 349; 151 N.E.2d 197; 175 N.Y.S.2d 39; 1958 N.Y. LEXIS 994


January 7, 1958, Argued  
May 15, 1958, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, Board of Regents of the University of the State of New York (board), sought review of an order from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York), which annulled a determination of the board denying respondent motion picture corporation a license for the exhibition of a motion picture. The appellate court remitted the matter with directions to issue a license for such exhibition.


OVERVIEW: The board denied the motion picture corporation a license for the exhibition of a motion picture entitled "Lady Chatterley's Lover." The court reversed the order that annulled the board's determination. The court confirmed the board's determination denying the license. The court held that N.Y. Educ. Law §§ 122, 122-a was constitutional because it furnished the licensor with a definite standard to apply, namely whether the motion picture portrayed acts of sexual immorality as proper behavior. It was undeniable that the board knew what an act of sexual immorality was and that adultery was such an act. The standard left no area of judgment or speculation open to the board. Their sole function under the statute was the fact-finding function as to whether a particular motion picture portrayed acts of sexual immorality as proper behavior. If it did, it was ipso facto immoral by statutory mandate. There was no need to consider whether the motion picture would perceptibly create a clear and present danger of antisocial conduct because obscenity was not protected speech. Refusing to license a motion picture which alluringly portrayed adultery as proper behavior was constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order that annulled the board's determination. The court confirmed the board's determination denying a license for the exhibition of the motion picture.


HEADNOTES

Licenses -- motion pictures -- Board of Regents properly denied license for showing of motion picture "Lady Chatterley's Lover" under Education Law ( §§ 122, 122-a), which requires denial of license for motion pictures which are immoral in that they portray "acts of sexual immorality * * * as desirable, acceptable or proper patterns of behavior" -- statute not unconstitutional.

Sections 122 and 122-a of the Education Law require the denial of a license to motion pictures which are immoral in that they portray "acts of sexual immorality * * * as desirable, acceptable or proper patterns of behavior." Under that statute the Board of Regents properly denied a license to the motion picture "Lady Chatterley's Lover". The statute is not unconstitutional.

 

502 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


64th St. Residences, Inc. v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


4 N.Y.2d 268; 150 N.E.2d 396; 174 N.Y.S.2d 1; 1958 N.Y. LEXIS 1027


March 24, 1958, Argued  
May 1, 1958, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff property owners challenged, on constitutional grounds, a judgment from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed the summary judgment rendered by the trial court in favor of defendant city and intervenor religious university. After an urban renewal plan had been approved, the property owners sought to invalidate the plan and to enjoin the city from carrying it out.


OVERVIEW: As part of an urban renewal plan, the religious university purchased a parcel of land at a price lower than the price paid by the city through condemnation. The property owners brought an action to invalidate the plan and to enjoin the city from carrying it out. The property owners argued that the sale to the university was an impermissible direct subsidy of public moneys to a denominational institution. The trial court denied the motion for a temporary injunction and granted the motion for summary judgment filed by the city and university. The appellate division affirmed. On further review, the court affirmed the dismissal of the action. The court held that the condemnation of the land and the sale to the university was not an unconstitutional grant or subsidy of public moneys to a religious corporation. The city and university paid for different things. The city bought land and buildings. The university bought the property to raze the buildings, relocate the tenants, and use the cleared land for a collegiate campus. The university paid for the re-use value of the land, and the city benefited by the achievement of its valid municipal purpose of eliminating a slum.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the summary judgment for the city and the university in the property owners' action challenging the urban renewal plan. The court dismissed the property owner's appeal.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- slum clearance project -- sale to denominational school -- condemnation and sale by city of area in slum clearance project to denominational university not void as unconstitutional grant of public moneys to religious corporation where facts show that square-foot price which university bid was at least equal to re-use value of land.

1. As part of the Lincoln Square Urban Renewal Project -- a slum clearance project in New York City -- each sponsor, such as intervenor Fordham University, agreed to bid at the auction provided for by section 72-k of the General Municipal Law, a stated minimum price and, if it should be the successful bidder, to relocate the prior occupants, clear its part of the site and redevelop that part as required by the plan, with buildings to be approved by the city. Fordham University agreed to bid, for the two-block part set aside for "educational purposes", at least $ 2,241,610, or $ 7 per square foot. The city on its part agreed to acquire that part (as well as the rest of the area) by condemnation, and, if Fordham University should be successful at the auction, to convey that part to the university at the auction price. The resale of this area to Fordham University, a Roman Catholic institution, is not a grant of public moneys to a denominational institution in violation of section 7 of article I of the New York State Constitution and of the First and Fourteenth Amendments to the United States Constitution. Both courts below have found that the $ 7 per square foot which Fordham agreed to bid, and did bid, is at least equal to the re-use value as established by several appraisals, all of which reported figures lower than $ 7 per square foot. Therefore, Fordham University is not getting a gift, grant or subsidy of public property.

2. Since this sale is an exchange of considerations and not a gift or subsidy, no "aid to religion" is involved. A religious corporation cannot be excluded from the bidding.

3. The system of selling these properties at auctions where there may be little likelihood of competing bids is not invalid.

Murtha v. Monaghan


Court of Appeals of New York


4 N.Y.2d 897; 151 N.E.2d 83; 174 N.Y.S.2d 648; 1958 N.Y. LEXIS 1042

504 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Steinbeck v. Gerosa


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


4 N.Y.2d 302; 151 N.E.2d 170; 175 N.Y.S.2d 1; 1958 N.Y. LEXIS 1031


February 20, 1958, Argued  
May 1, 1958, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant author sought review of an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed the determination of respondent city comptroller that the author was liable to pay the New York City General Business and Financial Tax.


OVERVIEW: The author, a resident of New York City, wrote novels and other literary works. He sought a refund of gross receipts taxes paid under New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code ch. 46, tit. B. The court affirmed the denial of the refund request, holding that the imposition of a privilege tax of general application upon an author did not interfere with the constitutional right to freedom of the press. The court further held that the relationship between an author and a publisher was not that of joint venturers. The court held that the commerce clause, U.S. Const. art. I, § 8, did not preclude imposition of the tax merely because the author's works were licensed and sold in other states and countries.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the determination of the author's tax liability.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- Gross Receipts Tax -- receipts from literary activities -- Gross Receipts Tax (Administrative Code of City of New York, ch. 46, tit. B) properly imposed by City of New York upon petitioner, author of novels, plays, motion picture scripts, magazine articles and other literary works, who received income from licensing of such works -- his literary activities constitute "trade, business, profession, vocation or commercial activity", within meaning of tax statute -- receipts taxable although attributable to contracts made in prior taxable years -- constitutional guarantee of freedom of press does not prohibit imposition of tax -- petitioner not engaged in interstate commerce although publisher may be -- relationship between petitioner and publisher is that of debtor and creditor.

1. A Gross Receipts Tax (Administrative Code of City of New York, ch. 46, tit. B) is imposed by the City of New York pursuant to section 24-a of the General City Law which authorizes the city to impose "a tax * * * upon persons carrying on or exercising for gain or profit within such city, any trade, business, profession, vocation or commercial activity * * * or making sales within such city". Petitioner, a resident of New York City, is an author of novels, plays, motion picture scripts, magazine articles and other literary works. Through his literary agents located in New York City, he negotiated for the licensing of certain rights in his literary works. The agents' principal function was to negotiate such contracts and they had complete authority and discretion in so doing, and had executed contracts on his behalf. As to books, the consideration to be paid to petitioner consists of a percentage of the amount charged by the publisher for all copies sold, less copies returned, but with no deduction for cash discounts or bad debts. This percentage is characterized as "royalties." As to motion picture scripts, magazine and newspaper articles, and plays, the consideration consists of a lump sum or a percentage or a combination of the two. Petitioner's activities in the city of New York constitute a "trade, business, profession, vocation or commercial activity" within the meaning of the tax statute.

2. Petitioner's receipts are taxable although the receipts in any one taxable calendar year may be attributable to contracts made in prior taxable years. The tax is measured by the "receipts received in and/or allocable to the city from such profession, vocation, trade, business or commercial activity exercised or carried on by him during the calendar year" in which the privilege period commences (Administrative Code of City of New York, § B46-2.0, subd. a). The statute requires merely that the receipts be attributable to the profession, vocation, trade, business, or commercial activity which the petitioner carried on in that year and not to any "particular activity". The fact that the contract may have been made in another year makes no difference. The receipt of income is as much a part of a business as the making of a contract that gives rise to such income.

3. The constitutional guarantee of freedom of the press does not prohibit the City of New York from imposing a Gross Receipts Tax on the receipts of an author derived from the grant of licenses to his rights in his literary works. The tax applies not only to writers and publishers but to all businesses, professions, trades, vocations and commercial activities. It applies to all without discrimination. It is a general tax and not, in any sense, aimed at the press or at writers. The city does not seek, through the guise of this tax, to regulate those covered by its provisions or to interfere with freedom of the press. Its sole object is revenue notwithstanding the fact that the tax is called a privilege tax.

4. While many sales may have been made by the publisher in other States and countries, and it may be that the publisher was engaged in interstate and foreign commerce as a result, it does not follow that petitioner was likewise engaged in interstate and foreign commerce. The relationship between petitioner and his publishers was that of debtor and creditor -- not that of joint venturers or partners. The sums paid to petitioner by way of "royalties" were merely the price for the licensing of certain of his literary rights.

5. Petitioner's activities are not so closely related to interstate commerce and foreign commerce that they must be treated as though they were activities in interstate or foreign commerce. The formation of a contract between persons in different States is not within the protection of the commerce clause unless the performance thereof is within its protection. The writing by petitioner of books, plays, magazine articles, motion picture scripts and radio scripts, and the granting, within the city of New York, of licenses to certain of the rights therein is separate and distinct from the subsequent sale by others of books, magazines, etc., in interstate or foreign commerce even though the granting of the licenses may induce or occasion interstate commerce.

 

505 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Lloyd Harbor v. Huntington


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


4 N.Y.2d 182; 149 N.E.2d 851; 173 N.Y.S.2d 553; 1958 N.Y. LEXIS 1064


December 2, 1957, Argued  
April 3, 1958, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In consolidated cases, appellant town sought review of the order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court (New York), which affirmed an order declaring that chapter 840 of the Laws of 1955 was invalid and that the acquisition of certain land was illegal and violated a town law and enjoining the town from establishing a park and public bathing beach in actions brought by respondents, village and residents.


OVERVIEW: The legislature enacted chapter 840 of the Laws of 1955 (the act) permitting the town to establish public bathing beaches, notwithstanding any other ordinance. The village and the residents brought an action alleging that the act was unconstitutional. The trial court decided in favor of the village and the residents, holding that the act was designed specifically to take from the villages within the town certain powers granted them by their charters, as embodied in the village law and, by reason thereof, the act must be deemed local in character and violative of N.Y. Const. art. III, § 17. The appellate court affirmed the decision of the trial court. On appeal, the court reversed the order of the appellate court, holding that the town had the authority, quite apart from the act, to establish a public bathing beach within village limits without village permission. That being so, the act confirmed and clarified a power which the town already possessed and could not, therefore, be subjected to any charge of unconstitutionality.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court, which affirmed an order declaring that an act was invalid and that the acquisition of certain land was illegal and violated a town law and enjoining the town from establishing a park and public bathing beach.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- authority of town to establish bathing beach in village -- town possessed authority to establish public parks in village territory within town limits (Town Law, § 81, subd. 1, par. [d]; § 220, subd. 4; L. 1906, ch. 87, as amd. by L. 1943, ch. 710) -- bathing beaches are parks -- chapter 840 of Laws of 1955 providing that town could establish public bathing beaches in town only confirmed power which town already possessed, and effected no change in village charter and is not violative of Constitution (N. Y. Const., art. III, § 17) -- section 198 (subd. 4) of Town Law not applicable and does not qualify other statutes -- village zoning ordinances limiting property entirely to residential uses invalid -- question whether establishment of public park serves governmental function not passed upon.

1. Chapter 840 of the Laws of 1955 provides that, "Notwithstanding any other general, special or local law, ordinance or regulation * * * the town of Huntington, in the county of Suffolk, may establish, maintain and operate public bathing beaches * * * on lands owned or leased by the town of Huntington * * * at any place within [its] boundaries". The Town of Huntington acquired lands in villages within the town's territorial limits for the purpose of operating public bathing beaches. Both villages were zoned entirely for residence purposes only and, in effect, the zoning ordinances excluded such beaches. Prior to the enactment of chapter 840 of the Laws of 1955, three other statutes constituted grants of authority to a town to establish public parks in village territory within town limits (Town Law, § 81, subd. 1, par. [d]; § 220, subd. 4; L. 1906, ch. 87, as amd.). Bathing beaches are parks. Accordingly, the town had authority, apart from chapter 840 of the Laws of 1955, to establish a public bathing beach within the village limits without village permission. Chapter 840 of the Laws of 1955 only confirmed and clarified a power which the town already possessed.

2. Subdivision 4 of section 198 of the Town Law does not qualify the other statutes or require village consent to the establishment of a public park within its area. It does not apply at all to public parks established by a town. It deals solely with a "park district" park, which is a completely different type of park from a town public park.

3. The village zoning ordinances limiting property to residential uses cannot prevent the town from setting up and maintaining such a park or beach. The zoning ordinances are contrary to the spirit and purpose of the zoning provisions of the Village Law pursuant to which they were enacted. They bear no substantial relation to zoning purposes and are void to the extent that they would prevent the establishment of the public bathing beaches. Moreover, section 177 of the Village Law requires that zoning regulations shall "facilitate the adequate provision of * * * parks". Villages may not zone against public parks and beaches.

4. Chapter 840 of the Laws of 1955 gave no added authority to the town and took none from the village, and, accordingly, effected no change or alteration in any village charter and cannot be regarded as violative of the Constitution (N. Y. Const., art. III, § 17).

5. The question whether the establishment and maintenance of a public park serve a governmental function is not reached and the court does not pass upon it.

 

506 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Wiggins v. Somers


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


4 N.Y.2d 215; 149 N.E.2d 869; 173 N.Y.S.2d 579; 1958 N.Y. LEXIS 1069


December 3, 1957, Argued  
April 3, 1958, Decided



DISPOSITION:  Judgment accordingly. a town activity, here, the operation of a public bathing beach, may be regarded as governmental insofar as zoning is concerned (see, e.g., Brush v. Commissioner, 300 U. S. 352, 371), despite cases to the contrary in the field of tort liability. (See Augustine v. Town of Brant, 249 N. Y. 198, 204, supra; Caldwell v. Village of Island Park, 304 N. Y. 268, 273, supra.)

Regarding the plaintiffs' contention that chapter 840 of the Laws of 1955 violated the constitutional provision which forbids enactment of a private or local bill "Incorporating villages" (N. Y. Const., art. III, § 17), little, if anything, need be said. As already noted, the town long before 1955 was authorized to acquire lands within a village for use as a public beach without the consent of the village and despite zoning ordinances to the contrary; it follows, therefore, that the 1955 act merely confirmed and clarified the existence of a power already enjoyed by the town. In other words, the statute, giving no added authority to the town and, by the same token, taking nothing from the villages, effected no change or alteration in any village charter and cannot possibly be regarded as violative of the constitutional provision.

The judgment of the Appellate Division should be reversed and judgment directed adjudicating and declaring the rights of the parties in accordance with the views expressed in this opinion, with costs in all courts.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant town sought review of the order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the second judicial department (New York), which declared unconstitutional an ordinance prohibiting the transportation and dumping in the town of garbage originating outside the town. The town contended that the lower courts erred in dismissing its counterclaim to restrain respondent dumper's violation of the ordinance.


OVERVIEW: The dumper operated a garbage dump located within the town in which he dumped garbage that he collected in neighboring towns. Shortly after the ordinance was enacted, the dumper brought an action challenging the validity of the ordinance on constitutional grounds. The trial court dismissed the town's counterclaim as legally insufficient, finding that the ordinance did violate the constitution. The court held that the part of the ordinance prohibiting transportation in the town of garbage originating outside the town clearly exceeded the power of the town to regulate traffic and commerce. However, the court held that the part of the ordinance prohibiting the dumping in the town of foreign garbage was a valid exercise of the police power. Garbage dumps were inherently nuisances. The town could limit the amount of garbage dumped in the town and the limitation to garbage generated within the town was not arbitrary. The discrimination in the ordinance was not based on quality. It was simply a measure to reduce, in a manner consistent with the interests of the citizens of the town, the quantity of garbage dumped into the town.


OUTCOME: The court severed the prohibition against transportation from the prohibition against dumping and sustained the prohibition against dumping. It modified the judgment of the appellate court declaring the ordinance unconstitutional and remitted for further proceedings in accordance with its opinion.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- power to limit dumping of garbage -- ordinance of town of Somers prohibiting dumping of garbage originating outside of town within said town not arbitrary or capricious and is within constitutional power of town -- ordinance, insofar as it prohibits transporting said garbage through town, invalid -- town has statutory power to regulate garbage dumping (Town Law, § 130) -- no conflict between ordinance and Sanitary Code of Westchester County ( § 8, subd. b) -- prohibition against transportation severable from prohibition against dumping.

1. An ordinance of the town of Somers provides that no person may transport or dump, within the town, garbage originating outside of the town of Somers. A parcel of real property in the town of Somers is used by plaintiff as lessee for the purpose of dumping and disposing of the garbage he collects in his carting business. The parcel serves as a dump not only for the garbage collected in the town of Somers, but for all of the garbage collected from customers outside the town in outlying and surrounding communities, villages and towns. The ordinance, insofar as it prohibits dumping of garbage, originating outside the town of Somers, within the town, is not arbitrary or capricious and is within the constitutional power of the town.

2. The prohibition against the use of the roads in the town of Somers (which include a State and Federal highway) is invalid in that it exceeds the power of the town board to regulate traffic.

3. The town has power to regulate garbage dumping within its confines (Town Law, § 130).

4. There is no conflict between this ordinance and the Sanitary Code of Westchester County ( § 8, subd. b).

5. The prohibition against transportation is severable and independent from the prohibition against dumping.

People v. Dunn


Court of Appeals of New York


4 N.Y.2d 746; 148 N.E.2d 912; 171 N.Y.S.2d 864; 1958 N.Y. LEXIS 1221

508 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Koster v. Holz


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


3 N.Y.2d 639; 148 N.E.2d 287; 171 N.Y.S.2d 65; 1958 N.Y. LEXIS 1256


October 21, 1957, Argued  
January 23, 1958, Decided



DISPOSITION: Orders reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff appealed the ruling of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court of New York County, which dismissed the plaintiff's cause of action for the granting of an insurance broker's license.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff took the examination for an insurance broker's license and passed. However, he was denied a license due to a statement in his application concerning his conscientious objection to war and military service on philosophical and religious grounds. The defendant denied the plaintiff's license citing the plaintiff's information furnished on the application. The court held that the order had to be reversed because the defendant must produce a rational basis for denying the plaintiff a broker's license.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the orders of the district court because the defendant could not make every conviction of a crime ipso facto a ground for the denial of an insurance broker's license.


HEADNOTES

Insurance -- insurance broker's license -- conscientious objector -- Insurance Law ( § 119) requires Superintendent of Insurance to hold formal hearing on issue of trustworthiness of applicant for insurance broker's license; matter remitted to Superintendent of Insurance since no hearing was held -- Insurance Law does not make conviction of crime ground for denial of application; despite petitioner's conviction of violation of Selective Service Act of 1948 (U. S. Code, tit. 50, Appendix, § 462) because he did not qualify as conscientious objector, if Selective Service Board believed him to be sincere, denial of application for license, where conviction is only evidence of untrustworthiness, would be arbitrary and capricious.

1. Petitioner, who applied for a license as an insurance broker under section 119 of the Insurance Law, had been convicted of a violation of the Selective Service Act of 1948 (U. S. Code, tit. 50, Appendix, § 462) when he sought to be classified as a conscientious objector. Although he passed the required examination, his application was denied on the ground that he could not be regarded as a "competent and trustworthy person" within the contemplation of the Insurance Law. Section 119 of the Insurance Law, read in the light of its history and purpose, requires that the Superintendent of Insurance hold a formal hearing on the issue of petitioner's trustworthiness. Since no such hearing was held, the matter is remitted to the Superintendent of Insurance.

2. Petitioner may have been denied classification as a conscientious objector either because the Selective Service Board determined that he was insincere or because it felt that petitioner's beliefs, though sincerely held, did not fall within the definition of a conscientious objector as stated in the United States Code (tit. 50, Appendix, § 456, subd. [j]). The Insurance Law does not make conviction of a crime a ground for denial of an application. If it is determined upon evidence that the Selective Service Board believed petitioner to be a sincere person but one whose beliefs did not entitle him to status as a conscientious objector, denial of his application for a license, where this is the only evidence of his untrustworthiness, would be arbitrary and capricious. On the other hand, if it is found that the board believed him to be insincere, then denial of the petitioner's application is within the discretion of the Superintendent.

3. Petitioner has failed to show a denial of equal protection of the laws upon his allegation that a license was previously granted to a person in a similar situation. There was no discrimination among persons in a similar situation at any given period.

 

509 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Firth


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


3 N.Y.2d 472; 146 N.E.2d 682; 168 N.Y.S.2d 949; 1957 N.Y. LEXIS 708


October 14, 1957, Argued  
December 5, 1957, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought relief from an order of the County Court, Monroe County (New York) that reversed a judgment entered by a justice of the peace convicting defendant of a violation of N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 56(1) and that dismissed the information. The county court found that the statute was unconstitutional because it was too vague and indefinite to be enforced. The county court reversed, vacated the judgment, and ordered the fine remitted.


OVERVIEW: Defendant struck a young girl riding a bicycle. He was not charged with violating any of the express speed limits, nor was he charged with the crime of reckless driving under N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 58. Rather, he was charged with a criminal traffic infraction under the statute. On appeal, the court affirmed. It found that a criminal statute had to be sufficiently definite, clear, and positive to give unequivocal warning to citizens of the rule that was to be obeyed. The statute prohibited a person from driving at a speed to endanger other things, which was impossible at any speed, and only at a speed that allowed the vehicle to stop without endangering anything, which was impossible. While, the statutory draftsmen did not intend the statute to mean what it did and the prosecution offered a different interpretation of the statute's meaning, the court refused to twist the statute's language to render it valid. The court concluded that the statute was too vague to constitute a sufficient definition of criminal conduct, and contained no sufficient standard by which a driver's conduct could be tested, to be valid.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- constitutional law -- excessive speed -- section 56 (subd. 1) of Vehicle and Traffic Law, prohibiting operation of motor vehicle "at such a speed as to endanger the life, limb or property of any person" or "at a speed greater than will permit such person to bring the vehicle to a stop without injury to another or his property", too vague and indefinite -- defendant's conviction properly reversed on ground statute was unconstitutional -- rules of criminal law applicable to this "traffic infraction".

1. Subdivision 1 of section 56 of the Vehicle and Traffic Law provides that "1. No person shall operate a motor vehicle or a motor cycle upon a public highway at such a speed as to endanger the life, limb or property of any person, nor at a rate of speed greater than will permit such person to bring the vehicle to a stop without injury to another or his property." The statute is too vague and indefinite to constitute a sufficient definition of criminal conduct and contains no sufficient standard by which a driver's conduct may be tested.

2. Accordingly, defendant's conviction under that statute was properly reversed on the ground that the statute was unconstitutional.

3. The conviction was not for a crime but for a "traffic infraction" (Vehicle and Traffic Law, § 2, subd. 29). Rules of criminal law, however, are applicable to such prosecutions.

 

510 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Church of Holy Trinity v. Melish


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


3 N.Y.2d 476; 146 N.E.2d 685; 168 N.Y.S.2d 952; 1957 N.Y. LEXIS 709


October 8, 1957, Argued  
December 5, 1957, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant church members appealed the decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court of Kings County (New York), which ruled in favor of plaintiff church that the provisions of the N.Y. Relig. Corp. Law § 42 did not apply to elections of church rector.


OVERVIEW: The church brought an action requesting a judgment declaring an individual to be rector of the church, and to restrain defendant members from interfering with the conduct of religious services or with the property or management of the church. The trial court found in favor of the church members, and held that the New York Religious Corporation Law (Act), N.Y. Relig. Corp Law § 42, governed the presence or absence of a quorum in elections for church rector. The appellate division reversed, and held that the ecclesiastical nature of the election required on constitutional grounds that church law, not the Act, governed. On appeal, the court affirmed the decision of the lower court. Without reaching the constitutional claims, the court ruled that, on its face, the Act did not apply to the election of a rector. The court noted that Section 20 of the Act stated that no provision of the Act authorized the calling or governance of a meeting regarding the choice or removal of a minister. The court further noted that Section 20 of the Act was in accord with the history of the Act, which did not presume to govern qualifications of a quorum for ecclesiastical matters.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court, which granted the relief sought by the church and restrained interference from the church members who opposed the election of the church rector.


HEADNOTES

Religious corporations -- election of rector -- rector of Protestant Episcopal Church validly elected at meeting of vestry attended by 2 churchwardens and 4 vestrymen, all of whom voted for his election -- 6 of 11 members of vestry were present and constituted quorum -- Canons of Protestant Episcopal Church provide that majority of vestry constitutes quorum and that majority of that quorum shall be competent to act.

1. The vestrymen of the Episcopal Church of the Holy Trinity in Brooklyn, as authorized by the church's charter and by statute (Religious Corporations Law, § 41), consisted of 2 churchwardens and 9 vestrymen but there were two vacancies among the vestrymen. Two churchwardens and 4 of the vestrymen, being a majority of the whole authorized vestry of 11, attended a vestry meeting and communicated to the Ecclesiastical Authority of the Diocese of Long Island, in which this parish is situated, their proposal to elect plaintiff Dr. Sidener as rector. The next day the Ecclesiastical Authority notified the vestry of the approval of the election and on that next day the election of Dr. Sidener was unanimously voted by the 6 members present. General Canon 11, section 2, of the Protestant Episcopal Church of the United States provides that of any body consisting of several members, "a majority of said members, the whole having been duly cited to meet, shall be a quorum; and a majority of the quorum so convened shall be competent to act". Since the two meetings of the vestry were attended by a majority of the whole number of the vestry and all voted for Dr. Sidener's election, he was validly elected.

2. Section 42 of the Religious Corporations Law provides: "To constitute a quorum of the vestry or board of trustees, there must be present either: 1. The rector and at least a majority of the whole number of wardens and vestrymen, or 2. One churchwarden and one more than a majority of the vestrymen or both churchwardens and a majority of the vestrymen". That law is not applicable to the election of a rector. The only applicable law is the Canon law of the church.

3. Moreover, section 42 of the Religious Corporations Law also provides: "The vestry may, subject to the canons of the Protestant Episcopal Church in the United States, and of the Diocese in which the parish or church is situated, by a majority vote, elect a rector to fill a vacancy occurring in the rectorship of the parish, and may fix the salary or compensation of the rector."

4. In addition, section 25 of the Religious Corporations Law provides that: "No provision of this act authorizes the calling, settlement, dismissal or removal of a minister" and that a meeting of a church corporation for such purpose shall be governed only according to the laws of the religious denomination or ecclesiastical governing body with which the church corporation is connected.

 

511 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Hotel Asso. of New York City v. Weaver


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


3 N.Y.2d 206; 144 N.E.2d 14; 165 N.Y.S.2d 17; 1957 N.Y. LEXIS 882


April 12, 1957, Argued  
July 3, 1957, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant hotel owner sought review of a judgment from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court (New York), which dismissed the hotel owner's petition filed pursuant to N.Y. Civ. Prac. Act, art. 78 and N.Y. State Residential Rent Law § 9 (1946) against respondent rent administrator.


OVERVIEW: The hotel owner brought a proceeding against the rent administrator for an order decontrolling the rents of rooms contained in the hotel. The rent administrator denied the hotel owner's petition and determined that the hotel owner was not entitled to a public hearing concerning the matter. The hotel owner filed a petition pursuant to article 78. The hotel owner appealed after its petition was dismissed. The court affirmed and held that the rent administrator's determination could not be disturbed if it had warrant in the record, a reasonable basis in law, and was neither arbitrary nor capricious. Finally, the court determined that a judicial function was exhausted when there was found to be a rational basis for the conclusions approved by the rent administrator.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.


HEADNOTES

Landlord and tenant -- rent regulations -- hotels -- State Rent Commission properly refused to find that hotels are class of housing accommodations within meaning of State Residential Rent Law (§ 12, subd. 1) providing for abolition of rent control when percentage of vacancies in "any particular class of housing accommodations is five per centum or more" -- petitioners, including membership corporation representing hotels in New York City, also failed to show right to decontrol under part of statute authorizing decontrol where availability of adequate rental housing accommodations make rent control unnecessary.

1. Subdivision 1 of section 12 of the State Residential Rent Law (L. 1946, ch. 274, as amd.) provides that whenever the State Housing Rent Commission shall find that "the percentage of vacancies in all or any particular class of housing accommodations is five per centum or more", rent control shall be abolished, except that the commission must hold a public hearing. The commission properly refused to find as requested by petitioners that hotels are a class of housing accommodations within the meaning of the statute, and that the phrase "in * * * any particular class" as contained in the statute refers, not to the location within a certain type of building, but to accommodations within a particular price range. The commission had the authority to interpret those words as it did and did not abuse its discretion.

2. Petitioners, including a membership corporation representing hotels in New York City, have also failed to show a right to decontrol under that part of the statute which authorizes decontrol in "any particular class of housing accommodations" where "the availability of adequate rental housing accommodations and other relevant factors are such as to make rent control unnecessary for the purpose of eliminating speculative, unwarranted, and abnormal increases in rents and of preventing profiteering and speculative and other disruptive practices resulting from abnormal market conditions caused by congestion".

3. The right to a hearing under the statute would follow only if the right to decontrol were established prima facie.

4. To refuse to place hotel accommodations, which include not only shelter but services also, in a separate class from other accommodations does not violate due process nor does it deny petitioners the equal protection of the laws.

5. The Legislature has recently indicated that the emergency has not ceased as far as hotels are concerned, and the finding has not been shown to be without factual basis.

 

512 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re Roel


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


3 N.Y.2d 224; 144 N.E.2d 24; 165 N.Y.S.2d 31; 1957 N.Y. LEXIS 884


May 23, 1957, Argued  
July 3, 1957, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant foreign lawyer sought review of the judgment of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court (New York), which affirmed an order in the trial court that adjudged him to be in contempt of court and enjoined him from practicing law within the state, in favor of appellee lawyers association.


OVERVIEW: The foreign lawyer was a Mexican citizen and lawyer admitted to practice in the courts of that country. Although he was never a citizen of the United States nor a member of the state bar, he maintained offices in the city for a considerable period of time. At his offices, the foreign lawyer advised members of the public on Mexican law, including Mexican divorce law, and he acted as a consultant to members of the bar on Mexican legal matters. He also prepared legal papers and documents, which were required for the institution of divorce actions in Mexico. On the other hand, the foreign lawyer never gave advice as to state law, and he advised his clients to consult a state lawyer. The court affirmed the judgment by holding that the foreign lawyer was in contempt of court because he was not a member of the state bar and he did, indeed, engage in activities that constituted the practice of law pursuant to N.Y. Penal Law § 270. The court found that the foreign lawyer was guilty of practicing law in the state without being licensed to do so, even though he practiced exclusively foreign law.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment, which affirmed an order in the trial court that adjudged the foreign lawyer to be in contempt of court and enjoined him from practicing law within the state, in favor of the lawyers association because he was not a member of the state bar, and he engaged in activities that constituted the practice of law, even though he practiced exclusively foreign law.


HEADNOTES

Attorney and client -- unauthorized practice of law -- Mexican law -- Mexican citizen and lawyer, never citizen of United States nor member of New York Bar, who advised residents and citizens of New York within city and State of New York as to Mexican law, including Mexican divorce law, properly adjudged in contempt (Judiciary Law, § 750, subd. B) on ground he practiced law in this State in violation of section 270 of Penal Law -- term "law" as used in statute does not refer to New York law alone.

1. Section 270 of the Penal Law provides that it shall be unlawful for any person to practice as an attorney at law or to hold himself out to the public as being entitled to practice law without having first been licensed and admitted to practice law in this State. A Mexican citizen and lawyer, admitted to practice in the courts of that country, but never a citizen of the United States nor a member of the New York Bar, has maintained offices in the city of New York and advertised in a daily newspaper that he is a Mexican lawyer in New York and advised members of the public on Mexican law, including Mexican divorce law. He gives legal advice and renders legal services within the city and State of New York upon other matters pertaining to the laws of Mexico, prepares and draws legal documents, contracts, agreements and other papers applicable and pertaining to the laws of Mexico, and renders such services and advice within the city and State of New York to residents and citizens of New York. He was properly adjudged in contempt (Judiciary Law, § 750, subd. B) on the ground that he practiced law in this State without being licensed to do so in violation of section 270 of the Penal Law, and was properly enjoined from practicing or assuming to practice law. The activities which he engaged in constitute the practice of law as referred to in section 270 of the Penal Law. The term "law" as used therein does not refer to New York law alone.

2. Section 270 of the Penal Law does not deprive him of liberty and property without due process of law. It is not unreasonable to require that a person desiring to engage in the practice of foreign law be admitted to the Bar here and be subject to the same rules as every other member of the Bar of this State.

 

513 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Excelsior Pictures Corp. v. Regents of University


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


3 N.Y.2d 237; 144 N.E.2d 31; 165 N.Y.S.2d 42; 1957 N.Y. LEXIS 885


March 5, 1957, Argued  
July 3, 1957, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant university regents sought review of an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York), which annulled the regents' decision to deny respondent movie corporation a license to exhibit a motion picture in New York State.


OVERVIEW: The movie corporation sought to obtain a license to show its movie, which was a fictionalized depiction of members of a nudist group in a secluded camp. The regents denied the request for a license on the ground that the movie was "indecent." The appellate court annulled the decision of the regents and they appealed. On appeal, the court affirmed the order of the appellate court, holding that the movie could not have been banned unless it was "obscene." Nothing sexually impure or filthy was shown in the movie and so there was no legal basis for censorship. The term "indecent," as used in N.Y. Educ. Law § 122 meant "obscene." However, the movie was not obscene. Obscenity, real, serious, not imagined or puritanically exaggerated, was a public evil, a public nuisance, a public pollution and there was no reason why democratic government should not have used democratic processes on a high administrative level, under the control of the courts, to suppress such obscenity. However, censorship was a necessary evil, a last resort, used only when necessary and limited to the necessity. The regents went far beyond even the permissible maximum of censorship.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the appellate court, which annulled the regents' decision to deny the movie corporation a license to exhibit its movie.


HEADNOTES

Licenses -- motion pictures -- Board of Regents should not have denied license to petitioner to exhibit motion picture called "Garden of Eden" on ground that it was "indecent"; word "indecent" (Education Law, § 122) means obscene; picture is not obscene, and Board of Regents did not declare it to be obscene -- section 1140-b of Penal Law, read in connection with section 122 of Education Law, does not prohibit licensing of motion picture showing group of nude people of both sexes.

1. Section 122 of the Education Law requires the Board of Regents to issue a license for the exhibition of a motion picture unless it is "obscene, indecent, immoral, inhuman, sacrilegious, or is of such a character that its exhibition would tend to corrupt morals or incite to crime". The Board of Regents should not have denied to petitioner a license to exhibit in this State a motion picture called "Garden of Eden" on the ground that it was "indecent". The word "indecent" means obscene. The picture is not obscene, and the Board of Regents did not declare it to be obscene.

2. Section 1140-b of the Penal Law, read in connection with section 122 of the Education Law, does not create a statutory prohibition against the licensing of any motion picture showing a group of nude people of both sexes. The statute (Penal Law, § 1140-b) was not intended to make criminal in New York State any practice of nudism, wherever and by whomever practiced, even in secluded private grounds and by family groups, and it was not intended to deal with the exhibition of any motion picture. Even if the statute made criminal any and every practice of nudism in New York State, it does not follow that picturing such activity becomes criminal or indecent or justifies censorship.

 

514 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Cutler v. Herman


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


3 N.Y.2d 334; 144 N.E.2d 353; 165 N.Y.S.2d 449; 1957 N.Y. LEXIS 894


May 8, 1957, Argued  
July 3, 1957, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant town board sought review of a decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which annulled the town board's denial of an application that the town board consent to the annexation of certain lands by a nearby village and directed the town board to execute their consent in a proceeding by respondent citizen under N.Y. Civ. Prac. Act. 78 contesting the town board's action.


OVERVIEW: The village was apparently attempting to annex certain territory pursuant to N.Y. Village Law § 348, presented the town board with a petition containing the signatures of the majority of the registered voters and the owners of a majority in value of the real property of the territory, and requested the town board's approval. The town board objected on the grounds that the annexation was a diminution of a special purpose district in violation of N.Y. Town Law § 202-c. The court affirmed the decision of the lower court and found that there was no basis for the town board to reject the petition and the town board should have approved the application. Approval was not in conflict with § 202-c because approval did not accomplish the actual annexation. On the village was capable of accomplishing the annexation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the lower court in favor of the citizen.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- annexation of territory -- petition for annexation of territory to Village of Rockville Centre complied with provisions of section 348 of Village Law -- chapter 818 of Laws of 1954, amending Nassau County Civil Divisions Act and intended to supersede section 348 of Village Law, violates New York State Constitution (art. III, § 17) prohibiting enactment of private or local bill incorporating villages -- order directing members of town board to execute consent to petition affirmed.

1. A petition for the annexation of certain territory to the Village of Rockville Centre, signed by registered voters and by the owners of a majority in value of the real property of the territory, as assessed upon the last preceding regular assessment roll, and verified, complied with section 348 of the Village Law. There was no basis for the determination by the Town Board of the Town of Hempstead that the petition failed to comply with the statute.

2. Since the board's determination refusing to consent to the annexation, the Legislature enacted chapter 818 of the Laws of 1954 amending the Nassau County Civil Divisions Act (§ 255.20), by which it attempted to supersede section 348 of the Village Law. It is however in violation of section 17 of article III of the New York State Constitution prohibiting the enactment of "a private or local bill * * * Incorporating villages". It is expressly limited to Nassau County. No classification is attempted other than that based on that locality.

3. Accordingly, the order annulling the determination of the town board and directing the members thereof to execute their consent to the petition is affirmed.

 

515 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Presnell v. Leslie


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


3 N.Y.2d 384; 144 N.E.2d 381; 165 N.Y.S.2d 488; 1957 N.Y. LEXIS 899


April 4, 1957, Argued  
July 3, 1957, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner amateur radio operator sought review of the decision of respondent board of appeals that upheld a building inspector's denial of a permit for an antenna on a 44-foot steel tower. The Supreme Court at Special Term, Nassau County (New York) dismissed the operator's petition. On appeal, the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court (New York) affirmed. The amateur radio operator appealed.


OVERVIEW: The amateur radio operator contended that the denial of the right to erect the tower constituted a deprivation of property without due process of law in contravention of state and federal constitutions and an unlawful invasion by the municipality into a field of regulation fully pre-empted by federal law. The court affirmed the denial of the permit, as the operator failed to establish that the proposed 44-foot rotary beam antenna tower was an accessory use customarily incidental to a highly classified residential area and that the Village of Westbury, N.Y., Amended Building Zone Ordinance, art. III, § 8, was unconstitutional as construed and applied. The court concluded that the ordinance was justified under the police power of the state in balancing the unlimited exercise of the operator's hobby and the public interest. The court found that federal law had not preempted the field, especially as to matters left to local regulation like public safety.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the board of appeals denying the amateur radio operator a permit for a 44-foot steel tower.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- zoning -- radio tower -- petitioner, amateur radio operator, properly denied permit to replace 10-foot wooden pole, used for holding antenna, with 44-foot steel tower on premises located in "Residence A" zone of village of Westbury.

1. Petitioner, whose residence is located in a "Residence A" zone of the village of Westbury, is an amateur radio operator, and for seven years operated his station using an antenna on a 10-foot wooden pole. He was properly denied a permit to replace it with a 44-foot steel tower.

2. The proposed tower is not an accessory use of the premises.

3. There was no sufficient showing that it was customary to have towers of the kind petitioner desired to erect in a residence area of a suburb. There was no showing of the number of the 146,000 licensed amateur radio operators in the United States using antennae of this type or where amateur operators having such towers reside. In the absence of such evidence, it cannot be said as a matter of law that the erection of this tower is a customarily incidental use of residential property or one which might commonly be expected by neighboring property owners.

4. There was some evidence as to the tendency of children to climb on such towers, and petitioner's offer to fence in the tower could have been regarded as insufficient protection. There was evidence as to the effect of the proposed tower on property values, and also evidence from which it could be found that the tower would be an "eyesore" in an exclusively residential community occupied by private homes situated close to one another, and not in conformity with the character of the neighborhood. It has not been shown that the ordinance is not justified under the police power of this State.

5. A regulation of the Federal Communications Commission that "No new antenna structure shall be erected * * * without prior approval by the Commission, in any case when * * * the antenna structure proposed to be erected will exceed an overall height of 170 feet above ground level" (Code of Fed. Reg., tit. 47, § 12.60, subd. [a]) was not intended totally to exclude all State and municipal governments from the right to regulate the height of proposed antennae. The local regulation does not conflict with the purposes of the Federal legislation.

6. Petitioner has failed to establish that the Zoning Ordinance of the Village of Westbury as construed and applied herein is unconstitutional.

 

516 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Mercury Machine Importing Corp. v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


3 N.Y.2d 418; 144 N.E.2d 400; 165 N.Y.S.2d 517; 1957 N.Y. LEXIS 902


April 10, 1957, Argued  
July 3, 1957, Decided



DISPOSITION: In Mercury Mach. Importing Corp. v. City of New York: Order of the Appellate Division reversed and the judgment of Special Term reinstated, with costs in this court and in the Appellate Division. First question certified answered in the negative. Second question certified not answered.

In United States Envelope Co. v. City of New York: Judgment affirmed, with costs.

In Berkshire Knitting Mills v. City of New York: Judgment affirmed, with costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant corporate taxpayers sought review of the order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court (New York) which affirmed the judgment of the lower court in favor of respondent city, on appellants' action for reimbursement of taxes paid under a law subsequently held to be unconstitutional when applied to interstate business. Respondent sought review of an order which allowed the reimbursement.


OVERVIEW: Appellant corporate taxpayers voluntarily paid taxes to respondent city under a tax law that was later held to be unconstitutional. Appellants' request for reimbursement were refused. The lower court awarded judgment to respondent and dismissed appellants' complaint. The lower appellate court affirmed two judgments, but reversed a third on the basis that the payments had been made in mistake of law and thus should be reimbursed. On appeal, the court affirmed in part and reversed in part. It held that it would not do to permit parties to avoid completed transactions whenever an old rule of law was overturned by the courts or when a new rule of law was made in a case of first impression, which might have affected the transaction if it had preceded it. When there was no statute or authoritative court decision in existence upon the point involved at the time of transaction, then no recovery could be made on a mistake of law. Therefore, reimbursement was not required in any of the cases. The court reversed the lower appellate court on the judgment which it had reversed and reinstated the lower court's summary judgment. The court affirmed the other two summary judgments.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part and reversed in part the order of the lower appellate court, which had affirmed summary judgments by the lower court in favor of respondent city on reimbursement claims by two appellant corporate taxpayers and had reversed the lower court and allowed recovery by another corporate taxpayer. Appellants were not entitled to reimbursement of taxes that were paid voluntarily before the law was held to be unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- voluntary payment of illegal tax -- taxes paid without protest may not be recovered -- section 112-f of Civil Practice Act, which provides that when relief against mistake is sought it shall not be denied merely because mistake is one of law rather than fact, was not intended to permit recovery of taxes paid without protest.

1. Taxes levied under the New York City General Business and Financial Tax Law were illegally levied since the local law has been held to be unconstitutional when applied to interstate business. Taxpayers who paid the taxes without protest and not under duress are not entitled to a refund of the money paid.

2. Voluntary payments of a tax made under a mistake of law cannot be recovered.

3. Section 112-f of the Civil Practice Act, which provides that "When relief against mistake is sought in an action or proceeding or by way of defense or counterclaim, relief shall not be denied merely because the mistake is one of law rather than one of fact", does not require refunding these payments. The statute empowers the court to act only in appropriate cases involving a mistake of law. It does not require that mistakes of law shall be treated in all instances as though they were mistakes of fact but merely removes a technical obstacle against granting relief for mistakes of law. Recovery of taxes paid without protest does not come within the legislative intent in enacting section 112-f of the Civil Practice Act.

People v. McCann


Court of Appeals of New York


3 N.Y.2d 797; 144 N.E.2d 643; 166 N.Y.S.2d 1; 1957 N.Y. LEXIS 906
