Injunction - fraudulent transaction in securities - judgment obtained against defendant for violating Martin Act (General Business Law, art. 23-A) restrained him from engaging in sale of securities - in Court of Appeals defendant argued, in part, that Martin Act was unconstitutional and that injunction was so broad as to deny defendant right to work - judgment properly affirmed.

 

518 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


N. H. Lyons & Co. v. Corsi


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


3 N.Y.2d 60; 143 N.E.2d 392; 163 N.Y.S.2d 677; 1957 N.Y. LEXIS 977; 32 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P70,722


April 11, 1957, Argued  
May 24, 1957, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, operator of men's lodging houses, sued defendant state industrial commissioner, seeking to have declared invalid the commissioner's wage order, which established minimum wages for men in the hotel industry pursuant to N.Y. Lab. Law § 663-a. The operator appealed from a judgment of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed the trial court's order dismissing its complaint.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed the appellate court's decision. N.Y. Lab. Law § 663-a provided that no male 21 years of age or over could be employed in an occupation at less than the minimum standards or rates of wages fixed for women and minors in such occupation under a minimum wage order. While § 663-a was part of article 19 of the New York Labor Law and article 19 was headed "Minimum Wage Standards for Women and Minors," § 663-a had a reasonable relation to the enforcement of the general policy of protecting women and minors in industry. The legislature realized that its objective of providing wage and hour protection for employed women and minors could not be realized if men workers could take the same jobs at wages lower than those set for women and children. The court held that § 663-a was constitutional. It was no longer open to question that the fixing of a minimum wage was within the legislative power and that the bare fact of its exercise was not a denial of due process under the Fifth more than under the Fourteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's decision, which affirmed the trial court's decision dismissing the hotel operator's complaint.


HEADNOTES

Master and servant -- minimum wage -- Labor Law ( § 663-a) provided that no male 21 years of age or over should be employed at less than minimum wage fixed for women and minors; State Industrial Commissioner may enforce mandatory wage order for hotel industry as against "flop houses" although no women are employed there.

1. Section 651 of the Labor Law, contained in article 19 of the Labor Law which provides for minimum wage standards for women and minors, states that it is the public policy of the State that women and minors should receive wages sufficient to provide adequate maintenance and to protect their health. Section 663-a, also contained in article 19, provides, "To effectuate the aim of this article and to protect the minimum wage standards and rates provided for women and minors, no male twenty-one years of age or over shall be employed in an occupation at less than the minimum standards or rates of wages fixed for women and minors in such occupation under a minimum wage order." Section 663-a, although it deals directly with wages of men rather than women, has a reasonable relation to the enforcement of the general policy of protecting women and minors in industry. The State Industrial Commissioner may enforce a mandatory wage order for the hotel industry as against "flop houses" although no women are employed there.

2. Section 663-a of the Labor Law is not unconstitutional as a general minimum wage act for men.

3. Moreover, it is not clear that a general minimum wage act for men would be unconstitutional.

4. The fact that the wage order as applied to men contains no safeguards such as notice, hearing, and decisions of wage boards such as are required by article 19 of the Labor Law in connection with fixing the wages for women and children raises no constitutional question. The Legislature could take notice that the needs of men for maintenance and health are not less than that of women and that the value of the services of men and wages ordinarily paid to men in a given industry are not less than those of women.

5. Claims of invalidity and unreasonableness such as that the order is improperly applied to lodging houses where no women are employed and that cheap lodging houses giving no services beyond providing beds should not be included under the term "hotel" must be raised first by administrative review before the Board of Standards and Appeals (Labor Law, §§ 110, 662).

6. If the question were reached, the court would hold that these lodging houses are properly included under the term "hotel".

7. The possibility that plaintiff will not be able to pay the minimum wage does not render the order unconstitutional.

 

519 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Trio Distributor Corp. v. Albany


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


2 N.Y.2d 690; 143 N.E.2d 329; 163 N.Y.S.2d 585; 1957 N.Y. LEXIS 1010


March 1, 1957, Argued  
May 16, 1957, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment of Appellate Division reversed and that of Trial Term reinstated, with costs in this court and in the Appellate Division.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, distributor and driver, sought review of an order from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York), which reversed the trial court's decision adjudging that defendant city's ordinance pertaining to vending in the public streets and public places was unconstitutional and which dismissed their complaint.


OVERVIEW: The distributor peddled ice cream in city streets by means of trucks. Each truck was uniform in type and equipment and was run by a single employee. The city enacted an ordinance requiring that, in order to conduct such a business, the trucks had to have an attendant on them whose sole duty was to protect and safeguard children from injury. The distributor and driver brought an action for judgment declaring the ordinance unconstitutional and enjoining the enforcement of it against them. On appeal, the court found nothing to indicate that a second employee to maintain a general lookout for children would be more intimately related to the public safety, than the presence of a second employee to prevent the driver from handling prepackaged ice cream products would be related to the public health. The court held that the second employee would have no authority over the children and that the mere presence of two persons upon one vehicle signified nothing by itself. The court determined that the ordinance amounted to prohibiting the conduct of an occupation under the guise of regulation. The court concluded that the ordinance was unconstitutional and reversed the appellate court.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court and reinstated the judgment of the trial court, which held that the city's ordinance was unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- regulation of vendors from vehicles -- ordinance of City of Albany providing that vendors or peddlers from vehicles in streets of city shall be accompanied by attendant whose sole duty shall be to protect and safeguard children from injury and hazards of street vehicle traffic is invalid -- second employee on truck not sufficiently related to public safety.

1. The City of Albany adopted an ordinance providing that "When any person shall vend or peddle from a vehicle in the public streets and places in the City of Albany, and, in the pursuit of such business or activity, children shall collect, assemble or gather about such vehicle for the purpose of making purchases, such person so vending and peddling, and the pursuit of such occupation, shall be accompanied by an attendant whose sole duty and occupation shall be to protect and safeguard the children from injury and the hazards of street vehicle traffic and he shall maintain a constant lookout for approaching vehicles and shall warn the children and guard them from injury." The ordinance is invalid. A second employee on the truck to maintain a lookout for children would not be intimately related to public safety. He would have no rules to follow in securing the children's safety or authority to enforce them.

2. The conduct of an occupation may not be prohibited under the guise of regulation.

3. In making the test of its application dependent on those who may be construed to be peddlers of products attractive to and popular with children, the ordinance is ambiguous.

 

520 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Parker v. Hoefer


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


2 N.Y.2d 612; 142 N.E.2d 194; 162 N.Y.S.2d 13; 1957 N.Y. LEXIS 1067


March 6, 1957, Argued  
April 12, 1957, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff judgment creditor appealed from the order of the Supreme Court, Appellate Division, First Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed the trial court's grant of a motion to strike out the defense of defendant judgment debtor and a motion for judgment on the pleadings in an action to enforce a tort judgment obtained in Vermont against the debtor.


OVERVIEW: The judgment creditor, a resident of Vermont, obtained a final judgment against the judgment debtor, a resident of New York, in an action for alienation of her husband's affections and for criminal conversation. When the judgment could not be collected in Vermont, the creditor brought an action to enforce it in New York. The debtor interposed a defense based upon the provisions of Civil Practice Act art. 2-A relating to actions against the public policy of the state of New York which included actions based upon alleged alienation of affections and criminal conversation. The court noted that Article 2-A did not abolish an action on a judgment rendered in a sister state by a court having proper jurisdiction, and that U.S. Const. art. IV, § 1 provided that a judgment of a state court should have the same credit, validity, and effect in every other court in the United States which it had in the state where it was pronounced. The court concluded that the action was one to collect a debt, and as such was upon an entirely different cause of action from the Vermont tort action which merged in the judgment, and could not be called a suit on an action abolished in New York.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the appellate court affirming the trial court's striking of the defense and entry of judgment on the pleadings in the judgment creditor's action to enforce the judgment obtained in Vermont was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Judgments -- foreign judgment -- actions against public policy -- resident of Vermont may enforce in New York judgment recovered in Vermont against resident of New York in action for alienation of husband's affections and for criminal conversation, although such actions abolished in New York (Civ. Prac. Act, art. 2-A).

1. Plaintiff, a resident of Vermont, is entitled to enforce in New York a judgment which she recovered in Vermont against defendant, a resident of New York, in an action for alienation of her husband's affections and for criminal conversation. Article 2-A of the Civil Practice Act provides that action for "alienation of affections, criminal conversation, seduction and breach of contract to marry" may not be enforced in New York. The present action in New York is a different cause of action from that which merged in the judgment. An action on a judgment rendered in a sister State by a court having jurisdiction of the person and the subject matter does not contravene New York policy.

2. The statute refers to acts "done within this state" (Civ. Prac. Act, § 61-d). Defendant stated in her answer that "many of the acts alleged therein were alleged to have occurred in the State of New York". Defendant has not, by such answer, placed herself within the reach of the statute. Plaintiff has not alleged, nor has defendant admitted, that the Vermont judgment is based on transactions which occurred within New York State, nor is there anything in the record indicating that the acts actually took place in New York State.

 

521 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Estate of Nowakowski


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


2 N.Y.2d 618; 142 N.E.2d 198; 162 N.Y.S.2d 19; 1957 N.Y. LEXIS 1068


January 17, 1957, Argued  
April 12, 1957, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant husband challenged an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court (New York), which affirmed a decree in favor of appellee executors declaring that the husband waived and released his right of election to take against his wife's will.


OVERVIEW: The executors sought a determination as to the validity of the husband's waiver and release of his right of election to take against his deceased wife's will. The surrogate court directed the clerk to take and report the testimony. The clerk ruled on 60 objections and interrogated the witnesses. The surrogate court declared that the waiver and release were valid. The appellate court affirmed. The husband appealed. The court affirmed because the waiver itself and the absence of worthy evidence of overreaching supported the finding that the waiver was not induced by fraud. The court held that the husband waived, by acquiescence, the question of improper delegation of judicial authority by actively participating in the taking of testimony and the original appeal without demurrer.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed, with costs, the declaration in favor of the executors that the husband's waiver and release of the election were valid.


HEADNOTES

Wills -- election to take intestate share -- waiver -- Surrogate properly determined that husband had waived right of election to take against his deceased wife's will.

1. The Surrogate's Court properly determined that a waiver and release of husband's right of election to take against his deceased wife's will was valid.

2. The question whether the execution of the waiver had been induced by any actual or constructive fraud practiced by the decedent was one of fact. The finding that it was not is supported by the record. The sole testimony which relates to the alleged deceit of decedent was adduced by the husband's attorney. The Surrogate was not in error in attaching little weight to this testimony as the husband was an interested witness, and the trier of the facts is free to disbelieve uncontroverted testimony.

3. The Surrogate directed the clerk of the court to take and report the testimony. An alleged improper delegation of authority to the clerk is not reviewed because the husband consented to the procedures pursued by the clerk and the attorneys, not only by appearance at the hearing, but also affirmatively, by conduct during the hearing, and before and after the decision, and on the appeal to the Appellate Division.

4. Statements by the Surrogate as to the burden of proof were surplusage and should be treated as dictum and, in any event, the Appellate Division clearly defined the proof requisite to prove prima facie fraud in transactions between husband and wife.

5. Since the record was submitted to the Surrogate, presiding without a jury, errors in the taking of testimony may be disregarded, particularly where the husband did not object to the Surrogate concerning the manner in which the testimony was taken.

 

522 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Metropolitan Convoy Corp. v. New York


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


2 N.Y.2d 384; 141 N.E.2d 550; 161 N.Y.S.2d 31; 1957 N.Y. LEXIS 1150


January 23, 1957, Argued  
March 8, 1957, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment of Appellate Division reversed, with costs in all courts, and matter remitted to Special Term for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff corporation sought review of the decision from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the first judicial department (New York), which granted judgment in favor of defendant city in the action by the corporation to declare New York, N.Y., ch. 46, tit. E, § E46-2.0 tax law unconstitutional and for a refund of taxes paid.


OVERVIEW: The corporation operated an interstate trucking business out of its principal offices within the city. The city enacted a tax that collected revenue from all commercial vehicles used principally in connection with a business carried on within the city and the corporation paid the tax and filed an action for a refund of the tax and to invalidate the law. The trial court granted judgment in favor of the city and the corporation challenged the decision. On appeal, the court reversed and held that constitutionality of the law was not an issue because the law was not applicable to the corporation. The corporation did not carry on its principal business within the city, it merely made deliveries within the city. All of the truck routes driven by the corporation started in other cities and some ended in the city. The mere fact of making final delivery of some goods in the city did not constitute the carrying on of the corporations business within the city and, therefore, the tax was not applicable to the corporation.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment in favor of the city in the action by the corporation to invalidate a tax law and obtain a refund of taxes paid.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- motor vehicle use tax -- tax imposed by local law of City of New York (Administrative Code of City of New York, ch. 46, tit. E, § E46-2.0) on use of motor vehicles not applicable to trucks and trailers of plaintiff which operates as authorized interstate motor common carrier -- vehicles not "used principally in connection with a business carried on within the city" (Administrative Code, § E46-1.0).

1. Pursuant to an enabling act (L. 1947, ch. 278), the City of New York adopted a local law (Administrative Code of City of New York, ch. 46, tit. E, § E46-2.0) imposing a tax on the use of motor vehicles, and defined "Use" as "Any use of a motor vehicle upon the public highways or streets of the city" and "Commercial vehicle" as "motor vehicles * * * used principally in connection with a business carried on within the city" (Administrative Code, § E46-1.0). The tax imposed by the local law is not applicable to plaintiff, which operates as an authorized interstate motor common carrier. Plaintiff's trucks and trailers are not "used principally in connection with a business carried on within the city". Since under the language of the local law the tax does not apply, no constitutional question is reached.

2. Plaintiff is a Pennsylvania corporation. Its executive office and its corporate records are in New York City, and its corporate meetings are held there. Its fleet of trucks and trailers is garaged at and dispatched from a site in Pennsylvania. It transports products of a manufacturer of automobiles and parts to the manufacturer's dealers in New York, New Jersey and Pennsylvania. There are no "return loads". In summer, plaintiff picks up automobiles in Buffalo, brought there by Great Lakes steamships. During the winter the automobiles are picked up by plaintiff at Detroit. During these seasons plaintiff has small offices at Buffalo and Detroit from which bills are sent for payment at the New York office. Plaintiff has 50 to 75 consignees in New York City, but no shipper-customers there. About 65% of its income is received from delivery of automobiles to dealers in New York City, but its transportation charges are based on mileage and 95% or more of its total mileage is outside New York City. On a weekday, 20 to 25 of plaintiff's 120 automobile-carrying tractors enter New York City. Plaintiff's operations are such that its trucks and trailers are not "used principally in connection with a business carried on within the city".

3. The legislative intent must have been to tax trucks of a business which is itself carried on principally in New York City. New York City is not the place where plaintiff's interstate business is "principally" carried on.

 

523 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Lerner v. Casey


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


2 N.Y.2d 355; 141 N.E.2d 533; 161 N.Y.S.2d 7; 1957 N.Y. LEXIS 1210


November 28, 1956, Argued  
February 28, 1957, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner city employee challenged an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York) that affirmed an order of the supreme court that granted a motion by respondent New York City Transit Authority (authority) to dismiss the employee's petition that alleged wrongful termination.


OVERVIEW: The employee was discharged from his position of subway conductor by the authority on the ground that a reasonable basis existed for the belief that, because of his doubtful trust and reliability, his employment in the position of conductor endangered the security or defense of the nation and the state. The employee had refused to answer a question as to whether he was a member of the Communist party. The court affirmed and held that: (1) the authority was a board, body, or commission of the state or of any civil division thereof within the intendment of the Security Risk Law (Law), L. 1951, ch. 233, and the city commission of investigation had jurisdiction to conduct the inquiry; (2) the authority performed a function necessary to the security or defense of the nation and the state and thus it had been properly denominated a security agency under the Law; (3) the Law authorized the authority to suspend and discharge the employee merely upon a showing that the employee refused to answer whether he was a member of the Communist party and then stated that to answer might incriminate him within the meaning of the U.S. Constitution; and (4) the Law was constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the appellate division that affirmed the supreme court's dismissal of the employee's wrongful termination action, with costs.


HEADNOTES

Civil service -- removal -- subway conductor who refused to answer questions as to whether he was Communist party member, because to do so might tend to incriminate him, was properly discharged as security risk by New York City Transit Authority pursuant to State Security Risk Law (L. 1951, ch 233).

1. At a hearing at the office of the commissioner of investigation of the City of New York, petitioner was asked whether he was then or had been a member of the Communist party, and he refused to answer on the ground that to do so might tend to incriminate him and that he was entitled to claim the privilege against self incrimination afforded him under the Fifth Amendment to the United States Constitution. Petitioner has been properly discharged by the New York City Transit Authority upon the ground that a reasonable basis exists for the belief that, because of his doubtful trust and reliability, his employment in the position of subway conductor endangered the security or defense of the nation and the State.

2. The Security Risk Law (L. 1951, ch. 233, §§ 4, 5, 7) provides that a finding of disqualification and a determination of suspension, removal or transfer may be based upon evidence of previous conduct, which may include but shall not be limited to, membership in any organization or group found by the State Civil Service Commission to be subversive. The State Civil Service Commission listed the Communist party of the United States and the State of New York as subversive within the meaning of the Security Risk Law.

3. The New York City Transit Authority is a "board, body or commission of the state or of any civil division thereof" within the intendment of the provision of the Security Risk Law (§ 5) which authorized petitioner's discharge.

4. The city's interest in the transit system is not only that of lessor of the physical properties -- an interest which petitioner argues does not justify its interrogation of employees of the Transit Authority. Although it has leased the rapid transit facilities to the Transit Authority, the city is required to pay the cost of the capital improvements, and so has a proprietary interest in preserving and protecting those facilities from sabotage or destruction.

5. Moreover, the very existence of the city is dependent on the safe and uninterrupted operation of the transit system. An investigation to determine whether employees of the transit system are members of subversive organizations or are of doubtful trust and reliability is "in the best interests of the city" (New York City Charter, § 803, subd. 2). That being so, the investigation was properly initiated by the commissioner of investigation.

6. The Public Authorities Law confers upon the Transit Authority the right to avail itself of the services of the officers and agencies of the city government (see Public Authorities Law, § 1803, subd. 3, par. b). The authority must be said to have exercised its right to make use of the services of the commissioner of investigation and, in effect, to have authorized the commission to conduct an investigation on its behalf when it directed the petitioner to appear before the commissioner to answer questions designed to ascertain whether he was of doubtful trust or reliability.

7. The Transit Authority was properly designated a security agency (§ 2). The Transit Authority performs a function necessary to the security or defense of the nation and the State.

8. The refusal to reply to the question as to Communist party membership is "evidence" of "doubtful trust and reliability". Petitioner was not discharged on the ground that he was a Communist party member. He was discharged for having created a doubt by declining to answer whether he was or was not a member. By refusing to answer the question asked by his employer whether he was then a member of the Communist party, petitioner would have given evidence of his own untrustworthiness and unreliability. The situation is not changed when he goes further and says that he refused to answer because to do so might tend to incriminate him.

9. The wisdom of the Legislature in extending the Security Risk Law beyond the period of the Korean War has been confirmed by world events transpiring since the Korean truce, and it may not be said that the Security Risk Law is unconstitutional because an emergency no longer existed in 1954 when petitioner was dismissed.

10. Petitioner's position of subway conductor has a rational connection with the national security.

11. The Security Risk Law, as construed and applied to petitioner, is not unconstitutional ( Slochower v. Board of Educ., 350 U.S. 551, distinguished).

12. Petitioner's argument that he asserted his constitutional privilege with respect to a Federal crime and as a part of his national citizenship so that his dismissal abridged his privileges and immunities as a citizen of the United States is untenable in this situation, where he uses the privilege to thwart his employer in ascertaining whether or not he is a member of a criminal conspiracy.

 

524 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Saperstein


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


2 N.Y.2d 210; 140 N.E.2d 252; 159 N.Y.S.2d 160; 1957 N.Y. LEXIS 1269


October 11, 1956, Argued  
January 10, 1957, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed the trial court's conviction of defendant for five counts of criminal contempt, under N.Y. Penal Law § 600, subject only to a remission directed in a separate case.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of five counts of criminal contempt under N.Y. Penal Law § 600 because he refused, as a witness under subpoena before a grand jury, to state definitely who were the participants in five telephone conversations which had been wire-tapped and the recordings of which were read to defendant before the grand jury. Defendant had been one of the speakers, and in each case the other speaker was well known to defendant. The court affirmed, rejecting defendant's contention that he had been prevented from answering by the Federal Communications Act (Act). There was no support for defendant's contention that the Act granted any kind of privilege to a telephone talker against divulgence by the person on the other end of the telephone of the contents of the talks. Further, because defendant pretended that he did not remember the voices and the conversations, it was proper to show the trial jury that defendant must have recognized the voices because he had had numerous and important transactions with those persons. The state established to the satisfaction of the trial jury that defendant did remember the conversations.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's conviction of defendant for criminal contempt.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- criminal contempt -- trial jury justified in finding that defendant's failure to answer questions put to him by Grand Jury as to identity of one conversing with him in wire-tapped telephone conversations amounted to criminal contempt.

1. Defendant, an insurance broker, was indicted for five crimes of criminal contempt as the result of the Grand Jury's inquiry into alleged violations of the Insurance Law and of the Penal Law. The trial jury's verdict of guilt amounted to findings that defendant, on his appearances as a witness under subpoena before the Grand Jury on March 11 and 15, 1954, had been five times guilty of contumacious refusals to answer legal and proper questions, in failing to state definitely who were the participants in five wire-tapped telephone conversations, the recordings of which were read to defendant before the Grand Jury. On the trial, it was proved that in each of those wire-tapped telephone conversations defendant had been one of the speakers; that the other speaker and his voice were well known to defendant, and that each such conversation had to do with defendant's business dealings. The trial jury was justified in finding that defendant's failure to answer those questions and his evasiveness when he did answer amounted to criminal contempts.

2. The recordings made of the telephone conversations, which had been intercepted by wire tappings authorized by court orders pursuant to section 813-a of the Code of Criminal Procedure, were admissible in evidence. Such admission did not violate section 605 of the Federal Communications Act (U. S. Code, tit. 47, § 605), which provides that no person, who is not authorized by the sender, shall divulge an intercepted communication to any person.

3. Section 813-a of the Code of Criminal Procedure is constitutional.

4. The divulgence asked of defendant before the Grand Jury did not come within the language of the Federal Communications Act (§ 605), and such divulgence would not have been a voluntary one but under compulsion of a subpoena. Section 605 is intended to prevent or punish intrusion into the telephone conversation of others, but the section does not grant any kind of privilege to a talker on a telephone against divulgence of the contents of the talks by the person on the other end of the telephone wire.

5. The giving of the testimony here would not be a crime under the New York Penal Law ( § 1423, subd. 6) which forbids wire tapping.

6. It was not prejudicial error to let the trial jury hear all of defendant's examination before the Grand Jury. Before the Grand Jury, defendant swore that his failure to state whether he had taken part in such telephone conversations was because he could not recall the conversations. The prosecution showed, by some parts of defendant's Grand Jury testimony, that defendant must have recognized the voices because he had had numerous and important transactions with those persons.

7. No prejudice to defendant could have resulted from his pre-immunity Grand Jury testimony on March 2, 1954, which was put before the trial jury, since such March 2 testimony was preliminary and background testimony as to the place, personnel and methods of operation of defendant's insurance brokerage business, and it had no tendency in itself to convict defendant of contempt. The alleged contempts themselves were all committed on March 11 and March 15, after an effective grant of immunity to defendant.

8. Thirteen of the grand jurors, who voted for the true bill, were present on March 11 and 15, 1954 -- the two days on which the recordings were read to defendant and on which he failed to identify the voices -- and on May 13, 1954, the day the indictment was voted. Fewer than 12 of those jurors who indicted defendant were in attendance on March 2 and on April 20, on which days testimony of background and explanatory material was heard by the Grand Jury. Enough testimony was heard on March 11 and 15 to justify the indictment, and more than 12 of the voting jurors heard all of the testimony on those two days.

9. Defendant was guilty of five contempts because his contumacious conduct had to do with five separate telephone talks.

10. Defendant's rights were not invaded where the prosecutor presented to defense counsel, prior to the trial, copies of all the investigation reports which he had as to the special jurors comprising the "blue ribbon" trial jury and, accordingly, defendant's counsel had the same information that the prosecutor had, well before the trial.

 

525 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Somers v. Covey


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


2 N.Y.2d 250; 140 N.E.2d 277; 159 N.Y.S.2d 196; 1957 N.Y. LEXIS 1274


October 19, 1956, Argued  
January 10, 1957, Decided



DISPOSITION: Upon reargument: Order affirmed. Question certified answered in the affirmative.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a committee representing an incompetent, sought reargument after the United States Supreme Court reversed the prior decision of the court, which affirmed an appellate court decision that affirmed a trial court judgment to respondent town, allowing the town to foreclose a tax lien on property owned by the incompetent pursuant to N.Y. Tax Law § 65 et seq.


OVERVIEW: The committee claimed that the incompetent landowner was not aware of the town's claim for unpaid taxes and the proceedings to enforce its tax lien against the incompetent's property and that enforcement of the tax lien was a violation of due process. The town claimed that N.Y. Tax Law § 165-h(7) provided the exclusive remedy to the committee and the committee, having failed to elect that remedy, was not entitled to challenge the presumption that the proceedings were valid. The court affirmed the appellate court's order that affirmed the trial court's judgment in favor of the town and held that compliance with § 165-h(7) was the exclusive remedy of the committee to challenge the action taken by the town and the committee had not chosen to follow that procedure, so it waived its right to do so after the time limitations of § 165-h(7) had expired. The court further held that § 165-h(7) provided adequate due process when the committee knew that the proceedings had been initiated by the town to enforce its tax lien, even though the committee was representing an incompetent person.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's order that affirmed the trial court's judgment for the town, which foreclosed the town's tax lien on the property of the incompetent, who was represented by the committee.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- tax titles -- incompetent persons -- where deed of property of incompetent has been delivered and recorded in in rem tax foreclosure proceeding, attack on sufficiency of notice prescribed by Tax Law ( §§ 165-a, 165-b) may be made only in action under Tax Law ( § 165-h, subd. 7) within statutory two-year period; motion by committee of incompetent to open default in foreclosure proceeding properly denied.

1. In an action in rem by a town to foreclose a tax lien, the committee of the incompetent owner moved, about one year after a deed of the property was delivered to the town, to open the owner's default and to vacate the judgment of foreclosure. The Court of Appeals affirmed the order of the Appellate Division which affirmed an order of the County Court denying the committee's motion, and granted a motion by the committee to amend the remittitur and recited that, on the appeal, there was presented and necessarily passed upon a question under the United States Constitution, viz., whether the taking of the property by the town was, "on this record, a deprivation of due process and equal protection of the laws under the Fourteenth Amendment" (308 N. Y. 798, 941). The Court of Appeals meant by the amendment that the State may properly impose a time limit in which a defendant could answer or redeem, and that such a time limit does not constitute a violation of the Fourteenth Amendment in a case such as this where the incompetent has a period of two years after the recording of the deed in which to bring an action to set it aside (Tax Law, § 165-h, subd. 7), a committee had been appointed 20 months before the expiration of such period, and the committee had been advised by the opinion of the County Court well before the expiration of said two-year period that an action to set aside the deed, and not a motion to vacate the default, was the proper remedy. Had such amendment of the remittitur stated the precise ground of the decision of the Court of Appeals, the United States Supreme Court would not have reversed the determination of the Court of Appeals, inasmuch as the Supreme Court stated that "no constitutional question could have been reached if the Court of Appeals had been of the opinion that the appellant [committee] had pursued the wrong remedy" ( Covey v. Town of Somers, 351 U.S. 141, 144). Accordingly, upon reargument following remand by the United States Supreme Court to the Court of Appeals, the order of the Appellate Division should be affirmed.

2. The court has not held that the notice, prescribed by sections 165-a and 165-b of the Tax Law to the effect that, if the amount of the unpaid tax lien, together with interest and penalties, was not paid within seven weeks or an answer interposed within 20 days after the last date for redemption, any person having the right to redeem or answer would be forever foreclosed of all his interest and equity of redemption in the affected property, is sufficient to satisfy the demands of due process in the case of a known incompetent. Where, however, a deed has been recorded and delivered, as here, an attack on the sufficiency of the notice may be made only in an action under the Tax Law ( § 165-h, subd. 7).

Murtha v. Monaghan


Court of Appeals of New York


2 N.Y.2d 819; 140 N.E.2d 746; 159 N.Y.S.2d 834; 1957 N.Y. LEXIS 1290


Argued November 15, 1956  
January 10, 1957. - Decided


HEADNOTES

Horse racing - licenses - prohibition against licensing of public employees - application of schoolteacher whose annual salary was $6,700 for license to act as mutuel clerk at harness race track was rejected by State Harness Racing Commission on ground that petitioner was public employee earning more than $5,000 per annum and thus barred by law - Pari-Mutuel Revenue Law (§ 63, subd. 1; added by L. 1954, ch. 514) provided that no public employee should hold employment with firm licensed to conduct pari-mutuel racing - subdivision 6 of such section (added by L. 1954, ch. 515) provided that prohibition against employment by such firm should not apply to public employee earning less than $5,000 per year if local governing board authorized such employment - contention by petitioner that statutes were unconstitutional - Appellate Division dismissed petition for order diecting that license be issued - order affirmed.

 

527 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Decina


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


2 N.Y.2d 133; 138 N.E.2d 799; 157 N.Y.S.2d 558; 1956 N.Y. LEXIS 631; 63 A.L.R.2d 970


October 4, 1956, Argued  
November 29, 1956, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Prosecution appealed a judgment of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court of New York, in the Fourth Judicial Department, which reversed a judgment against criminal defendant for violating N.Y. Penal Law § 1053-a.


OVERVIEW: Criminal defendant struck and killed a number of children after allegedly suffering a seizure while driving. Defendant was placed under arrest and taken to a hospital for treatment. While at the hospital, defendant related his medical history to a physician who diagnosed defendant as suffering from epilepsy. Defendant was charged with violating N.Y. Penal Law § 1053-a. At trial, the physician testified as to his conversation with defendant. Defendant argued that his actions were not sufficiently culpable to violate § 1053-a and that the physician's testimony should have been inadmissible. The court first held that defendant's conduct arguably fell within the statute's requirement that defendant exhibit a disregard for the consequences that would ensue from his actions. The court then held that, as the physician diagnosed defendant and defendant was given no reason to believe their communication was not confidential, the conversation was barred under N.Y. Civ. Prac. Act § 352.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the reversal of the judgment against criminal defendant in fatal automobile accident, because physician's testimony at trial was inadmissible.


HEADNOTES

Crimes -- criminal negligence in operation of vehicle -- indictment which stated that defendant, knowing that he was subject to epileptic seizures, was culpably negligent in consciously operating automobile and while so doing suffered seizure causing his automobile to drive over sidewalk and kill 4 persons (Penal Law, § 1053-a), sufficient -- communications between defendant and hospital doctor, who took his medical history while other hospital doctors treated him, privileged under Civil Practice Act ( § 352) -- presence in hospital room of police guard within hearing of communications did not destroy such privilege; judgment of conviction improper for erroneous admission of communications.

1. An indictment which stated that defendant, knowing that he was subject to epileptic attacks rendering him likely to lose consciousness, was culpably negligent in that he consciously operated his automobile and while so doing suffered an epileptic attack causing his automobile to drive over a sidewalk, causing the death of 4 persons (Penal Law, § 1053-a), is sufficient.

2. Defendant, after he was arrested, was sent to a county hospital in custody of policemen, a police guard was stationed in the doorway of his room, and a staff doctor visited defendant. Although the doctor testified that he personally did not treat defendant, he admitted that other doctors in the hospital did treat him for Jacksonian epilepsy, and he admits he made that diagnosis. A physician-patient relationship existed between the doctor and defendant even though there was a division of duties among the staff of the hospital.

3. The communications made to the doctor by defendant relating to his epileptic seizure and his past medical history were necessary for the doctor to act in his professional capacity, and were therefore privileged under section 352 of the Civil Practice Act.

4. The presence of the police guard in the doorway of the hospital room where he could overhear the conversation between the doctor and defendant did not destroy the privilege arising under section 352 of the Civil Practice Act. Accordingly, defendant's communications to the doctor should not have been admitted in evidence and the judgment of conviction was properly reversed.

5. Defendant cannot raise the question that the hospital record was improperly received in evidence before the Grand Jury, and that the indictment should therefore be dismissed, where he made no motion for an inspection of the minutes of the Grand Jury and such minutes are not part of the present record and the court is not in a position to know what other proper evidence may have been adduced as a basis for the indictment.

 

528 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Smith v. Smith


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


2 N.Y.2d 120; 138 N.E.2d 790; 157 N.Y.S.2d 546; 1956 N.Y. LEXIS 648


October 1, 1956, Argued  
November 16, 1956, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county sought relief from a decision by the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York) affirming an order issued by the trial court denying its motion to vacate an order directing it to pay for the transcript of appellee wife's matrimonial case. The wife was granted such relief upon receiving permission to prosecute her appeal as a poor person under N.Y. Civ. Prac. Act §§ 199, 1493.


OVERVIEW: When the wife presented her order to the county, the county resisted payment and asserted that the trial court lacked jurisdiction to issue the order, the wife did not statutorily qualify as poor, and the wife failed to properly notice the county about her application for such relief. On appeal, the court affirmed the lower courts' decisions. It found that the appellate court did not abuse its discretion in determining that the wife was poor within the statutory prerequisites, even though it did not consider the real property jointly owned with her husband as part of her worth, because the statute contemplated valuation of property actually and presently available. The fact that her action was tried by a referee was immaterial. The trial court was authorized to grant her requested relief. When judgment was entered on the referee's report and the trial court lost jurisdiction, the wife properly applied to the appellate court for permission to appeal as a poor person. The statute was silent about prior notice to the county. But, the issue of notice did not need to be considered because the county moved to vacate and had a hearing on the merits that constituted due process.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's decision affirming the trial court's order.


HEADNOTES

Appeal -- poor persons -- stenographer's fees -- Special Term in contested marital action properly directed, under Civil Practice Act ( § 1493), that county furnish wife, whom Appellate Division permitted to prosecute appeal as poor person, transcript of testimony of trial in said action -- omission from section 1493 of requirement of prior notice to county of wife's application does not render it unconstitutional.

1. A husband's complaint for separation and his wife's counterclaim for similar relief were dismissed, and husband and wife appealed. The Appellate Division permitted her to prosecute the appeal as a poor person. Thereafter, Special Term, pursuant to section 1493 of the Civil Practice Act, properly directed that the clerk of the county furnish to the wife an original and a copy of the transcript of the testimony of the trial, and that the statutory fees of the official stenographer for preparing the same be paid by the county treasurer.

2. The Appellate Division had power to permit the wife to prosecute her appeal as a poor person where she showed that she was not worth $ 300 "in cash or available property" (Civ. Prac. Act, § 199; Rules Civ. Prac., rule 35). The Appellate Division correctly treated the real property jointly owned by the wife and the husband as not "available" within the meaning of the statute, for the statutory phrase "available property" contemplates property actually and presently available. In any event, the granting of the relief rested in the court's sound discretion.

3. Section 1493 of the Civil Practice Act authorizes either the trial judge or the court to grant the relief demanded, and the court here was the Supreme Court held in Sullivan County, notwithstanding that the marital action was tried before an Official Referee.

4. When judgment was entered on the Official Referee's report, his jurisdiction terminated and the wife properly applied to the Appellate Division for permission to appeal as a poor person.

5. The omission from section 1493 of a requirement of prior notice to the county does not render it constitutionally invalid. The issuance of a certificate to an official stenographer, as provided in section 1493, does not actually prejudice the county since the county's public moneys are not actually taken until the certificate is paid. Whether or not notice is to be implied need not be considered since the county moved to vacate that part of the order directing it to pay the costs of the minutes and had a hearing on the merits.

People v. Faxlanger


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]
