Accordingly, defendant was properly convicted of unlawfully operating a filling station in that he willfully failed to procure a license from said department pursuant to section 283-a.

 

530 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Lincoln Bldg. Associates v. Barr


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


1 N.Y.2d 413; 135 N.E.2d 801; 153 N.Y.S.2d 633; 1956 N.Y. LEXIS 739


May 22, 1956, Argued  
July 11, 1956, Decided



DISPOSITION: In each proceeding: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Municipal Court of the City of New York, Borough of Manhattan, Ninth District (New York) dismissed appellant landlord's claim that the Business Rent Law, 1955 N.Y. Laws 701, was unconstitutional in the landlord's actions against appellee tenants. The landlord challenged the orders.


OVERVIEW: The business rent control statute was passed during World War II when nonessential civilian construction of business space stopped. Because of increasing demands for that space, business tenants were in a hopeless bargaining position with their landlords. The statute was reenacted for several years up through 1955. The court affirmed the order finding that the statute was constitutional. There was a reasonable basis for the legislature to find that the continuance of the rent controls was justified. The resolution of the question as to the existence of an emergency and a need for rent control was properly for the legislature. The court could not substitute its own evaluation of the weight of conflicting proof as to that question for that of the legislature.


OUTCOME: The orders dismissing the landlord's action against its tenants, and holding that a statute providing for rent control for office space was constitutional, was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- rent regulation -- office space -- 1955 re-enactment of Business Rent Law (L. 1955, ch. 701), insofar as it affects office space, constitutional where debatable question raised by evidence as to existence of emergency and need for rent control; rational basis existed for legislative finding of emergency.

The 1955 re-enactment of the Business Rent Law (L. 1955, ch. 701), insofar as it affects office space only, is deemed constitutional where the representatives of the landlord, by their own evidence at this trial in summary proceedings to remove a tenant from real property and before the legislative commission, did no more than raise a conflict of testimony and opinion -- a debatable question as to the existence of an emergency and the need for rent control as to office space. The resolution of that question was properly for the Legislature. There was a rational basis for the legislative finding of an emergency, and no court may substitute therefor its own evaluation of the weight of such conflicting proof.

 

531 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Spielvogel v. Ford


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


1 N.Y.2d 558; 136 N.E.2d 856; 154 N.Y.S.2d 889; 1956 N.Y. LEXIS 757


May 23, 1956, Argued  
July 11, 1956, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner electrician challenged the order of the Supreme Court at Special Term in New York County (New York), which denied his request for an order, setting aside respondent Commissioner of the Department of Water Supply, Gas and Electricity of the City of New York's (commissioner's) suspension of his electrician's license as a master electrician. The electrician argued that New York City, N.Y., Admin. Code § B30-16.0 was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The electrician was licensed as a motion picture operator and as a master electrician by the commissioner. He admitted that he had been employed for as a motion picture operator, but claimed that he was engaged as a master electrician during the hours when he was not engaged as a projectionist. The commissioner suspended his master electrician license until he demonstrated that he was not principally engaged in other business in violation of § B30-16.0. The electrician argued that the part of § B30-16.0, which enabled the commissioner to suspend or revoke a license on the ground of being principally engaged in business other than that of a master electrician, was violative of N.Y. Const. art. I, §§ 6, 1 and also U.S. Const. amend. XIV, § 1. The court affirmed the finding that § B30-16.0 was constitutional and that the electrician's master electrician license was properly suspended for being engaged in another business. The court held that the city had a reasonable basis upon which to act to suspend the license under § B30-16.0 and that § B30-16.0 was not arbitrary and confiscatory. The court found that supervision and responsibility was a reasonable basis for the legislation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of the electricians' request to have the commissioner's suspension of his master electrician's license set aside. The court held that the licensing procedure in the New York City, New York Administrative Code was constitutional.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- licenses -- master electrician -- Administrative Code of City of New York (§ B30-16.0), which permits city commissioner to suspend or revoke license of master electrician on ground that he is principally engaged in other business, is constitutional.

1. Petitioner was licensed as a motion picture operator and also as a master electrician by respondent New York City Commissioner of the Department of Water Supply, Gas and Electricity. Petitioner admitted that he had been employed for three nights a week as a motion picture operator at a theatre; and at a hearing held by the department's license board stated that he was a "projectionist", that he had his name on a window of a hardware store and that the store owner took orders for his services as an electrician. The board recommended to the commissioner that petitioner's master electrician's license be suspended, and respondent commissioner notified petitioner of the resolution of said board. Petitioner's motion for an order to annul the determination of respondent and to direct him to issue a master electrician's license to petitioner was properly denied.

2. The portion of the Administrative Code of the City of New York (§ B30-16.0), which enables the commissioner to suspend or revoke a license, after a hearing before said board, on the ground of the licensee's "being principally engaged in * * * business" other than that of a master electrician, is constitutional.

3. The City Council had a reasonable basis on which to enact section B30-16.0 of the Administrative Code, in view of the inherently dangerous nature of electricity.

4. There is a reasonable basis for requiring that master electricians be not principally occupied in any other business. Under the Administrative Code, the master electrician is the person who has "the full responsibility" for the determination of the manner in which the work is done, the materials used, and the selection, supervision and control of employees (§ B30-4.0, subd. 54) and no firm, partnership or corporation may have more than one master electrician in its employ (§ B30-4.0, subd. 55). If a person who is a master electrician is not principally engaged in the electrical business but is principally engaged in some other endeavor, the degree of responsibility, which is forced upon him to adhere to the legislative enactments and to the board's regulations and to prevent him from "cutting corners" in order to save time and money, is greatly reduced. The power which the board has over such a person of revoking or suspending his master electrician's license is not as certain a method of control as it is over those who are principally engaged in the business, for those principally engaged in the business must protect their business interest by protecting their master electrician's license, or their business will no longer exist by reason of the provisions of the Administrative Code and of the rules thereunder.

 

532 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Diocese of Rochester v. Planning Bd. of Brighton


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


1 N.Y.2d 508; 136 N.E.2d 827; 154 N.Y.S.2d 849; 1956 N.Y. LEXIS 751


April 23, 1956, Argued  
July 11, 1956, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order of the Appellate Division and that of Special Term reversed, the determinations of the planning board, the board of appeals and the town board of Town of Brighton annulled, as of June 8, 1956, and the matter remitted to those bodies for further proceedings not inconsistent with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner a diocese appealed an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Fourth Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed an order entered in an N.Y. C.P.L.R. 78 proceeding in the trial court and dismissed the petition because no triable issue of fact had been raised and affirming respondent town planning board's decision to deny the diocese a permit to build a church and school in a residential area.


OVERVIEW: The diocese alleged that the town planning board's decision was arbitrary and unreasonable and an abuse of discretion and that an ordinance and decision violated the constitution. The trial and appeals courts held that the diocese could not attack the constitutionality of the ordinance and that they could not substitute their judgment for that of the town as long as there was some basis for the determination. The court reversed and held that the diocese could challenge the constitutionality of the way the ordinance had been construed and applied. The court examined the town's reasons, which included that no church or school could be located in a built-up area, that property values would drop, and that potential tax revenue would be lost, but found that none justified denial of the permit. The ordinance was enacted to promote the public health, safety, morals, and the general welfare. The court found that church and school would further public morals and general welfare and would not jeopardize the public health or the public peace or safety. The court annulled the town's decision because it bore no substantial relation to the ordinance's purposes and was arbitrary and unreasonable.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the orders of the trial and appeals courts, annulled the determinations of the town's planning board, and remanded the matter to the for further proceedings.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations -- zoning -- church, school and accessory uses -- church may not be excluded from residential district under zoning ordinance -- on record, decisions of respondent town bodies denying permit to construct church, school and accessory uses in residential district, bear no substantial relation to promotion of public health, safety, morals or general welfare, are arbitrary and should be annulled.

1. A zoning ordinance of the Town of Brighton provided that in Class A of the residential districts a religious building and accessory uses were permitted "if approved by the Planning Board". Petitioner Diocese of Rochester entered into a contract to purchase from copetitioner certain property located in a Class A residential district, and thereafter applied to the town planning board and to the town board of appeals for a permit to construct a Roman Catholic church and school, a meeting room, kindergarten, small games, open field and hard-top play areas, and a parking lot to accommodate 144 cars. Respondent town planning board denied petitioner's application and respondent town board of appeals denied petitioner's application for a variance. Thereafter, respondent town board affirmed the determinations of the planning board and the board of appeals. The determinations of all three said town bodies should be annulled and the matter remitted to them.

2. In general, a party cannot, in the same proceeding, rely upon a statute or retain benefits thereunder and attack its constitutionality, but even if the zoning ordinance here involved be deemed valid and constitutional for the purposes of this proceeding, the petitioners may attack the constitutionality of the decisions of the planning board, the board of appeals and the town board here involved, as distinguished from the constitutionality of the ordinance itself. An ordinance, constitutional on its face, may be construed and applied in an unconstitutional manner; and, if an ordinance, when construed and applied to a particular piece of property and a particular set of facts, is so arbitrary and unreasonable as to result in an invasion of property rights, the action of the boards thereunder will be invalidated.

3. Since this petition alleged that petitioners had been deprived of their property without due process of law and that they have been deprived of their rights to the free exercise of religion and to impart and receive religious instruction and education, petitioners need not allege or prove discrimination to establish their claim that the ordinance has been construed and applied so as to violate such constitutional rights.

4. A zoning ordinance may not wholly exclude a church or synagogue from a residential district. The planning board determined, in effect, that the proposed church and school could be built only in an outlying area.

5. An adverse effect upon property values is an unauthorized standard to apply in determining whether to grant this permit.

6. The State Constitution (art. XVI, § 1) and the Tax Law ( § 4, subd. 6) provide that real and personal property used exclusively for religious purposes shall be exempt from taxation. Accordingly, the permit could not be denied because of a loss of tax revenues.

7. Noises and other inconveniences are insufficient grounds to deny a permit to a church.

8. The accessory uses proposed by petitioner are allowed by the ordinance and are within the scope of a church's activities.

9. Since, on the record, the decisions of the planning board, the board of appeals and the town board bear no substantial relation to the promotion of public health, safety, morals or general welfare of the community, they are arbitrary and unreasonable and should be annulled.

 

533 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Easley v. New York State Thruway Authority


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


1 N.Y.2d 374; 135 N.E.2d 572; 153 N.Y.S.2d 28; 1956 N.Y. LEXIS 829


March 16, 1956, Argued  
June 7, 1956, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff individual brought a negligence action against defendant thruway authority. The Supreme Court of Albany County (New York) granted the thruway authority's motion to set aside the service of the summons and complaint and to strike out the complaint. The individual challenged the judgment.


OVERVIEW: The thruway authority performed part of the work of the state government in that it built and maintained a highway. The court held that the thruway authority was an arm or agency of the state. The court held that the New York Constitution did not so limit the jurisdiction of the court of claims such that the legislature could not validly confer on that court jurisdiction over claims that were not asserted against the state itself but against state agencies. Pursuant to N.Y. Pub. Auth. Law § 361-b, the legislature had authority to confer on the court of claims authority to hear claims against such agencies of the state as the thruway authority.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of the thruway authority.


HEADNOTES

State -- Court of Claims -- jurisdiction -- claims against State Thruway Authority -- section 361-b of Public Authorities Law, which confers exclusive jurisdiction on Court of Claims to hear and determine claims against State Thruway Authority for alleged torts or breaches of contract, is constitutional (N. Y. Const., art. VI, § 23).

1. Section 361-b of the Public Authorities Law, enacted in 1954, which confers exclusive jurisdiction on the Court of Claims to hear and determine all claims against the New York State Thruway Authority for alleged torts or breaches of contract, is constitutional. Section 23 of article VI of the State Constitution, approved by the People in 1949 and effective January, 1950, continues the Court of Claims as a court of record, fixes the number, method of appointment, terms, qualifications, etc., of the judges and provides that "The court shall have jurisdiction to hear and determine claims against the state or by the state against the claimant or between conflicting claimants as the legislature may provide." There is nothing in the language or history of section 23 of article VI to support a construction that the Legislature could not validly confer on the Court of Claims jurisdiction over claims that are asserted, not against the State itself, but against the State Thruway Authority, which is an arm or agency of the State.

2. Accordingly, the summons was properly set aside and the complaint dismissed in an action brought in the Supreme Court to recover damages by reason of the negligence of defendant State Thruway Authority.

 

534 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Beary v. English


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


1 N.Y.2d 338; 135 N.E.2d 313; 152 N.Y.S.2d 865; 1956 N.Y. LEXIS 850


May 31, 1956, Argued  
May 31, 1956, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner candidate challenged an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed an order of the trial court, which dismissed a proceeding by the candidate for an order pursuant to N.Y. Elec. Law § 330 in her action challenging the sufficiency of the form and contents of the Democratic Party primary ballot proposed by the Nassau County Board of Elections.


OVERVIEW: The candidate challenged for insufficiency the form and contents of the Democratic Party primary ballot proposed by the Board. The candidate sought to have the trial court declare N.Y. Elec. Law § 104 unconstitutional if the effect of the amendment of that section made by 1955 N.Y. Laws 405 was to group together on the ballot candidates running on a single petition for Member of State Committee so that they did not appear on said ballot separated by sex. The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court dismissing the candidate's petition. The court held that while N.Y. Elec. Law § 11 required that the primary ballot list the candidates for party position separately by sexes, the court did not read it as prescribing that the listing as to sex was to have been in any particular form or method, so long as the sex of the candidate for party position was clearly and plainly indicated. The court held that the party primary ballot in the form and contents as proposed by the Board was not violative of the Election Law.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court, which affirmed an order of the trial court, which dismissed an action brought by the candidate.


HEADNOTES

Elections -- primary elections -- ballots -- primary ballot valid where names of candidates for party positions of Member of State Committee (Male) and Member of State Committee (Female) will have printed after name of each candidate words "male" or "female" as case may be -- when Board of Elections groups names of candidates in accordance with same designating petitions (Election Law, § 104, subd. 2, as amd. in 1955), it may validly put at head of each group blank circle and instruction: "For a straight ticket mark within the circle" -- said 1955 amendment of Election Law ( § 104, subd. 2) providing that candidates for members of State committee designated by same petition shall be treated as one group, not unconstitutional.

1. A Democratic party primary ballot in the form and contents as proposed by the Nassau County Board of Elections does not violate the Election Law where the names of the candidates for party positions of Member of State Committee (Male) and Member of State Committee (Female) in a certain Assembly District will have printed after the name of each candidate the words "male" or "female" as the case may be. Section 11 of the Election Law requiring that, whenever the rules of the State committee provide for equal representation of the sexes from each unit, the primary ballot list the candidates for party position separately by sexes does not mean that the listing as to sex shall be in any particular form or method so long as the sex of the candidate for party position is clearly indicated.

2. When the Board of Elections proposes to group the names of the candidates in accordance with the same designating petitions, it is pursuant to specific authorization contained in the Election Law ( § 104, subd. 2, as amd. by L. 1955, ch. 405), and, when the board does so, it may validly put at the head of each group a blank circle, and surround it with the plainly printed instruction: "For a straight ticket mark within the circle", as provided in the Election Law ( § 108, subd. 6).

3. The amendment to subdivision 2 of section 104 of the Election Law (L. 1955, ch. 405), providing that candidates for members of a State committee designated by the same petition shall be treated as one group in regard to the order of names on the ballot, is not unconstitutional.

 

535 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Vanderbilt v. Vanderbilt


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


1 N.Y.2d 342; 135 N.E.2d 553; 153 N.Y.S.2d 1; 1956 N.Y. LEXIS 851


March 20, 1956, Argued  
May 31, 1956, Decided



DISPOSITION:  Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant husband appealed the judgment of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court (New York), in that it affirmed the decision that awarded plaintiff wife maintenance, counsel fees, and costs, and directed payment thereof. The wife appealed the judgment in that it (1) adjudged the Nevada divorce decree to bar a separation cause of action, (2) dismissed her complaint for a separation, and (3) failed to adjudge jurisdiction over the husband.


OVERVIEW: The wife filed for a separation and an award of alimony in New York. The husband, appearing specially, claimed lack of jurisdiction and contested the action on several grounds. The court affirmed the intermediate court's judgment. The court found that N.Y. Civ. Pract. Act §1170-b (1953) applied because the action was for a separation, where relief was refused because a divorce had previously been granted in an action where jurisdiction over the wife had not been obtained. Under § 1170-b the wife could obtain protection of those support rights that remained after and despite of the foreign divorce. Due process required personal service within Nevada to support a judgment against the wife, such that the part of the foreign divorce decree, which dealt with status, had to be given effect in New York as to terminate the marriage but was entitled to no effect at all so far as support or other property rights were concerned. A Nevada finding of fact, in an in rem proceeding, while conclusive as to the marriage status being dissolved, did not bind the wife personally nor was it conclusive as to any fact upon which the Nevada judgment was based.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment, with costs to plaintiff.


HEADNOTES

Husband and wife -- alimony for wife where foreign divorce previously granted to husband on nonpersonal jurisdiction -- section 1170-b of Civil Practice Act, providing that, in marital action where court refuses to grant relief by reason of finding that marital judgment had previously been granted to husband in action in which jurisdiction over person of wife was not obtained, court may nevertheless render, in same action, such judgment as justice may require for wife's maintenance, is constitutional -- State may validly grant alimony after and in spite of foreign ex parte divorce decree -- statute does not apply only to marriages where parties had lived together in this State as their matrimonial domicile.

1. In this action the trial court, holding that a Nevada divorce obtained by defendant husband on nonpersonal jurisdiction of the wife was binding in New York to dissolve the marriage, dismissed the wife's complaint insofar as it asked for a separation and properly awarded the wife alimony, counsel fees and expenses on the authority of section 1170-b of the Civil Practice Act, which provides that, in a marital action (here, an action for separation) where the court refuses to grant relief by reason of a finding by the court that a divorce had previously been granted to the husband in an action in which jurisdiction over the person of the wife was not obtained, the court may, nevertheless, render in the same action such judgment "as justice may require" for the maintenance of the wife.

2. Section 1170-b of the Civil Practice Act is valid. A State may grant alimony after and in spite of a foreign ex parte divorce. It is not necessary that the wife have her rights to support adjudicated before the husband secures such divorce.

3. Section 1170-b was not intended to apply only to marriages where the parties had lived together in this State as their matrimonial domicile.

4. The Nevada judgment has not been denied "full faith and credit" (U. S. Const., art. IV, § 1). Under the "divisible divorce" doctrine, the sole effect of the husband's Nevada divorce was to end the marriage, and it has been given that effect in New York.

5. The phrase "as justice may require" in section 1170-b is not unconstitutionally vague.

6. The statute itself gave adequate notice to the husband that, if no separation were granted, an award for maintenance might still be made.

7. The husband did not, by his subsequent activities in the case, waive his contention as to lack of jurisdiction (Civ. Prac. Act, § 237-a, subd. 3, par. [c]; § 237-a, subd. 4).

8. There was sufficient evidence to justify the finding that the husband had established a domicile in good faith in Nevada.

9. The amount of the award of alimony and the direction that it be retroactive to the date of the commencement of the separation action were proper.

10. The wife, having resided in this State for the required one-year period (Civ. Prac. Act, § 1165-a, subd 3), had standing to bring an action for separation under section 1170-b, and jurisdiction was effected by the sequestration of the husband's New York assets.

11. The Appellate Division had the power to make a finding that the husband had violently assaulted the wife and had been guilty of other cruel treatment which endangered and injured her health, although the trial court had not made such finding.

12. The Nevada court's determination in an in rem proceeding that the husband was entitled to a divorce because of the wife's cruelty, while conclusive as to the dissolution of the marriage status, did not bind the wife personally nor was it conclusive as to any fact upon which the Nevada judgment was based.

13. The husband was not denied a fair opportunity to present proof as to alimony.

 

536 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re Ginsberg


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


1 N.Y.2d 144; 134 N.E.2d 193; 151 N.Y.S.2d 361; 1956 N.Y. LEXIS 916


March 12, 1956, Argued  
April 27, 1956, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant bar association sought review of an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which dismissed the bar association's disciplinary proceeding against respondent attorney for professional misconduct. The bar association contended that, pursuant to N.Y. Jud. Law § 90(4), the attorney was automatically disbarred upon conviction of a felony, which was later reversed.


OVERVIEW: After the attorney was convicted of a felony, the bar association instituted a disciplinary proceeding against the attorney for professional misconduct. The attorney's felony conviction was later reversed and a new trial of the felony was ordered. The lower court confirmed a referee's report and dismissed the disciplinary proceeding against the attorney. On appeal, the court reversed and held that (1) pursuant to § 90(4), the attorney was automatically disbarred upon conviction of the felony, even in the absence of entry of any order of disbarment, and notwithstanding the fact that the felony conviction was later reversed; (2) the procedure for striking the attorney's name from the roll of attorneys was no more than a formal recording of the existing fact of disbarment, which was not affected by the fact that the procedure was never taken as to the attorney; and (3) upon reversal of the felony conviction, the attorney's remedy was to seek reinstatement to the bar pursuant to N.Y. Jud. Law § 90(5).


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's order that dismissed the bar association's disciplinary proceeding against the attorney for professional misconduct. The court remanded to the lower court for further proceedings.


HEADNOTES

Attorney and client -- disciplinary proceedings -- conviction of felony -- attorney's conviction of felony results in his automatic disbarment; immaterial that conviction subsequently reversed and new trial ordered or that his name was not removed from roll of attorneys.

1. Subdivision 4 of section 90 of the Judiciary Law provides that "Any person being an attorney and counsellor-at-law, who shall be convicted of a felony, shall, upon such conviction, cease to be an attorney and counsellor-at-law, or to be competent to practice law as such." In 1948 respondent, an attorney, was convicted of the crime of grand larceny in the first degree. Subsequently his conviction was reversed by the Appellate Division and a new trial ordered on questions of law only, the facts being affirmed. Respondent has not been retried and his name has not been stricken from the roll of attorneys. The language of the statute and the public policy which it expresses accomplished respondent's automatic removal from the profession upon his conviction. In the present proceeding, the Appellate Division improperly determined that the reversal of the conviction left respondent in the same status as if there had been no conviction.

2. The provision in the Judiciary Law ( § 90, subd. 4) for striking a lawyer's name from the roll of attorneys describes a procedure for the mere formal recording of the existing fact of disbarment.

3. Respondent's remedy is to apply for reinstatement.

 

537 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Brown v. Kingsley Books, Inc.


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


1 N.Y.2d 177; 134 N.E.2d 461; 151 N.Y.S.2d 639; 1956 N.Y. LEXIS 919


November 29, 1955, Argued  
April 27, 1956, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant booksellers sought review of a judgment of the Supreme Court in New York County, Special Term (New York), which ruled in favor of appellee City of New York (city). The booksellers argued that the remedy of an injunction under N.Y. Code Crim. Proc. § 22-a, which supplemented criminal sanctions against the sale of obscene materials, was an unconstitutional "prior restraint," interfering with freedom of speech and press.


OVERVIEW: The city determined that the booksellers were selling obscene materials and brought suit against the booksellers pursuant to § 22-a for an injunction directing the booksellers to surrender the materials so that the sheriff could destroy them. The supreme court found that the booklets were plainly obscene and pornographic. The supreme court granted judgment enjoining the booksellers from selling the booklets, and directed them to surrender all copies to the sheriff. On appeal, the booksellers argued that the remedy was an unconstitutional "prior restraint" in violation of the rights to free speech and press. The court ruled that § 22-a did not operate as a pervasive restraint on all expression relating to sex. The court determined that the statutory procedure to enjoin the sale of obscene materials could only be invoked after a formal adjudication found the works to be of the type and character condemned by statute. The court further held that a judge's decision to enjoin the sale of such materials was subject to strict procedural requirements and subject to appellate review. The court ruled there was no unconstitutional prior restraint and affirmed the supreme court's judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the supreme court's judgment granting the city an injunction to enjoin the booksellers from selling additional obscene materials.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- injunctions -- obscene books -- First Amendment to United States Constitution guaranteeing freedom of speech and press does not prohibit type of prior restraint contained in section 22-a of Code of Criminal Procedure.

1. The First Amendment to the United States Constitution guaranteeing freedom of speech and press does not prohibit the type of prior restraint contained in section 22-a of the Code of Criminal Procedure which gives the New York Supreme Court jurisdiction to enjoin, on suit of the chief executive or legal officer of any city, town or village, the sale, distribution, acquisition or possession of "obscene, lewd, lascivious, filthy, indecent or disgusting" written or printed matter.

2. Accordingly, a judgment was properly granted in favor of plaintiff Corporation Counsel of the City of New York, pursuant to section 22-a of the Code of Criminal Procedure, permanently enjoining defendant booksellers from distributing, selling or acquiring possession of booklets collectively entitled "Nights of Horror" -- which had been found displayed for sale in their stores and are conceded by them to be obscene -- requiring the booksellers forthwith to surrender to the sheriff for destruction all copies in their possession and directing the sheriff to seize and destroy such copies in the event the booksellers failed to surrender them.

People v. Bonino


Court of Appeals of New York


1 N.Y.2d 752; 135 N.E.2d 51; 152 N.Y.S.2d 298; 1956 N.Y. LEXIS 924


Reargued February 7, 1956  
April 27, 1956. - Decided


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the trial court (New York), which convicted him, along with a co-defendant, of felony murder and sentenced him to life imprisonment.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of felony murder. His co-defendant's confession was apparently admitted against him. He appealed twice and his conviction was affirmed. His co-defendant sought a writ of habeas corpus and argued that his confessions were inadmissible. The federal court of appeals agreed and granted the requested relief. The court then, in the interest of justice, reversed defendant's conviction and granted a new trial.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court, which convicted defendant of felony murder and sentenced him to life imprisonment, was reversed and a new trial was ordered.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - murder in first degree - confessions - since United States Court of Appeals held that writ of habeas corpus must issue to codefendant because his confessions were inadmissible, appellant herein should, in interest of justice, receive new trial with his (Bonino's) confessions excluded.

 

539 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Knapp v. Fasbender


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


1 N.Y.2d 212; 134 N.E.2d 482; 151 N.Y.S.2d 668; 1956 N.Y. LEXIS 922


October 3, 1955, Reargued  
April 27, 1956, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant people challenged the judgment of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court (New York), which held in favor of respondent town. The appellate division dismissed the complaint that questioned the authority of a local town board to delegate power to a local board of trustees through a proposition adopted at a special town meeting.


OVERVIEW: The people argued that the board of trustees usurped the powers of the town board and could not engage in certain town improvements without prior approval because trustees were separate and apart from the town board. The court affirmed the judgment that dismissed the complaint and held that the contracts in question, which were analogous to the facilitation of transportation, were proprietary in nature. Prior authorization or subsequent approval of the electors was not required and adoption by the town board was therefore needless. The court held that the legislature had the power to enact a statute that defined, clarified, confirmed its powers, and ratified the exercise of such powers. The court concluded that prior decisional laws and legislative acts conclusively established that the board of trustees had the power to enter the contracts described without the approval of electors at town elections. The court held that every statute was presumed to be constitutional, and every intendment was in favor of its validity.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that dismissed the people's complaint and held that a local board of trustees had the power to enter the contracts in question without prior approval of electors.


HEADNOTES

Towns -- powers of board of trustees of Town of Huntington -- "Board of Trustees of Town of Huntington" has power to enter into contracts for purchase of beach, parking and recreation areas and for sale of gravel and sand and for dredging of Huntington's harbors and bays without approval of electors -- chapter 816 of Laws of 1952, confirming powers of said board of trustees to acquire and manage real property and ratifying its acts, is constitutional.

1. A resolution of the town board of the Town of Huntington provided for a special election of the voters of the town to be held on May 15, 1951, to vote on a single proposition approving or disapproving the action of the "Supervisor, Justices of the Peace and Town Clerk of the Town of Huntington" -- who constitute the "Board of Trustees of the Town of Huntington" in contradistinction to the "Town Board of the Town of Huntington" of which the town clerk is not a member -- in acquiring beach property for the town, in making lease-purchase agreements for acquiring parking and recreation areas in the town, in making contracts with a certain dredging corporation for the sale and disposal of gravel and sand and for the dredging of Huntington's harbors and bays, and in using the revenues from said contracts for the purposes of the acquisition of the beach and the parking and recreation areas. The complaint, in a taxpayer's action for a judgment declaring the resolution of the town board and the proposition as approved at the special election void, was properly dismissed.

2. Chapter 816 of the Laws of 1952 ratifying and confirming the powers of the board of trustees of the Town of Huntington to acquire and manage real property and ratifying and confirming the acts of said board of trustees, which chapter was enacted after this action was tried and after said proposition was approved by the electors, is applicable to this action, since chapter 816 was enacted to clarify the right of the board of trustees to exercise certain powers then under dispute, and since, moreover, this action must be decided upon the basis of the law as it exists at the time of the decision of the Court of Appeals.

3. Chapter 816 of the Laws of 1952, together with prior legislative acts and decisional law, establish the powers of the board of trustees of the Town of Huntington to enter into the contracts described in the proposition without the approval of the electors at a town election. Such construction of the statute does not extend the trustees' proprietary powers so as to include important governmental powers or to interfere with the co-existing town board. Chapter 816 does not ratify the contracts included in the proposition, but only confirms the power of the trustees to enter into the contracts; and such confirmation is not a ratification of any corrupt or improvident conduct indulged in by the trustees in connection with the exercise of such powers, and cannot protect them against the consequences of a suit or proceeding in which a violation of their trust is proved.

4. The presumption of constitutionality of chapter 816 of the Laws of 1952 has not been overcome.

5. Chapter 816 of the Laws of 1952 does not violate the requirements of the State Constitution (art. III, § 15) inasmuch as chapter 816 embraces only one subject and its entire subject matter is expressed in its title.

6. The references in said chapter 816 to the Town Law, to article 79 and section 1307 of the Civil Practice Act are not such an incorporation by reference as is prohibited by the State Constitution (art. III, § 16).

 

540 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Farrington v. Pinckney


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


1 N.Y.2d 74; 133 N.E.2d 817; 150 N.Y.S.2d 585; 1956 N.Y. LEXIS 976


January 4, 1956, Argued  
March 23, 1956, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayers sought review of a decision of a Special Term of the Supreme Court, Ulster County (New York), which denied their motion for judgment on the pleadings and granted defendant state officials' cross motion for judgment on the pleadings in a suit seeking to declare 1954 N.Y. Laws 305, as amended by 1955 N.Y. Laws 864, unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The law in question established a uniform jury system, but excepted all counties with a population of 1,000,000 or more and all counties with a population of less than 100,000. The taxpayers argued that this was a local law in violation of N.Y. Const. art. III, § 17 and N.Y. Const. art. IX, § 1(b). The court disagreed and affirmed the judgment below. The court noted that legislation is presumed to be constitutional. The population classification used was not novel or unusual, as there were a host of statutory provisions in New York law that differentiated between counties and cities of different sizes. The classifications were reasonable because similar laws would affect small, middle, and large-sized counties differently. The law created a true class, so it was a general law, not a local law, and did not violate N.Y. Const. art. III, § 17, or N.Y. Const. art. IX, § 1(b).


OUTCOME: The judgment in favor of the state officials was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law -- special or general law -- jury systems -- three systems provided for selecting jurors: one system for 5 counties in City of New York, one system for about 15 other counties of 100,000 population or more, and one system which any of about 42 other counties of under 100,000 population may adopt if it does not wish second system (Judiciary Law, arts. 16, 17, 18); such legislative classification is reasonably suited to purpose of requiring uniform jury systems within economic capacities of three classes of counties, and is not "private" or "local" or "special" but valid (L. 1954, ch. 305, as amd. by L. 1955, ch. 864; N. Y. Const., art. III, § 17; art. IX, § 1, subd. [b]).

1. The Legislature has established one system for selecting, drawing, summoning and empaneling jurors in counties within cities having a population of 1,000,000 or more, that is, in the 5 counties within the City of New York (L. 1940, ch. 202 [Judiciary Law, art. 17, and related statutes]); and another system for all other counties having a population of 100,000 or more (L. 1954, ch. 305 [Judiciary Law, art. 18, and related statutes]), that is, for 15 counties, if one uses the 1950 census; and another system which may be optionally adopted by counties having a population of less than 100,000, that is, according to the 1950 census, 42 counties (L. 1955, ch. 864 [Judiciary Law, art. 16, and related statutes]); and, if any of such smaller counties fails to exercise such option, its jury system is to be the same as that required for the 15 medium-sized counties. The classifications thus constructed by the Legislature are not arbitrary. They are reasonably suited to the purpose of requiring uniform jury systems within the financial and economic capacities of the three classes of counties. They are not "private" or "local" or "special", but are "general" and valid (N. Y. Const., art. III, § 17).

2. The 1954 legislation (L. 1954, ch. 305) was a proposed fundamental change in up-State jury systems, and was to go into effect on July 1, 1955, but was in turn fundamentally reshaped by the 1955 legislation (L. 1955, ch. 864, eff. July 1, 1955). Each Legislature has this fundamental power of repealing or reshaping its predecessors' acts, where not restricted by the Constitution.

3. If there is some reasonable and possible basis for a legislative classification, it cannot be held to be special and unconstitutional, but must be given effect by the courts. The class created by the 1955 Legislature -- counties of less than 100,000 population -- was based on economic and financial conditions which could be recognized as possibly common to all counties in that class and reasonably related to the subject of the legislation.

4. Since the statute under consideration is not local in its terms or effect, assuming, without deciding, that the statute is a law relating to the organization and government of counties, it is not violative of section 1 (subd. [b]) of article IX of the Constitution, which provides that the Legislature may not enact a law which is local in its terms or effect where the subject relates to the organization and government of counties.

 

541 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Fink v. Cole


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


1 N.Y.2d 48; 133 N.E.2d 691; 150 N.Y.S.2d 175; 1956 N.Y. LEXIS 981


January 9, 1956, Argued  
March 22, 1956, Decided



DISPOSITION: Order of Appellate Division and that of Special Term reversed and petition dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner horse owner originally appealed a decision of respondents Racing Commission of the State of New York denying his application for an owner's racing license pursuant to the State Racing Commission Act § 9-b(2), 1951 N.Y. Laws 324. The Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York) affirmed a trial court order annulling the commission's determination. The commission appealed.


OVERVIEW: The owner originally sought to set aside the denial of his application for a license because the denial was based on improper proof and was voided by procedural errors. The commission appealed after the appellate division affirmed an order setting aside the commission's action. The court held that there was no applicable hearing requirement, either statutory or implied, and that the commission had no power to reaffirm an act of a prior agency which had been found to lack jurisdiction to enter an order. However, disagreeing with the appellate court, it held that the commission was entitled to consider evidence obtained in connection with prior hearings because it was entitled to rely upon any facts, regardless of source, so long as they were trustworthy and reliable. The court also found that even though there were procedural irregularities in the manner in which the hearing was held, the errors did not require remission to the commission, that the evidence was sufficient to support the commission's action, that further consideration by the commission was not necessary, and that the lower court orders voiding its action and remitting the matter to the commission were reversed.


OUTCOME: Holding that the horse owner's application for a license was properly denied, the court reversed the orders of the lower courts which had reversed the racing commission's order denying the application and dismissed the horse owner's petition.


HEADNOTES

Licenses -- horse racing -- application for owner's racing license properly denied by State Racing Commission -- said commission not barred from considering evidence before joint board which, in prior proceeding, was found to be without jurisdiction.

1. After the Court of Appeals had annulled a determination denying petitioner an owner's racing license on the ground that the hearing board -- a joint board consisting of three members of the State Racing Commission and two stewards of The Jockey Club -- had no lawful existence ( Matter of Fink v. Cole, 302 N. Y. 216), petitioner applied to appellant State Racing Commission for such a license. The license was properly denied by the commission.

2. There is no statutory or implied requirement for a hearing on petitioner's application.

3. But the State Racing Commission, operating under a new statute with new standards, cannot reaffirm a decision of the joint board which had been annulled on the ground that it was without jurisdiction.

4. However, the commission was not barred from considering the reports, testimony and exhibits in its files which had been obtained in the prior proceeding. Under this class of licensing statute, all relevant information known to the commission must be considered and reconsidered.

5. Since the record discloses that the commission found nonconformity with the statute and since petitioner was accorded representation by counsel before the full commission, errors, if any, in the administrative denial of the license without specific factual findings are not so prejudicial as to call for a remission to the commission.

6. The evidence now before the court is more than sufficient to support the action taken by the commission. The reconsideration and reaffirmation of the prior decision were additional acts unnecessary for a valid denial of the application.

 

542 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Cahen v. Boyland


[NO NUMBER IN ORIGINAL]


Court of Appeals of New York


1 N.Y.2d 8; 132 N.E.2d 890; 150 N.Y.S.2d 5; 1956 N.Y. LEXIS 998


February 15, 1956, Argued  
March 16, 1956, Decided



DISPOSITION: Judgment of Appellate Division and that of Special Term reversed, with costs in all courts, and matter remitted to Special Term for further proceedings in accordance with the opinion herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer sought review of a decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed the trial court's decision to grant the motion for judgment on the pleadings filed by defendant city tax commission and which declared that the 1955 amendment to N.Y. Tax Law § 292-b was applicable to the tax review proceedings she had pending in the city.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer filed four separate proceedings, in the City of New York, to review the tax assessment levied against her property by the commission. The commission filed a motion for judgment on the pleadings and argued that the proceedings were abandoned because they were not heard within four years of their commencement. The trial court granted the commission's motion and held that N.Y. Tax Law § 292-b applied to the taxpayer's four proceedings. On appeal, the appellate court affirmed the trial court's decision. On further review, the court reversed and determined that under the plain language of N.Y. Tax Law § 292-b, proceedings brought in the City of New York were not subject to the four-year abandonment rule. The court determined that the New York City was not included within the statute because it applied only to counties with a population of less than 100,000 and it applied only to petition and notice proceedings. The court found that the tax review procedure in New York City did not include petition and notice proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decisions of the two lower courts and remitted the matter to the trial court for further proceedings. The court held that the four year limitation was not applicable to the tax proceedings commenced by the taxpayer.


HEADNOTES

Taxation -- review of assessments -- New York City -- section 292-b of Tax Law (as amd. by L. 1955, ch. 651), providing that unless proceeding upon "petition and notice" to review assessments shall be brought to hearing within four years from date of service of petition and notice, proceeding shall be deemed abandoned, does not apply to proceedings in New York City; proceeding there begun by petition alone (§ 290-b).

The certiorari procedure for a review of real estate tax assessments has been replaced by a simplified procedure. (Tax Law, art. 13, § 290 et seq.; L. 1949, ch. 551, as amd.) The new procedure is commenced by a petition and notice, "except that * * * in a city having a population of one million or more" -- that is, in New York City -- it is "commenced by the service of such petition alone." (Tax Law, § 290-b; see, also, §§ 291, 293; Administrative Code of City of New York, § 166-1.0; Sixteenth Annual Report of N. Y. Judicial Council, 1950, p. 18.) Section 292-b contained in article 13 of the Tax Law at first stated that, "In a county having a population of less than one hundred thousand, unless the proceeding upon the petition and notice shall be brought to a hearing * * * within four years from the date of service of the petition and notice, the proceeding shall be deemed to have been abandoned". This section was amended (L. 1955, ch. 651) so as to delete the first-quoted phrase and start with the word "Unless". The section was thus made applicable to the 15 counties which had been excluded by the population limitation, but not to New York City, since the words of art, "the proceeding upon the petition and notice", were retained. Accordingly, section 292-b, as amended in 1955, does not apply to proceedings in New York City.

 

543 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Abelson


Court of Appeals of New York


309 N.Y. 643; 132 N.E.2d 884; 1956 N.Y. LEXIS 1038


Argued November 28, 1955.  
February 17, 1956, decided



DISPOSITION: CONWAY, Ch. J., DESMOND, FULD, FROESSEL, VAN VOORHIS and BURKE, JJ., concur; DYE, J., taking no [***2] part.

Judgments reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants were convicted of conspiracy and book making. The Appellate Division of the Supreme Court (New York) affirmed several of the convictions and reversed others. Defendants sought review.


OVERVIEW: Defendants claimed that there was insufficient evidence to support their convictions. The court agreed and reversed the convictions and remanded for a new trial. The court found that the evidence consisted primarily of the testimony of a special investigator who listened in on over 100 telephone conversations and identified defendants as possessing voices he heard. The court found that the voice identification was inadequate because there was no individual identification. The court held that this fell below the standard necessary to rebut the presumption of innocence and prove defendants' guilt beyond a reasonable doubt. The court also held that the evidence failed to show a conspiracy but only a series of disconnected transactions from which, at the most, inference of several conspiracies could be drawn. The court also held that the state failed to prove the corpus delicti of the crime of bookmaking because there was no showing that there were any horseraces, baseball games, or boxing matches on the days set forth in the indictment. In addition, the court found that there was no expert witness to explain the meaning of the jargon employed in the conversations.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's convictions for conspiracy and book making and remanded for a new trial.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - book-making and conspiracy - (1) judgments convicting defendants-appellants of book-making and conspiracy reversed and new trial ordered - (2) voices heard in tapped telephone conversations not sufficiently identified as defendants' - (3) evidence showed not single conspiracy but disconnected transactions - (4) no proof that on days set forth in indictment any races or games occurred or that names overheard were names of any horses or participants - (5) book-makers' jargon not explained to jury by any expert.

1. Judgments convicting nine defendants-appellants of book-making, and also convicting eight of them of conspiracy to commit book-making ( Penal Law, §§ 580, 986), are reversed and a new trial ordered, for the following reasons.

2. The People's case rested on the testimony of a special investigator who listened in, pursuant to court orders, on over one hundred telephone conversations during some two weeks' time for six hours each day and then, three months later, identified the nine appellants and at least ten others as possessing voices he had thus heard. The voices, except those of two appellants, were not previously known [***3] to him, and, while thus listening to many unfamiliar voices, he had been busily pencilling notes and recording the substance of the conversations. There was no individual identification of the voices but, rather, a collective or general description. His identification of appellants' voices fell below the standard necessary to prove their guilt beyond a reasonable doubt.

3. The indictment charged a single conspiracy, but the evidence showed a series of disconnected transactions, from which, at most, inference might be drawn of several conspiracies.

4. It was not proved that on the days set forth in the indictment (a) any horse races were run, (b) the names mentioned in the conversations were the names of horses, (c) any such horses were entered or ran in races on such dates, (d) any baseball games were played on the dates designated, or (e) any boxing matches were staged on such dates or that the names heard in the conversations were the names of participants who engaged in such boxing matches. Thus the corpus delicti of the crime of book-making was not proved.

5. The People did not qualify any expert to explain to the jury the meaning of the jargon used by the telephone [***4] conversants.

 

544 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Defiance Milk Products Co. v. Du Mond


Court of Appeals of New York


309 N.Y. 537; 132 N.E.2d 829; 1956 N.Y. LEXIS 1051


Argued October 18, 1955.  
February 16, 1956, decided



DISPOSITION: FULD, VAN VOORHIS and BURKE, JJ., concur with DESMOND, J.; CONWAY, Ch. J., dissents in an opinion in which FROESSEL. J., concurs; DYE, J., taking no part.

Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Commissioner of Agriculture and Markets of the State of New York challenged the judgment of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Third Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed a judgment of the trial court in favor of respondent milk producer holding that N.Y. Agric. & Mkts. Law § 64(2) was unconstitutional, invalid, and void.


OVERVIEW: The milk producer manufactured and sold, in 14 1/2-ounce containers, evaporated skimmed milk. N.Y. Agric. & Mkts. Law § 64(2) prohibited the sale in the state of evaporated, skimmed milk in containers containing less than 10 pounds. The milk producer brought an action claiming that the statute was unconstitutional because no reasonable basis existed for an absolute ban against evaporated skimmed milk. The trial court held, and the appellate court affirmed, that the statute was unconstitutional, finding that there was no rational ground for the arbitrary and unnecessary prevention of the sale of a wholesome food product for household use. The court affirmed the judgments below, holding that the property of a citizen including his right to sell nondeleterious substances could not be taken from him without rhyme or reason. The ostensible legislative purpose was to see to it that customers did not get evaporated skimmed milk when they were trying to buy evaporated whole milk. The prohibition of all evaporated skimmed milk products was, as matter of law, not a reasonable way of dealing with such confusion or possibility of confusion as the legislators might have found to exist.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the appellate court affirming the trial court's finding that a state law prohibiting the sale of evaporated skimmed milk in containers containing less than 10 pounds was unconstitutional.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law - milk control - evaporated skimmed milk - (1) judgment declaring unconstitutional that part of Agriculture and Markets Law ( § 64, subd. 2) which prohibited sale of evaporated skimmed milk unless in minimum 10-pound containers, proper; statute had effect of arbitrarily barring wholesome [***2] product from retail sales - (2) if some shopkeepers, when asked for evaporated milk, sold evaporated skimmed milk, proper remedy was not total ban on retail sales - (3) plaintiff sustained burden of proof by showing that no reasonable basis existed for statutory ban.

1. Subdivision 2 of section 64 of the Agriculture and Markets Law, insofar as it prohibits the sale of "evaporated skimmed milk, except it be in containers or packages containing ten pounds * * * or more", amounts to an unreasonable and arbitrary prohibition against all sales at retail of a concededly wholesome, useful, and widely accepted product. Judgment was properly granted declaring the above-quoted portion of the subdivision unconstitutional and void, in this action by a corporation which sells such product in 14 1/2-ounce sealed and sterilized containers.

2. Plaintiff's containers were plainly labeled "Evaporated Skimmed Milk", as required by the subdivision. Defendant, the State Commissioner of Agriculture and Markets, showed that some shopkeepers nevertheless tried to sell some of his inspectors evaporated skimmed milk when they asked simply for evaporated milk. However, the legislative remedy - in effect [***3] banning all retail sales of this useful product - was unreasonable and confiscatory.

3. Plaintiff has sustained the burden of proof by showing that no reasonable basis existed for an absolute ban against evaporated skimmed milk.

 

545 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Le Grande


Court of Appeals of New York


309 N.Y. 420; 131 N.E.2d 712; 1956 N.Y. LEXIS 1056


Argued October 19, 1955.  
January 12, 1956, decided



DISPOSITION: CONWAY, Ch. J., DESMOND, FULD, FROESSEL and BURKE, JJ., concur; DYE, J., taking no part.

Orders reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The people appealed the orders of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York) that reversed the judgments of the county court that convicted defendants of the crimes of fraudulently obtaining and conspiring to obtain property for charitable or benevolent purposes in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 934, and conspiracy in violation of N.Y. Penal Law, § 580 and dismissed the indictment.


OVERVIEW: Defendants solicited funds on the streets of the city dressed in the habit or garb common to sisters or nuns. Each defendant was at all times in possession of a written certificate of authority to make collections on behalf of a religious corporation duly organized under the Religious Corporations Law. The jury found that the agreement between defendants and a religious corporation was that each defendant delivered $ 2.50 a day to the pastor and retained the rest of the collected funds as her personal property. Defendants were convicted of fraudulently obtaining and conspiring to obtain property for charitable or benevolent purposes in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 934. The indictments were dismissed by the appellate court on the ground that there was no evidence that defendants violated the provisions of the statute. The court reversed the appellate court and held that the funds obtained by defendants were ostensibly solicited for benevolent purposes in violation of § 934. The court concluded that it would have been a contradiction in terms to hold that the object of the Christian religion, in its broadest connotation, was not benevolent.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the orders of the appellate court that set aside defendants' conviction for fraudulently obtaining property for charitable purposes and ordered a new trial.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - fraudulently obtaining property for benevolent purposes - (1) solicitors, garbed in nuns' habits and holding cups, collected funds from public, and [***2] delivered $2.50 each day to church pastor, whose certificate of authority to make collection they held, and retained rest of collected funds; such action constituted violation of Penal Law ( § 934), which prohibits obtaining of money by false pretenses for "benevolent" purpose - (2) collections made for bona fide church, including payment of part of salary of pastor, do not violate section 934; but here main object was solicitors' own financial gain, and public was defrauded - (3) funds were ostensibly being solicited for pretended "benevolent" purpose which included pretended religious purpose.

1. Each of the defendants solicited funds by sitting in a public place dressed in a nun's habit and holding a cup in which each received coins from the public. Each had in her possession a certificate of authority to make collections on behalf of a duly organized religious corporation. The jury found that under the agreement between them and the religious corporation each defendant delivered $2.50 a day to the pastor of the church and retained the rest of the collected funds as her personal property. By these acts defendants violated the Penal Law ( § 934), which makes it a crime for [***3] a person willfully, by a false writing or other false pretenses, to obtain any money for any alleged or pretended charitable or benevolent purpose.

2. Collections made for the purposes of the church, including payment of part of the salary of a pastor, would not be in violation of section 934. However, here the main object of defendants' solicitation was their own financial gain, and there was a fraud upon the public.

3. The collections were for a pretended "religious" purpose, such purpose being included in a "benevolent" purpose. The church purports to be a Christian church, and the objects of such an organization are primarily the spiritual health and welfare of mankind, which is a "benevolent" purpose.

548 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Ross v. MacDuff


Court of Appeals of New York


309 N.Y. 56; 127 N.E.2d 806; 1955 N.Y. LEXIS 930


Argued April 12, 1955.  
July 8, 1955, decided



DISPOSITION: CONWAY, Ch. J., DESMOND, FULD, FROESSEL, VAN VOORHIS and BURKE, JJ., concur.

Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, Commissioner of Motor Vehicles (commissioner), sought review of the decision from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which annulled the determination of the commissioner that had suspended the driver's license of respondent driver.


OVERVIEW: The commissioner suspended the driver's license to operate a motor vehicle for habitual or persistent violations of the traffic laws and ordinances pursuant to N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 71(3). The appellate court annulled the determination. On appeal, the court reversed. The court stated that the administration of the traffic rules was left to the reasonable discretion of the administrative official. The court stated that the magistrate that convicted the driver of his last speeding violation had apprised the driver that he had enough points to fall within the definition of an habitual or persistent offender. Based on the record, the court stated that the determination of the commissioner was neither arbitrary nor capricious and found that the suspension was justified.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court and reinstated the determination of the commissioner that suspended the operator's license of the driver.


HEADNOTES

Motor vehicles - suspension of license - habitual or persistent violations - warning that license may be suspended or revoked - (1) petitioner admitted six prior convictions for traffic violations; Commissioner of Motor Vehicles properly suspended petitioner's operator's license for "habitual or persistent violation" of traffic laws (Vehicle and Traffic Law, § 71, subd. 3, par. [d] ) - (2) statute not unconstitutional for invalid delegation of legislative powers - (3) when petitioner had enough demerit points on last speeding violation to fall within commissioner's definition of "habitual" or "persistent" violator, Magistrate properly apprised respondent of provision of Code of Criminal Procedure [***2] (§ 335-a); license, therefore, properly suspended.

1. Petitioner admitted six prior convictions for traffic violations, and the Commissioner of Motor Vehicles suspended petitioner's license to operate a motor vehicle for fifteen days, pursuant to the Vehicle and Traffic Law ( § 71, subd. 3, par. [d] ), which permits such suspension "for habitual or persistent violation" of traffic laws and ordinances. The commissioner's determination was improperly annulled and should be reinstated.

2. The Vehicle and Traffic Law ( § 71, subd. 3, par. [d] ) is not unconstitutional on the claimed ground that legislative functions have been delegated to an administrative body without providing any criteria or standards definding the words "habitual" or "persistent". The circumstances under which a licensee may be deemed guilty of "habitual or persistent violation" vary with the changes in highway conditions, amount of traffic, type of control, power and speed of vehicles, changes in local traffic regulations and ordinances and other elements, necessitating the delegation of the formulation of specific rules to administrative officials.

3. When petitioner was charged with speeding in [***3] 1952, he had two prior convictions against him for speeding and he had been found guilty of passing three red lights. Under the commissioner's point system, there were seven points against petitioner at that time or one point short of finding him a "habitual" offender by the commissioner's definition. Therefore, with the last violation, one of speeding, petitioner acquired two more points or enough to cause him to fall within the commissioner's definition of "habitual" or "persistent", and, accordingly, the Magistrate had the discretion to suspend or revoke petitioner's license, and at that point it was necessary that the convicting Magistrate apprise petitioner of the provisions of section 335-a of the Code of Criminal Procedure. The Magistrate did so at that time. Accordingly, petitioner's license was properly suspended.

New York v. Nelson


Court of Appeals of New York


309 N.Y. 94; 127 N.E.2d 827; 1955 N.Y. LEXIS 936


Argued March 3, 1955.  
July 8, 1955, decided



DISPOSITION: CONWAY, Ch. J., DESMOND, DYE, FULD, FROESSEL and VAN VOORHIS, JJ., concur; BURKE, J., taking no part.

Order affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Taxation - sale of valuable real property for failure to pay water charges - court without power to afford relief to owners of parcels of real estate assessed at $52,000, title to which city acquired on foreclosure sale for nonpayment of water charges amounting to $887, due to default of trusted bookkeeper; result suggests need of legislation.

For the nonpayment of water charges amounting to $887, the City of New York [***2] acquired title to two parcels of real property assessed at $52,000, in an in rem foreclosure sale. The nonpayment of water charges was due to the default of a trusted bookkeeper and was discovered when he attempted suicide. One of the parcels of property had a gross annual rent income of $9,275, and the other parcel was resold at a price in excess of its assessed valuation of $6,000, the city retaining the entire sale proceeds of $7,000. The owners paid the real estate taxes due on the property and the city continued to bill the owners for the real estate taxes on one of the parcels for two years after the city had acquired title. The court is without power to afford relief. The result suggests the need of legislation.

 

550 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Oneida County Forest Preserve Council v. Wehle


Court of Appeals of New York


309 N.Y. 152; 128 N.E.2d 282; 1955 N.Y. LEXIS 945


Argued June 8, 1955.  
July 8, 1955, decided



DISPOSITION: CONWAY, Ch. J., DESMOND, DYE, FULD, FROESSEL and VAN VOORHIS, JJ., concur.

Ordered accordingly.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, New York State Conservation Commissioner and New York State Director of Lands and Forests, challenged an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Fourth Judicial Department (New York), which granted a motion by respondent corporation for consent to institute an action against appellants pursuant to N.Y. Const. art. XIV, § 4.


OVERVIEW: The corporation, a public interest group, sought to enjoin appellants from granting contracts for the cutting down and carrying away of blown down timber. The corporation claimed that the contracts violated N.Y. Const. art. XIV, § 1, which protected State forests. The corporation filed a motion for consent to institute an injunctive action against appellants. Appellants claimed that the corporation lacked standing because it was not a "citizen" within the meaning of N.Y. Const. art. XIV, § 4. The trial court granted the corporation's motion. On appeal, the court modified the trial court's order and affirmed the order as modified. The court held that the corporation was a membership corporation and that such corporations were "citizens" within the meaning of § 4. Therefore, the court determined that the corporation had standing to institute the action against appellants. The court ruled that the trial court's order erroneously granted the corporation the right to invoke all available remedies. The court modified the order by limiting it to consent to institute an action to enjoin a violation of N.Y. Const. art. XIV, § 1.


OUTCOME: The court modified the trial court's order by limiting it to the relief sought by the corporation. The court affirmed the order as modified.


HEADNOTES

Corporations - right to sue - corporation as "citizen" - (1) membership corporation is "citizen" within meaning of State Constitution [***2] (art. XIV, § 4) permitting suit by any "citizen" to enjoin violation of article XIV - (2) since said section 4 limits type of action, order should recite that motion by membership corporation is granted only insofar as it seeks consent to institute suit to restrain violation of article XIV.

1. A membership corporation is a "citizen" within the meaning of the New York State Constitution (art. XIV, § 4) providing that violations of any of the provisions of article XIV may be restrained at the suit of the People or with the consent of the Supreme Court in the Appellate Division, on notice to the Attorney-General at the suit of any "citizen".

2. But the State Constitution (art. XIV, § 4) limits the type of action which a citizen may bring to restrain violations of the article, and does not authorize the Appellate Division to consent that the applicant may "invoke such other remedy or remedies in the premises" as it may be advised. Accordingly, in view of the broad relief sought in the moving papers, the order should recite that the motion by petitioners-respondents, Oneida County Forest Preserve Council and Ralph Atwater, its president, is granted only insofar as it seeks consent [***3] to institute suit to restrain the violation of section 1 of article XIV of the Constitution.

 

551 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


O'Day v. Yeager


Court of Appeals of New York


308 N.Y. 580; 127 N.E.2d 585; 1955 N.Y. LEXIS 965


Argued March 9, 1955.  
June 9, 1955, decided



DISPOSITION: CONWAY, Ch. J., FROESSEL, VAN VOORHIS and BURKE, JJ., concur with FULD, J.; DYE, J., dissents in an opinion; DESMOND, J., taking no part.

Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner clerk brought an action against respondent surrogate for an order directing the surrogate to reinstate the clerk to the surrogate's court. The surrogate appealed from an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court (New York), which reversed an order of the Supreme Court at Special Term (New York) that dismissed the clerk's petition.


OVERVIEW: The clerk claimed that the surrogate's revocation of his appointment as the clerk of the surrogate's court violated N.Y. Civ. Serv. Law § 22, which accorded him protection as a veteran. The surrogate argued that the statute did not protect the clerk because the clerk was an independent officer with specific statutory duties. The court reversed the order of the appellate court and reinstated the special term's order that dismissed the clerk's petition. The court found that the clerk was not a subordinate employee entitled to the protection of § 22, as the clerk performed many vital tasks on his own responsibility that were not subject to the surrogate's direction. The court concluded that the clerk's removal from the position was not regulated or controlled by the provisions of the statute.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the appellate court that reversed the special term's dismissal of the clerk's petition against the surrogate. The court reinstated the order of the special term.


HEADNOTES

Civil service - veterans - removal of clerk of Surrogate's Court - (1) petitioner veteran properly removed from office of clerk of Surrogate's Court of Erie County without hearing provided for in Civil Service Law ( § 22); statute intended to apply only to subordinate position, not to independent one - (2) one is independent officer whose position, powers and duties are statutory and who exercises initiative and independent judgment - (3) Surrogate's Court Act, providing for appointment [***2] of surrogate and his powers and duties, indicates that clerk of Surrogate's Court of Erie County is independent officer.

1. Petitioner, clerk of the Surrogate's Court of Erie County and an honorably discharged veteran of World War II, was not entitled to the protection accorded veterans by section 22 of the Civil Service Law, and his removal from said office of clerk without the hearing provided for by section 22 was proper. Section 22 was intended to apply only to those holding positions of a subordinate nature and not to officials filling independent positions.

2. One is an independent officer whose position is created and whose powers and duties are prescribed by statute and who exercises a high degree of initiative and independent judgment.

3. The provisions of the Surrogate's Court Act providing for the appointment of the clerk of the Surrogate's Court of Erie County and prescribing his powers and duties indicate that he is an independent officer. The statute, taken as a whole, manifests a heavy responsibility granted to the clerk and high confidence reposed in him, and marks him as the one who has charge of the administrative work and machinery of the court.
