562 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Williams v. Aircooled Motors, Inc.


Court of Appeals of New York


307 N.Y. 332; 121 N.E.2d 251; 1954 N.Y. LEXIS 961; 26 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P68,620


Argued May 26, 1954.  
July 14, 1954, decided



DISPOSITION: LEWIS, Ch. J., CONWAY, DESMOND, DYE, FROESSEL and VAN VOORHIS, JJ., concur.

Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant employer sought review by permission of an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Fourth Judicial Department (New York), which, in an action for wages under N.Y. Elec. Law § 226, reversed on the law an order of the county court that affirmed a judgment of the municipal court in favor of the employer.


OVERVIEW: The employee was employed as an inspector in the employer's factory. As a result of the operative collective bargaining agreement the employee was entitled to overtime for all work in excess of eight hours a day or 40 hours a week. The established workday was nine hours per day and five days per week. In order to vote, the employers designated the two hours immediately after the employee's performance of seven hours of work. The employee left the plant to vote, but he only received his normal compensation, not the overtime that he would have normally received. The employee filed suit. On review, the court found no error. The court held that the purpose of N.Y. Elec. Law § 226 was to eliminate any penalty for exercising the right of suffrage and to remove a practical obstacle to getting out the vote. The statute assured that, if the employee worked for seven hours and took off the two allowed him to vote, he should have been paid the amount he would have received had he not voted and had remained at his employment for his accustomed nine hours. The court rejected the employer's argument that the employee only should have received straight time.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate division's order with costs.


HEADNOTES

Master and servant - wages - time off for voting - (1) established workday at defendant employer's plant in week including Election Day was nine hours per day; employee entitled under collective bargaining agreement to straight time for eight hours and time and one half for excess of eight hours; employer who designated last two hours of nine-hour day for voting must pay time and a half for last hour - (2) provision of Election Law [***2] (§ 226) entitling person to absent himself from employment for two hours on Election Day intended to guarantee against any loss in compensation by reason of time off for voting - (3) employee not entitled only to usual hourly rate of wages - straight time as distinguished from overtime.

1. Under the collective bargaining agreement between plaintiff's labor union and his employer, plaintiff was entitled to $1.80 an hour for straight time and time and one half, or $2.70 an hour, for all work in excess of eight hours a day or forty hours a week. In the week which included Election Day, the established workday at defendant employer's plant was nine hours a day and five days a week, which meant that plaintiff earned $17.10 a day. Defendant designated the two hours immediately following plaintiff's performance of seven hours of work as the time to absent himself for voting on Election Day, and paid plaintiff $16.20, instead of the $17.10 which he ordinarily received. The figure of $16.20 was arrived at by paying plaintiff at the rate of $1.80 an hour for the two additional voting hours. Summary judgment was properly granted in plaintiff's favor in the sum of ninety cents.

2.  [***3] The provision of the Election Law (§ 226) that a person shall be entitled to absent himself on Election Day from his employment for two successive hours and that no deduction shall be made from the usual salary or wages of such voter and no other penalty shall be imposed upon him by his employer by reason of such absence, guarantees the employee against any loss in compensation by reason of his taking time off to vote. He is assured that, if he takes the time off to vote, he will be paid the amount he would have received if he had not voted, but had remained at his employment for the accustomed time.

3. The statute was not intended to entitle plaintiff only to his usual hourly rate of wages - straight time as distinguished from overtime. Such an interpretation of the statute would discriminate against workers employed at an hourly rate rather than by the day or week. That was not the legislative intent, and there is no constitutional objection to the construction whereby plaintiff is paid time and one half.

 

563 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Day v. Mruk


Court of Appeals of New York


307 N.Y. 349; 121 N.E.2d 362; 1954 N.Y. LEXIS 964


Argued May 27, 1954.  
July 14, 1954, decided



DISPOSITION: LEWIS, Ch. J., CONWAY, DESMOND, DYE, FULD and FROESSEL, JJ., concur.

Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner police officer sought review of a judgment of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court (New York), which reversed a judgment of the trial court directing respondents, the members of the Board of Police Pensions of the City of Buffalo, to grant the officer's application for retirement and pension.


OVERVIEW: The officer entered the police department on June 5, 1930 and became a member of the police pension fund. At the time of his employment in 1930, the City's charter provided that members of the police department were entitled to pensions after 20 years of service. In 1940, a new provision was enacted providing for 30 years of service, but the number of years was reduced to 25 in 1945. The officer applied for retirement after 22 years of service. His application for a service pension was, however, rejected because he had not served in the police department for 25 years. The trial court granted the officer's petition for an order directing respondents to grant his application for retirement. The appellate court reversed and, on appeal, the court affirmed. The city's pension laws were enacted and in force prior to July 1, 1940, the date referred to in N.Y. Const. art. V, § 7 as the date on which membership in a public pension fund constituted a contractual relationship. The officer, who was a member in the pension fund prior to retirement and prior to § 7, not having fulfilled the statutory conditions when application for pension was made, had no vested interest therein.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's judgment.


HEADNOTES

Civil service - pensions - (1) Buffalo City Charter (§ 464, subd. [2] ), when petitioner became patrolman in 1930, provided for pension after twenty years; charter amended June 30, 1940, to require thirty years; amended again in 1945 to require only twenty-five years; petitioner, who applied for retirement in 1952 after twenty-two years of service, not entitled to retirement and pension - (2) twenty-year clause in section 464 did not give petitioner vested contractual right to pension after twenty years' service - (3) prior to adoption of State Constitution [***2] (art. V, § 7, eff. July 1, 1940, providing that membership in public retirement system shall be contractual relationship, benefits of which shall not be diminished), Roddy v. Valentine (268 N.Y. 228) stated that policeman obtained vested right to pension upon his actual retirement but not prior thereto, "Where the statutory scheme creates a fund wholly or largely out of public moneys"; quoted words in opinion were dictum and not intended to define precisely when patrolman would obtain vested right - (4) change of provisions of pension requirements prior to July 1, 1940, not unconstitutional where Buffalo City Charter (§ 462) provided that member, upon resignation, was permitted to withdraw what he himself had paid into fund - (5) moreover, Buffalo police pension fund was not created mainly out of private contributions.

1. On June 5, 1930, petitioner entered the police department of the City of Buffalo as a patrolman and became a member of the police pension fund. At that time section 464 (subd. [2] ) of the Charter of the City of Buffalo provided that members of the police department were entitled to pensions "after twenty years of actual service in said department".Such provision [***3] was repealed by Local Law No. 8 adopted June 30, 1940, and in substitution therefor it was enacted that any member who had completed thirty years of actual service should be entitled to a pension. The "thirty years" was reduced to "twenty-five years" by Local Law No. 1 of 1945. Petitioner applied for retirement in September, 1952, effective October 1, 1952, thus having served as a patrolman for a period of twenty-two years. His application for a service pension was rejected, and he is still serving as patrolman. An application for an order directing respondents, as members of the board of police pensions of the City of Buffalo, to grant petitioner's application for retirement, effective October 1, 1952, and a pension, was properly denied.

2. The twenty-year clause, as it existed in section 464 of the Charter of the City of Buffalo in 1930 when petitioner entered the police department, did not constitute a vested contractual interest inuring to petitioner's benefit, and it could be constitutionally altered in 1940 and 1945 when the length of time required in which to entitle him to a pension was increased from twenty to thirty years and then reduced to twenty-five years.

 [***4] 3. Prior to the addition of section 7 of article V to the State Constitution in 1938, providing that after July 1, 1940, membership in any public retirement system shall be a contractual relationship, the benefits of which shall not be diminished or impaired, it was held in Roddy v. Valentine (268 N.Y. 228) that a policeman obtained a vested right to his pension upon his retirement, and that prior thereto no such contractual right inhered in a member of a pension fund. The court stated: "Where the statutory scheme creates a fund wholly or largely out of public moneys, the interest of the member down to the point where there has been compliance with all precedent conditions and the award has been or as of right should have been made, can hardly be deemed contractual." Assuming that prior to 1940 the members of the police pension fund of the City of Buffalo paid about one third of its annual receipts while the city subscribed the remaining two thirds, as petitioner contends, the first clause of the quoted language in Roddy v. Valentine, on which petitioner relies herein, was dictum that the interest of the member was noncontractual "Where the statutory scheme creates [***5] a fund wholly or largely out of public moneys". There was no need, and it was not intended, thereby to define precisely under what circumstances a policeman might obtain a contractual right if the retirement plan differed from the customary and purported to grant such a right.

4. Even if petitioner did, along with other members of the fund, pay a substantial sum in comparison to that contributed by the city, no unconstitutionality would appear to be involved in changing the provisions of the pension requirements prior to July 1, 1940 (N.Y. Const., art. V, § 7), since a means was supplied under section 462 of the Charter of the City of Buffalo, as amended, to refund to a member, who resigned from the fund, the moneys he had himself paid in.

5. Even if the language of Roddy v. Valentine was to be adopted as a precise criterion, it could not be held that the Buffalo police pension fund was created mainly out of private contributions when, even according to petitioner's contention, at no time was more than one third contributed privately by the members of the fund.

 

564 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Black River Regulating Dist. v. Adirondack League Club


Court of Appeals of New York


307 N.Y. 475; 121 N.E.2d 428; 1954 N.Y. LEXIS 977


Argued February 26, 1954.  
July 14, 1954, decided



DISPOSITION: LEWIS, Ch. J., CONWAY, DESMOND, FULD, FROESSEL and VAN VOORHIS, JJ., concur.

Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant property owners sought review of an order from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Fourth Judicial Department (New York), which reversed a lower court's summary judgment dismissing the complaint in favor of defendant in plaintiff water regulating district's action seeking the enforcement of a plan to construct a reservoir. Defendant contended that it was barred by the Stokes Act, N.Y. Conserv. Law § 445.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff water regulating district filed an action for a judgment declaring it entitled to maintain condemnation proceedings against defendant property owners for the purpose of constructing a reservoir. Defendants contended that the construction of the reservoir was barred by the Stokes Act, N.Y. Conserv. Law § 445, and that plaintiff lacked the status necessary to challenge a prior decision. The court concluded that § 445 effectively disposed of the plan, as it only excepted the construction of a reservoir for the purpose of a municipal water supply, which was not the situation in the instant case. The court noted that plaintiff's authority was revocable by legislation and that such did not create an impairment of rights or render the legislation constitutionally invalid. The court held that plaintiff lacked the status to challenge the law because it was a government agency, and plaintiff's authority had been constitutionally withdrawn. Accordingly, the appellate decision was reversed and the trial court's judgment dismissing the complaint was reinstated.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate division and held that plaintiff water regulating district's authority to construct a reservoir and to condemn defendant property owner's land in furtherance thereof had been effectively and constitutionally revoked by legislation which served to protect the public waterways. The court further held that as a governmental agency, plaintiff had no status to challenge the withdrawal of its authority.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law - validity of statute prohibiting river regulating district from constructing reservoir - (1) Stokes Act (L. 1950, ch. 803, amdg. Conservation Law, § 445) prohibits construction of reservoirs, except for municipal water supply, on south branch of Moose River by any river regulating board; Ostrander Amendment to State Constitution (art. XIV, § 2, adopted in 1953) revoked previous constitutional authority which had permitted [***2] use of lands of State Forest Preserve for construction of reservoirs to regulate flow of streams; action by plaintiff regulating district and plaintiffs, individually and as members of board of said district, for judgment declaring that plaintiff district is entitled to maintain condemnation proceedings against defendant and that Stokes Act, in its application to reservoir project, is invalid; Appellate Division reversed order of Special Term dismissing complaint; Stokes Act declared constitutional; plaintiffs lack status to challenge validity of act and of Ostrander Amendment, and are barred from maintaining condemnation proceedings; order of Appellate Division reversed and matter remitted - (2) in previous case court ruled that Stokes Act disposed of board's reservoir project and declined to pass upon constitutionality of Stokes Act - (3) Stokes Act and Ostrander Amendment enunciated public policy that State Forest Preserve should be "forever kept as wild forest lands"; issue decided in accord with law as it exists at time of decision of Court of Appeals - (4) Ostrander Amendment not limited to prospective effect - (5) alteration of powers of regulating district by Legislature presents [***3] no question of constitutionality - (6) district is State agency - (7) inclusion of reservoir in official plan did not vest in petitioners, per se, unimpairable contractual interest in proposed site or in construction of reservoir - (8) issuance of certificates of indebtedness did not confer upon plaintiffs status to test validity of Stokes Act.

1. The so-called Stokes Act (L. 1950, ch. 803, eff. April 20, 1950, amdg. Conservation Law, § 445) prohibits the construction of "reservoirs for the regulation of the flow of streams or for any other purpose except for municipal water supply * * * on the south branch of the Moose river by any river regulating board". In November, 1953, the People adopted the so-called Ostrander Amendment to the State Constitution (art. XIV, § 2, eff. Jan. 1, 1954), canceling and withdrawing the prior constitutional exception permitting the Legislature to allow a portion of the State Forest Preserve to be used for the construction of reservoirs to regulate the flow of streams (art. XIV, §§ 1, 2), so that the People have rendered the lands of the State Forest Preserve inviolate for use in regulating the flow of streams. Plaintiff Black River Regulating [***4] District and the individual plaintiffs, for themselves and as members of the regulating district board, instituted this action for judgment declaring that the regulating district was entitled to maintain condemnation proceedings against defendant and that the Stokes Act, in its application to the proposed plan to construct the Panther Mountain Reservoir and Dam, was invalid. Special Term dismissed the complaint. The Appellate Division, stating that, since the Court of Appeals in the previous case ( Matter of Adirondack League Club v. Black Riv. Regulating Dist., 301 N.Y. 219) had not passed on the constitutionality of the Stokes Act, reversed the order dismissing the complaint and held, as a matter of law, that, if the constitutional invalidity of the Stokes Act was established, "the issues which were rendered moot by the passage of the Stokes Act would be no longer moot". The Stokes Act is declared constitutional. Plaintiffs, either as a body politic or as individuals, lack status to challenge its validity, and, in such capacities, are barred from maintaining condemnation proceedings. The order of the Appellate Division should be reversed and the matter remitted.

2.  [***5] When the parties hereto were previously before the court (301 N.Y. 219, supra), the Court of Appeals ruled that, since the reservoir planned by the board was not "for municipal water supply", and since no construction had actually been commenced, the Stokes Act completely disposed of the board's plan. The Court of Appeals did not reach the merits or deem it a situation requiring special treatment and, at the same time, declined to pass upon the constitutionality of the Stokes Act as such question was not then before it.

3. While the Stokes Act and the Ostrander Amendment became effective subsequent to the approval of the proposed reservoir project, they nonetheless enunciated a public policy concerning the use of the lands in the State Forest Preserve in order to make sure that such lands should be "forever kept as wild forest lands". The issue should be decided in accord with the law as it exists when the decision of the Court of Appeals is made, a policy which is in harmony with Federal authority and with the unwillingness of the court to perpetuate a judgment contrary to existing law.

4. The withdrawal by the Ostrander Amendment of the board's legislative authority [***6] to use lands of the State Forest Preserve "to regulate the flow of streams" is not in any way limited to prospective effect, nor may its effectiveness be suspended or postponed by plaintiffs' claim of a so-called vested right to proceed to construction because of prior approval of its official plan. That plan was effectively disposed of by the Stokes Act (see 301 N.Y. 219, 222, supra).

5. The number and nature of the powers of a regulating district, an agency of the State to promote the public health, safety and welfare, are within the absolute discretion of the State, and any alteration or impairment of those powers by the Legislature presents no question of constitutionality. Such powers are not protected by the due process clause of the Fourteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution.

6. The district board is no more than an agency of the State, notwithstanding that plaintiffs maintain it was voluntarily formed. The nature of the district is voluntary only to the extent that a person or public corporation may petition the State Water Power and Control Commission for its organization, but it is the order of said commission which creates the district (Conservation [***7] Law, §§ 432, 435).

7. The inclusion of Panther Mountain Reservoir in the official plan did not vest in plaintiffs, per se, a contractual interest either in the proposed site or in the construction of a reservoir which could not be impaired by legislative action. Inherent in the grant of legislative power is a plenary power to alter or revoke, and the exercise of such power does not impair any obligation of contract. Actually there is only one party to a contract by which a governmental corporation is formed, and its board of directors are only trustees for the common good. Accordingly, the interests of plaintiffs are only those of the State, and the State cannot challenge its own acts.

8. The issuance of certificates of indebtedness by plaintiffs does not confer upon them an independent status to test the validity of the Stokes Act or to protect the interests of the creditors of the district. Whatever contract the certificate holders may have with the district is subject to legitimate governmental power exercised on behalf of the public welfare.

 

565 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re Potter's Will


Court of Appeals of New York


307 N.Y. 504; 121 N.E.2d 522; 1954 N.Y. LEXIS 980


Argued April 12, 1954.  
July 14, 1954, decided



DISPOSITION: LEWIS, Ch. J., DESMOND, DYE, FULD, FROESSEL and VAN VOORHIS, JJ., concur.

Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant successor trustees sought review of a decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court (New York), which declared that the deceased's trust terminated and that the next of kin of the testator were entitled to the trust fund.


OVERVIEW: A trust was created under the will of a deceased person, which expressed a charitable purpose of educating children of the poor through an academy. The academy was later absorbed by another school system. The successor trustees filed a petition against the school district, which prayed for a construction of the will and a direction as to the application of the funds. The trial court held that the trust terminated and that the next of kin of the testator were entitled to the trust. The successor trustees appealed. On appeal, the court reversed and held that where a testator apparently sought to leave money for a charitable purpose, a liberal construction was to be given to the terms of the will in order to uphold it and validate the bequest. The court held that the entire applicable portion of the will was to be read and considered, and not merely isolated segments, and the intent was to be gleaned from that complete consideration. Finally, the court held that the intention of the testator in creating the trust was a question of law and not one of fact.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment in favor of the school district.


HEADNOTES

Charities - charitable trusts - limited in time or perpetual - intent - (1) testator died in 1841 leaving will in which he created trust in perpetuity for "exclusive education of the children of the poor" and placed $6,000 in trust for "perpetual fund for the education of the children of the poor who shall be educated in the academy" in Huntington, New York; said academy was private school and functioned until [***2] 1858 when appellant Union Free School District of Huntington purchased it and its land, tore down building, and erected new building; in construction proceeding commenced in 1949 by successor trustees, erroneous determination made that trust failed when academy was absorbed into common school system; matter remitted to Surrogate's Court - (2) present litigants who succeed to interests of parties in Williams v. Williams (8 N.Y. 525 [1853] ), which concerned precise trust involved here, foreclosed from relitigating issues decided in that case; appellants, Attorney-General and Union Free School District No. 3 of Town of Huntington, not so precluded - (3) Williams case did not hold that said trust for education was limited in time to period of existence of particular institution, but did hold that charitable trusts had not been abolished by Revised Statutes - (4) testator's intent in creating trust question of law only - (5) testator intended to provide never-ending source of financial aid to needy students without limiting that charitable purpose to lifetime of any specific institution - (6) matter remitted to Surrogate's Court for determination of remaining issues and determination [***3] as to purposes to which trust funds shall now be put.

1. Decedent died in 1841 leaving a will in which, stating his "desire to raise the standard of intellectual and moral improvement among the poor", he created a trust in perpetuity for the "exclusive education of the children of the poor" and placed the sum of $6,000 in trust for a "perpetual fund for the education of the children of the poor who shall be educated in the academy in the village of Huntington". Testator then stated that the principal of the fund should be loaned on bond secured by mortgage, and one half of the interest annually accruing should be added to the principal until the whole fund amounted to $10,000, the other one half of the interest to be applied to the "education of the children of the poor", and, when the whole fund amounted to $10,000, then the whole interest of the fund should be applied to the "education of the poor in the academy in the village of Huntington". Then followed other provisions to insure the application of the fund to testator's purpose. Said academy, of which testator was one of the founders in about 1793, was a private educational institution. It continued to function until [***4] 1858 when appellant Union Free School District No. 3 of the Town of Huntington purchased the academy and the land on which it stood, tore down the building, and erected a new school building on such land and adjoining land.In this construction proceeding commenced in 1949 by successor trustees, it was erroneously determined that the trust failed when the academy was absorbed into the common school system. The matter is remitted to the Surrogate's Court.

2. Insofar as some of the present litigants succeed to the interests of parties in Williams v. Williams (8 N.Y. 525 [1853] ), which concerned the precise trust involved in the present case, they are foreclosed from relitigating issues decided in that case. Appellants, Attorney-General and Union Free School District No. 3 of the Town of Huntington, who apparently were not represented in court in the Williams case, are not precluded from litigating any issues here.

3. In the Williams case, the Court of Appeals did not hold that this trust for education was limited in time to the period of existence of a particular institution. The Williams case did hold that charitable trusts had not been abolished by [***5] the Revised Statutes. The State Legislature in 1893 approved of that rule by enacting the so-called Tilden Act (L. 1893, ch. 701, now Personal Property Law, § 12). That conclusion of legislative approval is further strengthened by the fact that in 1870 the Legislature enacted chapter 306 for the specific purpose of empowering the Union Free School District of the Town of Huntington to receive funds from the trust set up by the testator, if the trustees wished to give them.

4. Inasmuch as the facts are not in dispute, testator's intention in creating the trust for the children of the poor is a question of law.

5. Giving the terms of the will a liberal construction and considering the entire applicable portion of the will and not merely isolated segments, testator intended to provide a never-ending source of financial aid to needy students without limiting that charitable purpose of the funds to the lifetime of any specific institution. There are seven references in the will to children of the poor and their education, and testator qualified such references by the mention of the academy on but two occasions. Testator referred to "children" and to the "education of children"  [***6] on thirteen different occasions, while the academy was mentioned by name but five times, and only two of those times were instances from which respondents might draw their conclusion that this trust was limited to the lifetime of the academy. Testator indicated on at least three occasions that he intended that the trust should be "perpetual." He also indicated that, if the academy burned, the funds were to be used at the school next west of the academy. While such references to the academy are such that the more reasonable conclusion is that testator hoped and expected that the education of the needy children would be at the academy rather than elsewhere, the court cannot say, as a matter of law, that the language of the will must be construed to mean that the continued existence of the academy was to be a condition precedent to the continued education of the children of the poor from this fund.

6. The courts below have held that testator was not motivated by a general charitable intent and, therefore, found it unnecessary to determine any other question. Since the court determines that testator did have such a general charitable intent, the case must be sent back to the Surrogate's [***7] Court for the determination of other issues, if any, not previously passed upon, and also the question as to what purposes the trust funds shall now be put.

 

566 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Peters v. New York City Housing Authority


Court of Appeals of New York


307 N.Y. 519; 121 N.E.2d 529; 1954 N.Y. LEXIS 981


Argued June 1, 1954.  
July 14, 1954, decided



DISPOSITION: LEWIS, Ch. J., CONWAY, DESMOND, DYE, FROESSEL and VAN VOORHIS, JJ., concur.

Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff tenants sought review of the decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which modified and affirmed the decision of the trial court, which had cancelled the order of respondent housing authority that had requiring tenants to sign a certificate of non-membership in subversive organizations found on a government list.


OVERVIEW: The Congress provided in 66 U.S. Stat. 403 (1953) that members of organizations designated as subversive by the attorney general could not occupy housing built under the U.S. Housing Act of 1937. The city housing authority adopted a resolution requiring tenants to sign a certificate that they were not members of organizations listed pursuant to Exec. Order No. 9835. The tenants refused to sign. The tenants filed an petition to enjoin the resolution's enforcement. The trial court sustained the petition and annulled the resolution on the grounds that the compilation of the attorney general's list violated due process. The appellate division modified the order and dismissed the proceedings because later governmental actions cured the due process question. The court decided the case on nonconstitutional grounds and remanded it for further proceedings. The court determined that courts would not decide constitutional issues unless it was necessary, that evidence suggested that the tenant's housing was not built under the U.S. Housing Act, and that the list of organizations included groups that had not been designated as subversive by the attorney general.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the trial court, annulling the order of the housing authority that had required tenants to sign certificates of non-membership in designated subversive organizations, and remanded the case for further proceedings.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law - housing - tenant members of subversive organizations - (1) order dismissing proceeding by tenant of housing project, who alleged that New York City Housing Authority had violated rights guaranteed by Federal Constitution in requiring tenant to certify that she was not member of organizations on list containing names of 194 organizations, reversed and matter remitted to Special Term - (2) Independent Offices Appropriations Act of 1953 (66 U.S. Stat. 403; Gwinn Amendment), provided that "no housing unit constructed under the United States Housing Act of 1937,  [***3] as amended, shall be occupied by a person who is a member of an organization designated as subversive by the Attorney General"; record does not show housing project in which tenant lived was constructed under such housing act; facts and law should be developed and determination made whether housing project was encompassed by Gwinn Amendment or whether there was some other source of power for action of Authority - (3) only thirteen of 194 organizations on list have been designated as "subversive"; determination must be made as to whether Congress intended to refer only to organizations specifically designated as "subversive" - (4) if Special Term concludes that Authority exceeded statutory powers, it should consider whether in any event petition should be dismissed and also effect of such dismissal on intervening petitioners.

1. A provision of the Independent Offices Appropriations Act of 1953 (66 U.S. Stat. 403), known as the Gwinn Amendment, provided that "no housing unit constructed under the United States Housing Act of 1937, as amended, shall be occupied by a person who is a member of an organization designated as subversive by the Attorney General", and provided further that [***4] "the foregoing prohibition shall be enforced by the local housing authority". The New York City Housing Authority adopted a resolution providing that "No applicant shall be admitted to, and no tenant shall be permitted to continue to reside in * * * a federally-aided project" unless the applicant or tenant has signed a certificate to the effect that neither he nor any other occupant of the unit "is a member of an organization designated as subversive by the Attorney General". Petitioner, who lives in the Williamsburg Houses Project, was requested by the Authority to sign a certificate entitled "Certification of Nonmembership in Subversive Organizations", reading as follows: "I hereby certify that I am not a member of any of the organizations listed in the document entitled 'Consolidated List, Dated November 10, 1952, of Organizations Designated by the Attorney General of the United States As Within Executive Order No. 9835', attached hereto". The list attached to the certificate set forth the names of 194 organizations. The petition in the present proceeding alleges that the action of the Authority violates the rights of the petitioner guaranteed by the Federal Constitution in [***5] five enumerated respects and that the Authority's action is arbitrary, capricious and unreasonable. Interveners allege that they are tenants of the Authority residing in either the Red Hook Housing Project or the Kingsboro Housing Project, and assert that they are members of the International Workers Order, an organization cited by the Attorney General. An order dismissing the proceeding should be reversed and the matter remitted to Special Term.

2. Although the Gwinn Amendment speaks only of housing units "constructed under the United States Housing Act of 1937, as amended", the Authority has applied the amendment to any "federally-aided project", a term defined in a resolution of the Authority as "any project of the Authority receiving financial assistance from the Federal Government or any project leased by the Authority from the Federal Government." The record does not show that Williamsburg Houses Project was "constructed under" the Housing Act. It should not be determined whether the Authority may, consistent with the Constitution, evict petitioner for failure to sign the requested certificate, if the Gwinn Amendment did not empower the Authority to require such a [***6] certificate from tenants or occupants of Williamsburg Houses. The case should be remitted to Special Term so that the facts and the law may be developed and to determine whether the Williamsburg Housing Project is, in fact, encompassed by the Gwinn Amendment, or whether there was some other source of power for the action of the Authority.

3. The Gwinn Amendment excludes from such units only persons who are members of an "organization designated as subversive by the Attorney General". When Congress enacted the Gwinn Amendment, the Attorney General had been furnishing the Loyalty Review Board of the United States Civil Service Commission with a list of organizations, and in compiling that list had broken it down into six distinct and separate classes, only one of which was entitled "subversive". Although the list attached to the certificate which petitioner was asked to sign contained the names of 194 organizations, only twelve had been designated as "subversive" when the Gwinn Amendment was passed, and only thirteen had been so named by the time the Authority moved to enforce it. Congress may have intended to refer only to those organizations specifically designated by the Attorney [***7] General as "subversive". There was no showing in the record that a tenant, reading the list, had any way of discerning which were the organizations designated by the Attorney General as "subversive". Until there is some showing that the Authority was empowered to do what the record suggests it did, it would be improper and unwise to determine whether or not the Gwinn Amendment or the action of the Authority taken under it was constitutional.

4. Petitioner has pleaded and relied almost exclusively on provisions of the Federal Constitution. Accordingly, upon remission, if the court at Special Term concludes that the Authority exceeded its statutory powers, it should also consider whether it might not be more appropriate to dismiss the petition than to grant the relief sought. In that event, it is for Special Term to consider, in the first instance, what effect the dismissal of the original petition would have on the intervening petitioners.

 

567 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re Richardson


Court of Appeals of New York


307 N.Y. 269; 121 N.E.2d 217; 1954 N.Y. LEXIS 982


Argued June 28, 1954.  
June 28, 1954, decided



DISPOSITION: CONWAY, DESMOND, DYE and FROESSEL, JJ., concur with LEWIS, Ch. J.; VAN VOORHIS, J., concurs in the result with respect to the fourth Assembly district in the eighteenth Senate district, but votes to reverse the order of the Appellate Division and reinstate that of Special Term with respect to the [***2] other Assembly districts involved for the reasons stated in the dissenting opinion by FULD, J.; FULD, J., dissents and votes to reverse in an opinion.

Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant citizens sought review from the judgment of Appellate Division of the Supreme Court (New York), which reversed an order that held the reapportioning of four assembly districts were void.


OVERVIEW: Citizens brought an action challenging the reapportioning of four assembly districts. The lower court reversed a holding that the apportionment was void. The citizens sought review, contending that procedure employed by the city council to reapportion the districts was unconstitutional. On review, the court found that the reapportioning of the four districts complied with applicable constitutional provisions. The court noted that in the absence of proof before the court that the reapportionment challenged was arbitrary and based on personal and political considerations and not on the convenience of the voters or any factor relating to the public interest, and the lines were drawn with the purpose of assuring the continuous election of the candidates of one particular political party and the control of such respective districts by particular political leaders, and in the absence of proof that territory within the districts as reapportioned was not convenient, contiguous and in as compact form as practicable, the court concluded, that the apportionment was not improper.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate division's judgment and affirmed the judgment of the lower court.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law - Assembly districts - (1) lacking proof that reapportionment of county into Assembly districts was arbitrary and was made for political purposes and that territory within Assembly districts as reapportioned was not convenient, contiguous and in as compact form as practicable, no basis existed sufficient in law to support order of Special Term declaring reapportionment void - (2) burden of establishing that reapportionment is unconstitutional rested on petitioners asserting unconstitutionality.

1. In absence of proof before the Special Term that a reapportionment of Kings County into Assembly districts was - as alleged in a petition of citizens of said county and denied by the city council of the City of New York, acting as a board of apportionment - arbitrary and based on personal and political considerations and not on the convenience of the voters or any factor relating to the public interest, and that the lines thereof were drawn with the purpose of assuring the continuous election of [***3] the candidates of one political party and the control of such respective districts by particular political leaders, and in the absence of proof that territory within the Assembly districts as reapportioned was not convenient, contiguous and in as compact form as practicable, there was no basis sufficient in law to support the order made at Special Term declaring such reapportionment void as violative of the State Constitution (art. III, § 5). The new Assembly district lines, altered to conform with the new Senate district boundaries made pursuant to the Reapportionment Act of 1953 (L. 1953, ch. 893, as amd. by L. 1954, chs. 2, 497), could not be fixed without some resulting irregularity and without affecting to some degree - favorably or unfavorably - the factors of convenience and compactness. It is not claimed that the districts were not reapportioned as nearly equal in numbers, excluding aliens, as may be.

2. The burden of establishing that the reapportionment accomplished by respondents is unconstitutional rested upon petitioners, who are the parties asserting its unconstitutionality.

 

568 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


General Electric Co. v. Masters, Inc.


Court of Appeals of New York


307 N.Y. 229; 120 N.E.2d 802; 1954 N.Y. LEXIS 985


Argued April 19, 1954.  
June 4, 1954, decided



DISPOSITION: LEWIS, Ch. J., CONWAY, DESMOND, DYE and FULD, JJ., concur; VAN VOORHIS, J., taking no part.

In each action: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, who were nonsigners of plaintiff companies' free trade agreements, appealed three decisions of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed judgments in favor of plaintiffs in their actions to enjoin defendants from selling plaintiffs' fair traded products for less than their stipulated minimum prices.


OVERVIEW: Defendants in three separate cases were nonsigners of plaintiffs' fair trade agreements, and plaintiffs obtained injunctions to prevent defendants from selling fair traded products at less than their stipulated minimum prices. On appeal, the court affirmed. Defendants' attack on the Feld-Crawford Fair Trade Law, N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law §369-a, was unavailing. The delegation of power and due process arguments were disposed of by an earlier decision of the court, and were no longer open to question. Defendants' economic and policy arguments as to the doubtful wisdom of fair trade laws were properly addressed to the legislature. No equal protection violation was shown because contrary to defendants' assertion, the statute created no unreasonable or arbitrary discrimination against low cost, minimum service retailers. The Maguire Act, 15 U.S.C.S. § 45, which exempted fair trade agreements from federal antitrust laws, did not give the states power over interstate commerce that was denied them under the Constitution. Congress could redefine the distribution of power over interstate commerce, and it could permit states to regulate commerce in a manner that would otherwise not be permissible.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgments in favor of plaintiffs that enjoined defendants from selling plaintiffs' fair traded products for less than their stipulated minimum prices.


HEADNOTES

Trade-marks, trade names and unfair competition - fair trade laws - constitutionality of application of State Fair Trade Law to sales in or affecting interstate commerce pursuant to Federal permissive statute - (1) retailers whose transactions directly or indirectly involve interstate commerce properly enjoined under Feld-Crawford Fair Trade Law ( General Business Law, § 369-a et seq.) from selling manufacturers' products at less than minimum prices stipulated in fair trade agreements of which they were nonsignatories - (2) Maguire Act (66 U.S. Stat. 632) exempting from Federal antitrust laws agreements made pursuant to State fair trade laws and permitting their enforcement against nonsigners engaged in interstate [***3] commerce does not give States unconstitutional power over interstate commerce; Federal statute makes no distinction between sales "in" and those "affecting" interstate commerce - (3) not necessary that State statute be re-enacted - (4) State statute does not deny equal protection of laws; no arbitrary discrimination against low-cost, minimum service retailers; statute is reasonable regulation under police power - (5) State statute not unconstitutional delegation of legislative power nor deprivation of property without due process of law - (6) economic arguments as to wisdom of fair trade laws should be addressed to Legislature - (7) manufacturers' fair trade agreements complied with requirements of State Fair Trade Law.

1. Retailers whose transactions directly or indirectly involve interstate commerce and who are nonsigners of manufacturers' fair trade agreements, were properly enjoined, under the Feld-Crawford Fair Trade Law ( General Business Law, § 369-a et seq.) from selling the manufacturers' fair traded products at less than their stipulated minimum prices.

2. The Maguire Act (66 U.S. Stat. 632) which exempts from the operation of Federal antitrust laws fair trade [***4] agreements made pursuant to existing or future State laws and which permits their enforcement against nonsigners engaged in interstate commerce, does not give the States power over interstate commerce denied them under the Federal Constitution. The distinction between commerce, primarily local, which indirectly affects interstate commerce, and that which is directly of national concern, is drawn merely to determine the extent of State power in areas where Congress by silence has allowed its own power to remain dormant. Such distinction does not operate to restrict the power of a State acting under an express grant from Congress. Moreover, the Maguire Act makes no distinction between sales "affecting" and sales directly "in" interstate commerce but was, rather, designed to apply to commodities, contracts and activities in or affecting interstate commerce. "Compulsion" against nonsigners of fair trade agreements, expressly authorized by Congress, does not transgress any constitutional prohibition.

3. The Feld-Crawford Act was previously held to apply to nonsigners engaged in interstate commerce and, although such application was later declared unconstitutional by the United States [***5] Supreme Court, the subsequent passage of the Maguire Act removed the barrier to complete interstate application of the State statute. Under such circumstances the Constitution does not require that the State statute be re-enacted in order to be effective.

4. The New York Fair Trade Law does not create any unreasonable or arbitrary discrimination against low-cost, minimum service retailers and does not deny equal protection of the laws in violation of both Federal and State Constitutions. It is wholly inapplicable to unmarked goods and, as to marked goods, merely permits a manufacturer to prevent retailers from impairing the value of its trade-mark and good will by selling its identified products at unreasonably low prices. Moreover, the statute constitutes a reasonable regulation under the police power.

5. The State statute does not constitute an unlawful delegation of legislative powers to private persons in violation of the State Constitution (N.Y. Const., art. III, § 1), nor a deprivation of property without due process of law in violation of both Federal and State Constitutions. ( Bourjois Sales Corp. v. Dorfman, 273 N.Y. 167, followed.)

6. Economic and policy [***6] arguments concerning the wisdom of fair trade legislation are properly addressed to the Legislature. Such arguments, alone, do not provide sufficient reason for the court to reopen settled constitutional issues.

7. The separate fair trade contracts of the manufacturers involved here complied with that portion of the statute ( General Business Law, § 369-a) which sanctions such contracts between "vendor" and "buyer".

 

569 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re Estate of ENDEMANN


Court of Appeals of New York


307 N.Y. 100; 120 N.E.2d 514; 1954 N.Y. LEXIS 993


Argued April 7, 1954.  
May 27, 1954, decided



DISPOSITION: LEWIS, Ch. J., CONWAY, DYE, FULD, FROESSEL and VAN VOORHIS, JJ., concur.

Ordered accordingly.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant and respondent filed cross appeals to an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed a surrogate's court's order modifying a pro forma order fixing appellant's estate tax, thereby increasing the amount of the tax.


OVERVIEW: The trial court modified a pro forma order fixing an estate tax, thereby increasing the tax amount. Appellant filed an estate tax report, when decedent died, in which she valued the retirement benefits at zero. However, the appraiser and the trial court included it in the gross estate for tax purposes as a transfer taking effect at decedent's death, and valued it in accordance with a certificate of valuation. The lower appellate court held that the value of appellant's annuity, both at the time of its creation and at the time of decedent's death, was the same. The court on review modified this order. The court held that: (1) the state constitution did not forbid taxing the annuity; (2) decedent's choice of an option constituted a transfer to appellant intended to take effect at the time of his death; and (3) the appraiser used the correct method of valuing appellant's annuity.


OUTCOME: The court modified the lower appellate court's order, and affirmed it as modified.


HEADNOTES

Taxation - estate tax - exemption of retirement allowance from estate tax - (1) decedent, on retirement in 1936 from New York City employment, made irrevocable election of option 3 as to retirement benefits; option 3 provided him with life retirement allowance of $3,140.60 and his wife, after his death, with life retirement allowance of $1,570.20; tax appraiser included in gross estate for tax purposes, as transfer taking effect at decedent's death, widow's retirement allowance, valuing it at $8,572; appraiser's determination was correct -  [***2] (2) State Constitution (art. XVI, § 5), which subjects to taxation salaries and other compensation "except pensions" paid to public officers was intended to except pensions from income tax and does not forbid estate tax on retirement allowance payable to decedent's wife; nor is such taxation invalidated by Constitution (art. V, § 7), stating that, after July 1, 1940, membership in public retirement system shall be contractual relationship, benefits of which shall not be diminished, since estate tax, as applicable to retirement benefits, was in effect before 1940 - (3) decedent, when he chose option 3, made to his wife "a transfer * * * intended to take effect in possession or enjoyment at or after his death", which transfer was includible in his gross estate (Tax Law, § 249-r, subd. 3) - (4) appraiser was correct when he accepted valuation of $8,572, made by Superintendent of Insurance (Tax Law, § 249-v), of property right which came to widow at decedent's death.

1. Decedent, upon retirement in 1936 from his employment with the City of New York, made, pursuant to what is now the New York City Administrative Code (§ B3-46.0), an irrevocable election of "Option 3" in respect [***3] to retirement benefits payable by the New York City employees' retirement system, of which he was a member. Said option 3 provided him a retirement allowance of $3,140.60 for his life and, after his death, provided his wife a retirement allowance for her life of $1,570.20. When he died in 1947, his executrix filed a New York estate tax report in which she valued the retirement benefits at "0". The tax appraiser, and the Surrogate in his pro forma taxing order, included in the gross estate for tax purposes, as a transfer taking effect at decedent's death, the widow's retirement allowance, valuing it at $8,572 in accordance with a certificate of valuation thereof made by the Superintendent of Insurance for the appraiser under the Tax Law ( § 249-v). That $8,572 represented, according to the appropriate tables, the calculated present value, as of decedent's death, of a life annuity to the widow, then seventy-three years old, of $1,570.20 per year. On appeal from the pro forma order, the Surrogate modified the order by excluding from the gross estate any value for the widow's retirement allowance, on the ground that, although the widow's allowance was "a transfer * * * intended [***4] to take effect in possession or enjoyment at or after his [the decedent's] death" (Tax Law, § 249-r, subd. 3), the State Constitution (art. XVI, § 5) forbade taxation of it since it was a "pension". The modification by the Surrogate was improper, and the appraiser's inclusion in the gross estate of the widow's retirement allowance, valuing it at $8,572, was correct.

2. The State Constitution (art. XVI, § 5) does not forbid taxing this retirement allowance. When the 1938 Constitutional Convention adopted said section 5, making subject to taxation all salaries and other compensation "except pensions" paid to public officers and employees, it was putting into the Constitution the already existing statutory law that public officers and employees had to pay income taxes on their earnings and added, out of caution, the word "pensions" to make sure that pensions would not be subject to income tax. When, in 1938, section 7 of article V of the State Constitution was adopted, making membership in a public retirement system after July 1, 1940, a contractual relationship, the benefits of which were not to be impaired, retirement benefits were already subject to estate taxes by reason [***5] of the Tax Law ( § 249-kk) which was passed in 1930. In 1947, the Civil Service Law ( § 91) confirmed and continued the taxability of retirement benefits by the estate tax.

3. Decedent, when he chose option 3, made to his wife "a transfer * * * intended to take effect in possession or enjoyment at or after his death" which transfer was includible in his gross estate (Tax Law, § 249-r, subd. 3). Decedent could have decided to use up his retirement benefits by electing a $3,747.69 retirement allowance for himself, but instead he accepted for his life a lesser retirement allowance of $3,141.60, plus an agreement by the retirement system to pay his widow $1,570.20 per year after his death, should she survive. Thus, decedent, in effect, created an annuity for his widow to take effect at his death.

4. The appraiser was correct in accepting the valuation made by the Superintendent of Insurance under the Tax Law ( § 249-v, which says such valuation is "conclusive") of the property right which came to the widow at decedent's death. At his death the appropriate tables showed that the present value of $1 per year for the life of a person aged seventy-three (like decedent's widow)  [***6] was $5.45928, which, multiplied by the $1,570.20 retirement allowance, made a value of $8,572.

 

570 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re Fifth Madison Corporation


Court of Appeals of New York


306 N.Y. 949; 120 N.E.2d 221; 1954 N.Y. LEXIS 1247


Argued March 2, 1954  
April 22, 1954, decided


HEADNOTES

Mortgages - reorganization - allowances to counsel - corporate owner of real property, organized pursuant to reorganization of mortgage in 1937, applied for modification of trust indenture (Real Property Law, § 122-a) - modification plan not approved by Supreme Court and proceeding discontinued with direction that allowances to counsel be made - contention in Court of Appeals that court was without power to award counsel fees where plan was not approved and foreclosure action was not pending - order granting allowances properly affirmed.

 

571 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Daniman v. Board of Education


Court of Appeals of New York


306 N.Y. 532; 119 N.E.2d 373; 1954 N.Y. LEXIS 1013


Argued November 24, 1953.  
April 22, 1954, decided



DISPOSITION: LEWIS, Ch. J., FROESSEL and VAN VOORHIS, JJ., concur with CONWAY, J.; DESMOND, J., dissents in opinion in which DYE and FULD, JJ., concur.

Orders affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner teachers sought review of orders entered by the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the Second Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed orders of the trial court that denied the teachers' application for an order directing respondents, boards of education, to annul the teachers' dismissal from their positions and to reinstate them without prejudice.


OVERVIEW: The teachers appeared before a Senate subcommittee and refused to answer questions regarding Communist party membership, basing the refusal upon the privilege against self-incrimination. The boards of education terminated the teachers' employment and declared their positions vacant pursuant to the New York City, N.Y., City Charter § 903, which provided for the vacatur of an office or employment if, while appearing before a legislative committee, an employee of the city willfully refused to answer any question regarding the property, government, or affairs of the city on the ground that his answer would tend to incriminate him. The teachers commenced two proceedings challenging their terminations. The trial court concluded that § 903 applied and that it had been violated by the teachers. The appellate court affirmed. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that: 1) § 903 was applicable to a hearing before a federal legislative committee; and 2) the teachers were employees of the city and therefore subject to the provisions of § 903.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the appellate court that affirmed the orders of the trial court that denied the teachers' application for an order directing the boards of education to annul their dismissal.


HEADNOTES

Schools - teachers - termination of New York City employment by refusal to testify before Federal committee - (1) petitioners, New York City schoolteachers,  [***2] at hearing before United States Senate committee investigating security laws, refused to answer question as to their membership in Communist party, basing their refusal upon privilege against self incrimination (U.S. Const., 5th Amendt.); resolutions of board of education and board of higher education of New York City, terminating petitioners' employment under New York City Charter (§ 903), valid - (2) under section 903, assertion of privilege against self incrimination is equivalent to resignation - (3) no conflict exists between section 903 and equally valid though differing procedures under other State statutes - (4) Communist party is continuing conspiracy against our Government, and inquiry into membership therein concerns city employee's "official conduct" within meaning of section 903 - (5) section 903 is applicable to hearing before Federal legislative committee - (6) petitioners are employees of City of New York (Administrative Code of City of New York, § 981-1.0, subd. 7) - (7) that, when New York City Charter was approved and became effective, Legislature may not have had in mind specific purpose for which section 903 is now being used, immaterial.

1. Petitioners,  [***3] teachers in public schools and public colleges of the City of New York, in answer to subpoenas, appeared at a hearing before a committee of the United States Senate investigating the administration of the Internal Security Act and other internal security laws, and refused to answer a question whether each of them was presently or had ever been a member of the Communist party. Petitioners based such refusal upon the privilege against self incrimination (U.S. Const., 5th Amendt.). Resolutions of the board of education and of the board of higher education of the City of New York, which, pursuant to section 903 of the New York City Charter, terminated petitioners' employment and declared their positions vacant, were valid.

2. Section 903 of the New York City Charter provides that if any "employee" of the City of New York, who, having appeared before "any legislative committee * * *" shall refuse to testify or to answer any question regarding the property, government or affairs of the city or regarding the "official conduct" of any employee of the city on the ground that his answer would tend to incriminate him, his term of employment shall terminate and his employment be vacant.  [***4] Under this section, the assertion of the privilege against self incrimination is equivalent to a resignation.

3. There is no conflict between section 903 of the charter and the equally valid though differing procedures under the Feinberg Law (L. 1949, ch. 360) and under sections 2554, 2573 and 6206 of the Education Law, which guarantee to teachers the right to hold their respective positions during good behavior and efficient and competent service and not to be removed except for cause after a hearing by the affirmative vote of the majority of the board.

4. The Communist party is a continuing conspiracy against our Government; and an inquiry into past and present membership in the Communist party is an inquiry regarding the "official conduct" of an employee of the City of New York. Loyalty to our Government goes to the very heart of official conduct in service rendered in all branches of Government; and communism is opposed to such loyalty.

5. Section 903 of the charter is applicable to a hearing before a Federal legislative committee. The language in section 903 reading "any legislative committee, or any officer, board or body authorized to conduct any hearing [***5] or inquiry" was not intended to except a legislative committee of the United States.

6. Petitioners are employees of the City of New York. While the educational affairs of each city are under the general management and control of the board of education, such board is subject to municipal control in matters not strictly educational or pedagogic. Moreover, the State has power to determine what shall constitute a vacatur of public employment, and, in enacting statutes, to define terms used therein as in its wisdom it sees fit. Thus, the Legislature has said, in the New York City Administrative Code (§ 981-1.0, subd. 7), that "Any person whose salary in whole or in part is paid out of the city treasury" is an employee of the city; and petitioners, as teachers, were so paid.

7. That, at the times the New York City Charter was approved and became effective, the Legislature may not have had in mind the specific purpose for which section 903 is now being used, is immaterial, for section 903 protects the people of the city and the chartered municipalities of the State from dangers encompassed by the language of the statute, even though the precise danger may not have been envisioned [***6] at the moment of passage.

577 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Grant


Court of Appeals of New York


306 N.Y. 258; 117 N.E.2d 542; 1954 N.Y. LEXIS 1043


Argued October 21, 1953.  
January 15, 1954, decided



DISPOSITION: LEWIS, Ch. J., CONWAY, DESMOND, DYE, FULD and FROESSEL, JJ., concur.

Judgments reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Nassau County Court (New York), which affirmed a judgment convicting defendant of violating an ordinance prohibiting through or transient traffic in a certain area and declared unconstitutional that portion of the ordinance prohibiting transient traffic.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of violating an ordinance prohibiting through or transient vehicular traffic within a certain area while taking a fellow employee home after work to a location one and one-half blocks west of the area. Defendant challenged the ordinance on the ground that it was not a valid exercise of any power delegated to the town by the state. The ordinance was allegedly adopted as a result of complaints from residents who objected to the volume of traffic caused mainly by employees of a particular company. Reversing, the court held that under N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 54, residents of a particular area did not possess and could not be granted proprietary rights to the use of highways therein in priority to or exclusive of use by the general public. The court found that such power was not conferred upon town or village boards by § 90, either. Therefore, the ordinance was unconstitutional as a whole.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's judgment and dismissed the information against defendant.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations - prohibition against through traffic - validity of ordinance - (1) ordinance adopted by town and forbidding "through or transient vehicular traffic" in designated area in town or village, invalid - (2) streets are for benefit of all people - (3) authority to prohibit through traffic not delegated to local authorities (Vehicle and Traffic Law, §§ 54, 90, subds. 4, 5) - (4) elimination of word "transient" would not validate ordinance.

1. A town ordinance forbade all "through or transient [***2] vehicular traffic" in a designated area in or near a village and evidently had no purpose other than to reserve the streets in such area for the benefit of the residents of the area as against a large number of employees of a nearby plant. No arterial "through" highway or substitute was designated; nor was it shown that any physically suitable alternate route existed which could be so designated. This ordinance was an unreasonable exercise of the power to regulate traffic and was in excess of local power.

2. Political subdivisions and municipal corporations hold the fee of streets for the benefit of all the people. Streets are subject exclusively to regulation and control by the State except insofar as the Legislature has delegated power to the local authorities.

3. The Vehicle and Traffic Law ( § 54) stated that, except as otherwise provided, local authorities had no power to pass ordinances excluding people from the free use of public highways. Local authorities were authorized to make various types of traffic ordinances (§ 90) and "In addition * * * and subject to the restrictions hereinafter provided * * * to make * * * such additional reasonable ordinances * * * as [***3] special local conditions may require," (subd. 4) but they had no power to pass any ordinance inconsistent with the provisions of the Vehicle and Traffic Law ( § 90). This ordinance was not such an additional, reasonable one. The power to enact this ordinance does not parallel any of the other powers granted in section 90.

4. The elimination of the word "transient" would not change the scope of the ordinance or serve to validate it.

 

578 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Bell


Court of Appeals of New York


306 N.Y. 110; 115 N.E.2d 821; 1953 N.Y. LEXIS 774


Argued October 15, 1953.  
November 20, 1953, decided



DISPOSITION: LEWIS, Ch. J., CONWAY, DYE and FROESSEL, JJ., concur with VAN VOORHIS, J.; DESMOND, J., concurs in separate opinion in which FULD, J., concurs.

Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The people challenged the judgment of the Nassau County Court (New York), which reversed a judgment convicting defendants of violating N.Y. Penal Law § 1990-a(2) by loitering in a railroad station.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were convicted of loitering in a railroad station, in violation of N.Y. Penal Law § 1990-a(2), which conviction was reversed by the lower court. The court heard the case to determine the constitutionality of the statute in which defendants contended that the last clause of the statute rendered the statute unconstitutional and void for indefiniteness. The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court dismissing the charge for lack of proof. The court also held that the statute was constitutional and the last clause was not indefinite because it was limited in its effect by the preceding words to which it was an adjunct; this was merely to say, that a "satisfactory explanation," required by the last clause of the statute, was one which indicated that the suspected person was not loitering.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court, which reversed a judgment convicting defendants of loitering in a railroad station. The court also upheld the constitutionality of the statute which they were accused of violating.


HEADNOTES

Crimes - loitering about railway station - constitutional law - (1) person who loiters about railway station and cannot "give satisfactory explanation" is guilty of offense (Penal Law, § 1990-a, subd. 2); statute is not void for indefiniteness; "loiters" is well-understood word and in this context is further restricted by requiring questioning on scene; "explanation" need not satisfy any policeman but must show person is not trespasser -  [***2] (2) such construction follows from canon of construction ejusdem generis and legislative intent - (3) conviction of defendants properly reversed and information dismissed for lack of proof; possibility was not excluded that defendants remained in station for legitimate purpose.

1. The provision in the Penal Law ( § 1990-a, subd. 2) that "Any person who loiters about any toilet, station or station platform of a subway or elevated railway or of a railroad, or who is found sleeping therein or thereon and who is unable to give satisfactory explanation of his presence is guilty of an offense" is constitutional. Loitering is a well-understood term, in common usage. The inclusion of the words "and who is unable to give satisfactory explanation of his presence" does not render the statute void for indefiniteness. They do not broaden but restrict the offense. They outline a procedure to be followed in ascertaining whether a person is loitering. It was not intended that the explanation must be satisfactory to an individual police officer. The explanation must show that the person has come to the premises under circumstances which do not render him a trespasser.The words do not furnish [***3] a basis for conviction by themselves and do not add any substantive element to the offense.

2. This construction follows from the canon of construction ejusdem generis and from the legislative intent which was to prevent persons with no occasion to be there, from remaining in the stated premises and to prevent the danger to the public which might otherwise develop.

3. However, the conviction of the defendants was properly reversed and the information dismissed for lack of proof. The evidence was that they were observed in a railroad station about 5:00 P.M.; that they did not take the 5:11 train but remained there until about 5:25 when the police arrived and asked defendants what they were doing; that they said they had just arrived whereas in fact they had been in the station for about three quarters of an hour, and that no railroad tickets were found on them. The police failed to pursue their inquiry. The possibility was not excluded that defendants entered the station and remained there for some legitimate purpose other than taking a train.

580 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Kaskel v. Impellitteri


Court of Appeals of New York


306 N.Y. 73; 115 N.E.2d 659; 1953 N.Y. LEXIS 776


Submitted June 4, 1953.  
October 23, 1953, decided



DISPOSITION: On appeal from judgment: Judgment affirmed, with costs. LEWIS, Ch. J., CONWAY, DYE and FROESSEL, JJ., concur with DESMOND, J.; VAN VOORHIS, J., dissents in opinion in which FULD, J., concurs.

On appeal from [***2] order: Appeal dismissed (Civ. Prac. Act, § 589, subd. 4, par. [b]; § 603; Langan v. First Trust & Deposit Co., 296 N.Y. 60; Evadan Realty Corp. v. Patterson, 297 N.Y. 732). No opinion. LEWIS, Ch. J., CONWAY, DESMOND, DYE, FULD, FROESSEL and VAN VOORHIS, JJ., concur.

On appeal from judgment: Judgment affirmed.

On appeal from order: Appeal dismissed. [See 306 N.Y. 609.] 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer filed a complaint under N.Y. C.P.L.R. 106, 112, and 113 against respondent city. The taxpayer sought review of the order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which dismissed the taxpayer's complaint and denied a motion for an injunction pendente lite.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer disputed the trial court's order agreeing with the city that an area was substandard and insanitary. The court affirmed because there was ample evidence to justify the determination by modern tests and that the whole 6.32 acres, taken together, could reasonably be considered a single "area" for clearance and redevelopment purposes. Power to make that determination had been lodged by N.Y. Const. art. XVIII, § 1, and N.Y. Gen. Mun. Law § 72-k in the city planning commission and the board of estimate and their finding was not corrupt or irrational or baseless. There was nothing for the court to do about it, unless every act and decision of other departments of government was subject to revision by the court. Since no corruption or fraud was charged, the taxpayer could not succeed in such a suit.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed and the appeal dismissed.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations - eminent domain - slum clearance and redevelopment - (1) statute (General Municipal Law, § 72-k) which provides that "substandard and insanitary areas" may be acquired for slum clearance is constitutional; slum area need not be rehabilitated only by reconstruction for low cost housing; words "substandard and insanitary areas" supply sufficient legal standard - (2) record justifies determination by planning commission and board of estimate of New York City that whole of defined area might reasonably be considered single area for clearance and redevelopment purposes - (3) no question of fact presented; question is one of power and power to make determination has been conferred on defendants - (4) appeal on certified question from denial of injunction pendente lite [***3] dismissed (Civ. Prac. Act, § 589, subd. 4, par. [b]; § 603).

1. Section 1 of article XVIII of the State Constitution authorizes the Legislature to provide for slum clearance "by such means and upon such terms and conditions as it may prescribe" and provides that "substandard and insanitary areas" may be acquired for that purpose. Section 72-k of the General Municipal Law, which also provides that "substandard and insanitary areas" may be acquired for slum clearance, is constitutional. Slum clearance is in itself a public purpose separate and distinct from the objects to which the land may subsequently be devoted. The State Constitution does not require that slums be rehabilitated exclusively by reconstruction for low cost housing. The area may be rehabilitated according to any design and for any purpose which renders the area no longer substandard or insanitary. The words "substandard and insanitary areas" have been used in other statutes and supply a legal standard for the determination of what a slum is. The statute is valid against an attack that it fails to prescribe standards or safeguards governing the clearance, replanning, reconstruction and rehabilitation of [***4] substandard and insanitary areas, and the means, terms and conditions of carrying the constitutional provision into effect are stated with sufficient particularity.

2. The New York City Planning Commission and the Board of Estimate of the City of New York determined that a substantial part of an area covering 6.32 acres in the city of New York was "substandard and insanitary", and that the whole of the area might reasonably be considered a single area for clearance and redevelopment purposes. Approximately 20% of the land is occupied by dwellings, all but one of which are more than sixty years old, 7% by hotels and rooming houses, 34% by parking lots, where once there were outmoded buildings, and 39% by nonresidential structures. The record justifies this determination. The courts may not interfere when the finding made by these bodies was not made corruptly or irrationally or without basis.

3. Judgment was properly granted dismissing, without a trial, the action of a taxpayer (General Municipal Law, § 51) who demanded that a trial be held to determine the validity of the conclusion that the area was substandard and insanitary. Since no corruption or fraud was charged,  [***5] the taxpayer cannot succeed unless there be a total lack of power in defendants to proceed as they have. The acts complained of must be fraudulent or amount to a waste of public property in the sense that they represent the use of public property or funds for entirely illegal purposes. There is no real question of fact involved herein since the details as to the age, condition and present use of property are undisputed. The question is only one of power and the power to make this determination has been conferred by statute on these defendants.

4. An appeal, taken by permission of the Appellate Division on a certified question, from an order of the Appellate Division affirming an order of Special Term denying an injunction pendente lite, must be dismissed (Civ. Prac. Act, § 589, subd. 4, par. [b]; § 603).
