582 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Saint Nicholas Cathedral of Russian Orthodox Church v. Kedroff


Court of Appeals of New York


306 N.Y. 38; 114 N.E.2d 197; 1953 N.Y. LEXIS 784


Argued February 24, 1953.  
July 14, 1953, decided



DISPOSITION: LEWIS, Ch. J., DYE and FROESSEL, JJ., concur with CONWAY, J.; DESMOND, J., dissents in opinion in which FULD, J., concurs; VAN VOORHIS, J., taking no part.

Motion granted, etc. [See 306 N.Y. 572.] 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant church appointees filed a motion for an order to amend a remittitur to direct judgment for the appointees in plaintiff corporation's suit in ejectment against the appointees from a cathedral.


OVERVIEW: The cathedral was dedicated for the use and benefit of the faithful in the North American Diocese of the Russian Orthodox Church (diocese). The Russian Orthodox Church sent the appointees to take control of the diocese. The corporation was the holder of the record title to the cathedral premises. The corporation filed a suit in ejectment against the appointees. Upon remand, the appointees filed a motion to amend the remittitur for an order to direct judgment for the appointees. The court granted the motion to amend the remittitur the extent that a new trial was ordered. The court remitted the case for trial. The corporation that held title to the cathedral premises was entitled to possession thereof. There could be no doubt that the trustees of the corporation were charged with the duty of caring for and maintaining the property. There was no doubt that the trustees had to administer these temporalities in accordance with and subject to the denominational usage of the property for the beneficiaries for whom the trust was originally created. The constitutional validity of the common-law disposition of the case was still undetermined.


OUTCOME: The court granted the motion to amend the remittitur to the extent that a new trial was ordered. The court remitted the case for trial.


HEADNOTES

Religious corporations and associations - administration of church property - (1) United States Supreme Court reversed determination by Court of Appeals that article 5-C of Religious Corporations Law was constitutional and made judgment for plaintiff mandatory in ejectment action to recover [***2] possession of cathedral of North American Diocese of Russian Orthodox Church; new trial granted since in prior decision Court of Appeals had stated that, in any event, new trial would have been required under common-law principles - (2) United States Supreme Court did not consider validity of such common-law disposition - (3) civil courts may operate in disputes involving religious bodies - (4) discretionary power to determine whether leaders of North American metropolitan district were proper trustees to carry out religious trust, has not been exercised - (5) civil court must determine whether church authority is duly constituted - (6) proper administration of trust requires determination whether Archbishop of North American metropolitan district or appointee of Moscow Patriarchate be put in possession of cathedral.

1. In this ejectment action to recover possession of the cathedral, located in New York City, of the North American Diocese of the Russian Orthodox Church, the Court of Appeals (302 N.Y. 1) had decided that, under article 5-C of the Religious Corporations Law, final judgment for plaintiff, owner of record title to the cathedral premises and representing the autonomous [***3] North American district, was mandatory. The United States Supreme Court (344 U.S. 94) reversed that decision and remanded the case to the Court of Appeals "for such further action as it deems proper" and determined that said article 5-C, as construed, was an unconstitutional interference with the free exercise of religion. The Court of Appeals had pointed out that, were it not for the decisive effect of article 5-C, a new trial would have been required, in any event, under common-law principles. Accordingly, the matter should be remitted to the State Supreme Court for trial.

2. The United States Supreme Court did not determine the constitutional validity of the alternative common-law disposition of the case.

3. Civil courts can and must operate in some areas in controversies involving religious bodies ( Watson v. Jones, 13 Wall. [U.S.] 679, distinguished).

4. In the prior opinion, the Court of Appeals had stated that, as a matter of common law, there was room for an exercise of the discretionary power of the State Supreme Court over the conduct of trustees, in favor of the North American metropolitan district, and that the conclusion would have been warranted that [***4] the leaders of the North American metropolitan district were the trustees who might be relied upon to carry out more effectively and faithfully the purposes of the religious trust and who would administer the temporalities of the Saint Nicholas Cathedral for the benefit of the faithful for whose use it was originally dedicated. It was pointed out, however, that such discretionary power had not been exercised.

5. The highest church authority or tribunal, whose decision is to be accorded final and conclusive effect, must be capable of functioning freely, with its activities directed by churchmen in the interests of the church and in accord with the organic law of the church. Where a property right turns upon a decision of the church authority, the civil court is under a duty to ascertain whether the purported authority is duly constituted and functioning. To reject the claim of plaintiff that the central church authority had been absorbed by the Russian Government and its action deprived of religious significance, without hearing and weighing all the evidence, would be unjust and would constitute an infringement of the right to free exercise of religion.

6. Plaintiff corporation,  [***5] which holds title to the cathedral premises, is undoubtedly entitled to possession thereof, and the trustees of the corporation, who are now members of the North American metropolitan district, are charged with the duty of caring for and maintaining the property and must administer it in accord with and subject to the usage and for the beneficiaries for whom the trust was originally created. The question of the proper administration of the cathedral trust requires a determination as to whether the Archbishop of the North American metropolitan district or the appointee of the Moscow Patriarchate is to be put in possession of the cathedral.

 

583 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Franklin Nat'l Bank


Court of Appeals of New York


305 N.Y. 453; 113 N.E.2d 796; 1953 N.Y. LEXIS 787


Argued May 26, 1953.  
July 14, 1953, decided



DISPOSITION: LEWIS, Ch. J., CONWAY, DYE and VAN VOORHIS, JJ., concur with DESMOND, J.; FULD, J., dissents in opinion in which FROESSEL, J., concurs.

Judgment accordingly.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant national bank sought review of an order of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court, Second Judicial Department (New York), which reversed a trial court's judgment for the national bank and directed entry of a judgment in favor of plaintiff State, restraining the national bank from using the "saving" or "savings" in its advertising and from soliciting deposits as a savings bank.


OVERVIEW: The State brought an action against the national bank for alleged violations of N.Y. Banking Law § 258(1). The trial court found in favor of the national bank and dismissed the complaint. The appellate court reversed, restraining the national bank from soliciting "savings accounts" and advertising in violation of § 258. On further review, the court affirmed but modified the appellate court's order to strike portions that related to soliciting accounts as a savings bank because there had been no evidence of that violation. The court agreed that it was undisputed that the national bank had used the words "saving" and "savings" in its advertising and in the conduct of its banking operations in violation of § 258. There was no contrary federal statute or issue of preemption because the Federal Reserve Act, 12 U.S.C.S. § 371, did not regulate advertising to prevent deception but rather prescribed the particular kind of business that national banks could conduct. The purpose of New York's statute was to prevent the public from being misled into believing that commercial banks were mutual savings banks.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the appellate court's judgment for the national bank as modified.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law - banks [***2] and banking - national and commercial banks may not use word "savings" - (1) national bank properly enjoined from using or advertising word "saving" or "savings" (Banking Law, § 258, subd. 1) - (2) "savings" bank in this State means mutual nonprofit savings institution; commercial banks, whether State or national, may not use word "savings" - (3) national banks authorized to receive "savings deposits and to pay interest" (U.S. Code, tit. 12, § 371); our statute merely prohibits them from misleading public into believing that commercial bank is mutual savings bank; no direct conflict between statutes; national banks have prospered with interest-bearing accounts of this type without using word "savings" - (4) Banking Law (§ 258, subd. 1) also provides that bank such as defendant shall not "solicit or receive deposits as a savings bank"; but defendant does not violate this part of statute; injunction against violation of this part is stricken.

1. Judgment restraining a national bank from advertising or otherwise using the word "saving" or "savings" in its dealings with the public (Banking Law, § 258, subd. 1) should be affirmed.

2. The statute applies to commercial banks like [***3] defendant. Commercial banks, whether State or national, are profit-making corporations owned by stockholders; while savings banks in New York State are mutual institutions having no stockholders and earning money for the depositors, their fundamental function being the protection of small deposits by conservatism in investments (Banking Law, arts. III, V, VI). Our statute is old and reasonable (cf. L. 1858, ch. 132). It does not prevent defendant from carrying on a particular kind of business but does forbid a misleading description of that business.

3. National banks are authorized "to receive time and savings deposits and to pay interest on the same", and to exercise "incidental powers" (U.S. Code, tit. 12, §§ 24, 371). Restrictions on their advertising (U.S. Code, tit. 12, §§ 583-586; now tit. 18, § 709) contain no prohibition against use of the word "saving" or "savings". Nevertheless, national banks are subject in many ways to the general nondiscriminatory laws of the States in which they exist, and must abide by State regulations insofar as the latter do not collide directly with Federal laws or frustrate national banking policy or impair the position of national banks [***4] in discharging their duties. The New York statute permits national banks to advertise for and accept interest deposits of the savings type, but not to mislead the public by the use of the long-established word "savings" into believing that a commercial bank is a mutual savings bank. The statute does not directly conflict with any overriding Federal statute. Congress did not intend to authorize national banks to use deceptive verbiage in order to get interest deposits. The weight of evidence confirms the finding of the Appellate Division - which reversed Special Term - that the national banks in this State which have obeyed our statute have nevertheless prospered and have greatly increased the number of their interest accounts.

4. Section 258 of our Banking Law also provides that only a savings bank or a savings and loan association shall "solicit or receive deposits as a savings bank". But there is no evidence that defendant violates or intends to violate this part of the statute; hence this part of the injunction should be stricken.

 

584 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


People v. Learnard


Court of Appeals of New York


305 N.Y. 495; 114 N.E.2d 9; 1953 N.Y. LEXIS 792


Argued June 5, 1953.  
July 14, 1953, decided



DISPOSITION: LEWIS, Ch. J., CONWAY, DYE, FULD, FROESSEL and VAN VOORHIS, JJ., concur.

Order reversed, etc.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant State sought review from the judgment of the Montgomery County Supreme Court (New York), which dismissed charges against defendant for violating N.Y. Veh. & Traf. Law § 11(1), which dismissed the information, which discharged defendant, and which remitted to him a fine of $ 10.00.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, a New York resident employed by a Delaware corporation, was convicted of driving an unlicensed vehicle, a motor vehicle not registered and licensed in New York State. The vehicle consisted of two units, one a motor tractor that had been registered and licensed in Rhode Island, and the other a trailer that had been registered and licensed in North Carolina. The lower court reversed the conviction and the State appealed. On review, the court found that because the offense consisted of driving an unlicensed vehicle defendant driver could not escape prosecution by showing that another was the owner of the vehicle.


OUTCOME: The court remanded the matter to the lower court for a determination on the facts.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law - motor vehicles in interstate commerce - highways - State registration requirements - (1) truck driver, driving tractor trailer registered in other State from one city to another city in New York and containing goods transported from another State to first city, properly convicted of operating motor vehicle not registered in New York - (2) New York State does not require registration of motor vehicles owned by nonresidents whose States grant us reciprocal exemption (Vehicle and Traffic Law, § 11, subd. 1; § 51, subd. 1), but registration is required of vehicles [***2] transporting "property for hire or profit from one point in this state to another point in this state" (§ 51, subd. 3) - (3) States may regulate and exact compensation for use of highways - (4) Federal Motor Carrier Act does not occupy entire field of legislation - (5) truck driver guilty, even though he is not owner - (6) conviction erroneously reversed on law; matter remitted to County Court for determination on facts (Code Crim. Pro., § 543-a).

1. A motor vehicle, consisting of a tractor which had been registered and licensed in Rhode Island and a trailer which had been registered and licensed in North Carolina, contained several lots of merchandise, all in their original packages, being transported in interstate commerce on a single waybill from Maryland to a city in New York State. The goods had been carried to another city in New York State on another vehicle operated by defendant's employer and had stayed for a short period of time in the employer's warehouse in that city. The driver of the vehicle was rightly convicted and fined for operating on the public highways of New York State a motor vehicle not registered and licensed in New York State (Vehicle and Traffic Law,  [***3] § 11, subd. 1).

2. The Vehicle and Traffic Law prohibits the operation of any motor vehicle on the public highways of the State without first being registered under New York law (§ 11, subd. 1), but exempts vehicles owned by nonresidents whose States grant reciprocal exemptions (§ 51, subd. 1). However, it excepts from such exemption and requires the licensing of any motor vehicle "which is operated on any public highway of this state to transport * * * property for hire or profit from one point in this state to another point in this state" (§ 51, subd. 3).

3. The New York law imposes a burden of sorts on property moving in interstate commerce. But the States, under their police power, may, without violating the commerce clause of the United States Constitution, regulate the use of their own highways and exact compensation for that use.

4. The Federal Motor Carrier Act not only does not so occupy the field as to exclude State registration requirements of vehicles engaged in interstate commerce, but specifically states that it shall not "affect the powers of taxation of the several States" (U.S. Code, tit. 49, § 302, subd. [b] ).

5. Defendant driver could not [***4] escape prosecution by showing that another was the owner of the vehicle.

6. Since the judgment of conviction was erroneously reversed on the law, the case should be remitted to the County Court for determination on the facts (Code Crim. Pro., § 543-a).

Salzman v. Impellitteri


Court of Appeals of New York


305 N.Y. 414; 113 N.E.2d 543; 1953 N.Y. LEXIS 799

The statutes creating the New York City Transit Authority (L. 1953, chs. 200-208) are permissive only, in a field in which the State is concerned. Assuming that any transfer by the city to the Authority will not be by absolute conveyance, but by a lease of [***2] limited term with reversion to the city, it cannot be said that the legislation is on its face unconstitutional. Whether it will be constitutionally applied, and whether unforeseen constitutional questions will arise, are problems to be dealt with when presented.

587 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Schwarz v. General Aniline & Film Corp.


Court of Appeals of New York


305 N.Y. 395; 113 N.E.2d 533; 1953 N.Y. LEXIS 808


Argued March 4, 1953; reargued June 2, 1953.  
June 4, 1953, decided



DISPOSITION: DYE and FROESSEL, JJ., concur with DESMOND, J.; CARSWELL, J. *, concurs in separate opinion; FULD, J., dissents in opinion in which LEWIS, Ch. J., and CONWAY, J., concur.



* Designated pursuant to section 5 of article VI of the State Constitution in place of Van Voorhis, J., disqualified.

Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner appealed the decision of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), affirming the dismissal of petitioner's action, which sought an order directing respondent to reimburse petitioner's expenses incurred in defense of petitioner's antitrust violations indictment.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was indicted for violations of antitrust laws. Upon pleading nolo contendere and upon rejection by respondent for reimbursement, petitioner applied to the district court for an order directing reimbursement of his litigation expenses. The district court dismissed petitioner's action, holding that the federal court had no power to enter such an order in a criminal cause. The lower state court dismissed petitioner's complaint and held that petitioner's antitrust suit resulted in an adjudication of liability for misconduct in the performance of his duties and that, under N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law § 64, he could not be awarded reimbursement. On appeal, the lower state court's decision was affirmed. Petitioner appealed. This court affirmed, holding that, under Article 6-A of N.Y. Gen. Bus. Law, court-mandated reimbursement could not be had as to expenses of one defending himself against criminal charges.


OUTCOME: The decision, affirming the dismissal of petitioner's action, which sought an order directing respondent to reimburse petitioner's expenses incurred in defense of antitrust violations, was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Corporations - reimbursement of litigation expenses of corporate officials - criminal prosecutions - (1) officer and director may not be reimbursed by corporation for expenses incurred in [***2] defense of criminal prosecution for alleged violation of Sherman Anti-Trust Act, under article 6-A of General Corporation Law - (2) no evidence that provisions for reimbursement, enacted because of court decision to effect that corporation had no common-law duty of reimbursement, were intended to apply to criminal prosecutions - (3) legislation was concerned with civil causes only - (4) provision for reimbursement of expenses in "any action, suit or proceeding" (General Corporation Law, § 64) means civil proceedings only - (5) moreover, such statutory provisions should not be construed as conferring civil jurisdiction on courts having only criminal jurisdiction, in violation of Constitution (N.Y. Const., art. VI, § 11).

1. Petitioner sought reimbursement of expenses from a corporation in a proceeding under article 6-A (§§ 64-68) of the General Corporation Law, alleging that he and the corporation, of which he was an officer and director, had been indicted for alleged violations of the Sherman Anti-Trust Act, that he had pleaded nolo contendere and had been fined $500 and that he had expended money for attorneys' fees and disbursements. Petitioner may not be reimbursed for [***3] such expenses. The Legislature, in enacting article 6-A, did not intend to provide reimbursement to anyone for attorneys' fees incurred in defense of a criminal prosecution against himself.

2. The provisions for reimbursement in article 6-A were enacted after a court decision had been made that a corporation in whose behalf a stockholders' suit had been brought was not obligated at common law to pay legal fees incurred by its directors in defending themselves as individual defendants in such an action. Sections 27-a and 61-a of the General Corporation Law had been passed to overcome the effect of that decision as to fees of corporate officers. Neither of those sections had any relation to a criminal case. In 1945, section 27-a became section 63 in new article 6-A and the remaining sections (64-68) of article 6-A were added (L. 1945, ch. 869). Section 64 contains broadened provisions for assessment against a corporation of the expenses of any person who is made a party to "any action, suit or proceeding" because of his being an officer, director or employee unless it shall have been adjudged that he was liable for neglect or misconduct in the performance of his duties. There [***4] is no evidence that the Legislature intended that these provisions should apply to defense costs in a criminal case.

3. There are, on the other hand, affirmative indications that the Legislature was legislating about civil causes only. For example, the Law Revision Commission's report, with which these statutes originated, and the decisions and writings cited therein, refer only to civil proceedings. When the suggestions of the commission were enacted into law, they were included in the same chapter with the new statutes requiring security from certain plaintiffs in stockholders' suits, indicating that the Legislature considered articles 6 and 6-A in pari materia, and that everything in both articles referred to civil litigation. Article 6-A follows after and is an obvious supplement to article 6, which, in its sections 60 and 61, describes certain civil actions which may be brought by or on behalf of a corporation against its officers or agents for misconduct toward the corporation.

4. The words "any action, suit or proceeding" in section 64 might include criminal proceedings if they stood alone and there were no sure guides to their real significance, but that identical [***5] phrase was found in the old sections 27-a and 61-a which were never claimed to apply to criminal cases.

5. Moreover, section 64, being in derogation of common law, is to be strictly construed. It refers to section 65 which specifies that remuneration is to be sought in the action, suit or proceeding in which the expenses were incurred or in a separate proceeding in Supreme Court. Such provision should not be construed as an unconstitutional grant of jurisdiction in civil matters to a court which is vested with jurisdiction only in criminal prosecutions (N.Y. Const., art. VI, § 11). Since section 65 is thus limited to a civil action or proceeding, the legislative intention was to so limit section 64 also.

 

588 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Commercial Pictures Corp. v. Board of Regents


Court of Appeals of New York


305 N.Y. 336; 113 N.E.2d 502; 1953 N.Y. LEXIS 813


Argued January 7, 1953.  
May 28, 1953, decided



DISPOSITION: LEWIS, J., concurs with FROESSEL, J.; DESMOND, J., votes for affirmance in a separate opinion; CONWAY, J., in a memorandum, concurs in the opinions of FROESSEL and DESMOND, JJ.; DYE, J., dissents in an opinion in which FULD, J., concurs and votes for reversal in a separate opinion; LOUGHRAN, Ch. J., deceased.

Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner motion picture company challenged the judgment of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the third judicial department (New York), which confirmed a determination of respondents, the Motion Picture Division of the State Education Department and the Board of Regents of the University of the State of New York (board of regents), that a certain motion picture was not entitled to be licensed for public exhibition.


OVERVIEW: The motion picture company contended that N.Y. Educ. Law § 122, the statute under which the board of regents refused to license the particular French film, was invalid in that it imposed a prior restraint upon the exercise of freedom of speech and press. On appeal, the court affirmed the judgment of the trial court. The court held that the terms "immoral" and "tend to corrupt morals," as used within the statute relating to standards of sexual morality, were not vague or indefinite. The court held that the decision of the board of regents to refuse to license the film was not improper. The court noted the need to have an agency to which it could entrust the fact-finding power and that the film producer was given due process.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court affirming the board of regents' decision to refuse to license the film was affirmed.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law - censorship - refusal to license "immoral" motion picture - (1) Board of Regents' refusal to license foreign motion picture presenting story of sexual promiscuity, on ground it is "immoral" and "would tend to corrupt morals" (Education Law, § 122), properly confirmed - (2) although constitutional [***2] guarantees of free speech and free press include motion pictures, State may reasonably use its police power to censor films, for health and welfare of people - (3) quoted words of statute sufficiently defined standard for licensing and hence meet constitutional requirements of due process - (4) reasonable basis existed for board's finding and decision.

1. A determination of the Motion Picture Division of the State Education Department and of the Regents of the University of the State of New York refusing to license for public exhibition a foreign, revised motion picture called "La Ronde", which presented the story of a round of sexual promiscuity, on the ground that such film is "immoral" and "would tend to corrupt morals" (Education Law, § 122), was properly confirmed.

2. Although the constitutional guarantees of free speech and free press include motion pictures, the State may reasonably exercise its police power to censor them by prior restraint, for the health and welfare of its inhabitants and the protection of the institution of marriage and the home. Motion pictures have vast potentialities and there is no effective way to suppress the damaging ones except by censorship.  [***3] The people should not be relegated to the industry's own boards of self-review, nor, in the case of foreign films, to the discretion of customs officials. The State has properly placed the power of censorship in the Board of Regents, which is at the head of the State's educational system, rather than in local officials who may at times have varying provincial views.

3. The words "immoral" and "would tend to corrupt morals" (Education Law, § 122) define the standard of judgment sufficiently to meet the constitutional requirements of due process of law.

4. Section 122 of the Education Law was properly applied by the Board of Regents. There is reasonable basis for their finding that the film is immoral and tends to corrupt morals.

590 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re People by Bohlinger


Court of Appeals of New York


305 N.Y. 258; 112 N.E.2d 280; 1953 N.Y. LEXIS 817


Argued February 25, 1953.  
April 23, 1953, decided



DISPOSITION: LEWIS, CONWAY, DESMOND, DYE, FULD and FROESSEL, JJ., concur; LOUGHRAN, Ch. J., deceased.

Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent fraternal society and intervenor committee members sought review of an order by the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed an order dissolving the corporate existence of the fraternal society organized under N.Y. Ins. Law § 14 and directing petitioner Superintendent of Insurance of the State of New York to take possession of its property and liquidate its business.


OVERVIEW: The superintendent sought to dissolve and liquidate the fraternal society because of its involvement in financing Communist Party activities in violation of its charter. The fraternal society and committee members contended that liquidation under N.Y. Ins. Law § 511 applied only to the insurer's financial loss or risk of loss arising out of its business transactions and that § 511 violated due process and their rights to freedom of speech, press, and assembly. The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court. The trial court properly ordered the dissolution and liquidation of the fraternal society because the record supported the conclusion that further operation of the society would prove hazardous in a financial sense under § 511. The fraternal society financed Communist Party organizers and publications and supported political candidates in violation of its charter, N.Y. Ins. Law § 463(3)(c), (d), and N.Y. Penal Law § 671. The court rejected the contention that liquidation caused undue hardship to the society's members who could not otherwise secure insurance because the superintendent arranged for other insurers to provide similar insurance coverage to the society's members.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the lower courts directing the superintendent to dissolve the fraternal society and to take possession of its property and liquidate its business.


HEADNOTES

Insurance - fraternal benefit society - dissolution - (1) Superintendent of Insurance may dissolve and liquidate fraternal benefit society which failed to state its purposes truly in its charter and violated its charter and laws by assisting in financing of Communist party ( Insurance Law, § 463, subd. 3, pars. [c], [d]; § 511, subd. [f]; Penal Law, § 671) - (2) arrangements have been properly made [***2] for reinsurance of benefit society members.

1. The Superintendent of Insurance was properly authorized to dissolve and liquidate a domestic fraternal benefit society which (a) violated its charter and the laws of this State ( Insurance Law, § 511, subd. [f]; Penal Law, § 671) by assisting in the financing of the Communist party, and (b) failed to state its purposes fully and truly in its charter ( Insurance Law, § 463, subd. 3, pars. [c], [d] ).

2. Liquidation will not cause undue hardship to members who cannot otherwise secure insurance protection. Upon authority of the Superintendent of Insurance, his counsel has assured the court that arrangements have been made for reinsurance on the same basis and on substantially the same contract as such members now have.

 

591 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Sternberg v. Rubenstein


Court of Appeals of New York


305 N.Y. 235; 112 N.E.2d 210; 1953 N.Y. LEXIS 818; 36 A.L.R.2d 1136


Argued January 21, 1953.  
April 16, 1953, decided



DISPOSITION: LOUGHRAN, Ch. J., LEWIS, CONWAY and FROESSEL, JJ., concur with FULD, J.; DESMOND, J., dissents in opinion in which DYE, J., concurs.

Ordered accordingly.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent trustee, acting on behalf of creditors, filed suit to hold appellant buyer accountable, under the Bulk Sales Act, N.Y. Pers. Prop. Law § 44 for the value of a batch of off-season shoes sold to the buyer by the bankrupt. The Appellate Division of the Supreme Court (New York) ordered an accounting as well as ordered the buyer to pay the trustee the amount he paid for the shoes, and the buyer appealed.


OVERVIEW: The issue was whether the sale of the off-season merchandise constituted a sale in bulk other than in the ordinary course of trade and in the regular prosecution of the shoe business. The bankrupt had opened a shoe store and stocked the store. After only five months, the inventory declined considerably and consisted of off-season shores. To clear the shelves of those off-season shoes, as well as to obtain cash to pay debts and obtain credit for the purchase of new stock, the bankrupt sold 1,300 pairs of shoes to the buyer, who was a dealer in leftover footwear. The bankrupt conceded that he did not comply with the requirements of the Bulk Sales Act, but there was no indication that there was any intention to defraud any of the bankrupt's creditors. The bankrupt continued in business for six months, then filed a petition for bankruptcy. The court reversed the decision of the appeals court finding that the Bulk Sales Act was never designed to cover the sort of sale effected by the bankrupt and the buyer. The Bulk Sales Act was found to apply only in rare and irregular cases. The court found that the sale and purchase of off-season shoes was no rare and irregular occurrence.


OUTCOME: The judgment that ordered an accounting and ordered the buyer to pay the trustee the amount he paid for the shoes was reversed.


HEADNOTES

Sales - bulk sales - creditors' suit - (1) sale in bulk of part or whole of stock of merchandise except in "ordinary course of trade" and in "regular prosecution of said business" is void as [***2] against seller's creditors unless buyer notifies creditors and keeps inventory of cost prices to seller, for their inspection (Personal Property Law, § 44) - (2) sale in good faith by small retail shoe store of 1,300 pairs of off-season shoes to dealer, for best cash price obtainable, was valid; no intent to defraud creditors shown; seller did not discontinue any line of merchandise and continued in business as before until filing of bankruptcy petition six months later; action for accounting, by trustee in bankruptcy against buyer, dismissed - (3) large retailers of shoes have money to advertise clearance sales of their obsolete shoes or to own outlet stores, but small retailers must get cash from dealers; creditors may set aside fraudulent sales (under Debtor and Creditor Law and Bankruptcy Act).

1. The Bulk Sales Act (Personal Property Law, § 44) provides that "The sale * * * in bulk of any part or the whole of a stock of merchandise * * * pertaining to the conducting of the business of the seller * * * otherwise than in the ordinary course of trade and in the regular prosecution of said business, shall be void as against the creditors of the seller" unless the buyer obtains [***3] from the seller a verified list of all creditors and the amounts owed them, and notifies the creditors personally or by registered mail of the terms of the proposed sale, and obtains from the seller and keeps for the creditors' inspection an inventory showing the cost price to the seller of each article sold.

2. In this case, the seller had opened a family shoe store in the winter and stocked it at a cost of some $25,000. By May, his inventory had declined to around $19,000 and still consisted, in part, of fall and winter styles. To clear his shelves of the off-season shoes, as well as to obtain cash to pay debts and thus obtain credit for the purchase of new summer stock, he sold some thirteen hundred pairs of shoes for $3,549.50 to defendant, a dealer in leftover footwear. There is no suggestion that the seller did not get the best price available, and the Official Referee found that there was no intent to defraud creditors. The seller did not discontinue any branch or line or brand of his merchandise. He continued in business for six months after the sale, until the filing of a petition in bankruptcy. In this action by the trustee in bankruptcy against the buyer for an [***4] accounting, a dismissal of the complaint should be affirmed.

3. The record shows that shoe styles vary sharply from season to season, with the result that last year's "rage" may clog this season's shelves. A merchant seeks to clear his store as rapidly as possible of unsold shoes. Larger stores may widely advertise "clearance sales" or operate their own "special outlet" stores. But the smaller retailer lacks such means and must rely on dealers who specialize in unseasonable obsolete shoes. Such inevitably recurring sales are vital to the operation of the smaller independent retailer and must be regarded, in the words of section 44, as sales made "in the ordinary course of trade and in the regular prosecution of said business". If fraud or covert advantage is later unearthed, a creditor may set the sale aside (under Debtor and Creditor Law, § 276; or under Bankruptcy Act; U.S. Code, tit. 11, § 21, subd. [a], pars. [1], [2]; § 96, subd. [b]; § 107). If the retailer had first to find a dealer who was willing to subject himself to the requirements of section 44, the price would almost certainly be lower; or if the retailer instead held a below-cost clearance sale,  [***5] he would find it difficult later to sell shoes to his customers at regular prices, and thus, contrary to the purpose of section 44, the retailer's creditors would find themselves in a hazardous lot.

 

592 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Cifolo v. General Electric Co.


Court of Appeals of New York


305 N.Y. 209; 112 N.E.2d 197; 1953 N.Y. LEXIS 826


Argued January 5, 1953.  
April 9, 1953, decided



DISPOSITION: LOUGHRAN, Ch. J., DYE, FULD and FROESSEL, JJ., concur with DESMOND, J.; CONWAY, J., dissents in opinion in which LEWIS, J., concurs.

Judgments affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employees challenged the judgment of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the first judicial department (New York) which affirmed the grant of a motion by defendant employer for judgment dismissing the employees' complaints for injuries sustained on the job.


OVERVIEW: The employees contracted partially disabling silicosis as a result of the employer's negligence and violation of state labor laws. The workmen's compensation statute only provided recovery for total disability due to silicosis. The trial court dismissed the claim, and the appellate court affirmed. The court affirmed the dismissal and held that the workmen's compensation scheme was the exclusive remedy for the employees, even though it provided no compensation at all. The provisions of the workmen's compensation law was a complete bar to the maintenance of any of the causes of action in these two complaints.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that affirmed the order dismissing the employees' claim against the employer for work related injuries.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law - silicosis - workmen's compensation as exclusive remedy - partial disability - (1) for silicosis and other dust diseases, Workmen's Compensation Law provides benefits for total disability or death, but not for partial disability; scheme of Workmen's Compensation Law is exclusive; dismissal of common-law actions for such partial disabilities affirmed; due process of law not thereby denied - (2) even though plaintiffs allege that diseases were incurred [***2] because employer failed to provide pure air devices in violation of sections 200 and 299 of Labor Law, Workmen's Compensation Law is their sole recourse.

1. The benefits provided in the Workmen's Compensation Law, for injuries and diseases in employments covered by the law, are "exclusive and in place of any other liability whatsoever * * * at common law or otherwise" (§ 11). For silicosis and other dust diseases, the law provides compensation for total disability or death, but expressly states that "compensation shall not be payable for partial disability due to silicosis or other dust disease" (§ 39; L. 1936, ch. 887).Such limitation of benefits is constitutional and does not deny due process of law. (N.Y. Const., art. I, §§ 6, 18; U.S. Const., 14th Amendt.) Dismissal of these common-law actions for such partial disabilities should be affirmed. If this result is harsh, it is for the Legislature, not the courts, to improve the statute.

2. It is alleged in these common-law actions that plaintiffs incurred silicosis because of defendant employer's violations of the Labor Law ( § 200, entitled "General duty to protect health and safety of employees", and § 299, entitled [***3] "Ventilation, heating and humidity"). Nevertheless, for the reasons above set forth, the employees' only remedy is the Workmen's Compensation Law.

In re Estate of HARBORD


Court of Appeals of New York


305 N.Y. 622; 111 N.E.2d 736; 1953 N.Y. LEXIS 1191


Argued January 20, 1953  
March 6, 1953, decided


HEADNOTES

Taxation - estate tax - trust provided for payment of income to settlor's wife during her life and, upon her death, to settlor for life and, upon his death, for payment of principal to named persons, not including decedent or his estate - Federal taxing statute exempted such transfer from taxation but State statute was found to subject such transfer to estate tax - contention that under rule requiring uniformity of interpretation of Federal and State estate taxing statutes, transfer was not taxable - order properly affirmed.

 

594 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


In re GREEN


Court of Appeals of New York


305 N.Y. 148; 111 N.E.2d 424; 1953 N.Y. LEXIS 835


Argued January 7, 1953.  
March 5, 1953, decided



DISPOSITION: LOUGHRAN, Ch. J., LEWIS, CONWAY, DESMOND, DYE and FROESSEL, JJ., concur.

Order affirmed. [See 305 N.Y. 691.] 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant legatee sought review of an order from the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the First Judicial Department (New York), which affirmed an order of the surrogate court that settled respondent executor's account to have been deposited with the New York City treasurer, pursuant to N.Y. Surr. Ct. Proc. Act § 269.


OVERVIEW: The surrogate court directed that the deceased's bequest of her estate to the legatee, who was a national of Hungary, was to have been deposited with the New York City treasurer for the legatee's account, pursuant to N.Y. Surr. Ct. Proc. Act § 269. The appellate court affirmed despite the legatee's contention that § 269 was unconstitutional and that the bequest should have been placed in a domestic bank account handled by the Hungarian Consulate General. The court affirmed on appeal. The court held that § 269 was not preempted by federal law because its purpose was to insure the intended distribution of estates administered by courts and assure the bequest was transmitted to its proper designate, an area that was outside the scope of federal law. Absent any conflict with national policy, the court held that states were able to exercise power over the administration of estates and § 269 was a valid embodiment of such power. The court also held that § 269 was not in contravention to Hungary's treaty with the United States because that treaty did not sanction transfer of a bequest to the Hungarian Consulate General.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the appellate court that affirmed the surrogate court's order, which directed that the balance due to the legatee was to have been deposited with the New York City treasurer pursuant to the New York Surrogate's Court Procedure Act.


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law - appeal - United States statutes - treaties - wills - payment to legatee residing in communist country - (1) Federal Trading with the Enemy Act (U.S. Code, tit. 50, Appendix, § 5, subd. [b] ) and executive orders issued thereunder do not pre-empt area covered by State statute (Surrogate's Ct. Act, § 269); purpose of Federal regulation entirely unrelated to goal of State enactment; thus no conflict in [***2] application of Federal and State provisions - (2) application by Surrogate of State statute not in contravention of treaty between United States and Hungary; treaty provision allowing consular officers to receive distributive shares from estates on behalf of nonresident countrymen requires that officers shall be able to remit funds to distributees; United States law bars export of such funds to Hungary - (3) State statute does not encroach on national power to regulate foreign commerce (U.S. Const., art. I, § 8, subd. 3); statute adopted pursuant to State's power to regulate transmission of property by will and establish procedure governing wills and administration; State's procedure should be honored in absence of overriding Federal policy - (4) statute properly applied and necessary although funds have been frozen by Federal regulations; possible lifting of freezing order without assurance that legatee will receive funds as required by statute; Surrogate properly relied on Treasury Department's conclusion that conditions in Hungary precluded such assurance that legatee would receive bequest - (5) Surrogate's refusal to grant hearing not violative of due process; statute did not [***3] deprive legatee of property or right to its possession - (6) direction that bequest to resident of Hungary be deposited with city treasurer instead of in Hungarian Consulate's "blocked account" in domestic bank, properly made by Surrogate; statute, pursuant to which Surrogate acted, held valid - (7) constitutional issues necessarily decided; appeal properly taken as of right (Civ. Prac. Act, § 588, subd. 1).

1. Subdivision (b) of section 5 of the Federal Trading with the Enemy Act (U.S. Code, tit. 50, Appendix, § 5, subd. [b] ) which empowers the President to regulate or prohibit any "acquisition, holding, withholding, use, transfer * * * importation of or exportation of * * * any property in which any foreign country or any national thereof has an interest" and Executive Order No. 8389, issued pursuant thereto by the President, prohibiting transactions involving property in which Hungary or any national thereof has at any time on or since March 12, 1941, had any interest, serve a purpose entirely unrelated to the goal of section 269 of the Surrogate's Court Act. The Federal enactment seeks to check trading with the enemy and to stop such uses of foreign property rights as [***4] might imperil national defense, whereas section 269 aims to promote the basic obligation of courts of decedent devolution to effectuate a decedent's wishes respecting disposal of his assets on death by its application where the payment of a bequest to a person resident in a foreign country might be circumvented by partial or total confiscation. Inasmuch as section 269 treats a matter beyond and outside the scope of the Federal law, the area covered by it could not have been pre-empted by the national enactment. There is thus no conflict in the application of the two provisions.

2. The Surrogate's application of section 269 does not contravene provisions of the treaty between the United States and Hungary. Nothing in the treaty compels or sanctions transfer of the bequest to the Hungarian Consular officer. The proviso in article XXI of the treaty which allows a consular officer on behalf of his nonresident countrymen to "receipt for their distributive shares derived from estates in process of probate" requires that the officer must be in a position to "remit any funds so received * * * to the proper distributees". That cannot be done in this case inasmuch as the law of this [***5] country expressly bars export of the funds to Hungary.

3. The New York statute does not encroach on the national power "to regulate Commerce with foreign nations" (U.S. Const., art. I, § 8, subd. 3). The State adopted section 269 pursuant to its power to regulate the manner upon which property within its dominion may be transmitted by will and, incident to such power, to establish the procedure governing probate of wills and processes of administration. In the absence of any overriding Federal policy the State procedure should be honored in accordance with Federal authority which has upheld the right of a State to impose special taxes on legacies left to aliens, to limit the right of foreign consuls to administer estates of their nationals, to grant aliens an unqualified right to inherit property within its borders or to make that right dependent on the existence of a reciprocal right of United States citizens in the alien's country.

4. The Surrogate properly applied the provisions of section 269. The fact that the bequest consisted of "blocked funds" does not mean that the statute is not needed. Inasmuch as the blocking regulations are dependent upon national defense considerations,  [***6] the freezing order might be lifted without assurance that the legatee would receive the funds as required by section 269. The Surrogate properly relied on the conclusion of the United States Treasury Department that "local conditions in Hungary * * * are such that there is not a reasonable assurance that a payee in those areas will actually receive" payment or be able to negotiate them for full value (Code of Fed. Reg., tit. 31, § 211.3, subd. a). This conclusion was directly relevant to the Surrogate's determination and supplied sufficient basis for his application of section 269.

5. The Surrogate's refusal to grant a hearing did not violate the demands of due process and the legatee has not been deprived of her property. On the contrary the statute was designed, and was here invoked, to protect her property to which she retains title and which is hers for the asking when reasonable assurance is given that she will receive it.

6. The Surrogate's direction that a bequest in a will to a resident national of Hungary be deposited with the treasurer of the city of New York for the legatee's account rather than placed in a "blocked account" of the Hungarian Consulate General [***7] in a domestic bank was properly made. Section 269 of the Surrogate's Court Act, pursuant to which the Surrogate acted, is valid.

7. Inasmuch as the determination of the Appellate Division affirming the Surrogate's decree necessarily rested on a decision of constitutional issues, the appeal was properly taken as a matter of right. (Civ. Prac. Act, § 588, subd. 1.)

 

595 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Barsky v. Board of Regents


Court of Appeals of New York


305 N.Y. 89; 111 N.E.2d 222; 1953 N.Y. LEXIS 840


Argued October 23, 1952.  
February 26, 1953, decided



DISPOSITION: LOUGHRAN, Ch. J., LEWIS, CONWAY, DYE and FROESSEL, JJ., concur with DESMOND, J.; FULD, J., dissents in opinion.

Orders affirmed. [See 305 N.Y. 691.] 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner physicians challenged determinations of respondent Board of Regents of the University of the State of New York, which disciplined each of them pursuant to N.Y. Educ. Law § 6514(2)(b) after they were convicted in a federal court of contempt of Congress pursuant to 2 U.S.C.S. § 192. The physicians' proceedings for review under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78 were consolidated on appeal.


OVERVIEW: A federal court convicted three physicians of contempt of Congress in violation of 2 U.S.C.S. § 192 in that each of them failed to obey a subpoena requiring them to produce before a Congressional committee documents pertaining to their membership in a voluntary association. The board suspended the medical licenses of two of the physicians and censured and reprimanded the third. In consolidated proceedings under N.Y. C.P.L.R. art. 78, the physicians sought review. The court affirmed. Disciplinary action under N.Y. Educ. Law § 6514(2)(b) was not limited to those convicted of a crime specifically forbidden by a state penal statute because the statute's clear language referred to conviction either within or without the state. The word "crime" in New York law included misdemeanors as well as felonies. If the legislature had intended to limit the reach of § 6514(2)(b) to offenses involving moral turpitude, it would have stated that limitation explicitly. The court was without jurisdiction to evaluate whether the board's actions were too severe or whether it gave improper weight to certain matters in determining punishment because the board committed no errors of law.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the determinations made by the board in taking disciplinary action against three physicians who were convicted in federal court of contempt of Congress.


HEADNOTES

Physicians and surgeons - disciplinary proceedings - (1) Board of Regents had power to suspend, for several months, licenses of doctors who were convicted by Federal court of misdemeanor of contempt of Congress in disobeying subpoena of Congressional committee; they were "convicted in a court of competent jurisdiction, either within or without this state, of a crime" (Education Law, § 6514, subd. 2, par. [b] ) - (2) "crime" includes misdemeanor (Penal Law, § 2) - (3) Education Law ( § 6514, subd. 2, par. [b] ) not limited to crimes under New York law - (4) Board of Regents may use own good sense if confronted with act which is not criminal in New York - (5) statute is not limited to moral turpitude [***3] or professional misconduct - (6) statute needs no cutting down for constitutionality's sake - (7) Regents took voluminous testimony and must have considered moral right and wrong - (8) Court of Appeals has no jurisdiction to review administrative discretion as to denial of assertedly weighty considerations.

1. The Education Law ( § 6514, subd. 2, par. [b] ) authorizes the Board of Regents to discipline a physician who "has been convicted in a court of competent jurisdiction, either within or without this state, of a crime". Under this statute, the board was authorized, after a hearing, to suspend for several months the license of two physicians and to censure a third physician, each of whom had been convicted by a Federal court of the misdemeanor of contempt of Congress in disobeying a subpoena of a Congressional committee (U.S. Code, tit. 2, § 192) to produce the records of an anti-fascist refugee committee of which he was an executive, and each of whom had paid a fine and served a sentence of imprisonment therefor.

2. The word "crime" includes misdemeanors as well as felonies (Penal Law, § 2).

3. Section 6514 (subd. 2, par. [b] ) of the Education Law clearly does [***4] not limit itself to crimes forbidden by New York law. Therefore, in construing it, it is unnecessary to rely on the public policy implied in section 1330 of the Penal Law, which makes it a misdemeanor willfully to refuse to produce proper and material documents before a committee of the State Legislature.

4. Some reliance must be placed on the good sense and judgment of the Board of Regents, if, as and when they are confronted with a crime in some other State which is regarded in New York as noncriminal.

5. Section 6514 (subd. 2, par. [b] ) of the Education Law is not limited to crimes involving moral turpitude or to crimes directly related to professional ability or misconduct. The Legislature knows how to state such limitations when it so desires (e.g., Education Law, § 7406, subd. 1; and compare Public Health Law, former § 161, with present Education Law, § 6502).

6. Section 6514 (subd. 2, par. [b] ) needs no cutting down for constitutionality's sake. The State can attach exacting conditions to a professional license.

7. The Regents took voluminous testimony as to the character of the physicians and their purposes in disobeying the Federal subpoenas. In [***5] fixing the penalties, the Regents are presumed to have taken into account the moral right or wrong of the physicians.

8. The Court of Appeals has no jurisdiction to review the questions whether the Regents, in deciding on punishment, were too severe, ignored weighty considerations, and acted on matters not proper for the Regents' consideration.

People v. Slater


Court of Appeals of New York


304 N.Y. 896; 110 N.E.2d 503; 1953 N.Y. LEXIS 1086


Argued December 4, 1952  
January 15, 1953, decided


HEADNOTES

Crimes - defendants convicted of unlawfully placing out children for adoption and of receiving compensation for same in violation of Social Welfare Law (§ 374) and Penal Law (§ 487-a) - contentions in Court of Appeals that said statutes under which they were convicted were unconstitutional, that crimes had not been committed in county of New York, that trial court's charge was improper, that essential element of crime had not been proven, and that adopting parents were accomplices - judgments of conviction properly affirmed.

 

597 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Yonkers v. Rentways, Inc.


Court of Appeals of New York


304 N.Y. 499; 109 N.E.2d 597; 1952 N.Y. LEXIS 719


Argued October 20, 1952.  
December 5, 1952, decided



DISPOSITION: LOUGHRAN, Ch. J., LEWIS, CONWAY, DESMOND, DYE and FROESSEL, JJ., concur.

Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, owners and lessees of land, challenged a judgment of the Appellate Division of the Supreme Court in the second judicial department (New York), which affirmed the trial court's finding in favor of respondent city in the city's action to restrain and enjoin appellants from using a residential lot in violation of a city zoning ordinance.


OVERVIEW: Appellants operated a garage in a business district of the city. Immediately behind the garage was a vacant lot that was in a residential zone. Appellants later built a second story on the garage that included large doors for trucks to use as an entrance. The trucks then began entering the garage through the vacant lot. The city filed an action to restrain and enjoin appellants from using the vacant residential lot, claiming that such use was in violation of a city zoning ordinance. The trial court found in favor of the city, and the appellant court affirmed. On appeal, the court affirmed, holding that N.Y. Gen. City Law § 20(25) authorized the city to prohibit the location of a garage or any part of one on vacant residential land. Also, appellants use of their land in a residential district as a means of ingress to and egress from business property was the conduct of a "trade" or "industry" pursuant to § 20(25). Further, the city ordinance did not confiscate appellants' property or otherwise offend against constitutional guarantees because the second floor was constructed eight years after the enactment of the zoning ordinance that placed the vacant lot in a residential zone.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of the city in its action to restrain and enjoin appellants from using a residential lot in violation of a city ordinance.


HEADNOTES

Municipal corporations - zoning - injunction - (1) judgment properly restrained owners and lessees of garage from using rear adjoining residentially zoned lot, which they also own and lease, for access to and from residential street for garaging vehicles, for parking, and for underground gasoline tanks - (2) two-story [***2] addition built on garage lot in 1928; street on which rear residential lot fronts situated higher than garage lot, so vehicles might cross residential lot and enter second floor of addition through rear doors; municipality not estopped from enforcing zoning ordinance by previously granted building permit - (3) municipality not estopped by laches; builder knew or should have known of residential zone - (4) such vehicular access began after enactment of zoning ordinance, hence it is not pre-existing nonconforming use - (5) no confiscation of property - (6) no showing that second floor could not be profitably used by building of ramp from business street.

1. A city is entitled to an injunction restraining the owners and lessees of a garage in a business zone from using the rear adjoining lot - which they also own and lease and which faces a parallel street, but which is in a residence zone - as (a) a regular means of ingress and egress for the business of garaging vehicles, (b) for the parking of vehicles, and (c) for the installation of underground gasoline tanks. Such application of the zoning ordinance comes within the city's power "To regulate and restrict the location of trades [***3] and industries" ( General City Law, § 20, subd. 25).

2. The street on which the residential lot fronts is higher than the garage lot. In 1928 a two-story addition was built on the garage lot. Vehicles coming from the residential street and crossing the residential lot could enter the new structure at its second floor rear, where two doors were built for that purpose. The municipality is not estopped from enforcing its zoning ordinance by the issuance in 1928 of a permit for said building addition with rear doors.

3. Nor is the municipality estopped by laches. Moreover, the owners knew or should have known in 1928 that they were planning to use a lot which had been residentially zoned since the original zoning ordinance of 1920.

4. The present zoning ordinance was enacted in 1928. The Appellate Division has affirmed an Official Referee's finding that the residential lot was first used for such ingress and egress in 1929, and that therefore such use cannot be adjudged to be a pre-existing nonconforming use.

5. Thus, there is no confiscation of property or other violation of constitutional guarantees in restraining the owners from using the residentially zoned lot [***4] for daily moving of vehicles to and from the shelter of the garage building.

6. Furthermore there is no proof of confiscation in fact. Completely absent is any showing that the second floor's present use could not be continued by building a passageway or ramp within or alongside the building so as to permit access to the second floor from the business street or, if such construction were impossible, that such floor could not be converted to some other profitable use.

1165 Park Ave. Corp. v. McGoldrick


Court of Appeals of New York


304 N.Y. 857; 109 N.E.2d 719; 1952 N.Y. LEXIS 1030


Argued October 23, 1952  
December 4, 1952, decided


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law - landlord and tenant - rent regulation - complaint in action by owner of apartment building for judgment declaring State Residential Rent Law (as amd. by L. 1951, ch. 443) unconstitutional, dismissed on merits by Special Term - contention by owner in Court of Appeals that provisions of statute (§ 4, subd. 4, par. [a]), were discriminatory and arbitrary in limiting owners of apartments with five or more units to net return of 4% and allowing additional recoupment of operational costs to owners of four rental units or less, and to hotels, rooming houses and co-operative apartments - judgment dismissing complaint on merits affirmed.

Teeval Co. v. McGoldrick


Court of Appeals of New York


304 N.Y. 859; 109 N.E.2d 720; 1952 N.Y. LEXIS 1031


Argued October 23, 1952  
December 4, 1952, decided


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law - landlord and tenant - rent regulation - complaint in action by owner of apartment building for judgment declaring unconstitutional State Residential Rent Law (as amd. by L. 1951, ch. 443), dismissed on merits by Special Term - contention in Court of Appeals by said owner that provision of statute (§ 4, subd. 4, par. [a]) limiting owners of residential property to return of 4% was confiscatory, and that it was discriminatory since owners of commercial property were allowed return of 6% - judgment dismissing complaint on merits affirmed.

 

600 of 2939 DOCUMENTS


Gorman v. New York


Court of Appeals of New York


304 N.Y. 865; 109 N.E.2d 881; 1952 N.Y. LEXIS 1034


Argued October 24, 1952  
December 4, 1952, decided


HEADNOTES

Constitutional law - municipal corporations - New York City - police officers - power of city to amend pension legislation - action for judgment declaring unconstitutional Local Law No. 44 of 1951 of City of New York, which amended predecessor statute permitting immediate and automatic retirement, so as to provide that members of police pension fund could not be retired until thirty days after application therefor - contentions by plaintiffs that Local Law No. 44 was unconstitutional in that predecessor statute had been enacted by State Legislature and thus city was without power to modify or supersede it (N.Y. Const., art. IX, § 12), and that said local law contravened provision of State Constitution (art. V, § 7) which rendered membership in pension or retirement system contractual [***5] - complaint properly dismissed.
